science. In this chapter, we relate the work done so far to a single,
inﬂuential, philosopher of science, Karl Popper, to see both how the
elements introduced so far articulate together and what the necessary
limits are of such an approach to scientiﬁc understanding in linguistics.
Importantly, we will also see that thinking about the different elements of
Popper’s approach to science brings into focus concepts that, while
arguably acknowledged tacitly in corpus research, are not usually con-
sidered explicitly. These ideas are, we would argue, quite independently
of Popper’s framework, helpful ones for corpus linguists to consider.
The choice of Popper as the philosopher on whom to base our
approach was, in equal measures, a question of taste, inﬂuence and
necessity. It was a question of taste in that his philosophy, as we will
show in this chapter, aligns reasonably well with the choices made so far;
broadly speaking, he is a philosopher whose thinking is in line with our
own as presented in Chapter 1. Importantly, it aligns well with our choice
of a broadly scientia realis approach to the study of language – Popper is
deeply concerned with having a practical impact on the process of scien-
tiﬁc discovery.16 He is seeking to ‘have an impact on scientiﬁc practice
and several aspects of his philosophy of science clearly reveal this con-
cern with practical intervention’ (Mulkay and Gilbert 1981: 389). Corpus
linguistics, perhaps above all else, has also sought to have a practical
impact on the practice of linguists and others beyond linguistics who deal
with language data.
It was a question of inﬂuence in that Popper is ‘our most inﬂuential
contemporary philosopher of science’ (Robergs 2017) and he ‘continues
to be just about the only philosopher who can seize the imagination and
15 This common sense view of corpus linguistics is well reﬂected in works such as Biber and
Egbert (2020).
16 Note, however, that this alignment needs ﬁnessing – see the discussion of naturalism and
anti-naturalism in Section 4.1.
30
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 43 -->

command the loyalty of successful professional scientists’ (Godfrey-
Smith 2016: 104). His approach to science continues to be inﬂuential in
spite of challenges to it since it was initially written, which are considered
in Chapter 3.
It was also a matter of necessity. We noted at the beginning of this book
that corpus linguists have claimed that the corpus approach is scientiﬁc.
When doing so, corpus linguists often draw on ideas from Popper. He is
the major philosopher of science that has been cited by corpus linguists
when they have appealed to the scientiﬁc method as a justiﬁcation of the
corpus linguistics. For example, Stubbs has cited Popper’s work exten-
sively over the years.
Key Thinkers: Michael Stubbs (1947)
Michael Wesley Stubbs is a linguist who publishes widely on different areas of
linguistics, in particular phraseology and corpus stylistics. He was Professor of
English Linguistics at the University of Trier, Germany (1990–2013). In his
work, he provides multiple examples of notable patterns discoverable using
corpus techniques as well as discussions of more general epistemological
principles related to corpus data and important ideas in the ﬁeld.
Stubbs (2013) explores some of the arguments presented in Popper
(1975) when considering processes of induction in corpus linguistics and
when setting aside the distinction between corpus-based and corpus-
driven approaches to language as argued for by Tognini-Bonelli (2001).
Stubbs (2001a) uses Popper (1994) when considering the nature of corpus
data, while Stubbs (1994) cites Popper17 in an article considering the
potential contribution of corpora to critical linguistics. Stubbs (2007)
discusses dualist and pluralist models of knowledge in linguistics by
contrasting the views of Searle (1969) and Popper (1994). Similarly,
Leech (1992) appeals to Popper (1972) in his framing of corpus linguistics
as a scientiﬁc approach to the study of language. One of the elements of
Popper’s approach to science that Leech includes in the scientiﬁc
approach he outlines (Leech 1992: 112), falsiﬁcation, has been touched
on by a number of other corpus linguists too, including McEnery and
Hardie (2011) and Stefanowitsch (2020: 67–68). Corpus linguists, of
course, are not alone in appealing to Popper in this way – a memorable
example of a generative linguist appealing to Popper comes from
17 While no clear citation is provided, the author is clearly referring to Popper’s 1978
Tanner Lecture on Human Values, available in McMurrin (1980).
What Is Science?
31
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 44 -->

Brame’s (1976) attack on generative semantics, where he draws on
Popper to claim of generative semantics that (Brame 1976: 45):
these psychological theories cannot be considered scientiﬁc theories because
they cannot be refuted. This artiﬁce of immunization is, according to Popper,
what distinguishes prescientiﬁc or pseudoscientiﬁc theory from genuine science.
Brame stops short of turning the same critical lens on generative gram-
mar, which is to be regretted, especially as he concedes that ‘the ultimate
fate of any theory of language will be determined not so much by
metatheoretical arguments for it as by its ability to deal with the base
primary data in a revealing and insightful manner’ (Brame 1976: 3).
A fuller exploration of Popper’s views by Brame may also have cast
light on the apparent tension between Brame’s approving quotation
from Chomsky (Brame 1976: 4) on the value of a precise and technical
approach to linguistic theorising and Popper’s view that such an approach
‘leads us to empty controversies about words’ that Popper thought ‘to be
characteristic of linguistic philosophy’ (Stokes 1998: 90).
While it is helpful for linguists to reach out to the philosophy of science
in understanding and justifying what they have done, there is a problem
with the preceding approach. It is piecemeal. Philosophers are typically
cited in support of speciﬁc points. A sustained engagement with a whole
framework is vanishingly rare – some authors, such as Leech (1992) seem
to engage more fully with a framework, in this case Popper’s, but does not
clearly forge the link. Others, such as Stubbs (2007), engage in some depth
with speciﬁc issues but still fail to engage the whole framework proposed
by Popper. Yet we need a coherent framework as linguists as without it ‘in
the face of our complex problem . . . we can succeed only if we consciously
and consistently apply a methodology of science and only if this methodol-
ogy is not fundamentally ﬂawed’ (Wächtershäuser 1995: 185). This chapter
and the two following it attempts to see whether, through the application
of a consistent framework of science, we can ﬁnd a scientiﬁc approach to
linguistics, and corpus linguistics in particular. Should we see ﬂaws, we will
also consider those and what we can do about them.
Key Thinkers: Geoffrey Leech (1936–2014)
Geoffrey Neil Leech was a linguist who published widely on linguistics.
Beginning with his early works, principally rooted in stylistics through analyses
of advertising, prose and poetry, he also built upon his experiences at the Survey
of English Usage, where he studied and later worked with Randolph Quirk, to
begin to publish on English grammar and to build corpora. In building corpora,
32
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 45 -->

he also became aware of the need to process them in ways that were of use to
the linguist, notably by the automatic addition of linguistic information
to corpus texts. This led him to work with computer scientist Roger Garside to
construct the ﬁrst part-of-speech tagger based on a probabilistic rather than
a rule-based approach to the task. The resulting tool was one of the ﬁrst highly
effective automated language-processing tools. Further work included colla-
boration with Fred Jelinek and his team at the IBM T.J. Watson Research Centre
looking at a similar approach to parsing and anaphora resolution and to work
with market research companies to develop automated sense tagging to help
with opinion survey work.
As a result of the piecemeal approach to the philosophy of science in corpus
linguistics, many of the issues that Popper engages with that will be covered
in this book are not touched upon in previous (corpus) linguistic explora-
tions, even though they are, as we hope readers of this book will come to
agree, illuminating concepts to utilise. Using Popper, or other philosophers
for that matter, as a resource to cite selectively to support a particular point
is certainly convenient. Given the plurality of views that philosophers pre-
sent, it may also be said to be understandable. However, by citing a range of
philosophers, the possibility exists that researchers may pick their way
through philosophy, creating something of a hodge-podge of ideas that
reﬂect the views of no-one philosopher but that does conveniently support
the overall approach that the researcher would prefer. As a consequence,
ideas taken out of their overall context may become distorted – so, for
example, consider the statement ‘According to Popper, scientiﬁc theories
can never be proven, but only refuted’ (Fischer-Starcke 2010: 22). This is
a slight distortion of Popper – Popper, as will be seen, permits provisional
truths and, contrary to what a simple contrast of Kuhn (1962) and Popper
might lead one to believe, Popper has a place for the social in his philosophy
of science in determining provisional truths – his is a social theory of science
(Jarvie 2001). Also, as will be seen, falsiﬁcation is more often aimed at
statements within theories rather than at theories wholesale. Similarly,
Fischer-Starcke (2010: 22), in a discussion of Popper and scientiﬁc theory,
believes that ‘results from corpus analyses can be corroborated or doubted,
but neither veriﬁed or falsiﬁed . . . due to the probabilistic nature of corpus
linguistics’. Yet corpus linguistics does not encounter any restrictions that
do not apply to other sciences that depend upon observation and, as
Sampson (2002: 5) makes clear, Popper can ‘handle the case of statistical
theories’. The problems of induction that corpus linguistics faces are com-
monplace in the sciences and are well known and debated by philosophers.
What Is Science?
33
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 46 -->

Moisl (2015: 1), in a brief outline of Popper’s scientiﬁc method, also states
that ‘no scientiﬁc hypothesis is ever proven because it is always open to
falsiﬁcation by new evidence from observation’. Yet once again a brief
statement of the theory eliminates much subtlety from Popper’s thinking,
which provides for provisional truths and begs a number of questions
including ‘what is scientiﬁc?’, ‘what is a hypothesis?’ and ‘what counts as
observable evidence?’.
Popper’s ideas are part of a system – plucking parts from the system, or
trimming down the deﬁnition of the system to present an overview intro-
duces unhelpful distortions. So we will engage fully here with Popper’s
(2002a) work The Logic of Scientiﬁc Discovery and consider the extent to
which corpus linguistics conforms with it and, by viewing corpus linguistics
through the optics provided by that approach to science, gain an insight
into a range of issues in corpus linguistics. We will also draw from Popper’s
other works, and from interpreters of Popper, where needs be. By taking
one approach to the philosophy of science, we hope to demonstrate both
the breadth and subtlety of Popper’s system of belief while also retaining
the integrity of the concepts introduced within the whole system in which
they were developed. This work will continue into Chapter 3 and towards
the end of that chapter (Section 3.10) we will deal brieﬂy with the work of
Kuhn (1962) and Lakatos (1974) to present a slightly broader view of
Popper and his work in comparison with other philosophers of science.
As will become apparent, we believe that the differences between Popper
and Kuhn in particular have been overstated.
Engaging with Popper also achieves something else. It would be most
convenient if, by accident or more likely by design, we found in exploring
Popper a simple conﬁrmation for much of what has been written so far in
this book. In part we do, yet as will be seen, the ideas of Popper also allow
us to return to some of the concepts discussed in Chapter 1 and to
problematise them. This leads to some ideas being reﬁned and to new
ideas being introduced to help address the key issues that Popper raises.
As a result, our view of what it means to ‘do’ corpus linguistics is also
reﬁned in the process. Let us begin our exploration of Popper and his ideas
by returning to the question of what science and the scientiﬁc method is.
2.1 A Simple Model of Science
Let us begin by presenting Popper’s theory of science. In contrast to the
traditional view (e.g. Locke and Newton), for Popper science does not start
with an observation; science starts with a problem. Popper himself distilled
this process into what he called a tetradic scheme for problem solving
34
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 47 -->

(Popper 1972: 164, see Figure 2.1). We start with a problem that we attempt
to solve – ‘Knowledge does not start from perceptions or observations’
(Popper 1976: 88), the ‘starting-point . . . is always a problem’ (Popper 1976:
89).18 As a result of our work on that problem, we develop a tentative
hypothesis. We then cycle through a process of error elimination and
reformulation of the hypothesis until we feel the hypothesis to be corrobo-
rated to the degree that we can proceed to consider a further problem.19
This approach coheres well with the corpus linguist’s approach to the study
of language to such a degree that we propose the following principle:
Principle 9: Corpus analysis does not pave a pathway to certainty – it is
a problem-solving exercise and requires a constant engagement with new
problems and the revisiting of old problems.
Popper’s simple, elegant formulation of problem solving thus applies
as well, we would argue, to linguistics as it does to physics. However, what
appears simple at ﬁrst glance, as is so often the case, is a highly complex
matter when we explore it in more depth. To begin the plunge into those
depths, we need to consider how such a simple model of problem solving
can be undertaken scientiﬁcally.
What do we do when we explore a question in a scientiﬁc way? Some
philosophers have argued that it is about understanding, controlling and
predicting (Quine 1990: 2). Popper’s view aligns with this, but is oriented
strongly to the process of investigation and the nature of claims that we
may make based upon those investigations. In simple terms, we might say
that in science we make some claims – what we may call hypotheses –
about a subject and proceed to test them. Such hypotheses we shall also
call statements in line with Popper’s usage. Following on from the think-
ing presented in Chapter 1 of this book, we believe that the testing of
Problem 1
Tentative 
hypothesis
Error 
elimination
Problem 2
 
Figure 2.1 Popper’s tetradic scheme of problem solving
18 Though an important point for corpus linguists is that ‘observation becomes something
like a starting-point only if it reveals a problem; or in other words if it surprises us’
Popper (1976: 89).
19 For a further discussion of this model of problem solving, in which eight stages of error
elimination are identiﬁed, see Popper and Eccles (1977).
2.1 A Simple Model of Science
35
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 48 -->

these hypotheses can only proceed through tests against some objective
reality. Yet before the tests are carried out, we need to consider questions
such as ‘what counts as evidence?’ and ‘how does one explore hypotheses
in a meaningful way?’, which lie beneath the surface of this apparently
simple formulation of what science is. Even if we set these matters aside
for a moment, we also need to consider what it might be fair to say on the
basis of conclusions we draw from our experiments – or in the case of
corpus linguists from our engagement with observational data in pursuit
of a research question. So far, we have accepted that corpus linguistics
commonly proceeds through reasoning by induction – on the basis of
examining a ﬁnite body of language, one draws conclusions about lan-
guage in general (Principle 4). In practice, however, the boundary
between induction and deduction can appear blurred, and the two may
interact. Note that corpus linguistics is not alone in working in this way,
especially in its use of induction – it is common throughout the sciences,
and in other subjects that use large bodies of observed evidence – for
example, in the natural sciences or the newly fashioned data sciences –
conclusions are drawn broadly on the basis of inductive reasoning.
Though the data sets in question may be very large, they are a subset of
all possible data. While it might be possible to add to such data sets, it is
very difﬁcult, indeed perhaps impossible, to escape reasoning by induc-
tion when using them. Consider present-day British English. If we wish to
explore whether or not the were-subjunctive still exists in spoken present-
day British English, we could do so in many ways. For the sake of
discussion, here we will consider three approaches:
A) Native speaker introspection.
B) Controlled experiment.
C) Using corpus data such as the Spoken BNC2014 (Love et al. 2017).
In each case, inference of some sort would be used. In A we would be
generalising from personal reﬂection and observation. Similarly in B we
would generalise based on the results obtained from an experiment with
a ﬁxed number of respondents (for the sake of argument, let us say we
have six respondents) in a small-scale inductive experiment, while in
C we would be generalising from patterns of usage observed in approxi-
mately 10 million words of speech from 672 speakers. Let us assume that
in each case we ﬁnd that the were-subjunctive does indeed seem to have
disappeared from use at present. Can we extend the inference further,
concluding, without fear of contradiction, that this is the case for present-
day English as a whole and will remain so in the future? Can we predict,
based on observations of past behaviour, future behaviour? We can do
36
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 49 -->

both only on the basis of inference, most likely through ampliative
reasoning, because in each case a generalisation from a ﬁnite set of
observations to a much larger population is occurring and we are moving
from the observed to the inferred. The key role for observation is that it
‘lends only negative evidence’ (Quine 1990: 13) by refuting inferences.
Expressing this more technically, we are moving from singular state-
ments to making universal statements – while our hypothesis might be
that the were-subjunctive does not exist in present-day English, the
statements we can make about this are singular – as a native speaker
one can say that this statement is true in my experience (preceding
approach A), as an experimenter one can say that the results of my
experiment with my speakers says this is true for those speakers
(approach B), while with our corpus we can say that for the speakers in
the corpus it appears to be true that nobody uses the were-subjunctive
anymore (approach C). On the basis of any of these, the universal state-
ment is assumed, that is, the overall hypothesis itself, is true. This is the
very essence of induction, a model of inferencing whereby we base
‘general statements on accumulated observations of speciﬁc instances’
(Magee 1982: 19). Yet ‘no number of singular observation statements,
however large, could logically entail an unrestricted general statement’
(Magee 1982: 20). This is the very essence of the problem of induction,
which we will explore in the next section.
2.2 Problems of Induction and Veriﬁcation
Induction entails a leap of faith, from the known (observed) to the
unknown, that which has not been observed. A simple example, adapted
from Russell (1912: 98) will serve to show the problem of inductive
reasoning. A chicken is fed every day of its life by a farmer. It may, by
induction, conclude that every morning when the farmer appears it will
be fed. The induction serves them well right up to the last day of their life
when the farmer appears and sends the chicken to be served as lunch.
Slightly less violently, this problem of induction is often summed up by
a quotation, often attributed to Hume or Mill,20 which sums up a point
from Mill (1843: 378–380):
20 Curiously, the quote is often used and attributed to Mill or Hume, but we do not ﬁnd
that speciﬁc quote in the work of either philosopher. We use the quote here simply
because of its currency and the succinct summing up of the point it makes. We also note
that others in the literature have remarked on the absence of this quote from the
writings of Mill and Hume, for example, Reed et al. (2019).
2.2 Problems of Induction and Veriﬁcation
37
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 50 -->

no amount of observations of white swans can allow the inference that all
swans are white, but the observation of a single black swan is sufﬁcient to
refute that conclusion.
This shows in a rather direct way the problem of induction – Europeans
believed for many centuries that one could move without fear of contra-
diction, from the speciﬁc statement based on the observation of swans that
they could see, all of which were white, to the universal statement ‘Swans
are white’, that is, swans, anywhere in space and time, are white. This view of
the world was up-ended for Europeans when in 1697 Dutch explorers led by
Willem de Vlamingh encountered black swans in Australia (Figure 2.2
showing a black swan in New Zealand). The universal statement about
the colour of swans, based on observations in a limited area of space and
time, was proved to be false when the space in which the statement was
tested was expanded. The support for the universal statement on the basis of
observations in the world known at that point to Europeans alone was based
on a leap of faith, to wit that what they observed was typical of an
unbounded context in space and time. Yet the leap of faith nestled at the
heart of induction may clearly be misplaced.
This problem of induction poses a problem in particular for what we
may call the Newtonian view of science – that, through observation and
experimentation, we may discern immutable laws, which apply perfectly
to the real world. Within what is conceived of as Newtonian science, there
are no ifs, buts or maybes, rather we ﬁnd a ‘pervasive role of the quest for
Figure 2.2 Black swan in Christchurch, New Zealand – home of Popper’s ﬁrst academic post
38
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 51 -->

certainty in Newton’s thought’ (Guicciardini 2009: 20).21 This certainty
was rooted in a belief in induction as ‘Newton believed that science could
make valid assertions about unobservable entities and properties . . .
from the hardness of observable objects, one could infer the hardness
of their constituent particles’ (Gauch 2003: 59). Any uncertainty was
limited to cases that could be found and ultimately be either regularised
or classiﬁed as exceptions to general rules that would still stand (see
Newton in the translation of Cajori 1947: 400). Uncertainty is manage-
able and can be overcome in this worldview, it is not a central, inescap-
able feature of science. The Newtonian worldview is one in which
induction and deduction go hand in hand, yet it is one in which the two
are not equal partners, with ‘propositions collected by general induction
from phenomena’ being treated ‘as accurately or very nearly true’
(Williams and Steffens 1978: 286).22
Yet as Popper points out, induction does not and cannot lead to
certainty and veriﬁcation of hypotheses because of the gap in the logical
procedure (the leap of faith). Based on induction, what we are assuming
of the universal may not, in fact, be true either of the whole population at
any given point in time or through time. Further observations might show
this to be the case or it may not – this is ever present as a possibility but
essentially unknowable. This is something corpus linguists need to
acknowledge and be ever mindful of.
Principle 10: Corpus linguistics, being closely linked to processes of induction
(see Principle 4), also entails the problems of induction which in turn points
towards uncertainty (Principle 9).
So, at the very least, we should be cautious in making claims based on
induction. However, we would argue, and this chapter will show, that
there are some inductions that we should be more cautious of than others
and there are yet others that do not really qualify as sound bases for
scientiﬁc induction at all. We will return to this, and our three example
methods, later in the chapter. For now, it is important to note that Popper
21 In reality, however, Newton himself came to accept that likelihoods rather than certain-
ties were what may be attained in some areas of science. See Shapiro (1993, 2004).
22 With reference back to Chapter 1 of this book, Newton’s reliance on induction can in
part be understood as a reactions against the scientia rationalis of thinkers such as
Descartes who argued for deductive reasoning within a metaphysical framework of
thought. Newton’s scientia realis reliance on induction through experimentation and
observation runs counter to this. See Gauch (2003: 224) for further discussion.
2.2 Problems of Induction and Veriﬁcation
39
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 52 -->

does not accept induction, favouring rather what he calls a ‘quasi-
inductive’ (Popper 2002a : 276–278)23 approach to science for reasons
we can now begin to examine. Yet as with our discussion of deduction
and induction in Chapter 1, Popper’s work relates to both, but unlike
Newton a salience is given to deduction in Popper’s framework, as it is by
projection from theory that scientiﬁc research is directed.
Problems in linguistics as well as science, truly begin the moment we
think of a hypothesis. Why, in the previous example, did we light upon
the example of the were-subjunctive? There were many hypotheses we
might have made. Why this one? Why ask any question of a corpus, what
is the genesis of that question? Which are the good questions, which are
the bad or worthless ones? When considering questions such as these, we
would argue that, at the very least, we need a framework within which to
evaluate the questions that might be asked of the data. Some are surely
better, or more appropriate, than others? Initially this view can appear to
be limiting, indeed it may even be castigated as ascientiﬁc. Perhaps we
should approach questions in science in an utterly credulous fashion, not
paying regard to previous ﬁndings but simply demanding that any collec-
tion of the data describes itself to us with minimal human interference.
Some advocates of data science seem to believe that this may indeed be
possible, as claimed by Butler et al. (2018: 547):
With machine learning, given enough data and a rule-discovery algorithm,
a computer has the ability to determine all known physical laws (and poten-
tially those that are currently unknown) without human input.
These scientists seem entirely blind to the problem of induction.
Similarly, they are blind to the substantial human intervention hidden
behind machine learning – the coding of the algorithm, the setting of
parameters, the choice of data, the encoding of data, the development of
ontologies – all these are really about the choice of hypothesis to be
examined, at least indirectly. They are also silent on the role that humans
play in explaining the results produced by such analyses. What appears to
be a statistical magic wand is, in fact, a screen drawn across a lot of human
choice and, crucially, a screen drawn across the problem of induction. In
fact, this is a good example of an issue with induction that Popper calls
a priorism. One has to assume that some things are true, or given, to avoid
a problem of inductive recursion – something outside of the system has to
set the system so that it does not loop back on itself, running inferences to
23 Though see Talbot (2015: 20–21) for a more critical account of Popper’s quasi-inductive
approach and its relationship to the quasi-inductive framework of Hempel (1945).
40
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 53 -->

support inferences endlessly; if X, then Y – we believe we have X because
we have Y and we believe Y because we have X. This is broken by the
assumption, or silent acceptance of X, which no longer requires justiﬁca-
tion. The problem of induction, strongly associated in modern philosophy
with Hume (1748) courtesy of Kant24 dubbing it ‘Hume’s problem’,25
points to the problem that exists in moving from singular to universal
statements on the basis of limited data in what we may broadly conceive
of as space (assuming all swans are white, when in fact, beyond our
current ability to observe, there exist black swans) and, on the other
hand, time as the past may be no good guide to the future (just because
in the past all swans were white, one should not assume that all swans in
the future will be white also).
An equally misplaced view is that of the corpus-driven approach to
linguistic analysis. Consider the following:
In a corpus-driven approach the commitment of the linguist is to the integrity
of the data as a whole, and descriptions aim to be comprehensive with respect
to corpus evidence . . . theoretical statements are fully consistent with, and
reﬂect directly, the evidence provided by the corpus . . . . Examples . . . . are
not adjusted in any way to ﬁt the predeﬁned categories of the analyst;
recurrent patterns and frequency distributions are expected to form the
basic evidence for linguistic categories; the absence of a pattern is considered
potentially meaningful. (Tognini-Bonelli 2001: 84)
Of passing interest in this statement is the call to view data as being
inviolate, not capable of being manipulated – ‘not adjusted in any way’.
This is a scientiﬁc view that could be seen to hark back to the Aristotelian
view of the world in which data could only be that which was observed, no
manipulation of the observed being possible (Gauch 2003: 48–49). So, if
a geologist saw a rock on the beach, they could study the rock, but not
heat it, attempt to dissolve it or otherwise tamper with it to examine its
nature. This is not a very productive approach to science and it is not the
one proposed here. Of more interest in this case is the elevation of the
singular statement – what is described is what is in the corpus and only
what is in the corpus. The implication seems to be that this in itself is
sufﬁcient. However, the existence of negative evidence and inferences
drawn from it imply that the singular statement ‘X does not occur in the
corpus’ may be viewed to be a universal statement ‘X does not occur in
24 See Kant (1783). For a discussion of Hume’s impact on Kant see Kuehn (1983).
25 There were philosophers earlier than Hume who developed similar critiques of induc-
tion, however, notably the Greek sceptic Sextus Empiricus. See Weintraub (1995).
2.2 Problems of Induction and Veriﬁcation
41
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 54 -->

the language represented by the corpus’. This again is the problem of
induction writ large – because it does not occur within what we see in
a ﬁnite, space-time bound, data set, we will assume that in a non space–
time bound context, or a more loosely bound context, we will not see
X. Hanging over the quote are also questions such as ‘descriptions of
what?’, ‘chosen by whom?’ and ‘why choose to describe that?’. While
a naïve, Aristotelian, approach to corpus data seems to have its advan-
tages, it is likely to beg as many questions as it answers and to pose
genuine difﬁculties for the analyst, which is why the framework has
been abandoned in the study of science as it leads to ‘confusion about
the integration and relative inﬂuences of philosophical presuppositions,
empirical evidence, and deductive and inductive logic’ (Gauch 2003: 48) .
So the question of what question is to be asked is, actually, a vexed one.
In the example discussed earlier, the were-subjunctive was chosen
because one of the authors happens to have written on this topic so,
when thinking of what a sensible question to ask might be, they thought
of this one. Yet notice here that we too are pulling a screen across some of
our reasoning – what is a ‘sensible’ question and how do you distinguish it
apart from a nonsensical one? This is a problem that a philosophy of
science should allow us to address and it is one which, as this chapter will
discuss, Popper allows us to address. Note that in approaching this issue
we will accept that people have heads that overﬂow with ideas – we are
clearly not able to control that, nor would we wish to. Yet not all ideas are
scientiﬁc and while we have no wish to try to regulate thought in order to
push away the non-scientiﬁc, we do need a ﬁlter through which such
thoughts may pass to be accepted either as questions that may be sub-
jected to meaningful testing or not. We want to build and exercise what
Popper calls a logic of knowledge – a system which ‘consists solely in
investigating the methods employed in those systematic tests to which
every new idea must be subjected if it is to be seriously entertained’
(Popper 2002a: 8). The basis of such a system is what Popper eventually
comes to call quasi-inductive reasoning, as will be discussed towards the
end of this chapter. The meaningful testing that the ideas can be sub-
jected to link directly to a key feature of Popper’s thought, falsiﬁcation.
2.3 Falsiﬁability
Falsiﬁability is a key to building a ﬁlter through which our ideas may pass
and be judged to be meaningfully testable or not. Falsiﬁability is the
ability to falsify (prove to be false) a hypothesis based on a single obser-
vation. While, as was shown in the previous section, a hypothesis cannot
42
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 55 -->

be veriﬁed (proved to be right) by any number of single observations, it
takes, in principle, only one observation (a counter-example) to falsify an
hypothesis. The black swan example that we gave showed this point
clearly, as noted by Magee (1982: 23):
Popper’s solution begins by pointing to a logical asymmetry between veriﬁ-
cation and falsiﬁcation. To express it in terms of logical statements: although
no number of observation statements of white swans allows us logically to
derive the universal statement ‘All swans are white’, one single observation
statement, reporting one single observation of a black swan, allows us to
logically derive the statement ‘Not all swans are white.’ In this important
logical sense empirical generalizations, though not veriﬁable, are falsiﬁable.
This means scientiﬁc laws are testable in spite of being unprovable: they can
be tested by systematic attempts to refute them.
Falsiﬁability, as might have been guessed from quotations from corpus
linguists at the start of this chapter, is a key feature of Popper’s approach
to science. What is perhaps less well appreciated in the corpus linguistics
literature is that falsiﬁability is an important means of determining
whether a speciﬁc hypothesis is scientiﬁc or not.
Principle 11: A corpus is of use for testing the falsiﬁability of a hypothesis about
language.
To be a scientiﬁc hypothesis, a hypothesis must be falsiﬁable. In principle,
there must be a way in which the hypothesis can be tested and shown not
to be true. While we may, in any given test, fail to falsify a hypothesis, it
must be the case that falsiﬁability is a possible outcome of testing
a hypothesis for it to be considered a scientiﬁc hypothesis, as opposed
to what Popper calls a metaphysical statement. For example, imagine that
we state that each person speaks the way that they do because an invisible
being, in the far reaches of the universe, wills them to say each and every
word that they utter – let us call this being the Great Interlocutor. It
controls all linguistic communication in the universe. This is a decidedly
metaphysical statement – though not because it sounds far-fetched or
implausible. To the ecclesiastical mind in the Renaissance, much of what
Galileo said may well have sounded far-fetched and implausible. The
claim about the Great Interlocutor fails in a way that Galileo did not.
Galileo came up with hypotheses that were falsiﬁable. It was quite
possible for anyone – including readers of this book in the twenty-ﬁrst
century – to test and attempt to falsify Galileo’s hypotheses. The Great
2.3 Falsiﬁability
43
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 56 -->

Interlocutor cannot be falsiﬁed – you cannot prove that the creature does
not exist. However, precisely for this reason, the creature can be dis-
missed as an ascientiﬁc notion – it is not science because one cannot
design even a single experiment, nor make a single observation, that
might disprove the notion. Hence it does not fall in the realm of science.
Yet note that as instrumentation and theory change, so the boundaries
of science shift too. A mistake is to assume that in the set of all possible
hypotheses there are two sets which do not intersect, the scientiﬁc and the
metaphysical, because the membership of each is essentially ﬁxed. This is
not true – instrumentation, for example, can shift the metaphysical to the
scientiﬁc. So, if I had hypothesised that moons existed around planets
other than our own prior to the creation of the telescope, as used by
Galileo to observe the moons of Jupiter, my hypothesis would have been
a metaphysical.26 It was not, at that point in time, falsiﬁable. With hind-
sight we may say that it would not prove to be metaphysical, because we
know that an instrument would be created that would permit the obser-
vation to be made. However, prior to the invention of a device such as
a telescope, and then the insight occurring to point it not at objects on the
land, but at objects in the sky, the use of an unknown device to see
something that we hypothesise exists remains fanciful.27 However,
when a telescope was developed that could see such things, it became
possible to seek to falsify the hypothesis, hence the hypothesis transi-
tioned ﬁrmly from being metaphysical to scientiﬁc at the moment that the
possibility of falsiﬁcation developed.
Similarly, in linguistics a great many hypotheses that required word
frequency information were essentially metaphysical until suitable
machinery (computers) and data (corpora) were created to shift such
hypotheses from the metaphysical to the scientiﬁc. So methods and
instrumentation are important in shifting the membership of the set of
scientiﬁc and metaphysical hypotheses and part of the research effort of
scientists must, of necessity, take place at the border of the two. Pulling
important research questions across from the metaphysical to the
26 Of course we could also distinguish between those metaphysical hypotheses for which
we can conceive of new tools that may allow them to be explored and those for which no
credibly tool can yet be imagined. However, in the former case there is no guarantee that
the tool will be developed, while in the later case time and other developments may well
make a tool available for their exploration. Consequently, it is probably fruitful to think
of the transition from metaphysical to scientiﬁc through tools being more like
a continuum rather than a binary divide. We will treat it in a more binary fashion
here, though the continuum better represents the reality of the situation.
27 See Sluiter (1997) for an account of the invention and spread of the telescope.
44
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 57 -->

scientiﬁc may be viewed as at least as important, or perhaps more so in
terms of innovation, than exploring those hypotheses currently thought
to be scientiﬁc. The history of corpus linguistics in the past half century at
least has, in large part, been about this migration of the metaphysical to
the scientiﬁc.28 More generally, this is true in science as ‘it is out of
metaphysics – out of superstitious, mythical and religious conceptions
of the world – that science has emerged’ (Magee 1982: 47). Corpus
linguistics is closely linked to this process of migration, as the following
principle makes clear:
Principle 12: In developing new tools and data sets, corpus linguists are mindful
of the need, where possible, to transition hypotheses from the realm of
metaphysics to one where important principles of corpus linguistics may be
brought to bear, especially Principles 4, 5, 6 and 11.
What happens when we fail to falsify an hypothesis? Is it correct? The
answer to that is a clear and unequivocal ‘no’, in large part because of
Principle 10. No hypothesis can escape the possibility of falsiﬁcation – but
with each attempt at falsiﬁcation that it survives we may say that it has
been further corroborated.29 We may, if you will, develop more faith in
a hypothesis the more it deﬁes falsiﬁcation. We will return to a fuller
discussion of falsiﬁcation and corroboration shortly. For now, let us
continue to focus on the testing of theories.
2.4 Testing Theories
Assuming that we have hypotheses that permit falsiﬁcation, how else do
we establish a scientiﬁc theory? It begins with an idea – we have a notion
about language, we make a theory of its use, for example. We can then
proceed to test that theory in a number of ways by: i) comparing conclu-
sions to be drawn from the theory to see if they are mutually compatible;
ii) determining whether the idea passes a ﬁlter applied to discriminate
scientiﬁc from non-scientiﬁc questions; iii) comparing and contrasting it
with competing ideas to test which is, in some sense, ‘best’; and iv) testing
the applicability of the theory to real-world applications. Let us explore
28 Section 5.5 provides an example of this.
29 See Curry (2021) for an example of a study in which this approach is operationalised in
corpus linguistics – Curry tests hypotheses from multiple linguistic perspectives in
a range of language to subject his hypotheses to repeated attempts at falsiﬁcation.
2.4 Testing Theories
45
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 58 -->

each of these in turn. To do so, let us consider collocation as a theory in
linguistics. For ease of presentation, while there were antecedents of the
work that posited something similar to collocation (see McEnery and
Hardie 2011 for a discussion), let us assume that the work of Sinclair et al.
(2004) was the ﬁrst in which collocation was postulated.
The notion that Sinclair et al. started with was a simple one – that mean-
ing could, to some extent, be determined and conditioned by co-occurrence.
Our third test could be applied to this on dogmatic grounds and, at the time
when it was posited, it is almost certainly the case that we would have raised
a red ﬂag on the third criteria for this hypothesis – mainstream linguistic
theory at the time was principally concerned with determining logical mean-
ing through looking at how primitive elements of meaning could be
assumed to be attached to a word form (e.g. compositional semantics) or
through the application of logical models designed to try to capture the
interplay of semantics and syntax in a formal logic (see Lyons 1977, for
a good example of the approach to semantics in mainstream linguistics in
the 1970s). Distributional approaches to meaning, as proposed by Sinclair,
were not current. However, just because current theory does not support
a hypothesis, we should not simply abandon an idea – we should set aside
such dogma and move on to assess the other areas of testability.
The ﬁrst criterion asks whether we can draw mutually compatible
conclusions – we would argue the answer to that is yes. People have
explored conclusions arising from collocation relating, inter alia, to lan-
guage change (McEnery et al. 2019), language learning (Ellis et al. 2013)
and contrastive analysis (Xiao and McEnery 2006) and the collocation
has proved to be compatible with conclusions drawn in these areas.
The second asks, simply, whether the proposition can be put in a way
that is formalisable – and in the case of collocation the answer is ‘yes’.
Sinclair et al. deﬁned a system for determining whether something was
a collocate based on clearly identiﬁed criteria – there is a node word, the
word co-occurs with other words in a deﬁned span, and collocates are the
words appearing either side of the node word, within the window, which
occur there with more than random chance.
Returning to the third criterion, we can compare collocation as an
explanation for word meaning with other theories to seek, via testing
rather than through dogma, which better corresponds to reality as
observed under Principle 2.
Finally, we can ask what the correspondence to reality brings – if we
hypothesise that collocation is helpful for determining word meaning, is it?
Can we demonstrate that? So we have a test of utility which, while closely
meshed with a correspondence to reality, is not co-terminus with it, for
46
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 59 -->

example, we may show that collocations are systematically observable in
any corpus in any point of time but that does not mean to say that
observation itself has any application at all – that is a separable matter.30
Principle 13: The observable is not always of importance or utility.
This approach to reality stands on two premises. First, that there is
a world separate from thought – yet from the extreme of solipsism
through to weaker positions other theorists may claim that the world
cannot be set apart from the mind. Popper is a realist – as are we. He
focuses upon the external world.31 The contingent nature of belief in
Popper’s philosophy of science provides a useful safety valve through
which the worst excesses of this debate can be vented, allowing us to
focus on a reality which, until it is falsiﬁed, we believe to exist because
multiple tests have corroborated that view. The second presumption on
which the approach stands is that we assume not only that the external
world exists, we also presume that we are capable of directly accessing it
through data and showing what it is like through our theorising. The way
in which we make sense of that world may vary between disciplines, with
some relying on mathematics, others on logic, others on more narratively
oriented theorising. So the realisation of this second premise may be
particularist – it takes different forms in different domains of enquiry, or
within different traditions within the same domain of enquiry.
In a sense Popper is an empiricist and a realist – experience is the one
true source of knowledge and experience brings us into contact with the
world. Yet Popper’s realism is a critical realism – for him correspondence
of reality and theory is softened to a position where theory and reality
come into quasi-contact. This is an important insight for corpus linguistics
30 Note that throughout we assume observations of this sort to be those which can be made
either directly – we see words on a page, we hear words – or in a mediated fashion – we use
software to compile collocations from a corpus or a frequency list. This is quite in line with
Popper’s views in which instrumentation has a role to play in making observations – if it
does not, examples such as ‘By using a Geiger counter, we can then record those electrons’
(Popper 2002a: 221) are problematic. The issue of mediated observation does bring to the
fore the possibility that instrumentation itself may be the basis of falsiﬁcation; for example,
it might be proved that the Geiger counter used was faulty or it may be revealed that Geiger
counters do not produce results as thought. This theme is returned to in the discussion of
repetition in Chapter 6.
31 However, as we will see in the next chapter he does so without necessarily believing in its
objective truth.
2.4 Testing Theories
47
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 60 -->

and it is one that leads us to cast ourselves as critical realist. This apparent
contact with reality should be viewed sceptically.
Principle 14: Corpus linguists are critical realists who use corpus data as a way to
come into quasi-contact with language in use.
A brief consideration of what happens when we subject frequency data to
analysis shows the importance of this observation to corpus linguistics – if
we apply a simple mutual information score to word co-occurrence data,
we will gain one view of collocation. However, as Gablasova et al. (2017a,
2017b) have shown, frequency data can be analysed in many different
ways and provide different results, a point shown speciﬁcally for colloca-
tion in Gablasova et al. (2017b). The point of contact between textual
reality and theoretical interpretation is mediated by a whole set of
choices that mean that quasi-contact is what we should expect in linguis-
tics, just as it happens, for example, in the interpretation of data provided
on the existence or otherwise of planets circling other suns. The result is
that which we suggested when discussing inference in Chapter 1 – data
may give rise to many inferences, not just the ones we choose to make
from it. This leads to the possibility of underdetermination (Duhem 1906;
Quine 1969) – our data may not inexorably force us to adopt one theore-
tical position. It may provide evidence that can be used to critically
explore a range of theoretical positions with some ﬁnding that the data
falsiﬁes them, yet others ﬁnding that they resist falsiﬁcation by the data –
multiple hypotheses may be supported by our data. To give an example
from Physics used by Magee (1982: 29):
All the observational evidence which ﬁtted Newton’s theory . . . also ﬁtted
Einstein’s . . . it can be logically demonstrated – and had been long ago, by
Leibniz – that any ﬁnite number of observations can be accommodated
within an indeﬁnitely large number of different explanations.
Howmight oneworkagainstunderdetermination?Moredataareanobvious
response, but that will always be a response limited by Principle 10. Another
response is better data, and better data in this context are data more likely to
lead to the falsiﬁcation of the hypothesis (in line with Principle 11).32
32 Our response here is to suggest that the scale and nature of observation lies at the root of
underdeterminism. This may, or may not, be the whole answer to the problem, but we
believe that, unless such issues can be addressed, it is hard to dismiss Quine’s (1990: 100f.)
characterisation of underdeterminism as the ‘fantasy of irresolubly rival systems of the
world’.
48
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 61 -->

Principle 15: Corpora are theoretically underdetermined and hence may support
a range of theoretical perspectives.
Users and builders of data sets need to consider falsiﬁcation and to actively
accept that the value of observation as a source of quasi-contact crucially
depends upon the intention and nature of the observation. More speciﬁcally,
we must ask ourselves the question ‘did the observation, when used in
support of a hypothesis, occur in a context where it could have provided
falsiﬁcation?’ For example, consider that a linguist wants to ﬁnd out whether
or not people write using split inﬁnitives. They could consult their intuition
and claim that they do not – yet those intuitions would have been made on an
uncertain basis and would be of doubtful value. Adapting the reasoning of
Musgrave (1993: 280–281), while we may accept as justiﬁcation for a person’s
belief intheirintuitions theirconvictionthat they are true,wemayonly chose
to believe them if we share that positive evaluation of the justiﬁcation and we
have subjected it to serious criticism. As intuition is a poor tool to achieve
falsiﬁcation in Popper’s framework, they reject this evidence.33 They think
next of asking lots of people if they think they use split inﬁnitives – the
problem that their previous test encountered does not go away, it is simply
encountered again and again in each individual. They then strike upon the
idea of placing an advert in a newspaper saying ‘Please send me examples of
you not using a split inﬁnitive in your writing.’ They receive a million exam-
ples in the post – but the observation is problematic as it did not permit the
possibility of falsiﬁcation. So we must be mindful, when gathering corpus
data, that it will support genuine tests – it was in pursuit of such ideas that
corpus linguists talked of ‘total accountability’ (Leech 1992: 112; Svartvik
1992: 9) and ‘no prior selection’ (Leech 1992: 112). We should bear in mind
the following principle.
Principle 16: When building and using corpora, our goal is to facilitate (corpus
building) or perform a genuine test (corpus use) and a genuine test must
permit the possibility of falsiﬁcation.
The perspectives on testing discussed in this section are of importance to
the corpus linguist, so we ﬁnd common cause with Popper. Language may
be perceived and through its perception we may come to represent it. In
33 Andersson (2016: 140) gives a blunter reason for rejection in such cases: ‘The alternative is
to say that there are no good reasons for choosing a test statement, that the classiﬁcation
of a test statement as true is a kind of existential leap. Such a position is irrationalism.’
2.4 Testing Theories
49
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 62 -->

doing so, corpus linguists and other linguists using such data tend to be, in
epistemological terms, particularists – they take as fact something that they
believe they know based on expert judgement as ‘general reﬂection about
the nature of knowledge is subject to the constraint of judgement about
speciﬁc instances of knowledge’ (Sankey 2018: 49). Corpus linguists may
concur that usefulness of frequency data is a fact that they can agree on;
other linguists draw on corpora as repositories of examples to explore
some theory of language. They achieve this certainty via quasi-contact
with linguistic reality and exploration of hypotheses, and at some point
during this contact, we may presume, they elevate their hypotheses about
language to the status of ‘truth’, that is, statements that correspond to the
reality. Within such an approach to science, as it has been argued followers
of Popper proposed, notably Imre Lakatos (Sankey 2018), what is true
becomes tied to ‘the value-judgements of élite scientists’ when adjudicat-
ing ‘between competing theories of scientiﬁc method’ (Sankey 2018: 49).
Yet how does this cohere with Principles 2 and 9? An exploration of this
apparent conﬂict occupies most of what follows in this chapter.
2.5 Science versus Rhetoric
Why does any of this matter? It matters because, without due consideration
of the scientiﬁc method, linguistics would descend into a war of rhetorical
ﬂourishes, a war of words and opinions, devoid of a correspondence to
reality in any systematic sense. A good example of this can be drawn from
a discussion in the 1950s, pretty much at the point when some researchers
were trying to use early computers and corpora in order to shift the
metaphysical discussion of some feature of language into the scientiﬁc
domain. This was a pivotal moment that inﬂuenced how many researchers
even today understand language and its relation to science (cf. also
Chomsky 2021). In the following exchange we can see the alternative –
the discussion is about the different meanings of the sentences ‘The doors
are closed at six’ and ‘The doors are closed’. The discussants are Noam
Chomsky and Anna Granville Hatcher, with the ﬁnal comment coming
from Archibald Hill (Hill 1962: 28–29):
CHOMSKY:
Why not say these two things have different syntactic
structures and that the difference correlates with
meaning? Since there is a pattern overlap, it is to be
expected that the sentence will show some ambiguity.
HATCHER:
How do you know that this sentence has only one mean-
ing? Perhaps the sentence is used in this way – ‘Oh that’s
50
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 63 -->

too late. The doors are closed at six.’ meaning ‘you’ll
ﬁnd them closed if you go there then.’ It is even possible
that there might be correlation between different
orders, like ‘The doors are closed at six,’ and ‘At six,
the doors are closed’. The meaning of a sentence like
this one cannot be known because it is not used in
context. I think the only way to study sentences is to
study normal sentences, produced under no prejudicial
theories, in ordinary language use. When a grammarian
constructs sentences there are enormous distortions,
and when we try to decide what we would or would
not say, we are very likely to fool ourselves.
CHOMSKY:
The trouble with using a corpus is that some authors do
not write the English language. Veblen for example,
speaks of ‘performing leisure,’ and the verb perform
cannot take such an object.
HATCHER:
I admit it sounds unusual. But I bet that if you studied
the verb perform you would ﬁnd other expressions not
too far from this, pointing the way to this. He has gone
farther perhaps along a certain road but I do not
believe he has created something new.
CHOMSKY:
No, he has broken a law. The verb perform cannot be
used with mass-word objects; one can perform a task,
but one cannot perform labor.
HATCHER:
How do you know, if you don’t use a corpus and have
not studied the verb perform?
CHOMSKY:
How do I know? Because I am a native speaker of
English.
HILL:
I think at this point I would like to strike a blow for
liberty. There are certainly many situations in which we
can get information from a closed corpus. There are
certainly many other situations in which we work from
an open corpus, and an informant other than oneself.
There are still other situations in which it is advanta-
geous to act as one’s own informant. It seems to me
most unfortunate for anyone to insist that one of these
three procedures is the only one to be used in studying
language.
In this example, Chomsky famously makes a bold and categorical
statement about perform that is palpably wrong – one can perform
2.5 Science versus Rhetoric
51
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 64 -->

sex and one can perform magic, for example, as a quick study of most
general English corpora will show (McEnery and Wilson 2001: 11).
Chomsky proceeds by problematising reality – writers who use English
in a way he disagrees with ‘do not write English’ and the statements he
makes are bold and categorical, based on nothing but his opinion. At
that point falsifying what he says was difﬁcult – even though Hatcher
did have the presence of mind to think of a counter-example later in
the discussion, asking Chomsky if he would ‘object if the mass-word
were magic’ that was attached to perform (Hill 1962: 31). While
Chomsky accepts this example, he does not reverse his position, assert-
ing that he is sure that a generalisation is possible and trying to shift the
discussion, in what we may think of as a Newtonian direction, to
suggest that examples such as this are exceptions to an otherwise
hard and fast rule (Hill 1962: 32).
It is hard not to feel sympathy for Hatcher – she is on the cusp of shifting
an essentially metaphysical discussion, in which opinion counts as evidence
and counter-examples may be dismissed and claimed to be exceptions,
because she does not have ready access to large banks of examples in the
form of machine-readable corpus data with which to rapidly and effec-
tively test the claims made. In the absence of an appeal to a framework in
which she can systematically falsify such claims, the debate moves on, as
did linguistics in the Chomskyan tradition, as if such falsiﬁcations based on
attested evidence barely mattered – if it did, it was an exception or aberrant
data that was ‘not English’. Similarly, Hatcher’s hypothesis that ‘The
generalizations of the speaker of a given language are usually wrong’
(Hill 1962: 31) was also not falsiﬁable at that point in time. While one
may have been able to demonstrate it through one generalisation made by
one speaker, making a claim about one verb in one language at a speciﬁc
point in time by providing a single counter-example, this was no more
acceptable evidence of her hypothesis than Chomsky’s single incorrect
example was of his. In the absence of a framework that actively sought
to falsify such claims, and without the data and instrumentation to do so,
the claims are metaphysical. However, corpus data and computers were to
change that, though perhaps not as much as we might expect – a more
recent quote from Chomsky shows him arguing in a very similar way, this
time with reference to child language acquisition and what he claims is the
impossibility of a child acquiring verb paradigms from the linguistic evi-
dence it is exposed to (Chomsky, in Desagulier 2017):
Any child knows the inﬂectional paradigms, they get that very quickly, but
they don’t hear them, they hear only parts of them. Statistically it’s extremely
52
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 65 -->

unlikely that somebody will have heard the ﬁrst . . . the full paradigm. Things
just aren’t that frequent.
This claim has a notable virtue – it can be tested with a corpus. When it
is, the claim falls apart almost immediately. We quickly studied this
claim using the Spoken BNC2014 corpus, a corpus of conversational
English such as a child could easily overhear when forming hypotheses
of the sort that Chomsky claims could not be data driven. The very ﬁrst
verb we looked at was KICK. A look at the frequency of the different verb
forms of KICK reveals immediately that if a child was looking for evi-
dence of the inﬂection of this verb, they would ﬁnd it easily – the corpus
contains (all ﬁgures quoted for verbs) 245 examples of kick, 29 examples
of kicks, 230 example of kicked and 141 examples of kicking. If effect
size provides us with a way to look at what may be learnt from these
occurrences by way of inference, then the answer is much. For example,
off collocates with kick, kicked and kicking, in each case having a very
strong preference for the position one word to the right of the node
(accounting for 61.4%, 85.1% and 93.2% of all examples of off collocat-
ing with each form of the verb, respectively), revealing the phrasal verb
kick off. Similarly the expression kick up a fuss is revealed by kick
collocating with up 15 times, with 11 of the 15 examples being accounted
for by this expression. We next considered EAT, which has different
forms for the simple past and the past participle. Again we found
plentiful evidence (corpus frequencies in parentheses): ate (472), eat
(4,068), eaten (520), eating (1,311) and eats (161). As a ﬁnal example,
irregular forms are found in abundance in our data. Consider the verb
BE, for which we ﬁnd plenty of evidence, including for clitic forms: am
(3,291), are (41,093), be (57,788), been (5,902), is (103,015), ’m (42,980),
’re (57,974), ’s (252,441), was (129,797) and were (30,855). To para-
phrase Chomsky, things just are that frequent. As work such as Hart
and Risley (1995) has shown, in America, the children of educated
parents have, by the age of three, heard 11 million words of speech.34
This is a lot of data and, within it, we may presume, based on the
evidence we see in the Spoken BNC2014, that regular inﬂectional patterns
are repeated frequently and irregular patterns are clearly visible. There
is plentiful evidence for children to learn inﬂectional paradigms from
34 We set aside the issue here of whether we should be limiting ourselves to child-directed
speech or not – what we would note is that there are abundant examples, both in the
literature and in our personal experience, of children using language that was not
directed at them, demonstrating that children do attend, at least on occasion, to over-
heard speech and learn from it, as shown by Floor and Akhtar (2010).
2.5 Science versus Rhetoric
53
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 66 -->

a combination of child-directed and environmental speech.35 One can only
begin to imagine the basis on which Chomsky makes this claim about
inﬂectional paradigms, but we would hazard that, just as with the example
from ﬁfty years before, it was not made on the basis of corpus evidence and
at best it is simply his opinion. Rather more likely, however, is that the
claim about inﬂectional paradigms and the claim that was challenged by
Hatcher are the result of dogma – a problem in science discussed by
Popper that we will return to shortly.36
We ﬁnish this section with a brief consideration of the preceding quote
from Hill. That was included both because it mirrors the three methods
used in the example used at the beginning of the chapter and, impor-
tantly, because Hill’s statement, while close to being in line with the
scientiﬁc method, is not in some important respects. Yet before we can
demonstrate that, we need to explore the ideas presented in a little more
depth, in particular falsiﬁability.
2.6 What Is an Acceptable Theory?
Let us return to the question of what is an acceptable theory – what
factors allow us to push an idea across the line from the metaphysical to
the scientiﬁc? For ease, we will set aside the important role that the
metaphysical may have to play in progressing science, as discussed ear-
lier, and conceive of science as a system at one moment in time. If we take
that view, what constitutes not merely a hypothesis, but also a theoretical
35 We accept, of course, that the Spoken BNC2014 is not a corpus of child-directed speech,
but it certainly represents the type of environmental speech that we may expect a child
to be able to attend to.
36 A truly curious feature of Chomsky’s claim, and overall rejection of the value of corpus
data, is that he seems to back it with reference to the work of Charles Yang, his former
PhD student (see Yang 2001). Yang uses corpus data in his thesis (and has used it
subsequently, for example, Yang 2015), including to explore verb paradigms. The work
is limited by the scale of data Yang uses – for example, in refuting the arguments of
Pullum (1996), Yang (2001: 57) cites evidence from a corpus study of 20,372 utterances
of child-directed speech, produced by adults, directed at one child (though Yang 2015,
exploring a different issue, uses much more data). Considering that in the Spoken
BNC2014, 10 million words produces 1,197,091 utterances, it seems quite a leap to
claim that by the age of three a child has not been exposed to sufﬁcient examples of
any linguistic structure based on a limited volume of data experienced by one child.
Setting aside such criticisms of Yang’s conclusions, we note that his work at least
attempts to engage with corpus data and certainly includes some conclusions showing
that corpus data can provide useful input to ﬁrst language acquisition to the extent that
children are ‘learning from experience’ (Yang 2001: 103).
54
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 67 -->

system that we may conceive of as being scientiﬁc? First, as noted, it must
not be contradictory. It must describe a possible world – a system that
posits that on Earth bricks routinely ﬂoat upwards spontaneously ﬂies so
readily in the face of real-world experience, and other theories that have
repeatedly withstood falsiﬁcation, that we may easily discard this notion.
The theoretical system, as a whole, must ideally also be scientiﬁc – the
interlocking complex of statements that make up the theory must be
scientiﬁc, not a mixture of the scientiﬁc and the metaphysical. So, for
example, if we have a theory of linguistics that is based on a very good
series of hypotheses about basic building blocks of language and which
allows one to perfectly describe language itself in the ﬁeld, yet nestled in
the theory is a hypothesis such as the Great Interlocutor that explains
why sentences are produced, the theory fails to pass the barrier from the
metaphysical to the scientiﬁc because it is rooted in the metaphysical.
Finally, as was suggested in Chapter 1, the system must reﬂect our
experience of reality. A well-formed linguistic theory that predicts sen-
tences and interactions utterly unlike those that are actually produced is
not a scientiﬁc theory – it is a theory that is focused on some world other
than the one we inhabit, it is metaphysical. In determining whether
a system corresponds with our world experience, Popper (2002a: 17)
says we may determine correspondence with the world because the
system has been ‘submitted to tests, and has stood up to tests’.
The question of how and what to test obviously brings us back into
close connection with Hill’s comments once more and begins to point
towards an issue with his contribution, which seems to equate all three of
the methods he mentions as, arguably, equally useful and scientiﬁc. The
way along the path to a critique of Hill’s view is further illuminated when
we consider how Popper considers experience to be testable – the idea is
closely, indeed ineluctably, linked to method. Science has a distinctive
method – the theory of knowledge, the epistemology of science, is pre-
dicated not just on tests, but also on methods that properly support those
tests. What we have loosely described as correspondence to reality in the
ﬁrst chapter of this book is actually the process whereby we are able to
test experience – and this is a key criterion we can use when determining,
from a basic statement through to a theory, whether it is scientiﬁc or
metaphysical: we will count it as ‘empirical or scientiﬁc only if it capable
of being tested by experience’ (Popper 2002a: 18). This leads us to
a revision of Principle 6, which is now more strongly linked to
Principles 7 and 11. Note that, as we revise a principle, we add a prime
to the new principle to distinguish it from its earlier version, hence in this
case Principle 6 becomes Principle 60.
2.6 What Is an Acceptable Theory?
55
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 68 -->

Principle 60: Corpus linguistics inclines to scientia realis – it is the study of
observable language on which experience may be tested in accordance
with Principles 7 and 11.
Our goal in testing a statement, or a theory, is not to ‘prove’ anything but,
rather, to test and seek to falsify. To return to and reﬁne our view of theories
with metaphysical components – to an extent it may be possible that a theory
with a metaphysical heart may in fact be dissected in different ways – while
we may reject the theory as a whole, we may ﬁnd that we can test and
potentially falsify those parts of it that are not metaphysical. So while the
theory would not pass through our ﬁlter, individual statements may.
The ordering of statements has an important role to play in falsiﬁca-
tion. Where dependencies are set up between statements, the falsiﬁcation
of one may entail the falsiﬁcation of others. However, sometimes the
falsiﬁcation of higher-order statements may not entail the falsiﬁcation of
lower-order statements. Falsiﬁcation works because as well as our infer-
ences transmitting truth from our premises to our conclusions, they also,
if a premise is wrong, transmit falsehood. While our tests can never
exhaustively test for the transmission of truth, we can, in a focused way,
attempt to get our system to transmit falsehood, which is identiﬁable with
reference to reality, showing some premise to be wrong. This is at the
core of the strength of falsiﬁcation as postulated by Popper (see Popper
1976: 98; Andersson 2016: 128–131).
Consider collocation once again. Sinclair et al. (2004) made a series of
what may be interpreted as statements that allow us to begin to identify
collocates. Others added statements to the theory of collocation that posited
things such as discourse prosody (see Stubbs 2001b: 309), directionality (see
Gries 2013) and collocation networks (Phillips 1985). When testing these
statements, as well as using corpus evidence, researchers have sought to
falsify them by looking at psychological reality using methods such as eye-
tracking (Millar 2011) and EEG experiments (Hughes and Hardie 2019).
Consider the eye-tracking experiments. These deliberately distorted collo-
cation patterns to see whether some physical evidence of the processing of
collocation would be yielded – did the eye ﬂick back across the malformed
collocation trying to make sense of it, indicating that the original collocation
seemed to be something that was expected and useful for language proces-
sing? Imagine that this experiment did not ﬁnd such an effect (it actually did,
but for the sake of argument we will assume it did not). Let us also imagine
that, as part of research on a theory of mind, we have a set of statements,
which have withstood falsiﬁcation so far, that claim that the ill-formedness
56
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 69 -->

of an utterance causes perturbation in eye movement when reading. What
would the result of the failed collocation reading experiment be? It is
tempting to conclude that it would mean that the system of statements
that make up collocation would have fallen apart. It would have been
falsiﬁed because this test, which sought to check to see if the psychological
reality of collocation could be indirectly observed, did not show that.
However, that would not be the right conclusion to draw. What we may
conclude is that if the system exists, it cannot be falsiﬁed by this method –
but we can falsify any hypotheses that said that the system impacts on eye
movement during reading, as the second hypothesis does. The link between
the eye movement and collocation in statements in our system seems to
have been falsiﬁed. So while the collocation statement as such would not
have been falsiﬁed, the connectivity between this statement and any state-
ment about eye motion during reading has been severed. This may have an
impact on an overall theory of mind we are developing, which links reading,
eye movement and linguistic structure. So in falsifying the link between eye
movement and collocation, we may have not have falsiﬁed collocation but
we have disrupted the interdependence of statements in the theory. We
would need to develop a new theory to bring the statements about colloca-
tion, which seem to be repeatedly corroborated, back together in a powerful
explanatory framework that passes the test for being a scientiﬁc theory. The
system of statements would then be subject to testing by falsiﬁcation once
again. Contrast that with what would happen if some of the lower-order
statements were falsiﬁed – if we failed, for example, to identify collocations
in language as per Sinclair’s original suggestion. That would produce a chain
reaction across the statements that rely on that being true and lead to a more
general collapse of the theory.37 So the interconnectedness of statements in
a theory needs to be considered and the effect of falsifying a statement on
others needs to be considered. This leads to a new principle that is closely
connected to Principles 9 and 11:
Principle 17: When conducting a test with corpus data, one must consider the
overall impact of any falsiﬁcation produced by that test.
When dealing with falsiﬁcations, one clearly needs to guard against
explaining away, ignoring or hiding away a falsiﬁcation. It is clearly true
that science has been subject to a series of scandals caused by researchers
who simply strained too hard, either rhetorically or in some other way, to
37 Through modus tollens in classical logic.
2.6 What Is an Acceptable Theory?
57
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 70 -->

rescue unfairly a statement from falsiﬁcation. One way of evading falsi-
ﬁcation is to forever dodge the bullet – providing amendments to a theory
or revising it in some way to deﬂect rather than address falsiﬁcation head
on. Rather than dealing with the falsiﬁcation the temptation is to ignore it
and to move on to some new version of the theory in which that which
was falsiﬁed may even make a re-appearance. If that sounds a little far-
fetched, consider Chomsky’s response to Hatcher in the earlier quote.
She effectively demonstrated the danger of using introspection for mak-
ing generalisations yet the evidence was swiftly swept aside on the basis
of nothing more than misplaced conﬁdence in a process (well described
by Popper 2002a: 21) in which a supposedly logical ‘statement remained
obscure, and was described by correspondingly obscure expressions
which elucidated nothing, but slurred over difﬁculties’. Within Popper’s
view of science ‘statements are not dogmatically accepted on the basis of
some infallible kind of experience, nor are they conventionally accepted
by arbitrary decisions or pure acts of will’ (Andersson 1982: 62). Yet
Chomsky’s views certainly led to linguists claiming that corpora could be
replaced by ‘some infallible kind of experience’, as the following quote
shows. It is from Francis (1975: 15–16) who was recalling a conversation
with the Chomskyan linguist Robert Lees, after Lees asked Francis what
he had been doing lately:
I answered that I had a grant from the Ofﬁce of Education to assemble
a million-word corpus of present-day American English. He looked at me
in amazement and asked: ‘What in the world do you want to do that for?’
I burbled something about ﬁnding out the true facts about English grammar.
I have never forgotten his reply: ‘That is a complete waste of your time and
the government’s money. You are a native speaker of English; in ten minutes
