you can come up with more illustrations of any point in English grammar
than you’ll ﬁnd in many millions of words of random text.’
Given that Lees had not had access to millions of words of text, random
or otherwise, on which to test his claim, we can use this as a good example
of dogma in action. Beliefs that are held simply on the basis of a strength
of conviction are those that dwell ﬁrmly in the metaphysical realm.
Conviction will not save a failed attempt at falsiﬁcation – returning to
collocation, work such as Xiao and McEnery (2006) was undertaken
initially on the strong conviction that there were faults in the theory of
collocation that this study would show. The authors were convinced that
collocation was probably a by-product of the relatively high word-order
ﬁxedness of English, so they tested a language with a less rigid word
order, Chinese, to see if the statements supporting collocation could be
58
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 71 -->

falsiﬁed. While the authors strongly believed that they would be falsiﬁed,
they were not. The experiments provided corroboration for collocation
as the falsiﬁcation failed. The convictions of the authors of the paper at
the moment of corroboration did not matter at all – they had been
important in terms of selecting and designing the study admittedly, but
those beliefs did not change the results. Research ethics, of course, have
an important part to play in guarding against convictions distorting
evidence. Xiao and McEnery could have suppressed their results as
they may not have wished to corroborate the theory, yet they, rightly,
did not. While their study helpfully corroborates the collocation hypoth-
esis, it would arguably have been more important if the study had
achieved its initial goal – to demonstrate that the hypothesis was not
language independent. That would have required a wholesale revision of
the hypothesis, the corroboration left the hypothesis intact and ready to
face new attempts at falsiﬁcation. This leads to a principle, which ties on
closely with Principles 11 and 17.
Principle 18: Reporting falsiﬁcations is arguably more important than reporting
corroboration. At the very least, both should be reported.
2.7 Conviction, Evidence and Testing
The intersection of conviction and evidence clearly presents us with the
need to be clearer about how we deﬁne a test that we deem to be accep-
table in terms of allowing the passage of a statement from the metaphysical
to the scientiﬁc, so-called rules of acceptance (Miller 1982: 21). Taking our
lead once again from Popper (and indeed Quine 1990: 3), we want objec-
tive tests, which will be accepted as such if they can be intersubjectively
tested. Now it may well be that a speaker, A, has an opinion about some-
thing and asks you for your opinion and you produce one that agrees with
A’s. This is not intersubjective testing or, if it is, it is of a sort that Popper
(2002a: 25) describes as being ‘certainly not very high’. We need our tests
to be repeatable in a way that is independent of the context in which they
were conducted and Principles 4 to 7 work together to help to achieve this.
If A is generalising from their own experience, they may well be able to
repeat their opinion and stick to it. They may even ﬁnd one or two other
people who agree with them who are willing to do the same. Yet would all
people, if asked independently of me by other researchers, produce the
same opinion? Even then, one would query whether their opinion is in fact
2.7 Conviction, Evidence and Testing
59
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 72 -->

directly observable – there was a time in the history of the UK when it
would have been possible to ask people if God existed and they would
generally have replied ‘yes’. Yet this was, we presume, a shared opinion
based on conviction, not on the basis of objective tests that could give rise
to falsiﬁcation. So opinion, even when uniformly shared, may simply be
a reﬂection of a conviction shared society wide. It is not necessarily a form
of intersubjective testing.
Testing on the basis of shared perception, or the possibility of shared
perception, is clearly desirable and corpus linguistics certainly allows that
to be achieved, which is important as ‘a function of perception is to test and
correct our hypotheses or theories’ (Popper, personal communication
quoted in Petersen 2016: 91). Corpus data are sense data, and sense data
have ‘two important roles: ﬁrst they challenge us to make our
hypotheses; second, they help us to match our hypotheses – by assisting
in the process of selection and refutation’ (Popper and Eccles 1977: 429).38
Our need for methods that allow falsiﬁability is key in deciding which
methods will and will not allow a scientiﬁc approach to the study of lan-
guage. If an approach relies on methods that do not support falsiﬁcation,
then, perforce, the approach is not scientiﬁc – ‘no hypothesis may be
admitted unless there is some way in which, if necessary, it may be expelled’
(Miller 1982: 23). However, in seeking falsiﬁcation, we must not confuse the
unattainable goal of conclusively disproving a theory from that of falsifying
a statement in a given experiment. The retreat to the request for conclusive
refutation is one of the rhetorical strategies that theoreticians may use to
defend their theory unreasonably – they engage in dogmatism, sticking
rigidly to their beliefs until they are proved to be conclusively incorrect.
For the metaphysical, as noted, this is a powerful defence – we cannot
disprove the existence of the Great Interlocutor because it is not prone to
falsiﬁcation. However, even the scientiﬁc can be defended dogmatically.
While some element of dogmatism may be healthy in science as without it
‘we would never ﬁnd out what is in a theory – we should give the theory up
before we had a real opportunity of ﬁnding out its strength’ (Popper 2002b:
420), dogmatism can lead to perceptual and cognitive illusions, a world
actively misconstrued and misunderstood in support of theory (Petersen
2016: 76). Before proceeding with this discussion, let us consider the quote
from Popper about not giving up on a theory. Why on Earth would one not
give up on something that had been falsiﬁed?39
38 For more on making and matching, see Gombrich (1968: 99–100).
39 For now we will persist with the conﬂation of falsiﬁcation and rejection. However, in the
ﬁnal section of the next chapter we will return to this point and consider the distinction
60
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 73 -->

One possibility is that the falsiﬁcation may, in fact, be the product not of
science but of dogmatism. We would argue that the example from Chomsky
about verb paradigms, discussed earlier in this chapter, is just such an
illusion of falsiﬁcation conjured from dogmatism. Chomsky has a long-
standing view that child language acquisition is not explicable – or even
possible – based on the input that the child receives. This dogma is long-
standing, and can be broadly put under the label of the ‘poverty of stimulus’
argument (see Chomsky 2012 for a recent work on this topic). Part of the
dogmatic delusion is to set aside and ignore contrary evidence, to deﬂect. In
the inﬂectional paradigm argument given in this chapter, and in the
response to Hatcher, we see this deﬂection at work, with opinion presented
as fact and an avoidance of referencing or acknowledging work that runs
counter to that opinion. For example, Pullum and Scholz (2002) explore and
critique, in more detail than we have here, poverty of stimulus arguments
and ﬁnd them to be wanting. Yet such critiques do not pierce the dogmatic
shield to the extent that there seems to be no direct acknowledgement of
them in Chomsky (2012) or in any of his other works. What happens instead
is that the claims are dogmatically restated, albeit with new claims being put
forward to support the argument. Similarly, the methods that may falsify the
dogma, such as corpus linguistics, are sidestepped or derided even though,
as Pullum and Scholz show throughout their paper, corpus evidence has
a key role to play in the falsiﬁcation of claims relating to the poverty of
stimulus argument. Pullum and Scholz conduct similar searches, and report
on similar work, as we have with inﬂectional verb paradigms in response to
claims40 from Chomsky (in Piatelli-Palmarini 1980) such as ‘you can go over
a vast amount of data without ever producing such a case’ (Piatelli-
Palmarini 1980: 40) and ‘you can easily live your whole life without ever
producing a relevant example’. Needless to say, Pullum and Scholz pepper
their paper with corpus-based refutations of these claims and come to
a conclusion that we share with regard to claims made about the learnability
of the inﬂectional verb paradigm by infants from the input they receive.
Chomsky’s claims about the poverty of stimulus are ‘unfounded hyperbole’
(Pullum and Scholz 2002: 44).
The hyperbole is sustained by dogmatism. This is unhelpful because
corpus linguistics is clearly an important method that could, in principle,
be used to support the poverty of stimulus argument. So far, the evidence
between falsiﬁcation and rejection that Lakatos (1974) proposes which may help us
better understand this point and reﬁne our practices around falsiﬁcation.
40 In this case for correctly making an inference about making the main-clause–auxiliary
generalisation in preference to the competing, but incorrect, ﬁrst-auxiliary generalisation.
2.7 Conviction, Evidence and Testing
61
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 74 -->

provided by corpora have repeatedly falsiﬁed the claims made in support of
this hypothesis. It would be much better for those linguists who believe in
the hypothesis to look for the real absences in corpus data, should they exist,
and to consider how these might have consequences for the learnability of
a ﬁrst language. When corpus linguistics was essentially a metaphysical
venture, relying on metaphysical statements and conjecture was a more
understandable approach to the exploration of theories of language acquisi-
tion. Given the reality of a 10 million-word corpus (Spoken BNC2014) and
the computational power to explore it rapidly, there can be no excuse,
beyond dogmatism, for not looking at such data to verify what one might
imagine is in everyday speech, especially where such an exploration would
help to falsify statements based on nothing more than imagination at best
and wishful thinking at worst. Principle 11 in that context is a call for all
linguists to consider using corpus data where falsiﬁcation may be achieved
by that route.
To conclude this section, we note that Popper identiﬁes other spurious
defences that researchers should be wary of in scientiﬁc debate, all of
which are important for researchers to bear in mind.41 These include
a critique of the critic (claiming insufﬁcient mastery of the system by the
critic); an appeal to instrumentation (claiming that the measurements
underlying the critique are wrong) and making ad hoc amendments to
theories to deal with criticisms. Of course all of these may be valid to
a degree, but both the criticised and the critic need to be wary of these
defences – they may prove to be convenient diversions that may befuddle
falsiﬁcation. On those grounds, Popper argues that we should resist the
temptation to use such tactics in scientiﬁc debate Popper (2002a: 61).
Principle 19: In research practice and debate, corpus linguists should not produce
arguments proceeding from dogmatic positions. They are the negation of
everything we wish to achieve through an appeal to evidence and objectivity.
2.8 The Unattainable – Truth
It is tempting to reach out at this point and to deﬁne a method that, quite
incontrovertibly, will give rise to scientiﬁc certainty. At the same time,
41 He does this in the context of a discussion of conventionalist approaches to science as
championed by philosophers such as Duhem (1906). However, we outline the points
made here as we believe they have a wider applicability to scientiﬁc debate.
62
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 75 -->

one would reach for the data that would drive that method and begin to
fashion the perfect data set. Our attempts to eliminate error will end and
we will have conﬁrmation of our theory. Yet science ‘according to Popper
is not, need not be and should not be, dominated by this ideal of certainty’
(Miller 1982: 22) as ‘Conﬁrmation . . . is an illusion’ (Godfrey-Smith 2016:
106). Conﬁrmation is also a powerful weapon in the arsenal of the dog-
matic sceptic – those who repeatedly ask for irrefutable proof of the
evidence that supports a claim. Such so-called justiﬁcationism is a trap
into which any argument may fall, simply because it calls for what sounds
reasonable but is in fact impossible.
Popper is an indeterminist – principally because falsiﬁcation entails
that it must be possible to falsify a statement. Hence incontrovertible
truth, conﬁrmation, is not possible. Even trying to achieve conﬁrmation
by eliminating all other possible hypotheses is doomed to fail. While
scientists often conﬁdently assert that they have eliminated all other
possibilities, they tend to be rather over-conﬁdent in these assertions
(Stanford 2006). Even if they try to whittle down the number of possible
hypotheses by claiming they are only looking at relevant hypotheses (e.g.
dismissing nonsense like the Great Interlocutor) as Goldman (1986) has
proposed, the problem does not go away – in fact, we could see potential
danger in the inevitably subjective assessments of relevance that scien-
tists may make in such situations. Corpus linguists should be particularly
alert to this possibility, given that corpus-based hypotheses were long
dismissed – and dogmatically continue to be dismissed – as irrelevant by
some, as discussed in the previous section.
The preceding notwithstanding, the use of induction means that our
results, at best, are bound by the data we provide to the method – so if we
study a corpus based on the language of 627 people from one country at
one point in time, the inferences we make based on that about language
in general in that country, or even of those speakers in general – the
inductive movement from the part to the whole – is of necessity open to
falsiﬁcation (in line with Principle 10).
So rather than reaching for these unattainable perfections, what we can
do instead, and what science does, is deal with conventions; these are the
answer to the conundrum with which we ﬁnished Section 2.4. They are
provisional truths, deemed by the community of researchers studying
a topic, which, for example, tell us that a particular method is the one that
is to be taken as the accepted norm or that the data set to be used is
accepted as sufﬁcient for the purpose – as was discussed with reference to
particularism in Section 2.4. In making such decisions, they should not, of
course, attempt to escape the bounds of falsiﬁability. If they do so, their
2.8 The Unattainable – Truth
63
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 76 -->

musings are metaphysical. For example, much work has been undertaken
using the Brown family sampling structure. A convention has been
established that this sampling structure is one that may be used over
time. The data that are produced within this framework are widely used,
and not only for one variety of English (see Section 6.1.1), nor simply for
English (e.g. Chinese, see McEnery and Xiao 2004). Similarly, the corpus
methods used to compare them have approached some level of conven-
tion – there are procedures that are used across the studies, such as
signiﬁcance testing, collocation and keywords that are conventionally
accepted within corpus linguistics. Are these methods and are these
data the only methods and data that may be used to study a range of
linguistic phenomena? No – it is perfectly easy to imagine different
sampling structures and even different methods for looking at some of
the research questions studied. Does this prove that the sampling structure
or the methods in question are ‘wrong’ or ‘deﬁcient’ in some way? If this
was done as a prelude to the reach for the perfect set of methods or the
perfect data, this would be dangerous and, potentially, the beginning of an
intellectual dead end where researchers seek to get data sets and methods
‘right’. Science does not work by waiting for the perfect data and the
perfect method.
Principle 20: Corpus linguistics proceeds by convention to use methods, data
sets, ontologies etc. accepted by a community of scholars as ﬁt for the
investigation of language.
Science works by incremental reﬁnement in which interim ﬁndings are
replaced by other interim ﬁndings as science is not about decisive leaps to
truth, it is rather ‘a process of step by step approximations’ (Popper
2002a: 267),42 a point Kuhn ampliﬁes and builds upon, noting that
a paradigm shift is something that may entail operational consequences,
arguing, as a result, that ‘retooling is an extravagance reserved for the
occasion that demands it’ (Kuhn 1962: 76).
The sharpest critique, from the perspective of the scientiﬁc method,
that could be made of the work with the Brown family is that it deﬁes
falsiﬁcation and that therefore it is metaphysical. Yet this critique does
not hold – it is an irony that it is because our methods and data are fallible
42 We would add that those steps should typically also be small – one should ‘not to rock
the boat more than need be’ (Quine, 1990: 15) when amending a theory following
Quine’s (1990: 14) ‘maxim of minimum mutilation’.
64
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 77 -->

that we gain the greatest defence of those methods and data as scientiﬁc.
With this in mind, we can recast debates such as those between Millar
(2009) and Leech (2011) not as a dispute, but more appropriately as
a normal feature of scientiﬁc debate. One scholar (Leech) uses one set
of conventionally accepted data and methods to make a statement about
decline in modal use in English. Another scholar (Millar) seeks to falsify
that statement by using another conventionally accepted data set and
similar methods. If one accepts that Millar’s falsiﬁcation is right, it would
be as wrong to conclude that the new statement produced by Millar was
right, while that produced by Leech was wrong. Even if Leech’s state-
ment is falsiﬁed, Millar’s is as susceptible to falsiﬁcation as Leech’s was.
What if we decide Millar has not falsiﬁed Leech? In that case, we would
say that it was good scientiﬁc practice for Millar to attempt to falsify
Leech but we would not say that Leech was ‘right’ – it is simply that the
attempt at falsiﬁcation has failed and that Leech’s ﬁndings had gained
some degree of corroboration as a result. Studies like this are crucial, they
show the incremental steps by which knowledge progresses. By such
steps progress can be made and, crucially, in our theories of knowledge
we can draw out a history of why the set of statements that have currently
not been falsiﬁed are as they are – we can show which studies falsiﬁed
which prior statements and show how new statements came to be as
a consequence of that – a cycle of destruction by falsiﬁcation followed
by renewal as statements are developed, which do not run afoul of the
previous falsiﬁcation, and slowly drive science forward through a process
that might loosely be called eliminative inferencing. The Brown sampling
structure and the methods used by Leech, while they may be accepted by
convention, are also indubitably scientiﬁc by the standards adopted by
this book so far and, above all else, they are falsiﬁable and hence may
take part in this endless cycle of reﬁnement.
Principle 21: Corpus linguistics proceeds incrementally as testing by falsiﬁcation
proceeds and the cycle of hypothesis formation is driven onwards.
Of course, there are circumstances in which any study may evade falsiﬁ-
cation – when it limits itself to making speciﬁc comments solely about the
content of the data on which it is based. If we have a copy of the
Lancaster-Oslo-Bergen (LOB) corpus and we look within it and say
‘the string xxxx does not occur in the corpus’, then this cannot be falsiﬁed
as a statement. We know the set of strings which make up the corpus and
none of those strings are xxxx. However, given that we rarely study
2.8 The Unattainable – Truth
65
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 78 -->

language fruitfully in this way, no more than we can study the universe in
this way in the observational sciences, such speciﬁc statements – while
they achieve a limited form of so-called numerical universality (we can
observe all examples and make a statement based on that complete
knowledge) are of limited interest as they do not constitute a theory
that stretches beyond the very limited set of data observed.43 A theory
of language should aim to achieve an explanation of language that is
more general – and hence we cannot make statements based on numer-
ical universality; we observe but a fraction of language at best, just as an
astronomer observes but a fraction of the universe.
Consequently, theories are built on all-statements – those that do not
achieve numerical universality – because the observation cannot be com-
pleted, or indeed is incompletable: ‘the veriﬁcation of a natural law could
only be carried out by empirically ascertaining every single event to which
the law might apply, and by ﬁnding that every such event actually conforms
to the law – clearly an impossible task’ (Popper 2002a: 42). Linguistic theory
is also built on all-statements. Corpus linguistics holds by convention that no
matter how large a corpus of a living or dead language, we cannot help but
make all-statements as, though large, our corpora do not allow us to
approach a question through numerical universality. Some claims in corpus
linguistics could be viewed as being in line with Popper’s (2002a: 42)
recommendation for all-statements in the natural sciences – we make non-
veriﬁable statements that can be put in a form such as ‘Of all languages at
a speciﬁc point in time it is true that . . . ’. Such non-veriﬁable all-statements
may claim to reveal universals derived from corpus data as argued for by
researchers such as Levishna and Moran (2020) and Choi et al. (2022).
Language acquisition studies, for example, are a further important source
of linguistic universal all-statements (see, e.g., Goldin-Meadow’s 2015 work
on the resilient properties of language and the corpus-based study of uni-
versal mechanisms in second language acquisition studies by Werner et al.
2020). However, corpus linguistics may also engage with making speciﬁc
statements, typically limited to languages or speech communities – consider,
for example, word frequency lists based on speciﬁc corpora (e.g. Hoﬂand
and Johansson 1982; Leech et al. 2001) that provide nothing but statements
of frequency of the word forms in a speciﬁc corpus. Yet these do not achieve
numerical universality, they simply describe the contents of a corpus and
43 Controlled languages may prove to be an exception to this statement – though our focus
here is on naturally occurring human languages, not controlled languages. Hence
throughout this book we comment on unbounded, naturally occurring, human lan-
guages rather than controlled ones.
66
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 79 -->

then, through induction, claim that the description matches that of the
language as a whole, though such non-veriﬁable statements made on the
basis of such data should be clearly circumscribed as the interaction of
Principles 5 and 10 suggests. So, for example, some of Leech’s statements
should be recast in forms such as ‘Of all writers on British English between
years X and Y, it is true that overall they used modals less through time.’
The key is that the statements are made speciﬁc to what the language in the
corpus can allow one to say in some respect (in this case time), yet the claim
goes beyond the corpus itself – the inference drawn from the data is
temporally bounded, but there is a non-veriﬁable claim that goes beyond
a numerical universal claim about the data. It is an inductive process.
Importantly, the scope of the claim is expressed clearly and the claim is, as
scientiﬁc statements are, non-veriﬁable and falsiﬁable.
Note that the boundedness of the speciﬁc statements made by corpus
linguists are a direct reﬂection of the data that they use. While it is
possible to step beyond those statements to make broader or even uni-
versal all-statements, to do so is to appeal, once more, to induction and
convention. For example, the Brown family of corpora are accepted as
a proxy for the variety of English represented in them as a whole and
hence act as a basis for induction. However, it is the work of moments to
think of a register not represented in those corpora – repair manuals,
public notices and holiday brochures all spring to mind as examples.
Similarly, the spoken data in the BNC1994 is accepted by convention as
a proxy of spoken English of the time, though there are manifest issues
with the data, as reported by Love et al. (2017).
While it may seem to be an act of self-ﬂagellation to be open about
the issues with the construction of a corpus and to caution users of how
the data may or may not support the generation of speciﬁc or universal
all-statements, in a context where the scientiﬁc method is not fully
thought through, this can lead to the impression that corpus creators
are talking about deﬁciencies in their work that should lead users to
simply reject it. On the contrary, it is beyond doubt that to avoid
critique by relying on convention is the wrong route to take. It is
a form of dogma. We should be open and honest about our data sets,
in the full knowledge that a universal data set for language capable of
securely answering all research questions about language is about as
credible a scientiﬁc construct as the Great Interlocutor. Data sets are
as provisional as hypotheses and the two are ineluctably bound. As we
progress, we progress both because of and with our data sets, reﬁning
them as the data are critiqued and falsiﬁed to the hard limits of what is
practicable. Given that perfection in a data set is going to be
2.8 The Unattainable – Truth
67
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 80 -->

a vanishingly rare attainment, if it is attainable at all, corpus linguists
should accept constructive critiques of a corpus as a part of the scien-
tiﬁc method just as attempts to falsify hypotheses are a normal part of
the scientiﬁc process. This leads to the following principle.
Principle 22: Corpus linguists should be open and honest about the limitations of
their data and methods.
Yet critiques, especially self-critiques, by corpus builders, are relative rare in
corpus linguistics though there are some notable, laudable, examples (nota-
bly Stenström et al. 2002; Love et al. 2017; Love 2020; Saario 2021). While
critique is to be welcomed, however, the critique should be a set of scientiﬁc
statements about data that help one to warrant the formation of a set of
statements based on it and that allow one to scope the claims that may be
made from it, making it clear where the resulting all-statements are indivi-
dual or universal. What should be avoided at all costs are what we would
broadly term socially rather than scientiﬁcally motivated critiques – for
example, a critique through an appeal to conviction (I strongly feel that is
the wrong way to do it because I would have done it differently), selective
critique (critiquing some data sets while accepting the convention that
others are beyond reproach) or critique that proceeds from a dogmatic
adherence to any one set of hypotheses thought to be ‘true’ (refuting the
data because they falsify a hypothesis held to be true by convention).
2.9 The Attainable – Provisional Truths
All the preceding may sound rather damning – it is not, it is an honest
assessment of what scientiﬁc enquiry is about. Provisional results are
drawn from the best data available at the time when the statement is
tested. A test of their worth is that they stand to be falsiﬁed and replaced.
Data are collected, data are critiqued, data may, if possible, be re-
collected in response to a critique. The cycles of creation and destruction
pass through data and through hypotheses.
Principle 23: Corpus data are as provisional as hypotheses and in principle may
pass through a similar life cycle.
Yet may we not, at least, one might argue, put the data question to bed?
May we not at least get that ‘right’? Sadly, the answer must be ‘no’ – a few
68
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 81 -->

confounding factors alone can show how Grail-like this quest would be.
For any question of reasonable scale in linguistics, we are forced to build
data sets that do not allow us to proceed via numerical universality. If we
want to say something about the language of all British English speakers
based on a corpus, even if we can agree on the set of such speakers, we
cannot collect all of their utterances. As soon as we accept we cannot do
so, no matter how much effort we put into modelling the results, the
corpus will almost always be an approximation of the whole44 and the gap
between model and reality will always be one from which the non-
veriﬁable nature of our statements will emerge. Similarly, even if we do
collect all the utterances at one moment in time, the next moment in time
will bring with it a new set of utterances that may confound the state-
ments we make based on the one moment we have been able to gather. It is
for these reasons that we need to consider repeatability, to verify results,
and in particular to undertake replication in order to test whether, in the
gap between model and reality, there may be found data that confound our
hypothesis. We will explore this further in Chapters 6 and 7.
Even if we do collect all our utterances, and even if we do decide how to
represent those utterances (though even the apparently simple task of
marking up a corpus or transcribing it may be a source of dispute), we
then enter another space in which we need to theorise and where we have
plenty of scope for demonstrating that ‘true’ statements have no space in
linguistics – ontological knowledge. Within a data set we may begin to
generate inferences that seek to group and classify individual examples
into classes. For example, let us accept, for the moment, that the concept of
what a word is in our corpus is a given. As linguists we now typically begin
to categorise, moving from the singular objects (or individual names as
Popper calls them) of observation to classes of objects that we believe are,
in some sense, the same or at least similar. In Popper’s terms, we move
from singular cases (for our purposes here, a word) to a universal concept
(or names). For example, we may generate a statement that hypothesises
the existence of the category Noun and then use the criteria for member-
ship of the group Noun to both classify the words in question and to test the
universal concept of Noun in doing so. This form of ontological knowledge
is at the root of the subject, with different singular concepts (e.g. phoneme,
morpheme, word) being used to generate universal concepts of different
sorts. The same data may participate in the generation of these universal
44 In most cases, a corpus is a sample taken from a much larger population. An exception
to this will be specialised corpora of, for example, complete works of authors such as
Shakespeare or Dickens.
2.9 The Attainable – Provisional Truths
69
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 82 -->

concepts in different ways – so we might categorise the singular concept of
a word to the universal concept of part-of-speech, or the universal concept
of semantic ﬁeld.
The tangle of potential singular and universal concepts in linguistics is
considerable and is what, in many ways, makes the subject such an
engaging challenge. It also explains why Popper (2002a: 45) concluded
that ‘whether there are any individual things corresponding to
a description by means of universal names, and if so how many, must
always remain an open question’. The complexity of the task is something
that we might be able to manage if it were not for the preliminary nature
of the ontological knowledge that we can apply to the question. Yet we
cannot avoid a process of interpretation because ‘Interpretations are . . .
unavoidable: we cannot even describe without selecting, and once we
select we interpret, and even use principles of interpretation, knowingly
or not’ (Jarvie 2016: 287). In short, linguistic categorisations seem to be
non-veriﬁable also.
This is a substantial problem when trying to conceive of linguistics as
being in the same category as, for example, chemistry in being a science.
Consider the periodic table. This provides a ground truth for work in
chemistry in the sense that, while we should, of course, keep an open
mind about this categorisation, it has been corroborated repeatedly in
everything from the most complex of experiments to simple exercises in
school chemistry classes to the extent that we may conclude that the
‘Universe is a huge place. But from a chemical point of view it is quite
monotonous’ (Wächtershäuser 1995: 189). The periodic table provides
a sound, conventionally accepted, ontological basis for chemistry. The
same is not true in linguistics – the categories we apply to language, and
the membership of them, may vary permissibly. For example, Sampson
(2017: 3–5) has written in challenging terms about the folly of considering
linguistics to be a science, a view we largely share with him, as will
become apparent as this book proceeds. He holds out the possibility of
exempting phonetics from this view (Sampson 2017: 6). However, the
interpretive process of imposing categories on human speech sounds is as
contested as other attempts at building ontological knowledge in linguis-
tics – for example, Port and Leary (2005: 927) understand clearly that
research in linguistics stands on ontological knowledge, noting that
‘many formal linguists rely on the notion of a universally available
phonetic space deﬁned in discrete time . . . Discreteness at the phonetic
level guarantees the discreteness of all other levels of language.’
Unfortunately they conclude ‘decades of phonetics research demonstrate
that there exists no universal inventory of phonetic objects’ (Port and
70
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 83 -->

Leary 2005: 927). A further critique of the phoneme as a concept is
provided by Davis (2006). Port and Leary (2005: 959) also rightly draw
a conclusion about the dogmatic necessity of sustaining certain types of
ontological knowledge in linguistics: ‘there is no assurance that
a coherent static description of knowledge exists just because that is
what one wants to study’. Tellingly, both Port and Leary and Davis
appeal to observed evidence – in their case acoustic evidence.
So while we can posit categories and rules of membership for mapping
singular concepts into universal ones, areas of allowable disagreement
quickly emerge, meaning that competing mappings emerge also. Even
where convention means that such divergence may be masked, the diver-
gence in principle exists nonetheless. So, for example, in computational
linguistics there are well-known part-of-speech taggers that use well-
established part-of-speech tagsets. Let us imagine, for a moment, that
one of these taggers becomes the only one used on the planet for the
English language. Everyone says that they are happy with it. Yet does this
mean that the ontology of parts of speech are now ﬁxed and that there is
no allowable disagreement? Of course not – it is simply that, by conven-
tion, people have accepted one ontology over another. It is still the case
that ontological knowledge is as much a provisional construct as the
theories built upon it. All of that, of course, may also rest on corpus
data and tools that are also provisional and bounded. Some linguists may
be accustomed, through convention and conviction, to ignoring this, but
that is not a scientiﬁc objection. Working within one theory or paradigm
within which the singular to universal mappings are accepted may blind
the researcher to the issue, but this does not mean to say, given that no
one theory may claim to be ‘right’, that the issue is a non-issue for them –
it is simply one they choose not to engage with. As long as they are clear,
of course, that this theoretical perspective bounds and limits their claims,
we may accept this as part of the scientiﬁc method – but where the
blindness to the provisional nature of scientiﬁc knowledge is used to
avoid falsiﬁcation and a theory is pursued dogmatically, the paradigm
crosses the border back to the metaphysical.
Of course, we can draw a distinction between the existence of categories45
and category membership. While it may remain a matter of dispute whether
a singular object is or is not a noun, separate from that is the notion of Noun
itself – we may posit the existence of the category through an existential, or
‘there-is’ statement, not committing to enumerating the membership of the
45 We do not wish to re-ignite the philosophical debate about universals and their speciﬁc
ontological nature. For more information, see Moreland (2001).
2.9 The Attainable – Provisional Truths
71
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 84 -->

set, nor seeking to generate rules of classiﬁcation but, rather, asserting that
the set exists and that it has at least one member. Negating the universal
statement produced by an existential statement deﬁes falsiﬁcation by sin-
gular cases – if we say the category exists, then even if a parade of words are
produced that are not nouns, this does not preclude the existence of the
category Noun. Similarly, if we say parafabulate is an English word, one
cannot decisively show that it is not, at the point in time when the question
was considered, an English word. Of course, the inverse of such existential
statements can be tested and falsiﬁed – we can say parafabulate is not an
English word and, in the absence of evidence that it is, my hypothesis is not
falsiﬁed, but is it in principle falsiﬁable. The same would apply to the
statement ‘there are no nouns’.
Principle 24: Corpora can provide corroborations and falsiﬁcations of inverse
existential statements. However, they only provide corroboration of existential
statements.
While this may seem to be a somewhat abstract statement, it has con-
sequences in linguistics – consider the exchange between Chomsky and
Hatcher earlier in the chapter. Chomsky makes the inverse of a there-is
statement. Hatcher counters with a there-is statement, effectively falsify-
ing his statement. Corpora can be very powerful reservoirs of examples
that can produce existential statements about language, as was discussed
earlier in this chapter with reference to the work of Pullum and Scholz
(2002). Given that these statements are produced in ways that are in line
with the scientiﬁc method, their role in linguistics in providing such
existential statements is indispensable. What of the inverse of such state-
ments? Corpora can fail to falsify such statements, but they logically
cannot, unless they represent a set of utterances that can claim numerical
universality, prove the inverse of an existential statement. However, one
could argue that sufﬁcient evidence could be provided to make the
falsiﬁcation of the existential statement likely – the use of probability in
scientiﬁc reasoning will be explored later in the book in Chapters 3 and 4.
2.10 Popper and Theory
So far we have considered how corpus data relate to hypothesis forma-
tion and have proposed that those hypotheses come together in
a complex, interdependent, system to form a theory. What does Popper
say about theory? For him, theories are based on basic statements that
72
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 85 -->

produce the axioms of a theory – what one might call the fundamental
assumptions on which a theory is built. The axioms work in such a way
that, through a clear logical system encapsulating the axiom, the actions
of the system modelled by the theory can be explained from the axioms
upwards, so to speak. The system itself should also be parsimonious – the
number of axioms needed are just the number needed to perform this
task, no more. A system like this is familiar to linguists – claims relating to
how word formation, syntax and conversation work in language are all, in
Popper’s terms, axioms of linguistic theories that seek to explain human
language use. In corpus linguistics such axioms also apply – however, the
axioms of theories formed from corpus data more commonly have at
their core theoretical assumptions about frequency in linguistics as the-
ories such as construction grammar and usage based models of language
show clearly. Similarly, corpus linguistics has spawned axioms that may
then be accommodated in other theoretical systems – for example, col-
location could be viewed as an axiom, but for a long time the axiom was
separate from a theory of language. Hoey (2005) took collocation as an
axiom and placed it within an overarching theory of language that built
upon that axiom. Others used frequency data alone, and not collocation,
to form sets of axioms that varied by degree from collocation – for
example, within construction grammar (see Yoon and Gries 2016, for
an overview and selection of work), usage based models of language (see,
e.g., Tomasello 2000) and so-called distributional semantics in computa-
tional linguistics (Desagulier 2019).
Yet no matter where the axioms are deployed, the system within which
they are deployed must satisfy four conditions to be scientiﬁc. First, they
must not be mutually contradictory – a system cannot contain axioms
which conﬂict. Note that this is a key feature of Popper’s thinking that we
have encountered already in this chapter and that clearly aligns with
more general thinking about the scientiﬁc method (as outlined in
Chapter 1). Second, the axioms must be independent – it must not be
possible to infer the result of one axiom from the operation of another as
that would mean the axiom was redundant. So, for example, if we posited
an axiom of collocation but it was demonstrated that collocation was
something that could be predicted and modelled by an axiom relating to
grammar (as Xiao and McEnery 2006, initially believed – see Section 2.6),
then the axiom of collocation would be redundant. Third, the axioms as
a set should be sufﬁcient to allow the inferences that the theory is
required to make – for example, about language in use. Finally, the
axioms should be necessary – there should be no superﬂuous axioms in
the system, only those that allow the pursuit of the goal of the system
2.10 Popper and Theory
73
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 86 -->

itself. These rules are useful in linguistic theory in terms of making the
system parsimonious (see Section 1.6), determining how the axioms
should interact and, importantly, providing the possibility of falsiﬁcation
through clarity. If one is clear about what a theoretical system is and is not
supposed to address, and one simultaneously states clearly how the parts
of it are supposed to interact, then the conditions are established to
perform tests on the system without running into the rhetorical defence
of ‘ah, but we were not modelling that’, for example.
2.11 The Corpus and Theory
It is important to note that, if this book had been written thirty years ago,
the discussion of axioms and theory would be necessarily brief. Corpus
evidence and axioms arising from corpus linguistics were largely not
integrated into linguistic theory. However, in the intervening period the-
ories have developed that incorporate evidence from corpus data in the
creation of axioms in their system (e.g. construction grammar) or that are
wholly reliant on axioms arising from corpus linguistics research (e.g.
Hoey’s theory of lexical priming). Accordingly, Popper’s writing on the
properties of systems, and how axioms interact with and within them, is
more relevant for corpus linguists to consider today than it ever has been.
This is especially true as the relationship between this characterisation of
a theory and falsiﬁcation are so strong – the principles give researchers
ways of thinking about testing theories as opposed to simply testing state-
ments, it allows corpus linguistics to switch to reﬂecting critically on theory.
Let us consider collocation as an axiom. We can establish the possible
membership of the set of collocates for a node word through a series of
statement equations – such as ‘a collocate of a node word x has an MI
value > 3’ with other statements in the system establishing how to
calculate MI and the frequencies needed by the MI equation. Yet on
what basis do we establish the various parameters that we need to set in
order to test whether something is a collocate? We need to determine
important features such as which association measure to use, and why,
for example. Similarly, we need to set the span of the collocate, that is,
the number of words either side of the node word that we count as being
part of the collocation effect. These, and other issues, need to be
determined for us to properly deﬁne and use our axiom of collocation.
While we may be able to develop answers to these questions that will
allow an axiom of collocation to operate in consistent, logical, terms, the
question of its correspondence to reality will always arise. Axioms
typically rely on extra-logical constants in order to operate – those
74
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 87 -->

extra-logical constants are ‘statements about empirical objects’ Popper
(2002a: 53). To give an example used by Popper (2002a: 53), a line in
geometry may be viewed as such a constant as it can be mapped system-
atically and reliably to something in reality – for example, light rays in
homogenous media. So the way in which we complete our axioms, while
not invalidating them as logical constructs, may push them further away
from, or towards, a better correspondence with reality as the extra-
logical constants are revealed to be reliable constants or not, operated
upon in ways we judge to be in correspondence with reality, or not, for
example. Ultimately, we might assume that the best way in which this
axiom will be completed is through careful testing, rooted in falsiﬁca-
tion, of competing axioms, that is, axiom creation can be tied closely to
processes of hypothesis formation and testing. So elements of our
axiom, especially those that play the role normally reserved for extra-
logical constants, are, we would argue, often set by convention. We
hypothesise that these elements of the axiom are well corroborated
enough for us to provisionally accept the axiom as ‘true’. We rely on
convention to proceed yet as ‘to the suitability of any such convention
opinions may differ; and a reasonable discussion of these questions is
only possible between parties having some purpose in common’
(Popper 2002a: 15).
Statement equations can transition to being what Popper considers an
axiom to be if we can decide, in our case by convention, the set of
admissible values that can ﬁll in the blanks in statement equations such
as those discussed for collocation. In this case we may begin by stating
that the axiom may operate on the set of all words and we can constitute
collocation as an axiom constructed of a set of statement equations. The
axiom would work to categorise any given word as either being or not
being a collocate. The system generates outcomes consistent with the
statement equations, and, in itself, is not subject to falsiﬁcation – the
system operates logically and predictably. While we note that the state-
ment-equations of collocation may be set with wildly different para-
meters on occasion (see Gablasova et al. 2017b), the decision on what is
or what is not a collocate will not be determined on solely mathematical
grounds as we are seeking correspondence with reality, not simply logical
consistency. From a logical perspective, any system of statement-
equations relating to collocation will not be subject to falsiﬁcation, only
to resolution, that is, the system will categorise the data according to the
equations. Once successfully resolved, the possibility of falsiﬁcation does
not lie in the system of statement equations, but outside of it. So how do
our axioms by convention become falsiﬁable?
2.11 The Corpus and Theory
75
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 88 -->

Principle 25: Corpus linguists should seek to explore and develop axioms, which
are integrated, non-redundant, parsimonious and necessary.
Principle 26: When using corpora to explore theory, the critical lens provided by
Principle 25 should be applied to that theory.
Principle 27: Principles 25 and 26 apply equally to axioms and axioms by
convention.
One obvious place to look is at the conventions we have used to establish
our extra-logical constants – for example, ‘word’ in the axiom of colloca-
tion discussed thus far. The problem with this constant is that the map-
ping of the element of the axiom to some empirical constant is unstable.
A survey of tokenisation decisions alone reveal that word counts in
a corpus can vary by up to 17% depending on which corpus tool one
uses to count the words in a corpus (Brezina 2018: 39). If something as
basic as the deﬁnition of a word can vary so freely, it is fair to say that the
basic concepts of linguistics are far from being extra-logical constants,
especially in light of the earlier discussion of ontological knowledge in
linguistics. So the axioms of linguistics are, accordingly, best viewed as
axioms by convention in Popper’s terms.
To some extent, none of this alters the operation of our axioms. These
can still be well ordered, logical and, in their own right, ‘correct’. Yet
while these axioms are not internally falsiﬁable, it may still, of course, be
possible to take elements of the system resting on convention and subject
them to a test of correspondence to reality as entailed by our scientia
realis view of corpus linguistics. These attempts at ostensive deﬁnition,
correlating elements of the system with ‘real-world’ objects lies at the
heart of corpus linguistics (Principle 60). Yet while corpus linguistics
allows us to study, through quasi-contact (Principle 14), language
through real-world objects such as texts and transcriptions of speech
(Principles 2, 5 and 7), an axiom such as collocation, if it limits itself to
being an axiom relating to what one may observe in such objects, is quite
uninteresting – one might say that it approaches descriptive adequacy,
that is, it can provide a well-ordered system of observations about linguistic
data that are repeatable, but it lacks any explanatory adequacy. It does not
explain why collocations may be observed unless the axiom embodies
76
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 89 -->

a claim that, for example, through observing collocation in a text, we have
quasi-contact with the operations of the mind. This is a claim that some
(see Arppe et al. 2010, for a discussion of work in this tradition and the
challenges it faces) but not all (notably Teubert 2005: 2–3, who states
bluntly that corpus linguistics ‘is not concerned with the psychological
aspects of language’) corpus linguists embrace. We must embrace this
claim if we want linguistic theories of explanatory power.46 Hence
collocation for us is either an axiom that helps explain the ‘why’ of
language or it is simply one of many observations that we could make of
a text that are of no interest to linguistic theory (possibly falling under
Principle 13). However, in embracing it we accept that the reality we are
observing – corpus evidence – permits quasi-contact with a reality we wish
to observe, language in the mind (Principle 14). That has consequences for
our view of corpus linguistics as a science and for what ‘reality’ we are
observing. We will return to consider this in the next chapter.
Nonetheless, by reaching beyond the text to the mind, we thus set up
a condition for the axiom that permits falsiﬁcation – the statement
equations of collocation must not simply be an orderly system in them-
selves, they compose a system that must satisfy an ostensive deﬁnition of
collocate that corresponds to real-world observations. That is implied in
forcing the statements to be based on textual (and transcribed audible,
inter alia) reality. Yet beyond that we should have statements that force
the results of the statements in operation to correspond to psychological
reality, which it is much harder to test. So an interdependence between
collocation as observable through an ostensive, textual, deﬁnition and
collocation as an observable psychological phenomenon corroborated
through non-textual evidence is forged. In taking that route, the set of
statements to be attached to the axiom of collocation increases and, as
noted as a possibility earlier in the chapter, an interdependence with
other axioms is created, for example, if we believe that the movement of
the eye in reading can be impacted by malformedness, the system we set
up to encapsulate the beliefs we have about reading intersects with the
statement system underlying the axiom of collocation. At that juncture
we have a way into understanding, and purposefully controlling, the
interaction of different components of a theory and, through that, of
methods. To pursue questions relating to what we might provisionally
call a theory of reading, it is unlikely that we can use precisely the same
46 See Gries (2012) for a good, and at times quite entertainingly forthright, discussion of
the divide between those corpus linguists who believe that corpus linguistics is, or is not,
linked to an exploration of psychological reality.
2.11 The Corpus and Theory
77
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 90 -->

methods as we would for pursuing the collocation axiom. Yet by bringing
multiple methods to bear, not simply on a whim, but because the system
of interdependencies between theories and the elements that compose
them requires it, we may design and control such experiments in a way
that is more scientiﬁc, a phrase we use loosely here to indicate that it
permits the potential falsiﬁcation of an axiom that otherwise might be
viewed as sealed and not subject to falsiﬁcation, that is, the axiom of
collocation.
Throughout this book we will refer to axioms such as the axiom of
collocation discussed here as axioms by convention. On one level these
axioms are no different from any other axioms – they are well ordered
and logical. However, in using this terminology we are emphasising that,
within those axioms, there are assumptions being made about extra-
logical constants that may not be widely accepted or that may be falsiﬁed
in time. It is only to highlight the provisional nature of these constants
that we are using this terminology to remind the reader that whatever the
basis of our axioms, they still rest, potentially, on foundations of sand.
Principle 28: Methods should be combined so as to maximise the potential
falsiﬁcation of any hypothesis explored.
At this point, we touch upon method and the practicalities of testing the
interaction and operation of axioms. In the next chapter, we will explore
what Popper had to say about method and what that may, in turn, mean
for corpus linguistics.
78
What Is Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.004 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 91 -->

CHAPTER THREE
How to Do Science?
Popper is interested in the logic of scientiﬁc discovery rather than pre-
cisely how that discovery is pursued in practice. Consequently, Popper is
not the place to look for recommendations on speciﬁc methods to use to
investigate different areas of science. He does make some useful state-
ments on method, however, rooted in his logical system. Possibly the
most useful overall contribution to thinking about method is that he says
that we should construct methods that prevent the type of rhetorical
defences that may confound falsiﬁcation. Importantly, he recommends
persistence – where a system escapes falsiﬁcation by a rhetorical trick, set
the trick aside and persist in promoting the falsiﬁcation. In our terms,
Principle 11 in concert with Principle 21 should be the driver that keeps
the corpus linguist seeking falsiﬁcation; they should not satisfy them-
selves with a single corroboration.
Popper accepts that some of a researcher’s responses to falsiﬁcation
may be legitimate – for example, the introduction of ad hoc modiﬁcations
to a system – but he makes a presumption in favour of falsiﬁability.
Where presented with a choice, one should make no choice that reduces
the degree of falsiﬁability in a system. This later argument is one that runs
very much in favour of corpus linguistics and leads to the following
principle.
Principle 29: Corpus linguists, when selecting data and tools to examine a
hypothesis, should select those which maximise the potential falsiﬁability of
that hypothesis while also abiding by other principles, notably Principle 7.
Even if correspondence is only forced to textual reality, this is a better
road to veriﬁable falsiﬁcation than an appeal to opinion through intui-
tion. We must select on a basis that the choice that we make ‘does not
diminish the degree of falsiﬁability or testability of the system in ques-
tion, but, on the contrary, increases it’ (Popper 2002a: 62). Note that, in
our
reasoning,
this
would
cover
multi-method
and
triangulated
approaches also (see Baker and Egbert 2016 and Egbert and Baker
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 92 -->

2019 for plentiful examples of methodological triangulation involving
corpora) – by seeking falsiﬁcation across a range of systems and using
methods that may falsify different observations, the potential for falsiﬁ-
cation is increased. By contrast, if we choose not to extend our observa-
tions, where we may, or if we retreat to metaphysical modes of enquiry
when we need not do so, we are expressly breaking the broad methodo-
logical rules laid out by Popper.
Using such methods to test axioms and the statements on which they
are built, Popper builds to a deﬁnition of theory (Popper 2002a: 65):
A theory is to be called ‘empirical’ or ‘falsiﬁable’ if it divides the class of all
possible basic statements unambiguously into the following two non-empty
sub-classes. First the class of all basic statements with which it is inconsis-
tent . . . and secondly, the class of those basic statements which it does not
contradict.
The ﬁrst set Popper calls the class of potential falsiﬁers, while the second
is that which the system permits. The potential falsiﬁers are so called
because if, on inspection, they should not be excluded by the theory, then
the theory fails. Note the set of falsiﬁers cannot be empty – if it is, then the
system cannot be falsiﬁed and hence it is not scientiﬁc. Yet note also that
the examples are not necessarily falsiﬁers, just potentially so. In addition,
the rules that produced the set of permitted statements needs to be
complete for this simple bifurcation of the problem space to hold – if
our rules produce a set of permitted statements that do not correspond
well to reality, then the rules are ﬂawed from the outset.
Let us return to the axiom of collocation. Popper’s identiﬁcation of
potential falsiﬁers is a helpful way of thinking how Principle 21 may
operate – in each case, as we account for data, what we need to focus
on is not corroboration, but more opportunities for falsiﬁcation. Imagine
that we have a corpus containing writing produced in Mandarin in China
in the year 2009, and that we generate a system of collocation that
accurately identiﬁes all and only the collocates of each word in that
corpus. We enshrine that system of collocation in a set of statements in
an axiom. The axiom can then, for any given word, generate two sets – the
collocates of the word and the potential falsiﬁers, the non-collocates of
the word.
These ideas, while appealing, do not ﬁt perfectly with corpus linguis-
tics, let alone linguistics. The ﬁrst issue relates to perspective. The set of
potential falsiﬁers should not be viewed from the perspective of the
corpus alone. In principle, the set of potential falsiﬁers in this case is to
be generated from all texts produced in the space/time bound context the
80
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 93 -->

system is being produced to operate on. If we built the system with a
wider set of parameters – for example, making claims about all varieties
of Chinese at any point in time – then the potential set of falsiﬁers grows
accordingly. Even if it transpires that the potential falsiﬁers in corpus A
do not, in fact, falsify the system (as would certainly happen if the set is
empty) and we have access to no more data that matches the space/time/
mode that our system models, we do not have a system that we can
describe as accurate – we are merely denied the possibility of seeking
further falsiﬁcation, though it is in principle possible. That is why,
Principles 2 and 5 acknowledge that we will not have completeness in
our studies, yet Principle 16 still highlights the possibility of falsiﬁcation.
Falsiﬁcation is curtailed, not abandoned, by Principle 5. We also
acknowledge the provisional nature of our ﬁndings in Principle 9.
Hence we accept that the set of potential falsiﬁers typically remains of
indeterminate size in a corpus study and may, in effect, be unknowable
because the falsiﬁers exist beyond our access them – for example, in
unrecorded spoken Mandarin 300 years ago.
The second issue relates to the completeness of our rules – if we have
no way of assessing whether any of the words in the set of potential
falsiﬁers are, in fact, collocates, then it becomes impossible to falsify
our system as we lack the ability to act on the set of potential falsiﬁers.
We return to the issue of the fuzzy nature of ontological knowledge in
linguistics. Our response to it is enshrined in Principle 3 – we are clear
about how in a speciﬁc study we have, in all likelihood, arbitrarily yet
consistently resolved that fuzziness. Yet the potential for fuzziness
remains and instantiating ontological knowledge, permissibly and plau-
sibly, may generate different sets of permitted statements and potential
falsiﬁers. So the sets in such a case are contingent upon the ontological
knowledge used for categorisation – this explains the importance of
Principle 3 as it makes clear not only presuppositions in the ontology,
but also has a direct link through to judging statements as permissible or
potential falsiﬁers.
The third issue follows on from this – in such a context it may be very
difﬁcult to determine rules that select only the permitted. We may ﬁnd
that the indeterminate nature of the categorisation leads to loose axioms
producing falsiﬁcations within the set of permitted statements. While
logically the statements are permitted, quasi-contact with reality con-
founds that permissibility. For example, when we inspect our two sets, we
may see words that are clearly not collocates in the set of permitted
statements. In such a case we have approximate, heuristic rules rather
than precise rules. By extension, it also becomes impossible to state
How to Do Science?
81
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 94 -->

clearly that the statements in the axiom are ‘true’ just because they
generate these two sets.
It is the inherent tension between the provisional nature of ‘truth’,
permitted statements and the potential refutation inherent in the set of
potential falsiﬁers that sits at the heart of scientiﬁc theory in Popper’s
account of science. Yet here we see that the fuzzy nature of linguistic
categorisations interacting with quasi-reality may mean that the system
Popper uses to divide the set of statements we may encounter may not
apply so neatly to linguistics, at least as the ﬁeld stands. This will be a
theme to which we will return in this chapter, in particular in Section 3.6
when we introduce the concept of range that helps to deal with such
issues in the set of permissible statements. For now, to ease discussion in
the rest of this chapter up to that point, we will proceed as if linguistics did
generate axioms with well-deﬁned sets of permissible statements and
potential falsiﬁers.
3.1 Consistency
Popper also sets another important standard by which a theoretical
system must be judged – consistency. A system must be consistent in its
outputs. If one has a theoretical system and one asks it to judge a piece of
data and it permits it, then the system must consistently permit it. The set
of permitted statements is stable until and unless the system of state-
ments on which the theory is based is changed, that is, the theory is
revised. Popper accepts that we may have unstable systems that prove
to be useful, but they do not count as scientiﬁc as they are not consistent.
A good example of such systems can be found in natural language
processing – topic models, based on Bayesian reasoning, have an inherent
instability. Given the same parameters, each time they run they may
produce different results.47 Even if these results prove to be useful, in
Popper’s terms the system that generates topic models is not scientiﬁc as
it is not stable – Popper also notes the possibility that such systems may
simply be run and run again until one gets a convenient answer from
them. Brookes and McEnery (2019) have made precisely this criticism of
topic models – they are unstable and, even if useful, open a pathway to
47 This may be changed if the random seed that is used by the topic model system is saved
to be used in subsequent runs of the system. It should be noted, however, that any such
seed is arbitrary, one of many such possible seeds and may be selected and reselected
until the person running the system decides to select the results produced by one such
seed, should they wish to do so.
82
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 95 -->

‘cherry picking’ results. As an explanatory model of language, in line with
the scientiﬁc philosophy put forward by Popper, topic models, because of
their unstable nature, are metaphysical rather than scientiﬁc constructs.
Consistency gives rise to two sets of responses from a system: it divides
statements into those that are admissible and those that it contradicts.
These sets are stable. A theory in the process of being falsiﬁed will draw
from the pool of potential falsiﬁers and determine whether the state-
ments in question are admissible – if they are, but they are not permitted,
then falsiﬁcation has occurred. If they are admissible and permitted, then
falsiﬁcation has failed, the statement shifts from the set of potential
falsiﬁers to the set of permitted statements and the system gains corro-
boration. Consistency makes it clear that for the purpose of testing the
theory as it stands, the statement cannot, at this point, be placed back into
the set of potential falsiﬁers with the hope that when it is tested again the
result will be different.
Principle 30: Consistency is a necessary goal of corpus analysis, annotation and
construction.
Consistency also has a further corollary for the set of potential falsiﬁers –
it entails that the statements within it may all relate to the system equally.
No statements are singled out as being potentially incompatible, for
example. This has an important consequence that has been felt in linguis-
tics – what set of statements determines the set of potential falsiﬁers?
Corpus linguistics offers an answer – attested language use is what a
theory should be judged by, as per Principle 60 (though note that we
will return to this slightly underspeciﬁed deﬁnition in the following
chapter). One could conceive of an approach to linguistics that sought
corroboration by bringing together different methodological perspec-
tives, as suggested in this chapter, deploying at each turn a method that
is most likely to result in falsiﬁcation. Yet the statements themselves,
what it is that theory is trying to account for, remain ﬁxed. It is attested
language use. This has been captured informally in corpus linguistics in
ideas such as ‘total accountability’ and ‘no prior selection of data’ (see
Section 2.4 for a discussion of these). One should not favour certain types
of attested language use when testing (no prior selection) nor should one
disregard inconvenient data (total accountability). Of course, corpus
linguistics in generating a corpus does not achieve numerical universality
when considering any given natural language, yet still it aims for consis-
tency through these principles. This is in contrast to the example given in
3.1 Consistency
83
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 96 -->

the previous chapter where Chomsky drew the set of falsiﬁers more
broadly and more metaphysically than a corpus linguist would – the set
of falsiﬁers were conditioned by his intuition, which, as noted, is subject
to change, is hard to verify and is in essence metaphysical. Yet the
systems generated from such an approach to language, an approach
that focuses on an unobservable ideal form of language, rather than
language that gives us a scientiﬁc approach to testing observable reality,
are at heart metaphysical as a result of the scoping of the set of falsiﬁers
being based on a metaphysical process by which the set may be deter-
mined. In corpus linguistics, while the corpus data may be a subset of all
possible observations, the data give rise to the possibility that we may
theorise about language based on ostensive deﬁnitions that are, in the
sense outlined, scientiﬁc.
The issue of total accountability can be identiﬁed again when Popper
critiques other philosophies of science, most notably the work of Carnap
(1932) and Fries (1828) whose approach used so-called protocol sen-
tences – sentences that formally stated the grounds for a belief in order
to explicate theory. Popper’s objection to this approach hinged on a lack
of principled rules for deleting such sentences, that is, of falsifying them
within the logical system they construct. In the absence of such rules,
‘Every system becomes defensible if one is allowed . . . simply to “delete”
a protocol sentence if it is inconvenient’. Further, he comments ‘given a
good supply of protocol sentences, one could even conﬁrm [a theory], by
the testimony of witnesses who have testiﬁed, or protocolled, what they
have seen or heard’ (Popper 2002a: 78–79). This reﬂects back again on a
critique of a reliance on intuition alone as a source of evidence in
linguistics that has run through this account of Popper’s work. Corpus
linguistics could be argued to escape this critique by three means: total
accountability, no prior selection of data and intersubjective testing. The
ﬁrst imposes a strict ban on the ignoring of inconvenient data in a corpus,
yet the second and third means have a key role to play too. By explicitly
not editing or eliciting data to conﬁrm a theory, the corpus approach to
the study of language cannot easily be claimed to run foul of using data
that have been deliberately distorted so as to confound falsiﬁcation
(though a corpus might be used in this way if Principle 29 is ignored).
This folds the two principles of corpus linguistics – total accountability
and no selection of data that suits our theories – into an overarching
principle that effectively excludes both. If we ignore data, or if we wilfully
select data, we are not maximising the potential for falsiﬁcation in our
study. We can ﬁnd examples in the literature, however, where this prin-
ciple is breached. For example, Rühlemann (2013) collects from the
84
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 97 -->

Spoken BNC1994 sequences that conform to an overall pattern of narra-
tive that he wishes to explore. In a case like this, the set of falsiﬁers for
such a conception of narrative is artiﬁcially reduced. If the data are
ﬁltered by the application of what is, in essence, an axiom by convention
determining the structure of a narrative, then the result is a data set that
can only falsify if within the resulting set of utterances are sequences that
do not represent a narrative. Yet the set of excluded utterances are
potential falsiﬁers that may contain narratives not conforming to the
axiom, but these cannot be considered. A more common use of the
BNC1994 is to look at it as a whole with total accountability – rather
than asking people how they swear and basing his study on that, McEnery
(2005) also looked at the Spoken BNC1994, which was not speciﬁcally
collected for the study of swearing, in order to characterise the use of
swearing by speakers of British English. This study approaches the data
with a view to total accountability – all data that was annotated with
metadata relevant to the study was considered, whether or not it con-
formed to previous analytical schemes. While this too reduces the set of
data, it does so in a linguistically principled fashion – the goal was to look
at the operation of the sociolinguistic variables encoded in the corpus, in
combination, on the words studied, hence it abides by Principle 29. Data
that did not have the metadata marked on it was discarded because it
could be used neither to produce nor to refute sociolinguistic inferences
based on the data. In this case, ﬁltering on presence or absence of
metadata did not reduce the range of potential falsiﬁers, rather it identi-
ﬁed the set of potential falsiﬁers from a broader set of data that included
data that could not be used for falsiﬁcation.48
A corpus-based approach was undertaken because it is very easy to
imagine how elicitation may have distorted the view of the subject –
people might have been unwilling to report what they did, and were
very unlikely to be able to have any meaningful, veriﬁable intuitions
about how various demographic features may intersect with variations
in the use of swearing in English.49 Yet by looking at a corpus, not
exercising any prior selection on the collection of the data to skew it
48 We must acknowledge, of course, that the study in question still made assumptions –
ontological knowledge was used (the categorisation of swearwords), naming was
deployed (the category of bad language word was hypothesised) and singular cases
identiﬁed for labelling by a name. The allocation of singular cases to names does, of
course, represent a conventional ﬁlter within this process.
49 Indeed, their perception of the possibility of interaction of factors were likely to be
highly subjective, see Ramsey (1964). For an example of how poor intuitive estimates of
the likelihood of word usage is, see Alderson (2007).
3.1 Consistency
85
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 98 -->

towards one theoretical viewpoint or another and by then applying
the Principle 29, a study was possible that would not have been
otherwise.
Popper rejected much of Carnap’s work, and work derived from it, as
psychologism, the proposition that theory is tested by sense-experience.
This underpinned the thinking of Carnap and was rejected by Popper
for reasons touched upon previously – that the mode of falsiﬁcation in
such reasoning was unclear. In rejecting Carnap, Popper at this point
might be seen to be coming close to what we called for in Chapter 1 of
this book – theorising which, through method, tests its correspondence
with reality. Yet Popper avoids this by distinguishing between objective
science on the one hand and what he terms ‘our knowledge’ on the
other. He wishes to pursue objective science. Popper does not reject the
value of observation. Rather, he wants to be able to distinguish between
what we have observed and what is in some sense ‘true’. So, imagine a
man in his ﬁfties walking down a London street. He might observe that a
young person in the street uses init as some form of tag for a sentence.
Even if he does not understand the function of this tag immediately, he
may hypothesise that this tag is in widespread use by speakers outside of
his social and professional circle, in particular by speakers much
younger than him. He has this piece of observed knowledge – the use
of the tag – but this in itself does not allow him to test the truth of his
hypothesis. Popper wants to move from observation to testing of
hypotheses by asking ‘how do we test scientiﬁc statements by their
deductive consequences? And what kind of consequences can we
select for this purpose if they in their turn are to be inter-subjectively
testable?’ (Popper 2002a: 79–80). Popper accepts that we have convic-
tions and beliefs about ideas and experience – but he pushes those over
into the study of psychology. His argument is that scientiﬁc study
should be based, as has probably become apparent during your reading
of this book so far, on logical connections between statements in a
system of belief. These lead to what he terms the empirical statements
of science. Again as has become apparent by now, the system he argues
for is one in which small, interconnected, steps of reasoning build up a
whole system. In addition, Popper distinguishes the scientiﬁc from the
metaphysical by arguing that such a system should be constructed in
such a way that, once one has learnt the technique supporting the system,
an independent analyst can apply that technique to the system in order to
work through the proof of it. The only disagreements arising from this
would be one based on replicable tests, not opinions of admissibility
expressed in the quasi-formal form of a protocol sentence.
86
How to Do Science?
