https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 99 -->

3.2 Popper and Intuition
Popper does, of course, accept that intuition exists, as do corpus linguists.
Yet Popper treats intuition in the same way as linguists such as McEnery
and Hardie (2011) and Aarts (2011) have argued that intuition should be
used in corpus linguistics – as a stimulus to undertake a scientiﬁc study. In
this view of science, science is not a ﬁeld of study, it is an instrument – it is
the means by which theory is given a form that may be testable and
subjected to falsiﬁcation in a way that is intersubjectively testable. It
speciﬁes theory within a system that is ‘knowable’ – the system and its
working are fully and explicitly characterised. Once communicated, that
system can then be ‘knowable’ to others and, by extension, they may
carry out tests within that system that are warranted by the system and
that are, therefore, ‘knowable’ to others. It would be tempting at this
point to proudly state that corpus linguistics works in this way – the data
may be shared, the procedures applied may be shared and are knowable
to others, and results can be repeated, replicated and subjected to falsi-
ﬁcation. While it is certainly true in part that corpus linguistics represents
a system that seems closer to Popper’s ideal than what Fillmore (1992)
dubbed ‘armchair linguistics’, there are some issues for corpus linguistics
that have accumulated as this chapter has proceeded and will continue to
accumulate. For now, we simply caution readers not to overlook the
degree to which convention must be relied on in any linguistic analysis,
to note that the axioms of corpus linguistics are not necessarily well laid
out and to consider how the data used by corpus linguistics needs to face
the gulf between observation in a context where numerical universality is
not possible and the need to make general claims. With reference to the
gulf between observation and reality, inference is the way in which this
may be approached.
Principle 31: Intuition is a useful guide to hypothesis formation and revision in
corpus linguistics.
Popper is clear that inference based upon observation is the basis of the
scientiﬁc system – for him statements within a system are testable inter-
subjectively by observation. Of relevance for corpus builders, he also notes
that the observers must be suitably placed in space and time. Again this is
something widely accepted in corpus linguistics – observations of conver-
sational spoken English in the 1990s may be based on a corpus built by
observers in that point in time, likewise a corpus of conversational English
3.2 Popper and Intuition
87
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 100 -->

from the second decade of the twenty-ﬁrst century needs to be built by
observers with access to that point in time. So observability is as important
a concept in the philosophy of science as falsiﬁability. This leads to a
revision of Principle 5.
Principle 50: By studying corpora, that is, ﬁnite samples of language bound by
space and time, we can make claims about language itself, with those claims
bound by space and time in the same way as our data.
Corpus linguistics obviously offers that to a greater degree than other
methods for certain types of observations – but once again we will need to
return to that statement and carefully modify it later. However, for now,
let us proceed on the basis of the assumption that corpus evidence is,
unless made inaccessible to other researchers, observable in such a
manner that intersubjective observations of that data may be made,
hence corpora facilitate observability. In what appears to be a move
towards an approach to science rooted in triangulation, Popper also
notes that observable phenomena may be subject to test through differ-
ent senses, or even, perhaps, by perception. Whatever measure is used,
Popper is clear, however, that the observation in question is of a speciﬁc
event in space–time. Again, as a repository of such observations, a corpus
provides a valuable source of evidence on which to base a claim to
observability, though the degree to which that observability may facil-
itate a wider range of methods to focus on the same object through
different observations in space–time is something that we will return to.
3.3 Knowing When to Stop
A potential issue with corpus analysis, or other analyses in linguistics for
that matter, has been raised in this discussion. How far should we expli-
cate the system of statements of which, for example, an axiom is formed?
Fries encountered the problem of inﬁnite regression in the search for
proof – in seeking the basic truth from which a theory may be built, one
runs into a spiral of producing proofs to prove proofs ad inﬁnitum. The
problem is not an unfamiliar one in linguistics – in pragmatics, for
example, when dealing with inferencing about mental states, it is easy
to set up such chains of reasoning – ‘I think that you think that I think that
you think . . . ’ and so on. Linguists have used solutions to this problem
that are very similar to what Popper suggests – he accepts that, on purely
pragmatic grounds, one has at some point to simply accept the basic set of
88
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 101 -->

statements that one is going to decide to accept, reasoning by convention,
if you will. McEnery (1995) built on such ideas to suggest that probability
had a role to play in setting a threshold beyond which it was deemed that
an inference was true, thereby halting what might otherwise be an inﬁnite
regress of inferencing at higher and higher orders. The net effect of such
processes, if accepted, is to render inferencing in language an ampliative
process. This type of reasoning in response to this issue is not peculiar to
linguistics, of course – the shift from data to apparently true belief is as
observable in physics:
Starting from . . . the data that are available for it and on which it takes
support without trying to express them, [the] human mind always rushes
rapidly and as directly as it can toward a representation . . . As soon as such a
representation has been attained, it is spontaneously felt to be ‘true’ in a
primary, certain and absolute way . . . forgetting that it is just an economic,
hypothetical, retro-actively imagined construct. (Mugur-Schächter 2002: 181)
While ampliative reasoning may provide a framework for beliefs to be
revised rationally, in line with Quine’s (1969: 137) doctrine of revisability,
some such beliefs are not rational. People erroneously believe things
about their use of language – for example, on the basis of intuitions
about language all of the time – famously claiming not to use language
in a certain way, though they are observed doing so, as noted, for
example, by Labov (1975). The move to a pragmatic response to reason-
ing should be conscious and deliberate, not the result of a rapid rush – we
should reach for a normative epistemology in which we do not simply
study what people do believe, but concern ourselves ‘with what people
should believe, how conﬁdent they should be, and the conditions under
which they should believe it and be so conﬁdent’ (Schroeder 2015: 379).
This leads to the following principle, which clearly relates strongly to
Principle 4.
Principle 32: Corpus linguistics can be used as the basis for a normative episte-
mology of language.
As a consequence science, like corpus linguistics, ‘tangles with margin of
error, random deviation, and whatever else goes into the applied mathe-
matics and statistics’ (Quine 1990: 20).
Popper’s appeal to a form of pragmatic response to the problem of
inference control is something that is far from unfamiliar in linguistics
and, in statistical testing, it ﬁnds a close analogue in corpus linguistics. Of
3.3 Knowing When to Stop
89
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 102 -->

course, it could be the case that at this point linguists could say that they
will accept perceptions about language as the statements that they wish to
build their theories upon – but Popper expressly argues against relying on
personal experiences as ‘they will not be very suitable to serve as state-
ments at which we stop’ (Popper 2002a: 87). He argues instead that ‘we
do not stop at easily testable statements’.
Popper also wants any halt to be provisional – we may, if we wish, and
perhaps if circumstance dictates it, decide to revise our belief about the
basic statements we accept and to return to them to develop and test them
further, simply accepting them no more. By such a process, the sort of
incremental development of ideas that Popper argues for may be achieved.
So while the chain of reasoning may be inﬁnite in principle, in practice we
deem certain statements to be true and thereby curtail the inﬁnite regress.
It should be noted that observation is, once again, a good guide to when
such basic statements should be revisited. McEnery (1995) showed, at least
provisionally, that deeming of this sort is part of everyday reasoning, with
inferences being deemed to be true until an observed falsiﬁcation of that
belief occurred. Rather than a collapse of the system of belief at this point,
a revision of it occurs and the new system is believed to be true. This
mirrors closely how Popper proposes that the problem of inﬁnite regress
should be controlled in his philosophy of science.
Note Popper’s incrementalism, combined with the provisional nature
of deeming, means that the system moves in a glacial fashion, in accor-
dance with Principle 21. As instrumentation enables previously meta-
physical tests to shift into the scientiﬁc domain, in line with Principle 12,
or a system gains a high degree of corroboration, allowing us to proceed
by convention as if the system were truly in line with Principle 20, the
pressure builds to revisit the basic statements we have decided are true.
This may be either because a new horizon of possible but difﬁcult to test
statements has been revealed, or because the need to seek to falsify a
strongly corroborated system forces the analyst back onto reconsidering
basic statements. Needless to say, falsiﬁcation may problematise our
acceptance of basic statements also, in line with Principle 9.
One point that should be made is that there is clearly a link between a
process such as deeming and convention. Deeming is a process whereby
conventions are established. Yet deeming is expressly capable of revision.
This is important as it stops convention becoming dogmatism – where
ideas cannot be questioned and they are defended on non-scientiﬁc
grounds (see Principle 19). Hence the provisional nature of convention,
as captured in the notion of deeming introduced here has a dual purpose. It
permits scientiﬁc investigation to proceed (Principle 20) yet it also
90
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 103 -->

facilitates the overturning of convention when circumstances dictate that
the time has come for conventions to be put aside (Principle 9).
3.4 Interaction and Redundancy
So far the emphasis in a discussion of theory has been on the construction of
a system within which statements interact, but do not contradict each other,
in support of axioms that in turn combine to constitute theory. Redundancy
is to be eliminated throughout (Principle 25). The elimination of redun-
dancy and the organisation of the system in a hierarchical fashion to con-
stitute theory has another important corollary – the system itself becomes a
guide to the statements that are needed. For example, let us return to the
axiom of collocation. That requires a set of statements in support of it
including quite basic ones, which, for example, specify what constitutes a
word. From the fashioning of the idea of collocation, we proceed to work
through the different statements that are needed in support of it. What we
do not do is generate stray statements. The statements we should work on
are the statements that the system logically requires. Let us exemplify this –
imagine that we are interested in generating a system that demonstrates
how narrative structures operate in written English. In order to do this we
need to work on the axiom, specifying the statements that constitute the
system of narrative, detailing its structure, for example. As part of that work
we may come across quotations. At this point we may tease out Popper’s
point a little and say that we should not necessarily distract ourselves by
thinking about producing a set of statements about how quotation works, as
opposed to how quotation works in narrative. Quotation will occur beyond
narrative, so while we may say that there is an interaction between two
putative axioms – narration and quotation – the two are clearly separate.
Narratives may or may not contain quotation. Quotation may appear in
narrative or in a number of other macrostructures in language. Generating
statements about the basic form of quotation within an account of narrative
is clearly a digression from the direct needs of the system. From the point of
view of the axiom of narrative, any statements we are tempted to make
about quotation are stray statements that are properly a part of a different
system that interacts with, but is not contained within, narrative. While it
may be tempting to think of absurd stray statements – making statements
about the aerodynamic properties of swallows in ﬂight carrying coconuts
within an axiom of collocation – the stray statement injunction, as concep-
tualised here, is most helpful where it provides the ability to distinguish
between 1) statements which are required by and properly part of a system,
2) statements which may be required but are properly part of another
3.4 Interaction and Redundancy
91
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 104 -->

system and 3) unnecessary statements. This allows the researcher to com-
partmentalise the task they have in hypothesis and theory development
within coherent parts of a complex system, whether that be language or
language in the wider cognitive system, for example.
The point about interdependent systems encompassed within a larger
system has consequences not simply for reasoning about statements within
or interacting with an axiom, for example. It also has experimental conse-
quences. Popper is very clear that the test that a system of statements should
be put to is an applied one – in application a system of statements faces
falsiﬁcation, as suggested by our argument in Chapter 1 that science should
be judged by its correspondence to reality: ‘A theory is a tool which we test
by applying it, and which we judge as to its ﬁtness by the results of its
applications’ (Popper 2002a: 91). A system when tested may be falsiﬁed in
part or whole, with the interdependencies within a system allowing the
consequences of the falsiﬁcation of any part of it to spread out across the
logical dependencies within the system. In doing so, those dependencies
may, as suggested earlier, be quite local or may spread wide (Principle 17).
To return to our example involving narrative, imagine that we do have an
axiom of narrative and an axiom of quotation. If the statement that is
falsiﬁed in testing within the axiom of narrative touches upon quotation
and relies on the axiom of quotation, we may discover that the falsiﬁcation of
the axiom of narrative when tested is actually rooted in the axiom of
quotation. Yet note that once again the system is the guide – what test is
to be applied? Exactly what are we testing? The answer can only be the
systems we have established. The only admissible tests are tests that seek to
falsify the systems we have, that is, our theories, and the system of statements
that we have established to embody that theory, are the guide to testing.
We may, if we wish, engage in stray testing – playing with data, if you like,
in the hope of generating hypotheses. However, that form of data analysis is
not part of the scientiﬁc method – the scientiﬁc method as presented here
has tests conducted on theory. The tests are selected for their ability to test
the statements of the theory and the meaning of the falsiﬁcation is obvious
in advance because the system within which the statement being tested
resides is a set of clear and logically ordered statements. In the scientiﬁc
method, theory precedes testing as prior to this point: ‘the theoretician must
long before have done his work . . . it is he who shows the experimenter the
way . . . Theory dominates the experimental work from its initial planning
up to the ﬁnishing touches’ (Popper 2002a: 90). Of course, corpus linguistics
has long accepted this at least in the form of corpus-based studies. In line
with Popper’s reasoning, these can be characterised as part of the scientiﬁc
method – prior hypothesising and theorising is subject to test and
92
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 105 -->

reﬁnement using corpus data: ‘experiment is a planned action in which
every step is guided by theory’ (Popper 2002a: 280). Popper favoured a
‘searchlight theory’ of science (Popper 1945b: 247)50 in which ‘scientiﬁc
description will depend, largely, upon our point of view, our interests,
which as a rule are connected with the theory or hypothesis we wish to
test’. Similarly, Quine (1990: 21) argued for ‘checkpoints of science’, pre-
dictions arising from theory. By way of contrast, corpus-driven studies, in
which it is claimed that corpus data are explored in a theory free, naïve
fashion, are to all intents and purposes impossible. One might claim that this
approach represents an aimless, pre-theoretical approach to forming a
hypothesis that may bring a metaphysical investigation into a form that
eventually may be susceptible to being composed as a theory capable of
being submitted to scientiﬁc investigation.51 However, our expectation of
such procedures producing accidental discoveries should be modest accord-
ing to Popper, who acknowledges that accidental discoveries of this sort do
occur but they are ‘comparatively rare’ and what ‘compels the theorist to
search for a better theory .. . is almost always the experimental falsiﬁcation
of a theory’ (Popper 2002a: 90).52 The searchlight falls upon the checkpoint
and ideas withstand scrutiny or they are rejected.
Principle 33: Corpus linguistics typically works within a ‘searchlight’ paradigm of
research. The needs of theory and axiom construction guide us to speciﬁc
areas of enquiry.
3.5 Testability
When testing a system our preference, as noted, should be for methods
and tests that increase the likelihood of falsiﬁcation. Yet no matter how
severe the test we apply to a theory, we may never justify it nor any of
50 The searchlight idea also clearly inﬂuences Bartley (1962), who argues that, in order to
test a particular theory, we should look speciﬁcally for events incompatible with the
theory. The idea is also echoed by Quine (1990: 17) in so far that he notes that scientists
typically narrowly focus on parts of systems.
51 Note there is a parallel here with how the close but not exact link between form and
function in language may force us to explore in order to discover ways of pursuing
questions relating to function, working, by trial and error, to fashion ways of shifting the
metaphysical to the scientiﬁc. See Curry (forthcoming) for an example of this in action.
52 For a fuller critical discussion of the corpus-based vs corpus-driven distinction, see
Stubbs (2013).
3.5 Testability
93
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 106 -->

the statements it is based upon. We can, however, decide, on the basis of
the corroboration that is gained by resisting falsiﬁcation, that we deem
what we have tested to be decided, to be ‘conjecturally true’ (Miller
1982: 25). Yet once we have deemed something to be accepted, we may
then use it in a process of justiﬁcation as if it had previously been
justiﬁed. However, this absolute judgement is provisional and is never
incapable of revision in principle, as the discussion of deeming sug-
gested (in line with Principle 9).
Another feature of testing is that it is a question of degree. Theories
may be more or less testable. The further from being testable theories
are, the further we move into the realm of metaphysics and hypotheses
such as the Great Interlocutor. The closer they are to achieving the
desiderata laid out by Popper, then the closer they are to being testable
and, as the elements of any theory move towards testability, the further
away they move from metaphysics. Note that this argument can also be
considered in terms of the set of potential falsiﬁers and the set of admis-
sible statements. At one end of the spectrum, in the purely metaphysical
world the set of potential falsiﬁers is empty as falsiﬁcation is an impossi-
bility. As we transition towards a theory that is scientiﬁc, the set of
falsiﬁers will become non-empty. While not all elements of the system
may be subject to falsiﬁcation, those elements that are will entail the
generation of potential falsiﬁers. Returning to an example given at the
beginning of our discussion of Popper, systems based on statements
requiring precise frequency information about usage in language were
almost wholly metaphysical in the past. With the advent of computing
and corpus data, an ever greater range of data about the frequency
distribution of linguistic features has become available to linguists for
an ever-growing range of languages, meaning that statements that
require such data begin to become testable by degree as the set of
potential falsiﬁers is populated as corpus data and the ability to use that
data to generate suitable observations has become possible.
This in turn leads Popper to the initially surprising conclusion that
testability is in inverse proportion to the probability that a statement may
be judged to be true. The logic of the argument is, however, simple. If a
proposition such as the Great Interlocutor is made, then, given that it
cannot be disproved, it might be said to be highly probable. Indeed, the
chance of falsifying it is zero. On the other hand, if we make a claim in our
theory that, in English, members of the set of articles will have a less
volatile variation in frequency distribution across a range of registers
than members of the set of nouns, then, given that suitable corpus data
exists to potentially falsify the statement, the probability of that
94
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 107 -->

statement being proved false is non-zero. Hence the more testable
proposition is the one where the chances of it being false are non-
zero. This is important in rhetorical terms, of course – it explains clearly
in a simple example why faith-based arguments are not susceptible to
being disproved by rational investigation and argument. Likewise it
sums up the unfortunate strength of dogmatic argument. Yet it also
explains why such statements have no role to play in science. While they
may prove to be useful provocations, unless they at least have the
prospect of transitioning along the scale of testability from the extreme
position of non-testability, then they are of no concern in science. Of
course, as noted, as instrumentation or data improve it may be that what
was irredeemably metaphysical may transition from the point of total
non-testability (see Principle 12). Yet until such an idea does, it is of no
concern within the philosophy of science.
More importantly the link of falsiﬁability to testability is important in
terms of choices that may be made by the theorist: ‘The better testable
statement . . . is the one which is logically less probable . . . and the
statement which is less well testable is the one which is logically more
probable’ (Popper 2002a: 103). Where a choice exists for the theorist, the
presumption should be in favour of testability and all which that entails.
The role of the set of potential falsiﬁers is key as it determines what
Popper calls empirical content – the larger the set, the larger the empiri-
cal content of the system testable by the set (in line with Principle 29).53
Popper also claims that ‘the empirical content of a statement increases
with its degree of falsiﬁability: the more a state forbids, the more it says
about the world of experience’. Magee (1982: 36) sums it up thus:
Just as any fool can produce statements of a very high probability which tells
us practically nothing, so any fool can produce statements with a very high
informative content if he is not bothered about whether they are false. What
we want are statements of a high informative content, and therefore low
probability, which nevertheless come close to the truth.
The Great Interlocutor, amusing as it is, has a zero chance of being
proved false because it has an empty set of falsiﬁers, hence, in turn, it
has no empirical content. By contrast, our statement about article and
noun frequency does not have a zero chance of being proved false
because it has a non-empty set of falsiﬁers, meaning that it has an
empirical content greater than zero. If we can gather more data with
which to attempt falsiﬁcation, then we can increase the set of potential
53 See Quine (1990: 16–18) for a similar deﬁnition of empirical content.
3.5 Testability
95
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 108 -->

falsiﬁers for the statement, thus increasing its empirical content. So in
making a presumption in our choices as theorists and testers of theory in
favour of maximising falsiﬁcation, we are, of necessity, working to max-
imise the empirical content of our theories. Popper regards ‘the compar-
ison of the empirical content of two statements as equivalent to the
comparison of their degrees of falsiﬁability. This makes our methodolo-
gical rule that those theories should be given preference which can be
most severely tested . . . equivalent to a rule favouring theories with the
highest possible empirical content’ (Popper 2002a: 105).54 Corpus data,
as a reservoir of potential falsiﬁcations, clearly have a role to play in
making statements made about language testable and, in turn, provide a
potential way in which the empirical content of such statements may be
ampliﬁed.
3.6 Range
While it is clear that the concept of the set of falsiﬁers is important in
guiding our choices as researchers, what of the set of permissible state-
ments? These are key also, but in a different way, as they specify what
Popper terms the range – how far away, or otherwise, the degree of
correspondence with reality attained by our system is. The smaller the
range, the closer the ﬁt to reality. Popper puts it in slightly different,
though compatible, terms when he states that ‘the prevailing technique of
measurement determines a certain range – a region within which discre-
pancies between the observations made are permitted by the theory’
(Popper 2002a: 108).
However, range may be subject to limits – for example, the data
available for testing or disagreements over the best the conventions
used in establishing our axioms by convention. Hence, within our system
some of these limitations may be encoded, meaning that our system,
while being brought to bear on linguistic reality, may not describe it in
fact – the model is oriented towards a correspondence with reality but it
does not match it perfectly (this is a corollary of Principles such as 2, 50
and most importantly 15). This is what Popper terms the ‘degrees of
54 Note that Popper applies the same reasoning to the derivation of causation – the system
with the highest degree of falsiﬁability provides the best account of the interdependen-
cies between elements of a system. He avoids using the term ‘causation’ and charac-
terises it as metaphysical, presumably because it implies a higher commitment to truth
than that allowed within his philosophy. The interested reader is directed to read the
discussion in Popper (2002a: 105–107).
96
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 109 -->

freedom’ that the model exhibits with regard to its correspondence to
reality, which is in the range also. The range of a theory at any one point
in time may be wider than reality permits, but, at the point that we have
reached in the process of the incremental reﬁnement of a theory, this is
the narrowest that the range can be brought at this point. As falsiﬁcation
proceeds to reﬁne the theory, the range may narrow (assuming the theory
is not subject to collapse). This leads to a revision of Principle 21 as range
gives us a goal to judge progress against in the process of incremental
reﬁnement of our hypotheses:
Principle 210: Corpus linguistics proceeds incrementally as testing by falsiﬁcation
proceeds and the cycle of hypothesis formation is driven onwards with the
goal of reducing the range of those hypotheses.
In corpus linguistics, the idea of range is important, though it is not clearly
articulated. At any point in time the data available to explore a theory
may not be optimal and the measures used may be in dispute. While this
may sound like it is a problem for corpus linguistics, it is not; it is a
statement that could be applied to any area within the enterprise of
science. What is important is the degree to which range is narrowed
because of this. Hence, in an imperfect system, the question becomes
not which measures and data do we use to ‘prove’ our theory of language,
but what measures and data might we use to maximise the set of potential
falsiﬁers and limit the range of permissible statements to test my theory
of language (as per Principle 210). Given the nature of the scientiﬁc
method, once done this work would then be open to revision and reﬁne-
ment – gaining corroboration or encountering refutation through experi-
ments that used the data and methods best able to achieve the desired
effects on the set of permissible statements and potential falsiﬁers (pro-
ceeding in accordance with Principle 20). Those experiments themselves
should, of course, then be subject to testing in turn – with repeats of the
test and replications of the test both seeking to corroborate the corro-
boration (or, perhaps, corroborate the refutation) or otherwise with
results reported (in line with Principle 18). Throughout what is required
is an honesty about the data and methods used – an acknowledgement of
their limitations, as this helps us to understand how they contribute to
range, for example; ‘Our method of research is not to defend them, in
order to prove how right we were’ (Popper 2002a: 278–279) (in line with
Principle 22). We may, at that point, deem the data and methods to be
acceptable through convention and proceed to establish a theory that, by
3.6 Range
97
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 110 -->

extension, is one which is deemed to be in correspondence with reality by
convention. However, range is a reminder of the tacit nature of that
deeming and is also a yardstick by which we might judge whether a new
measure or data set (see Principle 23) might reduce the range and allow
us to revisit the assumptions made, subjecting them to test and reopening
the possibility that they were open to falsiﬁcation and revision or aban-
donment.55 Throughout, our goal would be to reduce range in the model
while maximising the possibility of falsiﬁcation (in line with Principles 16
and 28).
3.7 The Hair Shirt of Honesty
While all of this sounds very promising on paper, it must be said that such
honesty can be difﬁcult to ﬁnd in any science, let alone corpus linguistics.
On the one hand, being honest about limitations can provide a warrant
for destructive criticism and a ready defence for dogmatists to use – their
misplaced certainty can appear appealing when pitted against the hon-
esty of the scientiﬁc method, though Popper warns that ‘none of our
“anticipations” are dogmatically upheld’ (Popper 2002a: 278). On the
other hand, being honest about limitations may be a dispiriting experi-
ence for some as they view it as a moment where they must don sackcloth
and sprinkle ashes on their heads and confess their sins. Yet the myopia
of the dogmatists and the misplaced feelings of guilt of those who feel
they should be perfect should be resisted and set aside. What is important
is not perfection, for perfection, in the form of irrefutable proof, is
unattainable within science. What is important is measurably better (in
terms of narrower range) and demonstrably more subject to falsiﬁcation
through testing (in terms of increased empirical content) ﬁndings.56 The
overall goal, we might say, of a system within Popper’s view of science is
that it should not be ‘true’, rather it should be ‘sufﬁciently precise to be
capable of clashing with observational experience’ (Popper 2002a: 116)
and hence being falsiﬁed. In pursuit of that goal, we take our ideas and
‘Using all the weapons of our logical, mathematical, and technical
armoury, we try to prove that our anticipations were false – in order to
put forward, in their stead, new unjustiﬁed and unjustiﬁable assertions’
55 A sentiment reﬂected notably in Arppe et al. (2010).
56 While we will not rely on the argument here, Popper clearly states his belief that
objective measurement is an inherently superior basis for scientiﬁc investigation than
qualitative modes of investigation, arguing for ‘the superiority of methods that employ
measurements over purely qualitative methods’ (Popper 2002a: 110).
98
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 111 -->

(Popper 2002a: 279) because ‘We do not know, we can only guess’
(Popper 2002a: 278).
3.8 Achieving Balance and Simplicity
Note that the balance between the set of admissible statements and the
set of potential falsiﬁers is an important feature controlling the actions of
theorists and experiments. If one were to wish to reduce the range on its
own, one may make a hypothesis about a single sentence, limiting any
claims to that sentence alone. Apart from being, in all likelihood, deeply
uninteresting, such an approach sets the set of potential falsiﬁers to zero.
One may then wish to reach for perfection by working on and minutely
reﬁning a very small data set, eschewing larger but less orderly collections
of data. Yet in doing so one would be limiting the range of potential
falsiﬁers in order to reduce range. This brings us up against an issue that
corpus linguistics and other sciences rooted in observation face – the set
of potential observations relevant to the hypothesis being tested is what
populates the universal set of potential falsiﬁers. Yet our observations in
support of theory will always be bound by time and space, it will be a
subset of all potential falsiﬁers for the purposes of most investigations.
This dilemma is, of course, at the root of the problem of induction (see
Section 1.5). So we should not confuse the universal set of potential
falsiﬁers with what we will term here the set of experimental falsiﬁers.
The set of experimental falsiﬁers is typically not equivalent to the uni-
versal set of potential falsiﬁers, rather it forms a subset of it. So a corpus is
a sample of a larger set of utterances/sentences that constitute language
and forms a set of observations that, for experimental purposes, we use as
the source of the falsiﬁers that we may subject our theory to.
Popper also uses the ideas introduced to develop a concept of simpli-
city for his theory.57 Simplicity, as we argued in Chapter 1, is a key feature
of the scientiﬁc method, yet its deﬁnition is contested and may stray well
beyond an easy intuitive understanding of the word. That is the case with
Popper’s deﬁnition of simplicity where he equates simplicity with degrees
of falsiﬁability – the more falsiﬁable a statement, the simpler, in his terms.
This allows him to reinforce his overall objective for his philosophy of
science by saying ‘Simple statements, if knowledge is our object, are to be
prized more highly than less simple ones because they tell us more;
because their empirical content is greater; and because they are better
57 Note that Quine and Ullians’ (1978) ﬁve virtues a scientiﬁc hypothesis should aspire to:
conservatism, generality, simplicity, refutability and modesty.
3.8 Achieving Balance and Simplicity
99
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 112 -->

testable.’ Thus in conﬁguring the set of permissible statements, the set of
potential falsiﬁers (and the set of experimental falsiﬁers by extension),
the empirical content and the range of a theory, one is also setting
parameters for more or less testable, more of less simple, statements.
This allows us to reﬁne Principles 28 and 29:
Principle 280: Methods should be combined so as to: 1) maximise potential
falsiﬁability; 2) maximise experimental falsiﬁers; 3) maximise the empirical
content of the system under examination; and 4) minimise range. All of this
is done in the pursuit of simplicity.
Principle 290: Corpus linguists, when selecting data and tools to examine a
hypothesis, should select those which: 1) maximise potential falsiﬁability; 2)
maximise experimental falsiﬁers; 3) maximise the empirical content of the
system under examination; and 4) minimise range, while also abiding by
other principles, notably Principle 7. All of this is done in the pursuit of
simplicity.
The mention of degrees of testability may make some readers wonder
whether a different approach to simplicity might be possible – an approach
rooted in probability. To some extent McEnery (1995) was making this
argument but more notably the idea has been considered by philosophers
such as Weyl (1927), who discussed and rejected the idea. Yet probability
has been notable by its absence in the discussion of Popper’s work so far,
though ideas such as corroboration seem to appeal to concepts that, at least
informally, seem to be probabilistic in nature and Popper does indeed
consider probability in relation to corroboration. Given that Popper
expressly eliminates the possibility of proving a hypothesis to be true, then
it would seem that the next step would be for him to shift to a probabilistic
logic. Yet Popper distinguished corroboration apart from probability. He
maintains that probabilistic logics argue in favour of veriﬁcation, masking
too heavily the possibility of falsiﬁcation behind ampliative reasoning, for
example. Miller (2016: 233) in a discussion of probabilities sums up the
problem with them in Popper’s view of science well:
“The predicament for Popper, who had [. . . .] proposed falsiﬁability as a
criterion of empirical science [. . . .] was that they are also unfalsiﬁable: no
observable sequence of Heads and Tails formally refutes a hypothesis such as
p(Heads) = ½; a run of a million heads is not impossible.”
100
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 113 -->

Popper favours a view of science in which hypothetical assumptions ‘can
only be corroborated, in the sense that they ‘prove their mettle’ under ﬁre –
the ﬁre of your tests’ (Popper 2004: 259) meaning that there is no truth to
aim for, leaving us with expressly ‘provisional conjectures’ in our theories
(Popper 2002a: 264). Systems are capable of corroboration where they meet
the demands laid out in our discussion of Popper’s ideas so far. This in itself
only permits, it does not constitute, corroboration. Corroboration then
proceeds to be tested. Corroboration is not a simple numeric matter, how-
ever. A theory corroborated with 12 tests does not necessarily stand as being
better corroborated than a theory which has withstood four tests.
Corroboration relates to the severity of the tests undertaken more than to
their number. Imagine that we have a theory of aspect, a common feature of
human languages, of the sort that Xiao and McEnery (2005) developed. If
we test this theory on a random handful of test sentences on twenty occa-
sions, we clearly have not tested it as severely as we would if we subjected it
to being tested once on a corpus of 10 million words of data drawn from a
range of registers and covering different modes of production. So the
severity of the test, coupled with the degree of testability that the system
exhibits, determines the degree of corroboration. By considering the para-
meters by which we have established a system, we can come to a sense of
how to judge that system, this time in terms of degrees of corroboration yet
‘we cannot deﬁne a numerically calculable degree of corroboration, but can
speak . .. roughly in terms of positive degree of corroboration, negative
degrees of corroboration, and so forth’ (Popper 2002a: 266).
Principle 34: The weight of any given corroboration that we derive from a study,
and thus the conﬁdence we may express in convention, is proportionate to the
severity of the test to which we subject our hypotheses.
We will return to how to align Popper’s views on probability with the
views on probability and corroboration outlined earlier in this chapter
when we consider Popper and the social sciences in Chapter 4. For now,
let us focus on Popper’s views of corroboration.
3.9 Probability and Corroboration
While distancing himself from probability as a proxy for the degree of
corroboration, Popper does allow it to be used in a loose sense to provide
a rule of thumb for how corroboration may be increased by repeated
corroboration. In doing so, he rightly notes that it does not follow that
3.9 Probability and Corroboration
101
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 114 -->

something that is corroborated the same way again and again, in the same
context and by the same means, is proportionately corroborated – the
surprise of the corroboration may decrease, so to speak, hence the value
of that corroboration decreases also. However, if the context of observa-
tion shifts, then surprise may be achieved again – remembering back to
Russell’s chicken, the day that the farmer decides he wants roast chicken
for dinner the context of observation changes for the chicken and leads to
a surprise. In corpus linguistics, consider work carried out on the Brown
corpus (Francis and Kučera 1979). When the LOB corpus was created, as
a British English match for that corpus, claims about English based on
Brown could be corroborated, or falsiﬁed, with some claims being altered
to take the different varieties of English into account. Similarly, when
corpora, using the same sampling structure, were created for both vari-
eties of English at different points in time, the stability of the ﬁndings
based on Brown and LOB could be tested over time. So work such as that
of Leech et al. (2010) and Baker (2017) has enabled us to corroborate
results not simply on one variety of English at one point in time, but also
over two varieties of English synchronously and diachronically. As new
corpora were added to this collection, the possibility of falsiﬁcation of
previous corroborations is expanded, making the repeated corroboration
with multiple data sets a more trying test of theories than a repeated
corroboration with one corpus covering one variety of English at one
point in time. We will return to this discussion in Chapter 7. For now, let
us revise Principle 16 thus:
Principle 160: When building and using corpora, our goal is to facilitate (corpus
building) and/or perform a genuine test (corpus use) and a genuine test must
permit the possibility of falsiﬁcation while also achieving surprise.
When a theory shifts from one ﬁeld to another, it does not necessarily carry
its corroboration with it – corroboration in one ﬁeld of application, as Popper
calls it, may have no value in another. However, a theory that moves ﬁeld of
application and survives the surprise to achieve corroboration gains corro-
boration not simply from its tests within the ﬁeld in which it was developed,
but has also been shown to have a wider applicability. It thus has a higher
degree of falsiﬁcation and hence corroboration, providing another good
reason to pursue triangulation of research results as demonstrated by
Egbert and Baker (2019) within the remit of Principle 280. A potentially
interesting example of this arises in the way in which mathematical theorems
from physics and other disciplines have been applied to corpus data through
102
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 115 -->

culturomics and more generally in data mining.58 Such work often comes
well corroborated from the physical sciences in which it was employed and is
often applied as though that corroboration was a warrant in itself for the
application to language of the technique in question. Yet, when tested by
sceptical linguists, the falsiﬁcation of the ﬁndings of the technique should
lead all to doubt that such corroboration does carry across – indeed, if
Popper’s notes of caution were followed, the applicability of the techniques
would never have been assumed to be possible in the ﬁrst place without
extensive corroboration, we might argue. Topic modelling provides a good
example again. The basic technique, and adaptations of it, have been used
with apparent success in various applications in library science over the
years. The task of organising books into a pre-existing categorisation system
(e.g. the Dewey Decimal system) is something that the technique seemed to
be reasonably well adapted for. However, to use the technique to discover
new categorisations in data sets in a way that could be of immediate use to
researchers in a range of disciplines and in a number of ﬁelds of linguistics,
such as discourse analysis, was assumed rather than subject to rigorous
testing. In such a context it is perhaps unsurprising that, when tested in the
new areas of application, the techniques are found to be less useful than they
may at ﬁrst appear (see Murakami et al. 2017; Brookes and McEnery 2019).
A common defence of topic modelling also leads us to another rule of
thumb that Popper uses for degrees of corroboration – precision. If we
have a grammar, we may reasonably expect it to divide the acceptable
utterances of a language apart from the unacceptable ones (i.e. into
permitted statements and potential falsiﬁers). Similarly, our putative
axiom of collocation separates those things that are claimed to be collo-
cates, by some axiom by convention, from those that are not. The predic-
tions of such systems are precise and hence can be better corroborated.
Systems that provide vague or uncertain precision are, perforce, not cap-
able of being as well corroborated. This leads us to revise Principle 25:
Principle 250: Corpus linguists should seek to explore and develop axioms that are
integrated, non-redundant, parsimonious, necessary and as precise as possible.
Popper uses the example of the soothsayer to illustrate his point –
their claims are so vague that much can be read into them to make
58 See Gooding (2013) for a more general critique of culturomics. The fears in the article
that ‘a glut of mediocre copycat work could begin to emerge’ (Gooding 2013: 427)
certainly seems to have been released to a degree.
3.9 Probability and Corroboration
103
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 116 -->

them true. With topic modelling a similar criticism can apply, as
outlined by Brookes and McEnery (2019: 4–8): the discovery proce-
dure entails highly subjective interventions by analysts who, for exam-
ple, typically decide what the topics mean, discarding topics presumed
to be junk and, as the categorisation often tends to be done on
decontextualised sets of words, assuming much about what the groups
of words that constitute a topic may mean. Consider the following
from a paper using latent dirichlet allocation (LDA) to derive so-
called topic models:
We ﬁrst ran LDA with 100 topics, and took 36 that we found to be relevant.
We then hand-selected seed words for 10 more topics to improve coverage of
the ﬁeld. These 46 topics were then used as priors to a new 100-topic run. The
top ten most frequent words for 43 of the topics along with hand-assigned
labels are listed in Table 2. Topics deriving from manual seeds are marked
with an asterisk. (Hall et al. 2008)
The discovery procedure here seems to be, to say the least, ad hoc.
First, generate one hundred topics. Use intuition to select a subset of
thirty-six topics. On the basis of those topics, select ten words, on
grounds that are not stated, that must be in the next run of topics
you produce. Generate another ten topics using hand-selected seed
words, providing no grounds for their selection. Then generate one
hundred new topics based on the forty-six topics you like. Then show
and label forty-three topics and only look at the ten most frequent
words in them.
The result of this discovery procedure is a highly subjective ana-
lysis, which is not clearly capable of intersubjective testing, in which
the outputs are interpreted in a manner that leads to low precision
and low testability. This in turn compounds the problems of inaccu-
racy in the system. Topic modelling provides an interesting study of
how issues such as corroboration, objectivity, precision and falsiﬁca-
tion may be of direct help in critiquing a system that, initially at
least, appears highly scientiﬁc simply because of the great weight of
symbolic capital afforded to it by the presentation of the maths
underlying it.
While Popper does not provide a numerical guide to establishing the
degree of corroboration, he does apply a general rule to falsiﬁcation –
an intersubjectively testable falsiﬁcation is ﬁnal. Corroborations of the
system made past that falsiﬁcation are of no value. Note that we would
argue this is also true of corpus linguistics, leading to revision of
Principle 18.
104
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 117 -->

Principle 180: Reporting falsiﬁcations is arguably more important than reporting
corroboration. At the very least, both should be reported unless the report is
on a previously falsiﬁed hypothesis; then neither corroboration or falsiﬁcation
is of value.
Let us consider using the Spoken BNC2014 as our set of experimental
potential falsiﬁers. We wish to test a grammar of spoken English that is
a well-ordered set of statements about what is grammatically permis-
sible in English. We can apply that system to our corpus, taking
potential falsiﬁers (for the sake of argument, utterances) and proces-
sing them, passing them through to the set of admissible statements
where they satisfy the grammar and rejecting utterances that do not
accord with the grammar. If we inspect the set of rejected utterances
and discover sentences that, by appealing to some knowledge beyond
the system (e.g. grammaticality judgements), are shown to be perfectly
grammatical, then our system is falsiﬁed.59 Now at that point, while we
may be tempted to say ‘oh, the corpus is only ten million words, let us
test it on this corpus of 20 million words instead’, that cannot, logically,
negate the falsiﬁcation that has occurred. If the system works on the 20
million word data set and categorises all of the utterances in a satisfac-
tory manner, this adds no corroboration to the system because the
falsiﬁcation still stands. So once again falsiﬁcation is the key goal and
guide to the process of testing whereby experiment is applied to test
theory. It follows, therefore, that those theories with the highest degree
of falsiﬁability, have the potential to reach the highest degree of
corroboration.
3.10 Fault Lines
This draws to a close our consideration of Popper’s thinking on science –
but it does not quite conclude the chapter and it certainly does not
conclude our discussion of corpus linguistics, Popper and science. While
we believe that working with this framework has value, as indicated at the
start of this chapter, we know there are other frameworks and there are
59 Note that we are trapped, as predicted by Popper, to a degree by a priorism here as we
must assume an a priori concept of grammaticality that exists outside the grammar we
have created but that can be applied to judge the system. In short, we deem the
grammaticality judgements of some person inspecting the output of the system to be
correct. For this short example we will place our hand over this problem, but it remains a
problem.
3.10 Fault Lines
105
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 118 -->

critiques of Popper’s framework. Another, very notable, system is that of
Kuhn (1962), which we have mentioned already. It is easy to cast Kuhn as
being in opposition to Popper. That, however, is not our reading of Kuhn
(nor is it that of others – see, for example, Magee 1982: 40–41, and the
discussion in Lakatos and Musgrave 1970). Kuhn focuses very much on
the sociology of doing science – not the abstract system of Science (which
we will distinguish in this discussion through capitalisation). Given that
Popper acknowledged that there was a social dimension that intersects
with Science – for example, in the intervention of dogma in research and
the forming of convention – there is, in our view, a readymade way of
interfacing the two researchers, with Science being the preoccupation of
one and the day-to-day practice of ‘doing’ science being the preoccupa-
tion of the other.
Of more interest for the purpose of our discussion are the problems
that have been identiﬁed with Popper’s framework. Some of these are
matters of largely philosophical interest60 – for example, exactly what
type of inferencing does Popper use? In practice Popper himself is
unclear on exactly whether he is using a deductive process or not –
while often talking about deduction, and characterised as being
against induction by some corpus linguists (Stefanowitsch 2020: 61),
Popper (2002a: 276) says he is engaged in a quasi-inductive process,
building from deductions to something that looks like induction.61 The
problem of tying down Popper’s reasoning arises when reading
Popper (2002a) in part because of the scattered nature of Popper’s
writing. The Logic of Scientiﬁc Discovery is one, but not the only,
relevant contribution that Popper makes to scientiﬁc thinking and to
methods relevant to corpus linguistics. Piecing Popper together, as we
will continue to do in the next chapter, takes time as ‘Popper never
laid his thought out as a system. His ideas are highly integrated and
develop somewhat over time’ (Jarvie 2016: 295). This can lead to
readers having to make sense of a changing thought process expressed
over a long, but focused, research career. In such a context, summative
statements of the following sort are helpful in clarifying what Popper
meant (Corvi 1996: 35):
For Popper . . . quasi-induction is a deductive movement because it starts
from a broader and more general conception than the one at which it arrives.
60 For an accessible summary of such objections by Feyerabend, Kuhn and Lakatos, see
Andersson (1982).
61 Though for an alternative view that claims Popper’s procedures are not inductive see
Miller (1982: 24 and 26–49).
106
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 119 -->

But since this theory is no more than a metaphysical idea, or is at least
impregnated with metaphysical ideas, it acquires the status of a scientiﬁc
theory only when it succeeds in demonstrating its own testability and is thus
borne out by experimental controls. In short, the deductive procedure is
supplemented by empirical testing that moves from the bottom (particular
observations or experiments) towards the top (the theory subjected to test-
ing) – and it is in this sense that it follows an inductive direction.
Corvi’s description is helpful as it provides a looser deﬁnition of deduc-
tion than the classical account of it that we gave in Section 1.5. Given that
the description of quasi-induction may be viewed as calling for a more
top-down approach to hypothesis formation, which is entirely plausible
as an approach to corpus data, we must modify Principles 4 and 10:
Principle 40: Because we have a presupposition of reality and through that we
can measure hypotheses against observable, relatively objective data, we are
able to use the apparatus that has been developed over centuries to support
inductive and quasi-inductive reasoning, for example, through statistical
analysis.
Principle 100: Corpus linguistics, being closely linked to induction and quasi-
induction (see Principle 4), also experiences the problem of induction, which in
turn points towards uncertainty (Principle 9).
Let us now approach possibly the biggest problem with Popper’s
system. Popper spends much time in his work trying to evade the
bear trap of inductive recursion. It is ironic, though perhaps not
unexpected, that recursion rears its head at the heart of Popper’s
system, in falsiﬁcation, which is also the feature of Popper most
often invoked by corpus linguists, as noted at the beginning of
Chapter 2. What distinguishes a falsiﬁcation from other claims? Why
are falsiﬁcations not prone to falsiﬁcation? The alert reader may have
already identiﬁed this issue in what they have read so far – both our
critique of Chomsky’s claims about data in ﬁrst language acquisition
and the Leech/Millar debate involved falsiﬁcations, or at least
attempted falsiﬁcations, of falsiﬁcations. There is nothing to stop
falsiﬁcation turning in on itself, with the most obvious way of stopping
it being the somewhat undesirable device of convention, a form of a
priorism. We deem a falsiﬁcation to be irrefutable and move on,
3.10 Fault Lines
107
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 120 -->

though the truth of the falsiﬁcation is no more defensible than the
truth of what was falsiﬁed. This has led philosophers such as Lakatos
(1974: 248) to note that Popper ‘stubbornly overestimates the
immediate striking force of purely negative criticism’. Lakatos (1974:
249) also observes, as was noted earlier in this chapter, that falsiﬁca-
tion does not lead to the death of theory, which happens only ‘when
there is a better one to replace it’, dismissing Popper’s version of
falsiﬁcation as ‘naïve falsiﬁcationism’.62 Lakatos parses Popper’s fal-
siﬁcation apart, helpfully, between falsiﬁcation (negative results) and
rejection (the process of rejecting a tested hypothesis). Falsiﬁcation
does not entail automatic rejection in large part because falsiﬁcation
may be open to falsiﬁcation. While falsiﬁcation should cause the
analyst to pause, check and explore the nature of a falsiﬁcation, it
does not bring with it instant rejection, as Popper suggested, so we
should
emphasise, as Popper did from the very beginning, that an experimental
falsiﬁcation is not just a matter of confronting a hypothesis with refractory
evidence, more often than not it involves the subtle and delicate task of
ﬁnding out what is actually happening in the experiment. Indeed, no falsiﬁca-
tion is conclusive, if only because all test statements are themselves falsiﬁable
and open to dispute. (Miller 1982: 25)
So any given falsiﬁcation is best viewed as a conditional falsiﬁcation as ‘all
proofs are conditional and depend on the assumed truth of the used
premises’ (Andersson 1982: 55). So, if we assume a corpus represents
language in general, and we falsify a claim about language in general, this
is a conditional falsiﬁcation as it rests on the credibility of the claim that
the corpus does indeed represent language in general. Let us return to an
example discussed in this chapter: Miller’s falsiﬁcation of Leech’s claim
about the decline of the modal in English. Miller’s falsiﬁcation was
conditional upon the nature of the data he used to produce his falsiﬁca-
tion. If we do not believe that a corpus of copies of the Time magazine is a
basis on which to falsify a claim made about general English based on a
corpus composed of a mixture of text types, as Leech used, then Miller’s
conditional falsiﬁcation should be rejected, not Leech’s claim. This leads
us to revise Principles 160, 17 and 180.
62 Though others have disputed the view of Popper as a naïve falsiﬁcationist (Miller 1982;
Kuhn 1970: 14). Note also that other theorists discuss naïve falsiﬁcation in ways that are not
applicable to Popper. For example, Kuhn uses this term to refer to dogmatic rejections
while Feyerabend reserves this term for theories operating with a strict, inﬂexible, principle
of falsiﬁcation. Popper does not, in fact, advocate either of these positions.
108
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 121 -->

Principle 16″: When building and using corpora, our goal is to facilitate (corpus
building) and/or perform a genuine test (corpus use) and a genuine test must
permit the possibility of conditional falsiﬁcation while also achieving surprise.
Principle 170: When conducting a test with corpus data, one must consider the
overall impact of any conditional falsiﬁcation produced by that test. As con-
ditional falsiﬁcations accumulate, rejection may be warranted.
Principle 18″: Reporting conditional falsiﬁcations is arguably more important
than reporting corroboration. At the very least, both should be reported
unless the report is on a previously rejected hypothesis – then a falsiﬁcation
of a falsiﬁcation that led to rejection is of particular interest.
Another issue with what has been presented so far lies at the heart of
fallibilism. In a world where truth is not attainable, a path is potentially
revealed towards ‘scepticism and . . . its evil consequences, like relativi-
sim, irrationalism, mysticism’ (Lakatos 1974: 256). This undermines the
scientiﬁc worldview and enables metaphysical views of the world. While
we would contend that Popper carefully presents ways of separating the
metaphysical and the scientiﬁc, as we proceed by convention the distance
from science and the shift towards the metaphysical may make the evils
Lakatos identiﬁed seem more attractive. We will address this issue
squarely in the next chapter, proposing an approach to corpus analysis,
derived from Popper’s thinking, which avoids this.
Recursion within falsiﬁcation, the conﬂation of falsiﬁcation and rejec-
tion, and the tangling of fallibilism and scepticism represent worms that
may burrow into the heart of Popper’s system of belief. Oddly, the worm
of recursion does not concern us hugely because, as this chapter has
shown, linguistics relies on convention, which curtails this. In part it
does so because of the mutability of language. Popper (2002a: 50) notes
that certain natural processes can be studied on the basis of them being
capable of being axiomatised, that is, based on rules that enshrine a
system that is so stable it may be thus ordered. Gravity, we may assume,
works in just the same way now as it did a hundred years ago, it works the
same on Earth as on Jupiter. Language varies through time and space. Of
course, some linguists would retreat to universals in their pursuit of a
priori truths about language, immutable facts, from which to frame
linguistics as a science. Yet even if this were possible, the victory would
3.10 Fault Lines
109
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 122 -->

be partial. As the species evolves, so too the genome that encodes such a
priori assumptions that we could make about language, should they exist,
is prone to change too. Hence the degree of mutability may be less than
that discussed here, but the fact of mutability remains. Beyond that, this
chapter has identiﬁed point after point where convention must be
appealed to in linguistics where it is not in other observational sciences.
It is for that reason that, in the next chapter, we will explore Popper’s
thoughts on how to approach social phenomena such as language outside
the frame of science, but in ways that are compatible with it. In doing so
we not only shift our frame of reference but also shift away from burden-
ing ourselves with concern about the role of convention in controlling
falsiﬁcation. In the framework introduced in the next chapter, an appeal
to convention is inevitable, yet we will also see that an appeal to linguistic
reality is still possible as a remedy to the worst excesses of scepticism and
as a control on rejection proceeding too rapidly from falsiﬁcation.
In doing so, we will take much from the discussion in this and the
previous chapter, which has been a conscious effort, on our behalf, to
introduce a set of terms that are helpful for corpus linguists to consider
when they are building corpora, using corpora and testing and developing
theory. If we take away from Popper nothing more than a fresh apprecia-
tion and understanding of a host of concepts that help us think about
‘doing’ corpus linguistics (such as all-statements, ampliative reasoning,
a priorism, axioms, basic statements, consistency, conviction, corrobora-
tion, empirical content, fallibilism, falsiﬁability, falsiﬁcation, increment-
alism,
metaphycics,
names,
numerical
universality,
particularism,
potential falsiﬁers, quasi-contact, range, redundancy, singular objects,
testability and underdetermination), then we have been greatly enriched
by exposure to his work. It has certainly helped to ﬂesh out and reﬁne our
set of Principles. The following chapter, however, adds more.
110
How to Do Science?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.005 Published online by Cambridge University Press

<!-- page 123 -->

C H A P T E R F O U R
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
The work presented so far has provided an outline of the core of Popper’s
views on the scientiﬁc method. However, two notes of caution must be
sounded. First, as already remarked upon at the end of the last chapter,
Popper’s ideas are not easily compartmentalised. While The Logic of
Scientiﬁc Discovery, especially later editions that include additional work
by Popper, presents the bulk of his thinking on this topic, his other works
are also, to greater or lesser degrees, entangled with this one. So we may
need to look to them, as we have done to an extent so far anyway, for
clariﬁcation and further detail of his views. Second, the work presented
so far, while doubtless of use in framing and considering the relation of
science to linguistics in general and to corpus linguistics in particular, did
not focus on social and cultural reality. The points raised in this chapter
will thus expand the discussion to include corpus linguistics speciﬁcally
within the context of social science and to some extent also the digital
humanities. Reading Popper’s work, it is easy to come to a mistaken
conclusion that he is interested in the tangible and immutable (physical
objects and forces) and hence he has little to say about the dynamic and
ever-changing world of social and cultural reality. This is, as we will see in
this chapter, not the case.
4.1 The Rejection of Naturalism
To understand Popper’s interest in the cultural and social world, we need
to return to the central thesis of Popper’s approach to science – that
nothing can be known with absolute certainty, that falsiﬁability lies at the
core of science and that any understandings that we have are provisional.
So, while the aparatus he uses to express his ideas may appear to imply
that he is dealing with certainty, the truth is quite the reverse – his
philosophy is one of enabling rational decision making in a context
where uncertainty is a constant. Note that in doing so, Popper provides
us with a way of managing uncertainty. In contrast to approaches that
dissolve knowledge into a laissez-faire mush of post-modern thought
(anything can be true), Popper points out that while there is no certain
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 124 -->

knowledge, there is knowledge that is more rather than less likely to be
worth accepting on conventional grounds. That is the knowledge that is in
principle falsiﬁable, but that has survived, to date, attempts at falsifying
it. This reasoning extends also to reasoning about the social world (see
Popper 1945b: 253–254 for example). While Popper may distinguish
between interpretations (processes of theorising and sense making
about the social world) and scientiﬁc theories (Popper 1945b: 252), the
apparatus he brings to bear on both is, in essence, the same and is rooted
in fallibilism.63 Fallibilism is a position that recognises that our knowl-
edge is not deﬁnitive because human beings are potentially fallible
(hence the name) and our knowledge needs to be constantly revised.
When we shift the frame of reference from the physical to the social
sciences, as we intend to do in this chapter, it would be easy, yet pre-
mature, to jump to the conclusion that Popper was a naturalist.
Naturalism is a broad philosophical position that states that the only
reality is nature, the physical reality around us. Naturalism takes us
back to our discussion of scientia rationalis and scientia realis in
Chapter 1: naturalism, as discussed by Popper (2002a: 52–53), is clearly
an extreme form of scientia realis limiting the meaningful seeking of
knowledge to the investigation of natural reality through induction (see
Chapter 1 for the discussion of induction). Yet to view Popper as being a
proponent of naturalism would be wrong – instead one needs to look to
philosophers such as Quine (1990: 37–28) for champions of this position.
Popper (1976: 90) viewed naturalism as ‘misguided and erroneous’,
which reﬂects his critique of induction as the source of scientiﬁc knowl-
edge. Popper (2002a: 12) writes:
Positivists usually interpret the problem of demarcation in a naturalistic way;
they interpret it as if it were a problem of natural science.
Yet naturalism and its epistemologically reductionist form, positivism
(Bryant 1985), which is the target of Popper’s criticism, does occur in
the social sciences and humanities. Positivism claims that social reality is
subject to the same general laws as physical reality and should be studied
in the same way. A notable early example of positivism is Comte’s (1858:
401) understanding of sociology as ‘social physics’:
63 One may also wish to consider at this point Popper’s daring principle of transference –
‘what is true in logic is true in psychology’ (Popper 1972: 6). The point is not important
for the argument pursued here and to explore it would require an exploration of the –
false – impression that the principle implies that the human mind itself is logical. For
readers interested in exploring the transference principle, see Gregory (1988: 428) and
his exploration of Popper’s and Wittgenstein’s positions.
112
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 125 -->

Throughout the whole range of science, thus far, we have seen that the
conditions of combination and of progress are originally identical: and I
trust we shall see, after looking into social science in the same way, that
ideas of Order and Progress are in Social Physics, as rigorously inseparable as
the ideas of Organization and Life in Biology; from whence indeed they are,
in a scientiﬁc view, evidently derived.
Here, Comte draws a direct parallel between the biological reality of
human life and social structure, the latter being derived, according to
Comte, from the former. Comte thus concludes that social science and
natural science have the same grounding and methodological principles
as the label ‘social physics’ suggests. Another, this time current, example
of the naturalist approach is seen in much work in data science and so-
called Culturomics, as discussed and critiqued elsewhere (see Section
3.9). A psychology and psychologically inspired approach to the study of
the social sciences is also an area within which naturalism is dominant –
the result being a claim to study the science of the mind. In opposition to
this approach is conventionalism, or, as Popper terms it, anti-naturalism.
In an extreme form, the argument goes that there is no way in which a
naturalist study of the social can occur. The systems and processes of
social life are not amenable to scientiﬁc inquiry. This is a pathway to an
extreme form of scientia rationalis. Corpus linguistics has a role to play in
the naturalist approach to the social – hence corpora are now used
enthusiastically, if, at times, without much regard to what corpus linguists
actually do, by researchers in data science. Corpus linguistics has a less
clear role to play in the conventionalist view of the social world, as it is
one in which the type of system and order that might be perceived by the
use of corpus data appears not to exist. While a superﬁcial view of Popper
might allow us to presume he would be an anti-naturalist (the social is too
metaphysical to be science) the truth is rather different and much more
interesting for the corpus linguist.
4.2 The Natural, the Social and the Cultural
Popper advocated an approach to the social that blended the naturalist
and anti-naturalist positions. Importantly, he recognised that the social
and the physical world interact. Social constructs may be human made,
but they must obey physical laws. The application of that idea to linguis-
tics is relatively simple. For example, humans may generate languages,
but those languages are limited, of necessity, by the apparatus used in
their creation, production and reception – for example, the human brain,
4.2 The Natural, the Social and the Cultural
113
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 126 -->

subject as it is to processes that may be viewed through a naturalist optic.
This insight by Popper is one that should guide corpus linguists in inter-
preting their data. While the data may be viewed as a social construct,
they are also a product of a physical system. At the same time, the
observable product, the language itself, was constituted in a social con-
text with a social purpose. The naturalist and the anti-naturalist perspec-
tives on something like corpus data are false abstractions away from the
complex view of the data that we have, which is best approached in the
realisation encapsulated in Principle 35:
Principle 35: A corpus represents an amalgam of social and physical interactions.
To fail to see that the social and the natural are intrinsically bound
together and instead to give primacy to the naturalistic approach would
be to succumb to ‘naïve Monism’ in Popper’s view (1945a: 50). Monism is
a reductionist position, where everything is sought to be explained by a
single principle such as either the natural or social when interpreting
language. The danger of this approach is great because it can appear to be
possible to disentangle the two and to take, for example, a purely natur-
alistic approach. Yet when we do this, the anti-naturalistic will reassert
itself. A good example from corpus linguistics is in the work of Baker and
Potts (2013). In that study, they looked at auto-complete suggestions
made by the Google search engine. The study analysed auto-complete
suggestions of queries of the type ‘why are blacks/Muslims/gay . . . ’,
which revealed patterns of social bias in the auto-complete suggestions
by the search engine. For instance, ‘Muslims and Jewish people were
linked to questions about aspects of their appearance or behaviour, while
white people were linked to questions about their sexual attitudes’
(Baker and Potts: 2013: 187). The auto-complete was almost certainly
crafted by induction based on corpus data – the system had used past
searches to predict what future searches would look like in order to
provide helpful suggestions to users of the search engine. The approach
to the data was, presumably, treated as being entirely naturalistic. The
data, composed of previous searches, were taken and analysed using
complex mathematics, and an algorithm was produced for the suggestion
of completions to searches. What the analysts had not considered was the
social. The data were a tangle of the physical (what was written) and the
social (e.g. the social purpose of the search and the related social atti-
tudes). This reasserted itself in operation, however, with the search
engine reinforcing stereotypes of a racist and sexist nature as a result.
114
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 127 -->

The Google engineers did not, doubtless, wish to create a racist, sexist
and homophobic system, but because the data was reﬂective of the social
practices and society that created it, the result was inevitable. While the
processing of the data ignored this and focused on the naturalistic,
the anti-naturalistic (social) was present and made itself apparent in the
resulting system.
If language is an example of a system in which the naturalist and anti-
naturalist combine, how might we begin to understand the different ways
in which we might interact with language data? In the previous example,
two sets of researchers focused on language in very different ways: the
Google engineers seemed to take an entirely naturalistic approach to
their data and achieved a set of results (auto-complete suggestions),
while the linguists (Baker and Potts) emphasised the social in their
exploration of the results produced by the system and pointed out the
bias of the results. The answer to why different studies have different
standpoints relates directly to how Popper responded to the blended
naturalist/anti-naturalist character of objects of inquiry such as language
in our case. Instead of focusing on the objects themselves, Popper con-
siders the purpose of the analyst in approaching them. He considers
whether the analyst’s dominant research interest is theoretical, historical
or applied:
A dominant interest in explanatory laws of mechanisms made for a theore-
tical science, such as physics or sociology. A dominant interest in initial
conditions made for an historical science, such as geology or social history.
A dominant interest in natural application or prediction made for an applied
science, such as engineering. (Jarvie 2016: 287, reporting on lectures given by
Popper)
Popper also cautions that while what he calls extra-scientiﬁc results
(which we might broadly identify with applied research questions), aris-
ing from the scientiﬁc knowledge, were valuable, they should be sepa-
