rated out from the purely scientiﬁc.
In the example given, none of the researchers had any overt theoretical
objective – they were not trying to produce a theory of language or
prejudice, for example. It is arguable that Baker and Potts had a historical
objective – they were working from a set of initial conditions (the system
as it was instantiated) and trying to understand how those conditions
gave rise to the results that they encountered. The Google scientists, on
the other hand, were presumably applied in their focus – they simply
wanted to build a system that seemed to work to some measurable
degree. In doing so, the theories they were drawing on remain obscure
4.2 The Natural, the Social and the Cultural
115
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 128 -->

to us, but their work, in all likelihood, derived from a scientiﬁc approach
rooted in mathematics.64 Yet because of the entangled nature of the
scientiﬁc and the extra-scientiﬁc (Popper 1976: 97), it is inevitably the
case that the scientiﬁc and the extra-scientiﬁc are bound – the extra-
scientiﬁc informed the data that were the basis of the scientiﬁc in this
example, and an extra-scientiﬁc exploration and critique of the results
was fruitful.
The research question proposed by a researcher has an impact on their
approach to the object of study. For instance, the research question can
determine whether a speciﬁc study focuses on the scientiﬁc or the extra-
scientiﬁc. Of course, researchers may work with more than one question
and their research programme may span across the three areas of theo-
retical, historical and applied, and straddle the science/extra-science
divide. However, as a starting point, the threefold division that Popper
introduces provides a helpful framework in which to consider work with
corpus data. For example, it allows us to generally characterise the
different practices in corpus use and tool development in corpus linguis-
tics and natural language processing (NLP). NLP has a very applied
focus. So, in NLP, systems are often made to ‘do’ something and the
means by which that is achieved may have no bearing on our under-
standing of human language, as processed by the human mind, at all. The
science behind the systems relates typically to mathematics. The ends
justify the means, but the means have no contribution to make, necessa-
rily, to the understanding of human language. On the other hand, as
corpus linguistics has become increasingly focused on historical and
theoretical accounts of language, it is developing a scientiﬁc focus, though
it still contributes much work of extra-scientiﬁc interest. Hence historical
linguistics has used corpora to explore language change, proposing initial
conditions and then exploring processes of change (see Jenset and
McGillivray 2017, for an overview; Whitt 2018, for a recent selection of
work). Work on collocation has become increasingly focused on theore-
tical concerns as evidenced by work such as Hoey (2005), Barnbrook et
al. (2013) and Bruening (2020). The focus is on testing the psychological
plausibility of measures used (Hughes and Hardie 2019), the attempted
falsiﬁcation of the collocation hypothesis (Xiao and McEnery 2006) and
the examination of the different statements on which the collocation
64 Of course, when talking about disciplinary areas here, we are aware that these labels are
shorthand for ‘a conglomerate of problems and attempted solutions, demarcated in an
artiﬁcial way. What really exists are problems and solutions, and scientiﬁc traditions’
(Popper 1976: 92).
116
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 129 -->

hypothesis is based (Gries 2013; Gablasova et al. 2017b). While there may
be, and is, an interaction between researchers working in all three areas,
Popper’s distinction between theoretical, historical and applied allows us
to better understand the motivations and choices of researchers and
facilitate dialogue and collaboration.
Here we also need to make a brief note about the application of
Popper’s framework to a diverse area of research interest known as
the digital humanities (for details see Wymer 2021), where corpora
are also employed (Mahlberg and Wiegand 2020). Clearly, most of
the studies will have a broadly historical focus documenting social
and cultural processes and their outcomes; the tool development
aspect of the digital humanities could then be subsumed under
Popper’s applied focus as discussed in the connection with NLP.
We also need to bear in mind the nature of reality we are dealing
with and we have quasi-contact with. The reality may include social
and cultural institutions and traditions (see Section 4.4), which we
describe and interpret.
In the light of the preceding discussion, we will modify Principle 35:
Principle 350: A corpus, representing socially situated data, is an amalgam of
social and physical interactions. Our approach to analysing it should take this
into account, recognising the value of the theoretical, the historical and the
applied.
The work of Popper on the social world is key to solving what otherwise
might appear to be a simple-minded dichotomy – something is either
scientiﬁc or it is not. As already suggested throughout this book, this is
not a mere binary division. We also know that there is no certainty. Yet
there are ways of allowing ourselves to be more conﬁdent about the
conventional understanding of reality we adopt while keeping this under-
standing under constant review. While linguistics may not move along the
continuum from the unscientiﬁc to the scientiﬁc as far as a subject such as
physics may, if it abides by the principles outlined in this book, it will
move well away from the purely subjective enterprise it could be. The
idea that linguistics may be subject to a simple binary distinction between
the scientiﬁc and the non-scientiﬁc is old, as presented in this idealised
form by Householder (1952: 260):
On the metaphysics of linguistics there are two extreme positions, which may
be termed (and have been) ‘God’s truth’ position and the ‘hocus pocus’
4.2 The Natural, the Social and the Cultural
117
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 130 -->

position. The theory of the God’s truth linguists . . . is that language ‘has’ a
structure and the job of the linguist is (a) to ﬁnd out what the structure is, and
(b) to describe it as clearly, economically, and elegantly as he can, without at
any point obscuring the God’s truth structure of language. The hocus pocus
linguist believes . . . that a language (better, a corpus, since we describe only the
corpus we know) is a mass of incoherent formless data, and the job of the
linguist is somehow to arrange and organize the mass, imposing on it some
structure.
Here, we can, with the beneﬁt of nearly seventy years of subsequent
scholarship to draw upon, provide a commentary on this observation.
God’s truth is unattainable. As fallibilists we are fully aware that we are
in quasi-contact with the reality we are modelling; our aim is to approx-
imate to it, with clear goals in mind (Principles 280 and 290), not achieve it
(Principles 4, 60 and 14). In approximating to it, we seek, through incre-
mental development of our ideas (Principle 210) to transition elements of
our work from the metaphysical to the scientiﬁc (Principle 12) within a
system constituted ‘elegantly, and economically’ (Principles 25 and 30).
We test with a goal of achieving falsiﬁcation (Principles 11, 16″, 280 and
290) and may achieve rejection (Principle 170). We should select and
develop data sets that maximise the possibility of falsiﬁcation (Principle
290). Householder (1952) goes on to characterise the ‘hocus-pocus’ lin-
guist by asking readers to consider ‘language as a certain select portion of
a large pile of shapeless rocks which you may play with and rearrange to
suit your fancy (provided you don’t break too many of the rocks’
(Householder 1952: 260). Yet, because of our understanding that the
‘rocks’ are interconnected (Principles 1, 8, 170, 25 and 350) and our
constraint
to
look
for
arrangements
that
promote
falsiﬁcation
(Principle 16″) we know that the version of linguistics espoused by us is
far from this hocus-pocus view of the subject. Yet at the same time, we are
not God’s truth scientists either, who have ‘a respect for the facts, a
humility, a patience, a desire for perfection’ (Householder 1952: 260)
that he represents as the opposite of what the hocus-pocus linguist has.
While the call to humility is welcome, no amount of patience will provide
the perfection that linguists who believe in God’s truth pine for in vain.
Principles 9, 100, 14, 22 and 23 show this quite clearly. Similarly, ‘facts’ are
disputed and are linked to presuppositions (Principle 3) and conventions
(Principle 20). The ever-present danger that we see in a desire to reach
God’s truth is that it may appear to be attained by a rejection of reality
through a rational principle, that is, by deﬁning a version of reality in
which facts and certainty behave in a way that appears to be ‘scientiﬁc’.
118
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 131 -->

Yet any truth that arises from this is rooted in an illusion conjured by a
ﬂight from the reality of language use.65
By the application of our principles, corpus linguists ﬁnd themselves
placed on this continuum as a ‘good linguist should be somewhere
between the two poles and not at either end’ (Cook 2008: 306). We also
ﬁnd ourselves in that position when we consider our attitude to reality.
As Pullum (2019: 49) notes, Householder’s dichotomy of God’s truth vs
hocus-pocus is mapped to realists and anti-realists. Going beyond the
dichotomy, we approach language as critical realists: we approach corpus
data believing that an understanding of language is possible, but we also
do so realising that what we discover may be contested, falsiﬁed and
rejected (Principle 14) in a spirit of fallibilism.
4.3 A Focus on the Individual
Popper divides the social world into three parts: individuals, social insti-
tutions and traditions. These will be discussed in this and the next section.
The individual is a key feature of Popper’s approach to social science and
can be usefully applied when considering linguistics. For Popper, the
individual, not some grouping of individuals or theoretical abstraction
based upon them, is the key concrete object in social theorising (hence
Principle 32). This message is clearly one that is sympathetic to the corpus
approach – texts in a corpus, or recordings of the speech of a set of
individuals, gives us concrete, individualised, data points that we can
approach to use as the basis of our research on language. Such data in
itself is excellent because ‘test statements about observable events are
especially easy to test’ (Andersson 2016: 138).
We need to be cautious about rushing to such a conclusion, of course.
Not all texts are produced by an individual: think of multi-author docu-
ments. Nonetheless, the potential to view individual authors and speak-
ers in a corpus as being individuals opens up the approach to one in which
individuals can be studied. Yet Popper does not simply focus on the
individual – he does believe in social structures beyond the individual,
though he sees them as abstract in contrast to the individual who is
concrete. So with our corpus, we can experience the individual writers
and speakers within it as concrete, while theorising about ‘language’ in
the abstract on the basis of our study of individuals as a group. The
abstraction, however, needs to align with the reality of the individuals
in the corpus. For example, Brezina and Meyerhoff (2014) show how
65 Principles 37 and 38, to be introduced shortly, are also of relevance to this discussion.
4.3 A Focus on the Individual
119
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 132 -->

important it is to pay attention to the individual in corpus-based socio-
linguistic studies; disregarding individual variation, as their study shows,
often leads to spurious results.
This can drive our theorising – if in a historical corpus we see language
change, it is not because language itself has changed, it is because the
individuals whose behaviour collectively constitutes language have chan-
ged their linguistic behaviours. People change, language only reﬂects that.
Similarly, when looking at corpus data we need to be mindful of the
individual – generating an average from a large group may explain nothing
in explaining everything. When we look at the average relative to the
population, we may see no individual with average values, so we need to
be mindful of the individual within our analyses, because they are the
object of our study. Corpus linguists are mindful of this and more and
more is written on this point and statistical procedures are increasingly
reﬂecting this reality. Hence Popper’s focus on the individual is closely
aligned with current trends of thinking in corpus linguistics (see Barlow
2013, Gradoville 2019 and Wulff and Gries 2021 for further discussion and
examples relevant to this point). Accordingly, we must revise Principle 32:
Principle 320: Corpus linguistics can be used as the basis for a normative episte-
mology of language. However, this abstraction away from the individual
should not exclude or obscure the individual and variation from the normative.
4.4 Institutions and Traditions
While discussing only individuals so far, we have started to touch upon
Popper’s categories of institutions and traditions. Institutions are unde-
signed – they are systems that occur spontaneously with no or minimal
planning in human society. Language could be viewed as one such system.
Everyday language evolves in an unplanned way as a result of the interplay
between natural and social forces. They are classically those objects that, as
discussed already, represent a mix of the physical and the social. Each
individual capable of the production of language, placed in a context in
which it is used, becomes a bearer of this institution.66 The speciﬁc language
66 Note that if we follow Popper’s thinking as set out in Popper (2009: 33) regarding innate
predispositions, or anticipations as he calls them, and if we accept that the institution of
language is based, in part, on anticipation, then some reasoning about language, based
on a priori statements derivable from biology, would be possible, in an evolutionary
120
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 133 -->

they acquire, we might argue, is the result of tradition – the social norms and
practices of speakers over time transmitting the speciﬁc version of a language
to new potential speakers. More generally, however, traditions are explicitly
planned – and institutions and traditions may interact. Indeed, Popper goes
further to claim that institutions need traditions. So, for example, while
language may be characterised as an institution, the style guide of a major
newspaper is a tradition. It is a set of conscious rules applied to language in a
speciﬁc social context. Popper (2002b: 180) gives the following useful exam-
ple: ‘the English language, though passed down by tradition, is an institution,
while the practice of, say avoiding split inﬁnitives is a tradition’.
We may theorise both about institutions (as linguists have theorised
about language) and we may also theorise about traditions (as linguists
interested in issues such as genre and register have, for example). Yet we
must be forever mindful that these grow out of the individual – our
theorising should include, account for and be testable from that perspec-
tive. Nevertheless, we must also be aware that our theories about these
institutions and traditions are abstractions. Building upon Principle 350,
we may formulate a further principle:
Principle 36: Corpora facilitate the study of the institutions and traditions of
language.
Our theories about institutions, our interpretations, are not the institution,
they are an interpretation of it and the rules that we apply to test the
context. The acquisition of a speciﬁc language can then be seen as the result of an
assignment, that is, the process where the anticipation is realised and instantiated.
However, given the close ﬁt between the institutions of language and any given lan-
guage, and the still contested nature of the biological foundations of language, we will
continue, as Popper did, to view language, as realised in any speciﬁc language, as an
institution. This does not mean that we accept Quine’s (1990: 38) expressly behaviourist
view of language acquisition. We accept that anticipations have a role to play in
language acquisition, as Quine, paradoxically, seems to do himself (Quine 1990: 46)
when he notes that a language spoken ‘must have been simple enough’ to learn,
suggesting that there are biological parameters in language learning. If we have any
doubts as authors, they relate to whether the anticipation is expressly linguistic or
whether it is, in fact, rooted in broader cognitive abilities. We note also that, according
to Grattan-Guinness (1995: 19) in a piece published in Forum, the journal of the
Language Origins Society, Popper, in conversation, developed a more elaborate view
of language acquisition in which the ‘only innate capacity presumed . . . for mankind is
his instinct to utter one-word sentences, and to respond to them in others’. Beyond that
Popper’s approach ‘gives priority to experience in general’.
4.4 Institutions and Traditions
121
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 134 -->

adequacy of those theories are the same rules that we would apply to
scientiﬁc theories. In exploring institutions and traditions, Popper would
have us take a nominalist rather than an essentialist approach. The essenti-
alist approach would lead us, through a reliance on intuition, to, for exam-
ple, think through what we thought of a tradition until we had broken it
down into its essential components – its ‘essences’ in Aristotelian terms. By
contrast, a nominalist approach in Popper’s deﬁnition would have us rely on
intuition only as a heuristic to guide our investigation – we should produce
shorthand labels for a phenomenon and then seek to understand what the
label relates to. So, rather than thinking ‘there is such a thing as register
because we have thought about it long and hard and believe the system is
based on these essences and they work together in this way’, Popper would
have us think ‘we have a hunch that there might be something called register
and it seems to link form with function’. After using intuition as our hunch
to produce a label for some phenomenon we wish to investigate, we work
with the label for what we want to investigate and carry out that investiga-
tion on the quasi-reality of language we have access to, eschewing an
intuition-only approach. Nominalism extends to our study of language –
‘language’ is a label we apply to what we see in a corpus. That quasi-reality
of human communication is what we label ‘language’, and we look within it
to try to determine what the label ‘language’ means. Essentialism and
indeterminism are clearly mutually antagonistic, while nominalism is clearly
well aligned to indeterminism. This point does not need to be laboured here,
as the provisional nature of understanding lies at the root of much of
Popper’s thinking and has been outlined at some length in the previous
chapters. Yet it is useful to remember that this applies to our methods and
data as well as to our theorising. Our corpus is nominally language. It is not
language itself (Principle 14). Theorising about the corpus is theorising
nominally about language. Building an application based on a corpus is
building an application based upon the nominal form of a language. Yet
refraining from doing anything of the sort while working, like some indus-
trious gnome in a story from the Brother’s Grimm, on the perfect corpus
that actually ‘is’ language is a Grail quest, folly. The task is made all the
more foolhardy by the interaction that exists between our object or study
and our theorising ‘how we answer the question “what is so-and-so”
depends on our theory of so-and-so, and hence a theory change leads to a
conceptual change, and with no ﬁnality in science there are no ﬁnal entities
for the ﬁnal concepts to depict and no ﬁnal concepts for the methodological
essentialists to reach’ (Jarvie 2016: 290–291). As well as accepting that our
theories and ﬁndings are mutable, we need to accept that our methods are
too. We need to appreciate the nominal nature of the corpus, and the work
122
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 135 -->

based upon it, if we are to fully understand the nature and possibilities of
working with corpus data.
This brings us squarely back to the question of interpretations – our
theorising about institutions and traditions. To what extent can these be
objective? To some extent the answer in Popper’s view of the world is just
the same as in science. In science objectivity is judged within the tradi-
tions that control it – and for Popper such traditions should include
systems that apply rules of falsiﬁability to the results of scientiﬁc theoris-
ing. The institutions of science are critical and reﬂective and which
promotes, and constitutes, objectivity.67 So it is with interpretations –
they are subject to testing by the frameworks established to assess knowl-
edge in their domains and these should abide by the fallibilist approach
that Popper proposes to establish. Within that system their adoption by
convention may be viewed as provisionally true and objective, but they
must still be open to potentially falsifying critique:68 ‘conjectures have to
be criticizable if they are to deserve to be entertained, for critical argu-
ment is the sole control that we have over our meditations and our
dreams’ (Miller 1983: 11). Popper argued, much as this book did in
Chapter 1, for a common-sense approach – in the social world, indivi-
duals experience social entities as real. Popper views the social as being
the setting in which human life is experienced and part of that experience
is the setting itself – humans engage with it and it engages with them
‘institutions are experienced by us as if they were physical things’.69 Our
experience of language as we produce it and receive it is socially real,
hence social life and nature are equally approachable from a fallibilist
perspective. Indeed, Popper’s method of the social sciences (Popper
1976: 89–90) is similar, in many ways, to his method of the sciences.
4.5 Modelling Reality: The Oedipus Effect and the Rationality
Principle
In (social) science we model reality. The model is never an absolute
reﬂection of reality, nor is it God’s truth (see Section 4.2). This is in line
with our fallibilist position (e.g. Principle 23). Figure 4.1 illustrates the
67 See Popper (1976: 95–96) for a further discussion of this, in particular for his view on
objectivity not being rooted in the individual researcher but in ‘the social result of their
mutual criticism’.
68 We set aside from our consideration religious and magical reasoning here.
69 Popper, unpublished work ‘Ethical and Methodological Individualism’ quoted in Jarvie
(2016: 291).
4.5 Modelling Reality: The Oedipus Effect & the Rationality Principle
123
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 136 -->

process of modelling reality with a 3D wireframe model being a useful
approximation of the spatial properties of the object being modelled, an
apple in this example. However, the wireframe will never capture all
properties of the object (e.g. colour, smell and taste). In the same fashion,
our description of language based on corpus data approximates, in a
useful way, the reality of the social institutions of language, related
traditions as well as the individual speakers (see Section 4.4). This is,
however, only one side of the coin. Imagine that the model could exercise
power over reality, could change and distort it. It would be similar to a
situation, where the real apple in Figure 4.1a starts growing wires around
it to adapt to the model in Figure 4.1b. As absurd as this idea seems, there
are real risks of interaction between the model and the reality in social
science, which we discuss in this section.
We are going to consider two interactions in particular between the
reality and the model: 1) the Oedipus effect and 2) the rationality
principle.70 Popper accepts that in approaching the social with his
blend of naturalism and anti-naturalism he faced these two particular
problems.
a
b
Figure 4.1 Reality and a model (source: Augustus Binu, CC BY-SA 3.0, via Wikimedia
Commons and Shutterstock, image id: 1062943880)
70 We will not discuss what Popper calls the ‘argument from interests’ objection to the
social sciences here – that people are invested in social scientiﬁc research and hence
distort ﬁndings to match their interests, largely because this is true in the natural sciences
124
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 137 -->

The Oedipus effect is deﬁned by Popper in The Poverty of
Historicism as:
The idea that a prediction may have an inﬂuence upon the predicted event is
an old one. Oedipus, in the legend, killed his father whom he had never seen
before; and this was the direct result of the prophecy which had caused his
father to abandon him. This is why I suggest the name ‘Oedipus effect’ for the
inﬂuence of the prediction upon the predicted event . . . whether this inﬂu-
ence tends to bring about the predicted event, or whether it tends to prevent
it. (Popper 2002c: 11)
In linguistics, we can see some examples of the operation of the Oedipus
effect. They have, however, only a limited scope. They thus do not
represent a major distortion of linguistic reality by the model. Let us
now look at some of these examples. The Oedipus effect can be enforced
through technology such as spell checkers, grammar checkers or
(recently) AI-powered auto-complete options in email writing. These
will necessarily have some inﬂuence on literacy practices, affecting
some genres more than others. Baker (2017: 55–56) notes that spell
checkers enforce normative British and American English spellings,
leading the two varieties of English to ‘“bed down” into their differences’
(p. 56).
Other examples are connected with prescriptivism: the act of asserting,
from a position of authority, that a sentence is (un)grammatical may
make those listening agree with you, a form of response bias. However,
even the strength of this effect of prescriptivism may be doubted – while
authors such as Krizan et al. (2008: 560) may declare that ‘Split inﬁnitives
are not correct grammar’, the march of the split inﬁnitive goes on. In the
Spoken BNC2014, we ﬁnd a frequency of 113.459 per million words for
split inﬁnitives while in the demographic section of the BNC1994 the
frequency for the split inﬁnitive is 49.13 per million words. There is little
evidence here that an Oedipus effect has a strong inﬂuence on this usage
by speakers of English in spite of over 150 years of complaint about the
structure from self-appointed arbiters of linguistic choice. Arbiters such
as Alford (1864), who claimed placing an adverb between an inﬁnitive
marker and the inﬁnitive was ‘a practice entirely unknown to English
speakers and writers’ (Alford, 1864: 188). This is a classic dogmatic
argument almost crafted to be rejected by corpus data: a random casual
search of the Liverpool Mercury newspaper in the ﬁrst two decades of the
also, as the discussion of dogma in Chapter 2 would suggest. See Jarvie (1982: 93–94) for
a fuller discussion.
4.5 Modelling Reality: The Oedipus Effect & the Rationality Principle
125
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 138 -->

nineteenth century reveals many examples of split inﬁnitives produced
before Alford was born, including ‘to precipitately raise’ (23 July 1813),
‘to briskly charge’ (1 October 1810) and ‘to gradually diminish’ (2 November
1814).
Overall, language is dynamic, multifaceted and varied and thus seems
to be quite immune to the Oedipus effect. A good example of this can be
seen in the predictions made about pronunciation changes in English in
the UK. Rosewarne (1984) speculated that one accent, so-called Estuary
English, ‘may inﬂuence the speech of all but the linguistically most
isolated, among the highest and lowest socio-economic groups’ and
later claimed (Rosewarne 1994: 8) that it ‘may spread westwards into
Wales and northwards to the Scottish border’. Claims such as these have
generated substantial press coverage in the UK for over 20 years (see
Altendorf 2017: 171–172). If the Oedipus effect applied, then we would
expect to ﬁnd the accent spread because of the claims and the substantial
press coverage they received. However, not only is the deﬁnition of
Estuary English disputed, whether and how it has spread ‘is still unclear
and subject to considerable debate’ (Altendorf 2017: 177). The indivi-
duals who make up the speech community of what we might call ‘British
English’ may be engaged by articles on language change, but they are not
slaves to them to the extent that the Oedipus effect has even a limited
practical impact on the language they use.
Of more weight is the issue of the rationality principle. Popper, in
Notturno (1994: 169), deﬁnes it as
the assumption that persons or agents involved act adequately, or appropri-
ately – that is to say, in accordance with the situation.
He then restates this in terms of the explanatory power of a model:
[O]nce we have constructed our model of a situation, we assume no more
than that the actors act within the terms of the model, or that they ‘work out’
what was implicit in the situation. (Notturno 1994: 169)
In science it is often the case that simplifying assumptions make a complex
situation more tractable for modelling. The same happens in the social
sciences and the rationality principle is often deployed – it is assumed that
individuals within a system act rationally and move towards clear goals
with perfect knowledge, it is ‘the false but necessary assumption that we act
rationally’ (Jarvie 1982: 88). Similarly in philosophy, rationality can be
applied to language itself, controlling and distorting language with the
express purpose of abstracting it away from reality: ‘Ordinary discourse
is indeed seldom meticulous about ontology and . . . is apt to bespeak a
126
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 139 -->

pretty untidy world; but ontological clarity and economy can be promoted’
by making ‘our language regimented to ﬁt . . . predicative calculus’ (Quine
1990: 27–28). This is a self-confessed abstraction from reality.
We know, by personal experience and observation, that people are
not always rational, goals may be unclear and knowledge far from
perfect. Yet as a modelling assumption, the principle of rationality
helps greatly to simplify complex situations. Linguistics has its own
versions of the rationality principle, which we will for the purposes of
debate fold into it here: ideas such as grammaticality and idealised
speaker–hearer pairs are important simplifying assumptions in linguis-
tics that aid modelling. Rather than trying to engage with the messiness
of utterances deemed to be ungrammatical (even though the intended
hearers of the utterances appear to view that messiness as entirely
acceptable), linguists can invoke grammaticality as a ﬁlter through
which data must pass before it is considered, eliminating a great deal
of difﬁculty in the process. Similarly, in spoken interaction and prag-
matics we can think of an idealised speaker–hearer pair who, in all
likelihood, represent such an abstraction from any given speaker–
hearer pair that they do not represent any individual speaker–hearer
pair at all: we can all be ‘non-ideal’ on occasion. Indeed, the uncertain
nature of social interaction, and one may argue by extension of linguis-
tic interaction, is the ever-present possibility, and often reality, of a
mismatch between intention and outcome ‘it is one of the things about
social life that nothing ever comes off as intended’ (Popper 2002b: 166).
Hence linguistics, like other social sciences, can invoke simplifying
assumptions that make modelling easier, yet simultaneously move the
model produced away from the individuals, institutions and traditions
being modelled (see Sections 4.3 and 4.4). This does mean, however,
that we need to add a further principle:
Principle 37: By applying simplifying assumptions that ﬁlter and select corpus
data, corpus builders and users may weaken the nature of the quasi-contact
with reality established in Principle 14. When doing so, the rationale for the
simpliﬁcation should be presented and its impact on theory and the validity of
any tests undertaken considered.
This principle requires reporting of simpliﬁcations as they are very likely
to lead to a violation of Principle 290: the rationality principle, in simplify-
ing reality, seems bound to reduce the falsiﬁability of any axiom we test
on that simpliﬁed data. We permit this possibility for two reasons. For
4.5 Modelling Reality: The Oedipus Effect & the Rationality Principle
127
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 140 -->

corpus users, we wish to permit the case where the simpliﬁcation was
done as part of a process of incrementally testing hypotheses (in line with
Principle 210) with the goal of eventually engaging with data while not
applying the rationality principle. For corpus builders, we accept that
they should construct quasi-reality in their data sets and in doing so are
selective about what they include in their corpus, but they must report on
their decisions as the choices they make are inherited by corpus users
(this will become clear when we introduce Principle 39 later in this
chapter).
In making these assumptions, we limit the extent to which we may
approach an approximation of ‘the truth’ (Popper 1976: 101). This is an
important concept for corpus linguistics. Corpora, in so far as they claim,
for example, to permit one to model a language, speciﬁc domains of
language use or language at a certain point in time, will always and only
permit an approximation of the truth about language to emerge (see
Principle 14). This approximation may vary depending upon the match
between a research question and the corpus used – if we wish to look at
British English in the early 1990s, we might use the Freiburg-LOB
(FLOB) corpus. That will allow us to produce one approximation of
the truth. On the other hand, we may turn to the BNC1994 – this covers
a wider range of genres and covers speech as well as writing, so, depending
on the research question, we may expect the BNC1994 to provide a better
approximation of the truth than the FLOB corpus. It clearly permits more
surprise than FLOB and has a higher empirical content, so should be
preferred (in line with Principle 290). However, if we are interested in
change through time, because the FLOB corpus has sister corpora, using
the same sampling principles, for the 1930s, 1960s and early 2000s, FLOB
would produce a better approximation of change through time than a
single corpus at one point in time could. Similarly, if we wished to compare
British and American English, we might use the Brown family of corpora,
which includes FLOB. It contains matched pairs of corpora for British
English and American English, using the same sampling principles, over an
extended period of time, in two speech communities, permitting studies
such as those in Chapters 6 and 7 of this book. For questions of differences
between British and American English and change through time of both
varieties, the set of experimental falsiﬁers in the BNC1994 can be viewed
for most intents and purposes as being zero.
So while corpora may of necessity represent approximations of the
truth, and while they may be compared with one another in terms of
degrees of approximation, this question of degree varies by research
question (note the link to the idea of range discussed in Section 3.6).
128
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 141 -->

Yet importantly, we can also claim that the closer the approximation to
the truth that the corpus represents, the better the explanation produced
by it can be – it has greater explanatory power as correspondence to
reality, or at least quasi-reality, is an important governing principle
deciding the explanatory power of a hypothesis; we want theories to
explain the world as it is, not metaphysical abstractions (Popper 1976:
101; see Principles 60, 14 and 37).
The gap that opens between reality and a model is then further way in
which falsiﬁcation may occur and falsiﬁcations of falsiﬁcations may be
possible: a falsiﬁcation may erroneously arise from the gap between our
corpus and the linguistic reality it purports to model. An example of this
is easy to ﬁnd. Imagine that we have nothing but the speech of 19–29
years olds from the Spoken BNC2014 and an inventory of racist terms of
abuse to explore racism in young adults in twenty-ﬁrst-century Britain.
Using the data, we might conclude that there is potentially racism
towards people of African heritage in the UK because the word NIGGER
occurs in the corpus, but we may then conclude that there was no racism
towards people of South Asian heritage because the word PAKI does not
occur in the data set. This would be a classic error arising from induction:
because the word is not attested in the data does not mean to say it is not
in use in the society – racism is as present in the UK today as it has ever
been.71 The fact that the occasion, or the choice, to use a word that
betokens it did not occur in one group of speakers in 10 million words
does not change, or falsify, this well-corroborated observation. When
faced with incomplete evidence, we see the wisdom of Lakatos’ (1974)
distinction between falsiﬁcation and rejection. While we may have a
falsiﬁcation of the hypothesis, we should be cautious about making an
outright rejection of a hypothesis on these grounds.
A model should produce results that are relevant to the individuals and
speech communities in which language is used. Prising apart language
from other psychological and social processes, as Chomsky proposed in
his quest to study language competence, is to swing back to the naturalist
end of the methodological continuum and, consequently, to avoid the
dual social and natural character of language by a ﬂight towards an
abstract version of it composed of:
an ideal speaker-listener, in a completely homogeneous speech-community,
who knows its language perfectly and is unaffected by such grammatically
71 See, for example, the Equality and Human Rights Commission (2019), which covers
reports of racism experienced by students, of a very wide range of colours, nationalities
and ethnic/national identities at UK universities.
4.5 Modelling Reality: The Oedipus Effect & the Rationality Principle
129
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 142 -->

irrelevant conditions as memory limitations, distractions, shifts of attention
and interest, and errors (random or characteristic) in applying his knowledge
of the language in actual performance. (Chomsky 1969: 4)
Given the ﬂight from reality that this vision of language represents, it
requires the analyst to ‘embrace a stultifying method’ (Jarvie 2016: 298).
Note that the argument here is not that such an exclusively naturalist
position is never helpful. It may be, and as part of a more rounded process
of exploration, theorising and falsiﬁcation it has a role to play. Yet as an
exclusive position for researchers, it allows us to generate, without much
thought for the social context of the institution of language, a view of
language that, being devoid of that context, produces a distorted view of
language, stripped of the social and blind to the experience of the indi-
vidual. We align our view of language with Popper’s (2002c: 146) view of
explanations of the social in general in wishing to orient corpus linguistics
to ‘methodological individualism . . . the quite unassailable doctrine that
we must try to understand the collective phenomena as due to the actions,
interactions, aims, hopes and thoughts of individual men [sic] and pre-
served by individual men [sic]’ at the same time we also accept that our
task is to ‘construct and to analyse our . . . models carefully in descriptive
or nominalist terms, that is to say, in terms of individuals’ (Popper 2002c:
126). That is not to say that we are against theory that collectivises the
linguistic experience of individuals – we are obviously not. What we are in
favour of, and which we think corpus linguistics facilitates to a potentially
greater degree than any other research method, is the holding to account
of theories of the collective at the level of the individual in society as it is
at that level that language use and change is, ultimately, driven (as
permitted by Principle 320).
How might we begin to judge the degree to which any rationalisation is
helpful then? The answer was suggested in the discussion of Principle 37:
link the capacity for critique to the rational. Rationality may be judged by
the extent to which falsiﬁcation may apply to it. Hence, if a researcher
bases their theory of language exclusively on their own thoughts and
reﬂections about language, this would not be open to intersubjective
testing, hence it would not be readily falsiﬁable. If, on the other hand,
they make judgements about language based on evidence of the use of
the institution of language in the social context in which it is used, they
have a context that can be observed and revisited by other researchers,
either in terms of their body of data or in terms of returning to that
context to collect comparable data. Any such theory would be open to
critique and hence would, in Popper’s modiﬁed deﬁnition of rationality,
130
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 143 -->

be a more rational theory than that based on our introspections alone.
Hence, we embrace Popper’s concept of critical rationalism which:
with its emphasis upon argument and experience, with its device ‘I may be
wrong and you may be right, and by an effort we may get nearer to the truth’,
is . . . akin to the scientiﬁc attitude. It is bound up with the idea that everybody
is liable to make mistakes, which may be found out by himself, or by others,
or by himself with the assistance of the criticism of others. It therefore
suggests the idea that nobody should be his own judge, and it suggests the
idea of impartiality . . . This is closely related to the idea of ‘scientiﬁc objec-
tivity’ . . . . Its faith in reason is not only a faith in our own reason, but also in
that of others. (Popper 1945b: 224–225)
This is the grounds on which Popper rests his approach to the social
sciences and it is the grounds on which we lay the foundations of corpus
linguistics. However to do so requires a slight modiﬁcation of
Principle 19:
Principle 190: In research practice and debate, corpus linguists, as critical ration-
alists, should not produce arguments proceeding from dogmatic positions.
They are the negation of everything we wish to achieve through an appeal to
evidence and objectivity.
4.6 Critical Rationalism, Critical Realism and Social Reality
In the previous section, we characterised our approach to corpus data as
critical rationalism with its openness to critique and constant reviewing of
one’s position. This is our epistemological position. In this section, we will
seek to establish our ontological position – our understanding of (social)
reality. In this pursuit, critical rationalism allows us to achieve a balance
between two extremes. This is the balance between apparently paradox-
ical positions:
Popper’s theory of knowledge was resolutely fallibilist and opposed to all
forms of dogmatism. Popper’s metaphysics was resolutely realist and
opposed to all kinds of realism. (Musgrave 2016: 208)
The defence of reality was put forward in a common-sense way in
Chapter 1 of this book: we need to ground our claims in reality and
assume their correspondence to reality as it is commonly perceived.
This view underpins much research in science that has enduring, and
4.6 Critical Rationalism, Critical Realism and Social Reality
131
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 144 -->

generally predictable, effects in reality. We deliberately introduced the
common-sense defence of realism at the start of this book in preparation
for just this discussion because of the pervasive nature of reality in social
life – and ‘once realism is accepted at the common-sense level, it is
difﬁcult to prevent it from creeping upwards to encompass the scientiﬁc
level as well’ (Musgrave 2016: 209). Yet we do need to stop the creep of
realism to the point that it dominates our view of the social sciences.
Once we move from the actions of the inanimate to those of the animate,
wilful and socially aware realism becomes contested. We need to under-
stand social reality in both a social and a temporal context and recognise
the dynamic nature of social institutions and traditions. It is in balancing
this tension that critical rationalism has a strong role to play in allowing us
to bring to bear, in so far as is reasonably possible, the scientiﬁc on the
social.
To achieve this, we need to return to the concept of realism. There are
clearly different approaches to take to reality and, given that at the very
heart of corpus linguistics sits an attempt to connect to linguistic reality,
we need to clearly state our approach to reality now. Hence, we make an
adjustment towards critical realism, which we believe both helps the
critical rationalist approach to corpus data and seems to encapsulate
what we actually do when we engage with corpora as critical realism
‘admits our errors in perception without denying the existence of a
rationally knowable outside reality’ (Levinson 1982: 11). Our position
of critical realism is distinct from naive realism, ‘the view that the world is
literally as we perceive it to be’ and radical realism, ‘the view that errors
and differences in perception mean there is no real world, or no possible
intersubjective knowledge of the real world’ (Levinson 1982: 3). We
believe we do come into quasi-contact with linguistic reality via corpora,
though we also accept that processing errors by us as linguists or the tools
we use (e.g. part-of-speech taggers) may be a cause of fallibility in our
observations. Also, we accept that behind the data we observe lies a
system, which we access indirectly and which produces that data.
Between that system and the corpus evidence we use to explore linguistic
reality, performance errors may lead to errors in perception, an issue we
will return to later in the chapter. So, the corpus approach is an expressly
critical realist one rooted in objective indeterminism, in which we accept
that language exists in a form that is observable independent of the mind
that produced it, but where the perception and subsequent analysis of it is
potentially unpredictable. Note, however, the critical realist position
retains the advantage of being intersubjective. By viewing language in
this way, we build upon past scholarship in linguistics, which has been
132
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 145 -->

broadly compatible with this approach, relying on ‘commonsense con-
structs that have served us throughout intellectual history’ (Levinson
1982: 4). Our appeal is not directly to science, but to a common-sense
approach to language that uses concepts from science in order to shift the
study of linguistics from the metaphysical towards the scientiﬁc while
accepting that the goal of a purely scientiﬁc linguistics (causally determi-
nistic) is currently unobtainable. This leads to a revision of Principle 60:
Principle 6″: Corpus linguistics, drawing on scientia realis, works, as a social
science, in a way which is informed by concepts from science – it is the study of
observable language on which experience may be tested in accordance with
Principles 7 and 11.
It may be argued that anything short of science is not worth having: we
want rugged theories that have withstood vigorous attempts at falsiﬁca-
tion. As well as not being possible, this view also overlooks how even
imperfect theory can lead to practical insights as ‘we are able to accom-
plish tasks with a good deal of success even though we may be operating
with an incorrect theory. The reason for our success in these circum-
stances may be that our theory is not completely incorrect, or, more
likely, that we are in fact doing the right thing even though we may
think we are doing something quite different’ (Levinson 1982: 5).
So, for instance, we can use a challengeable ontology of parts-of-
speech as one of the building blocks of a grammar of a language. While
the ontology may be the subject of ﬁerce debate, the grammar could still
be of use, perhaps in pedagogical settings in which it proves to be of help
in creating a bilingual dictionary. The utility of the ontology is not proof
of it being right, but it is a strong indication that there is enough merit in it
that, from the point of view of critical realism (the data observed that the
ontology was based upon) and critical rationalism (the grammar provides
a description of the language that is good enough to be of utility), we have
theorised something that is useful.
So the critical realist basis of corpus linguistics is important because
critical rationalism and critical realism can be aligned. Critical realism
‘vindicates common-sense realist beliefs in atoms and molecules without
trying to make them certain’ just as corpus linguists want to embrace a
common-sense understanding of everyday experiences of language with-
out appealing to certainty in what we know about the system of language.
Having accepted this, we can then take the critical rationalist position
that it is ‘reasonable to believe (accept as true, adopt as true, prefer as
4.6 Critical Rationalism, Critical Realism and Social Reality
133
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 146 -->

true) whatever has withstood our criticisms, our attempts to show it is
false’ (Musgrave 2016: 225). While the results are not certain, and indeed
the observations are not certain, critical rationalism still allows us to
‘justify our believing it, tentatively, for the time being’ (Musgrave 2016:
225). Hence the problem of ontological knowledge, for example,
becomes more settled in linguistics if we take a critical realist approach
to it, based on the evidence we have, and then take a critical rationalist
approach to using the resulting ontology.
Corpus linguistics makes an important contribution in the discussion of
the institution of language. It is well placed to make socially situated
observations, albeit, of necessity, indirect ones. It is well placed to make
falsiﬁable claims and, if falsiﬁcations accumulate, engage in rejection. It
allows us to explore the traditions of language, and focus upon indivi-
duals. Yet it studies a representation of all these things, as is common in
science. Its view is, of necessity, abstract and partial. It allows a degree of
intersubjectivity and that in turn provides reasonable grounds for
attempting to falsify claims made using corpus data, which is good. Yet
it cannot, logically, act as the only method the linguist would employ nor
should it claim to give rise to truth. For example, a theory may require a
very precise set of observations and these may be so rare that the corpus
becomes an impractical tool for their investigation. A good area where
this seems to be the case is the acquisition of pragmatic knowledge in the
context of second language acquisition. As noted by McEnery et al.
(2019), the range of variables to be controlled for in a study of inter-
language pragmatics is so great that experimental, rather than corpus,
methods may be the best way to approach them. In making such a
determination, one is sacriﬁcing some degree of contact with reality –
rather than looking at language situated in a social context in which it
spontaneously occurs, one generates a context in which language may be
elicited, with due regard to the research question being explored, while a
host of variables are controlled for. Greater experimental control is
gained at the cost of some degree of the contact with reality attained.
Of course the answer to this distancing from reality is triangulation – the
same research question may be addressed using a number of methods, all
expressing different strengths and weaknesses. Hence, while the voice of
the corpus linguist is a loud and at times authoritative one, in the critical
rationalist debate, it is not the only one. It brings advantages relative to
other methods, yet it is not the only method that can, or should, be
pursued of necessity (see Principle 280).
When dealing with social reality, two important aspects need to be
considered: context and time. Language use alters by context – individuals
134
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 147 -->

use language in the context of traditions and we may presume that those
traditions are an important part of their decision making when speaking,
for example. So usage is bound by the institutions of language, by the
traditions of language (the speech community in which a speciﬁc version of
language is acquired and used), by the traditions of the social context (how
language is used by other individuals in that social context) as well as by
other traditions and institutions. These form an important part of social
reality.
So, for example, when speaking we may not only take traditions of
language into account – for example, using a certain register – but we may
also take traditions of social hierarchy into account, modifying our
speech to show deference to a social superior, for example. From the
interacting forces exerted by institutions and traditions a further force
enters the frame: the choice that appears rational to that speaker. For
example, the speaker may hold political opinions that mean that they
choose not to use deferential speech when talking to a social superior.
That may be their conscious choice, an active decision to ﬂout the tradi-
tion that they are well aware of. So we may assume that the decision
regarding what is said by an individual in any context is rational from the
perspective of the individual; they are cognisant of traditions but they are
not the slaves of them. One may, of course, say that they are slaves to the
institution of language, but even there we need to exercise caution. If we
mean, for example, that they are bound to be grammatical and that if they
are not, then this is some form of performance error, then we are
promoting the collective view – what counts as grammatical in a speech
community – over the individual view – the communicative choice made
by the individual in context. That rational choice of the individual
deserves closer inspection and respect. Their choice may be entirely
felicitous in context and entirely acceptable. It may even be the case
that using a grammatical form in context may not be rational. So, for
example, if we were to talk with somebody who accepted what we view as
nominally ungrammatical English and we wished to accommodate to
their language use, would it not be a rational choice to choose to eschew
what we deem to be grammaticality in favour of acceptability and accom-
modation? If so, then given what has been written so far, our job as
analysts is not to discard such data, it is to theorise upon the basis of it.
Corpora are excellent sources of such data and, consequently, represent a
valuable resource for theorising of precisely this kind.
Yet corpora, as any method would, only give a partial view of the social
context. What access we have to the broader social context of interaction
is a nominal form of that context – for example, in a spoken corpus, such
4.6 Critical Rationalism, Critical Realism and Social Reality
135
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 148 -->

as the Spoken BNC2014, the corpus metadata may provide information
on the age, social class, gender and occupation of the participants. It may
also give information on the physical context of the interaction. Yet it is
trivial to conceive of additional details that might be productive in
informing and hence understanding the rational choices of the speakers
and hearers in the data. The lack of such data, where it is important to our
research question, may lead us to adopt another method. However, the
mistake we must not make at this point, as should be clear from what has
been written so far, is to recoil from ever using corpus data because of
this. All such data are nominal, perfection is unattainable and a retreat
from this data to an apparently more satisfying, yet ultimately barren,
naturalism is tempting. Yet what researchers need to do is engage with
the data, form hypotheses and proceed with a fallibilist agenda in the
critical rationalist tradition and contribute to the debate regarding what
language is and how it is used in social context, proceeding according to
the scientiﬁc method adapted and reshaped for social research as out-
lined here.
We now turn to the aspect of time in social research, which pushes us
away again from a naturalist agenda. In this regard, studying society and
language is very different from studying, for instance, a hydrogen atom. If
we consider a hydrogen atom, a naturalist approach to the study of it not
only reveals something about the atom now, but also about that atom in
different places throughout time and in most contexts (excluding extreme
contexts such as black holes). A socially situated exploration of language
does not do this. It explores language in a social context, at a particular
time and with particular contributors. This represents a simpliﬁcation
and, as per Principle 37, this simpliﬁcation should be noted by corpus
builders and users. Synchronically, as we look across different contexts
and interlocutors, we may see differences and similarities and, in a
particularist fashion, we may draw out similarities and differences, form-
ing and testing our hypotheses. Yet diachrony brings another dimension
within which falsiﬁcation of those hypotheses can occur. What is true of
language today, in particular the traditions of language, may not be true
of language one century from now, or language a century ago. If we
accept that the institution of language is based upon anticipations (innate
predispositions of some sort), we may conclude that the process of
change will be much slower for anticipations, though given the nature
of genetics we would be foolish to assume that those anticipations are
entirely stable over time. This adds a substantial dimension of potential
falsiﬁcation and surprise for any hypotheses in the social sciences and
that includes the study of language. So in addition to synchronic
136
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 149 -->

falsiﬁcation and surprise, where factors such as social context and the
traditions of language are considered, diachronic falsiﬁcation and sur-
prise is also a very active possibility for hypotheses about language.
As noted already (see Section 2.8) one could, of course, set strict
boundaries and limits on hypotheses limiting claims to a speciﬁc context,
a speciﬁc set of writers and a speciﬁc time, for example. In an extreme
case, a linguist can hypothesise about their own language use in a speciﬁc
context at a given point in time. Yet with each limiting assumption the
empirical content of the hypothesis reduces as the set of potential falsi-
ﬁers shrinks. Given that we are aiming, in social science based on a
philosophy of science, for hypotheses with high empirical content, this
is not ideal. We want theories of something other than speciﬁc sets of data
at speciﬁc times. So diachrony is an important force both in theorising
and in corpus building. Our goal must be to maximise the empirical range
of our theorising by building corpora through time to permit diachronic
falsiﬁcation of hypotheses formed at speciﬁc points in time. For example.
if one theorises that spoken language works in a certain way based on
spoken data from the BNC1994 and one fails to falsify the hypothesis
using that corpus, the next step is not self-satisfaction. It is to expand the
empirical range of the hypothesis by using or constructing other corpora
to test the observation in a wider range of contexts synchronically and
diachronically if that is possible. Unless we work actively to construct
corpora that realise as large a set of potential falsiﬁers as possible, the
empirical range of our theorising based on corpus data, and the surprise it
brings to any study, will of necessity be limited. So embracing the chal-
lenge of both synchronic and diachronic falsiﬁcation in the social sciences
is key – because it provides further opportunities for falsiﬁcation. We also
embrace the inherent dynamism of social systems by seeing them as being
in continuous ﬂux through time – language will not arrive at some natural
endpoint. It will continue to change as long as there are humans to use it.
Hence, the future, as much as the past, is important for our falsiﬁcations
as we are working with a system in continuous change, rather than a static
system. So, returning to the Spoken BNC1994, it did not produce a
snapshot of spoken English that was deﬁnitive for all time, or even for
the early 1990s, in principle. On its production it represented a nominal
form of spoken British English in the early 1990s. It also immediately
shifted to being a historic rather than contemporary record. Hence,
researchers needed to consider previous points in time (as did Culpeper
and Kytö 2010) and future researchers needed to be prepared to look
at future points in time (as did Love et al. 2017) for the empirical range
of hypotheses based on the BNC1994 to be expanded and surprise
4.6 Critical Rationalism, Critical Realism and Social Reality
137
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 150 -->

engaged with. The task of expanding the empirical range of linguistic
hypotheses and enabling surprise, based on corpus data, is continuous,
not discrete.72
4.7 Propensity in Social Science
This discussion pushes us back ﬁrmly towards a consideration of probability.
As noted in Section 3.5, Popper’s views on falsiﬁcation created a problem
with probability for him in the examination of scientiﬁc hypotheses.
However, critical rationalism may have less of an issue with probability:
we are embracing uncertainty in critical rationalism and making provi-
sional, indirect, observations to come to provisional conclusions. Might
we deploy probability in such a context? We have already outlined a
form of ampliative reasoning in Chapter 3 that may help to explain the
real-world belief in certainty and reconcile it with both falsiﬁability and
an apparent real-world readiness to revise ostensibly certain beliefs.
Popper’s critique of probability in science led him to revise the basis of
probability and to resolve his critique in a way that aligns well with the
approach to probability in language, and we would argue more generally in
the social sciences.
Popper (1990)73 sought to shift the subject of debate in probability
away from what event is predicted, to what is the cause of what is
predicted: the so-called propensity theory of probability. Popper (1959:
28) argues that
probabilities must be ‘physically real’—that they must be physical propensities,
abstract relational properties of the physical situation, like Newtonian forces,
and ‘real’, not only in the sense that they could inﬂuence the experimental
72 Note that this view is consistent with Bayesian constraints on changes in such systems
over time. Given the close ﬁt between Bayesian systems and corpus data in machine
learning, the point made here about data sets is equally important to corpus linguists and
data scientists. See Godfrey-Smith (2016: 116) for a fuller discussion of Bayesian reason-
ing, time and conditionalisation. Note also, however, that in what follows we pursue a
line of reasoning in probability presented by Popper who was ‘objectivist and anti-
Bayesian, and his criticisms were directed against subjectivism and Bayesianism’
(Gillies 1995: 103). For readers interested in pursuing the anti-Bayesian dimension of
Popper’s view of probability, see Gillies (1995: 114–115).
73 We direct readers to Popper (1990) as an accessible account of propensity based on two
lectures given late in Popper’s life. His thinking on this matter goes back much further,
chronologically, than 1990, of course, and in this section we also draw on some of his
earliest work on the topic arising from Popper (1957b).
138
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 151 -->

results, but also in the sense that they could, under certain circumstances
(coherence), interfere, that is, interact, with one another.
To understand this, imagine that we have a list of 100 words, a mini-
dictionary. They form a set of words, a collective that we identify as
nouns.74 Let us assume for the sake of argument that in the language
we are exploring they are the only nouns. We are interested in seeing
what the probability is of each of the nouns to occur in everyday speech.
The ﬁrst thing that we need to do is make an apparently banal, but
ultimately highly important, admission. We will never know the actual,
irrefutable, probabilities. For reasons explored in Chapter 2, we cannot
observe the totality of language, which we would need to do in order to
derive that actual probability. Consequently, it is best to think of prob-
ability as a theoretical concept. When talking about probabilities we deal
with an observational proxy of the actual probability.
So what are the probabilities of occurrence of our 100 nouns? Let us
start by basing probability on a collective. We establish a reference
sequence based on corpus data – we take the speech of 1,000 adults
(500 males and 500 females) collected across the same span of space/
time. We then derive the frequencies of each of our 100 nouns from the
corpus. To our surprise we discover the words are occurring equiprob-
ably: each time a speaker uses a noun, there is a 1 in 100 chance of any of
the words in our set of nouns being produced. At this point, we explore
the speech of one further speaker. Our reference sequence says that the
probability of any noun occurring is 1 in 100. Yet in speaker 1001’s data
we ﬁnd some very frequent and some very infrequent nouns. Now a
problem emerges for classical probability theory: if we view the set of
100 nouns as properly being a set, in the same way that we view the six
sides of an die as being a set, then we assume that each member of the set
has the probability derived from the reference sequence. Yet we know
from observation that this is not true for speaker 1001. Popper’s solution
to this is to look not at collectives but conditions: probability becomes a
function of the conditions that give rise to it in individual cases, not of the
set that an event may be seen as a member of. So, we can no more talk of
the probability of a noun occurring than we can talk of knowing the
74 We use collective here in the sense of Von Mises (1951: 12). A collective ‘denotes a
sequence of uniform events or processes which differ by certain observable attributes,
say colours, numbers, or anything else’. Von Mises’ frequency theory only warrants the
introduction of probabilities where we have an empirical collective. The example used
here is an example of such a collective: a long sequence of meaningfully grouped events
that we can observe as the basis for our estimation of probability.
4.7 Propensity in Social Science
139
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 152 -->

probability of a die roll being a speciﬁc value for all throws of dice
throughout time: we know that there are loaded dice in existence and
hence we need to consider how the different dice and conditions in which
they are thrown inﬂuence probability, and thereby form sets in which
probability is shared. For the propensity theory of probability two ‘of its
principal features are that, unlike the frequency interpretation, it ascribes
probabilities sensibly to single event, rather than types of events, and . . .
it relativizes probability ascriptions to the whole current state of the
universe, rather than to the immediate locality’ (Miller 1995: 138). This
is another argument in favour of indeterminism – as propensity means
that the ‘world is no longer a causal machine – it can now be seen as a
world of propensities, as an unfolding process of realising possibilities
and of unfolding new possibilities’ (Miller 1995: 145).
What seems simple – the probability of a noun occurring or of a speciﬁc
number being rolled on a dice – is not as simple as it appears. This
accounts for a world that ‘is not wholly deterministic, but in many areas
is governed by such propensities producing real but non-determining
tendencies for events to happen in particular ways’ (O’Hear 1995: 2).
For Popper this means that we need to consider the generating conditions
when determining probability and ‘we have to visualize the conditions as
endowed with a tendency or disposition, or propensity, to produce
sequences whose frequencies are equal to the probabilities’ of the
observed frequencies as that ‘is precisely what the propensity interpreta-
tion asserts’ (Popper 1959: 35).75
In language, we may say that an utterance, or a linguistic structure, has
a propensity – it occurs in a context in which its form is inﬂuenced by
social context, past experience, the speaker, the hearer, over-hearers,
location and a host of other factors as we ‘live in a world of propensities’
(Popper 1990: 9). Yet, crucially, it is also inﬂuenced by speaker choice
and leaves open the potential for speakers to innovate. Propensities leave
space for creativity and individual choice in the generation of language,
hence they allow us ‘to think of probabilities as objective forces which
leave room for the exercise of freedom’ (O’Hear 1995: 2–3). So, rather
than looking at a word and asking the question ‘what is the probability of
that word occurring’ we ask instead ‘what is the propensity for that word
to occur given the set of circumstances in which it is being produced’. The
same question can be posed for any linguistic structure or feature. Note
75 Readers interested in how falsiﬁability, the theoretical concept of probability and
observation come together in the propensity theory of probability are directed to
Gillies (2011), chapter 7.
140
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 153 -->

that this distinction is important: it is an acceptance that linguistic struc-
tures or features, for example, do not have a probability of occurring that
is independent.
So, if we were to consider the word apple, we could claim that, because
it occurs 581 times in the 10 million words of the Spoken BNC2014, this
represents the likelihood of the word occurring relative to other words,
ascribing the likelihood to appear to the word itself. However, this is
clearly a misplaced assumption. The ensemble of phonemes that form the
word apple do not appear of their own volition. They are the result of
usage on occasion – the probability arises from the circumstances under
which the word is uttered and the intention and choice of the speaker who
says the word. That intention is not random and the words themselves are
not patterned randomly as ‘Language is never, ever, ever, random’
(Kilgarriff 2005: 263). So the probability of the word occurring is not a
function of the word, it is a function of a host of factors that determine the
selection of the word for use, on a speciﬁc occasion, by a speaker of
British English that happens to be represented in the Spoken BNC2014.
This leads to what Gillies (1995: 107) calls the conditionality difﬁculty: we
are ‘forced to consider probabilities as attached primarily to the condi-
tions which describe the event and only secondarily to the event itself’.
This is a key issue in corpus linguistics. If our corpus is the only record of
an event, then it follows that what is described in the corpus sets a limit on
how we might bring in factors to instantiate the probability of any event
we see in a corpus. Let us build towards a consideration of this that we
will enshrine in Principle 39 in the following text by way of a discussion of
personal and objective probabilities.
Classic frequency-based accounts of probability account for probabil-
ity through a class of events within which probability is calculated, as we
saw in the example involving our 100 nouns earlier in this section. Just as
Popper called for a focus on the individual in the social sciences, he
applied the same individual focus also to probability. He looked at the
propensity for a thing to happen given a very speciﬁc observational
context:
The main difference between the propensity interpretation and a purely
statistical frequency interpretation can be put thus . . . propensity statements
may be said to make assertions about virtual frequencies; but they may also
be said to make assertions about the experimental situation and the ‘tenden-
cies’ (something like ‘forces’) inherent in it: the propensity interpretation
extends the idea of ‘forces’ to mere open possibilities – weighted possibilities,
of course. (Popper 1957a: 88–89)
4.7 Propensity in Social Science
141
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 154 -->

While the context may be very speciﬁc, the precise nature of the context,
while apparently known, is actually subject to a range of forces that may
not be fully known. To appreciate this point fully it is worth eschewing
the usual examples used to discuss probability: coin tosses, dice rolls and
the like. These are simple processes by comparison to the use of language
in context. While we may consider forces inﬂuencing the role of a speciﬁc
dice, in a speciﬁc context, on a speciﬁc occasion, considering factors such
as whether the material in the dice was homogeneous for example, or
whether wind effected the results, achieving that for language use would
clearly be a much more challenging task . The challenge in part arises
from the fact that we view corpus data as a natural experiment – Popper
discussed propensity in experimental situations, with propensity being
‘similar to the idea of frequency in an ensemble of identical situations’
(Mackay 1957: 79). Yet to ‘determine the composition of your imaginary
ensemble, you have to know the physical parameters . . . propensity is a
physical property of the situation’ (Mackay 1957: 79).76 In exploring
propensity in the social sciences77 we keep within the spirit of the pro-
pensity view of probability, yet we also clearly introduce factors that
cloud the relationship of a speciﬁc ensemble to the utterances we
observe. While the utterances are observable, the set of forces inﬂuencing
them are not.78 If we could take a naturalist approach to language, this
problem may not be so acute. However, it is in some ways because we
face the question of the unknowability of the forces at work that we
should doubt the relevance of naturalism to the modelling of language.
This aligns well with the discussion of replication in this book (see Section
1.7). Variations in attempts at replication may have a number of sources,
as we will see more clearly in Chapter 6.
Equally important is what this means about methods in linguistics –
propensity and repeatable conditions are also clearly possible using other
methods. We will return to consider this point also in Chapters 6 and 7.
So, it would appear that the conditionality difﬁculty forces us to con-
sider all of the factors giving rise to propensity, yet the quasi-contact with
reality that our data affords us seems to preclude an entirely accurate
76 While the quotes here are from Mackay, Popper expressly endorses Mackay’s view of
propensity (see Popper 1957a: 88).
77 For further examples of the possible applicability of the propensity view of probability
to the social sciences, see Runde (1996) and Albert (2007) in economics and decision
making, respectively. Runde also gives a broader summative review of Popper’s work
on propensities.
78 For a discussion of the rationale for relaxing the role of experimental conditions in
Popper’s propensity approach, see Gillies (2011: 144).
142
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 155 -->

description of those factors. Given Principle 14, we should expect issues
such as this when we are quasi-contact with reality.
Principle 38: Our approach to probability in corpus linguistics relates to propen-
sity – we believe that there are a large number of forces acting on linguistic
propensity, which we need to actively model to the best of our ability, in order
to use statistical inferencing meaningfully. The forces form an important
context within which language is produced, interpreted and transmitted
between generations.
This principle now forces us return to the discussion of the conditionality
difﬁculty and to ask the question of ‘how do we model propensity?’.
For the purpose of the consideration of the following sets we will
rationalise for the moment by assuming that propensity is determined
