by forces that are equally important and equally independent (Figure
4.2). We further assume that those forces derived indirectly by inference

to explore

after a corpus is published have the same value as ones determined
before a corpus is published. We also only consider a speciﬁc language
at a given time.

example, A

sents a set containing all elements of A that also belong to B

elements of A, which are not in B

When approaching a speciﬁc corpus, we are provided with a series of
forces relevant to accounting for propensity in language, which we will
refer to simply as ‘forces’ for the remainder of this section for the sake of
brevity, explicitly provided prior to the publication of the corpus by the
corpus builder, that we may choose to use in our analysis. The set of pre-
publication forces, A, cannot be an empty set: it must minimally contain
those forces that are represented by raw corpus data, minimally words,
unadorned by metadata of any sort. The frequencies and association that
these words exhibit represents a base level set of forces that a corpus
provides. Without these forces, we are not dealing with what we would
call a corpus. In turn, however, we are able to explore some forces post-
publication by inferring them from the forces provided pre-publication.
There is a possible non-empty set, C, which is the intersection of A and B
(A⋂B) and is composed of information on forces that, while they may be
provided with the published corpus, might also be derived after publica-
tion. Finally, there are factors that may be explored only post-publica-
tion, that is, are in set B and not in A (B\A) or provided pre-publication,
that is, are in set A and not in B (A\B). Let us consider brieﬂy some
examples of each to illustrate the operation of the sets discussed before
considering U.
144
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 157 -->

Consider A\B. The simplest example of this imaginable is that if we do
not have corpus data, it may be impossible to infer anything about word
frequency distributions arising from it. So, for example, consider the
possibility of exploring an ancient language. For Babylonian, we can
provide corpus data and from that the propensity of Babylonian, as evi-
denced by those texts in the corpus, may be explored. Yet consider the case
of the earliest human languages – lost in time, spoken before the invention
of writing. A corpus approach to these is not possible precisely because the
set A\B is empty. While historical linguists may be able to use other
methods to approach some understanding of such languages, evidence of
the forces exerted on those languages as evidenced by extant texts that
may give access to pre-publication forces for these languages is non-
existent. As noted, the set A may not be empty for corpus linguistics –
where it is empty, a corpus approach is not possible.
What of A⋂B? A simple example relates to metadata – consider the
process of building a corpus of tweets. When creating the corpus, a corpus
builder may care to encode information in the corpus such as the time at
which a tweet was broadcast if they consider time to be an important
force that may be relevant for the analysis of the tweet. However, if they
do not include it, it may be possible for a corpus user to return to a Twitter
archive and retrieve that information, adding it into the corpus post-
publication. While it may be tempting to cast the new user as a new
corpus builder at this point, it is still important to consider the role of the
original corpus builder as their choices, more than those who subse-
quently use a corpus, are key in eliminating the possibility of certain
forces contributing to propensity being approached post-publication at
all. For example, consider a corpus builder who constructs a spoken
corpus by recording a spontaneous conversation, transcribing it and
discarding the original recording. Nothing can be done post-publication
to recover that recording, hence the role of those building a corpus should
be carefully separated from those who may make changes to it later on as
the initial decisions made when constructing a corpus are clearly of a
different order of importance, with regard to the exploration or forces,
than those made to a corpus once published. Hence, not all forces that
may be captured pre-publication in corpus building may also be captured
post-publication.
Finally, what of B\A? This is an artefact of what it was possible to
achieve post-publication. Consider the LOB corpus, for example. When
it was produced, there were some techniques that, while they are com-
monplace today, were not developed then. So, determining registers
using multi-dimensional analyses (Biber 1988) was not merely technically
4.7 Propensity in Social Science
145
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 158 -->

very difﬁcult at this point, but also the possibility of doing so had not been
thought of. Hence, B\A is a set that may shift over time, with items
migrating into B\A from U as time, technology and techniques permit
them to do so. Unlike B, set A does not change. Once published, we
consider set A to be ﬁxed. Change comes about in set B. We view later
editions of a corpus as separate, hence while between two editions of a
corpus the possible members of set A could vary, over time the applica-
tion of new techniques comes through B\A.
That brings us neatly to a discussion of U. This set is never identical to
A and B combined (A⋃B), that is, U ⊃(A⋃B) rather than U ⊇(A⋃B).
Why is this? First, for any given interaction, for any given written utter-
ance, we do not and cannot capture all of the forces relevant to under-
standing propensity in language production. So, for any given corpus it is
trivial to think of some potentially relevant factors that we may wish had
been included pre-publication but that were not and, alas, are lost to us
forever. Let us return to our notional Twitter corpus – we might plausibly
include the location from which the Tweet was sent, the gender, age and
occupation of the Tweeter and other forces, for example, if these pieces
of information are available for their account. Other things we may try to
infer post-publication may include the variety of English that they haveby
comparing their language use to other samples of language, inter alia. Yet
other, potentially relevant, forces are lost to us – for example, when they
tweeted, what were they doing, who was with them, who could see what
they were doing? Again, we could gather additional corpora and try,
post-publication, to infer answers to these questions. Yet the list of
potential forces is, at the very least, great and some may defy any
plausible attempts to model them post-publication – for example, deter-
mining the impact of prior life experience on the Tweet produced by a
Tweeter. Yet we must also accept the possibility that, beyond our current
understanding of language, there lie relevant factors that may help to
explain language use. So, on a practical level (the difﬁculty of accounting
for all known potentially relevant forces in corpus building) and at a
philosophical level (which we may characterise as a fallibilist approach
to determining forces), we believe that the statement U ≠(A⋃B) and
U ⊃(A⋃B) must be true.
If that is where our troubles with propensity ended, the situation would be
complex enough. However, we may consider that A, B and C might vary by
context or individual, for example. So if we take a personalist approach to
modelling language, it may be that the instantiation of the factors in U will
vary from individual to individual, meaning we may talk of propensities of
language rather than propensity, as propensity will be personalised too.
146
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 159 -->

People are not as simple as coins or dice – and their complexity relates
directly to the forces that shape the propensity of language. So the relation-
ship of probability to propensity, both pre- and post-corpus publication,
needs to reﬂect this. Similarly, we have to accept that the view presented so
far is expressly asynchronous – we are looking at propensity at one point in
space time. However, U may change through time. Hence, when building
diachronic corpora, we may wish, once more, to rationalise and assume that
U does not change through time in some way. A very good example of this is
the Brown family of corpora. These corpora ﬁx the forces, providing to
corpus users data using the same sampling principles through time. Within
such an approach changes that exist in the forces in U that lie beyond the
sampling approach are simply disregarded. In a similar fashion, the Brown
family also discards variations in space – the same sampling principles and
approach is applied synchronously to different varieties of the same lan-
guage (e.g. American, British and Indian English, see Leech et al. 2010;
Shastri 1988) and with minimal changes to different languages (e.g. Nepali,
see Yadava et al. 2009). This is a rationalisation that makes a corpus-based
approach to the synchronic, brachychronic and diachronic exploration of
varieties of English tractable. Similarly, it provides a reasonably common
frame of reference within which cross-linguistic studies of language can
happen. Yet as with all rationalisations, whether they be in an experimental,
lab-based context or in the construction and analysis of a corpus, the
resulting studies are a reﬂection of and are hostages to that rationalisation.
Their contact with linguistic reality is mediated by that rationalisation. We
approach quasi-reality, not reality itself, just as Popper argues, because the
forces we model when considering propensity are a subset of U.
This reasoning about propensity leads to a fundamental principle of
corpus building in several parts:
Principle 39:
Where:
U = the set of all forces relevant to the propensity of language
A = the set of forces that the corpus selected allows, on publication, the corpus user to
explore
B = the set of forces which, post-publication of the corpus, a corpus user may infer
C = the set of forces available on publication that the corpus user may also seek to derive
after publication of the corpus
Wherever possible: 1) corpus builders should maximise the size of A\B;
2) corpus users should increase the size of B\A; and 3) both corpus
builders and users should maximise the size of A⋃B as a subset of U.
4.7 Propensity in Social Science
147
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 160 -->

This principle represents a good guide for corpus builders and users
considering propensity. However, reality is not quite as simple as the
rationalisation of it that our consideration of these sets of forces was
based upon. As already noted, determining the forces establishing the
propensity of a real-world event is far from trivial and ﬁnding circum-
stances under which exactly the same set of factors applies is so vanish-
ingly unlikely that some philosophers have discounted the possibility of
doing so, calling for lab-based experimentation instead because, while
they represent a further abstraction away from reality, the factors that are
identiﬁed and modelled can be better controlled. Consider the following
interaction between Ayer and Braithwaite in Kő rner (1957: 79). While
not about language, or the application of propensity to linguistic analyses,
the discussion about horse racing is as applicable, in our view, to a
consideration of the modelling of propensities in linguistic interaction
as if it had been about that topic:
AYER.
It is not at all clear to me what propensities are
ascribed to. In the case of a die the situation is
clear; but that is not so in the case of the horse
race and I don’t see how one is going to evaluate
the probabilities here in terms of propensities.
BRAITHWAITE:
I certainly shouldn’t try to apply propensities to
the horse-race situation. I should use it as an
explanation of probability only as applied to
what Popper calls ‘experimental situations’.
If we accept this view, linguistics should proceed by controlled experi-
mentation. However, that would be to ignore the many, and valid, argu-
ments against viewing linguistics as a pure science presented so far. The
key to this problem lies in the social scientiﬁc view presented here – we
can apply the propensity view of language as a social science precisely in
the same way that we can bring other aspects of the scientiﬁc method into
the social-scientiﬁc study of language. It is in full knowledge of our critical
realist approach to studying language. We can dare to model language in
use – or horse racing for that matter – because we accept that when we do
so we are not ‘doing’ science. Simply treating the study of language as if it
were science will not produce results that are science. What that will
produce is scientism – something with the outward appearance of science,
its trappings, but not science as such just as Sampson (2017) has argued.
Propensity and the unknowability of the full range of forces relevant to
the instantiation of propensity work together, once again, to make lin-
guistics a social scientiﬁc, rather than scientiﬁc, enterprise.
148
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 161 -->

Of course, one response to the problem outlined may be to reach for a
method that does not exhibit this problem. Sadly, as should be apparent
from the argument so far, we believe there is no such method for the
study of language. That is not to say that we have no use or regard for
other methods in linguistics – we have made it clear already, and will
return to the theme, that we need to take a range of approaches to an
essentially social–scientiﬁc problem: the uses and nature of language.
Each method engages with a slightly different quasi-reality and, in
doing so, provides different perspectives, of often complementary
value, to the study of language. Yet all are subject to reasoning about
propensity. For example, we might take Popper’s view, when considering
the natural sciences, that propensity is best considered in the context of a
carefully controlled experiment in which, as far as possible, all the forces
contributing to propensity are balanced and accounted for in experimen-
tal situations, allowing us to observe propensity in the ensemble of
identical situations that Mackay discusses. These ensembles might then
be used to explore replication and meta studies might be undertaken to
explore the extent to which, across a wide range of identical experiments,
replication occurred. Yet when we look at experimental studies, even
before we begin to consider more subtle hidden forces that may inﬂuence
results, we often see fairly coarse ones that obviously do but convention
has either blinded researchers to that reality or has claimed it has not. A
very good example of this is given in Andringa and Godfroid (2020) in a
discussion of sampling in studies in applied linguistics. They write from a
position where they believe that as ‘language scientists we tend to strive
for knowledge that has universal value’ (p. 134). They then point to a
major way in which studies that they have looked at have sought to deal
with forces inﬂuencing propensity ‘such generalisation rests on the pre-
mise that samples are randomly drawn from the population under study’
(p. 134) because if ‘the selection of participants is somehow biased, then
the reliability of researchers’ statements about the behaviours under
investigation is compromised’ (p. 134). Setting aside for the moment
whether we can label and segment the population under study so that
we know all the forces that we wish to model when considering propen-
sity, we will assume that, at a general level, these statements are right – we
do need to build models of populations of speakers if we are to make
generalisable statements about them on the basis of our observations. We
can largely set aside the objections we may make about unseen, or hard to
account for forces, simply because the study goes on to show that
researchers such as Arnett (2008) have demonstrated that there is an
obvious bias in terms of ethnicity, level of education, cultural
4.7 Propensity in Social Science
149
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 162 -->

background, age and other factors in major psychological studies.
Speciﬁcally in linguistics, researchers such as Kormos and Sáfár (2008),
Mackey and Sachs (2012) and Plonsky (2016) have raised concerns about
sampling bias in L2 acquisition studies, with Plonsky claiming that 67% of
samples used in L2 research are drawn from university students alone.
Plonsky’s ﬁndings seem corroborated by Andringa and Godfroid (2020:
138) when they ﬁnd, in a review of meta-analyses of L2 acquisition from
2014 to 2018, that a ‘staggering 88% of all adult samples are university
student samples’ and that overall ‘insight from Applied Linguistics
research is strongly shaped by information gathered from university
students, and these tend to be enrolled at prestigious research institutes
in North America’. Andringa and Godfroid discuss possible sources of
the bias, but in all of the cases cited we would suggest it has one source:
convention. Convention becomes a licence for normal science and all the
studies that exhibit this sampling bias are working within the paradigm of
normal science – they are accounting for forces that convention accepts
that they should within the normal science paradigm. Yet a moment of
thought and reﬂection is all that is needed to consider that the forces
being considered in these studies represent, in our terms, a very small
subset indeed of U in Figure 4.2. However, we can see value in such
studies – because we interpret them through the social scientiﬁc lens. We
value different perspectives and, as long as we can understand clearly
what forces may and may not be included in a study, we are able to
consider not an ensemble of identical situations, but an assemblage of
studies, focused on the same broad object of study (language) that vary
from one another in terms of method, forces and so on to greater or lesser
degrees.
That assemblage will vary across time for a number of reasons, but here
we will focus on two. First, observation may force a shift from normal
science towards a paradigm shift and second the nature of the metaphy-
sical may shift. Let us illustrate these points with a brief discussion of dark
matter. On the face of it, dark matter is a difﬁcult thing to believe in – the
proposition being that the gravity of visible objects, as apparently
explained satisfactorily by Newtonian physics, is in fact subject to a
signiﬁcant, unmodelled, substance: dark matter, an ‘unseen, non-lumi-
nous substance that interacts with ordinary matter’ (Sanders 2010: 1).
This peculiar substance, should it exist, was required to explain a set of
observations of the movement of galaxies that could not be explained by
Newtonian physics. Importantly, as physics stood, galaxies that are rotat-
ing, such as the Milky Way, should tend to ﬂy apart from the centre. They
do not. This leads to the hypothesis that dark matter provides enough
150
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 163 -->

gravity that rotation works against that gravity to support the cohesions
of the galaxy against this force that would tear it apart. Starting with
Ostriker et al. (1974), it was hypothesised that the failure of galaxies to
fall apart due to rotation could only be satisfactorily explained if, in fact,
such galaxies were surrounded by a substantial halo of matter that is
invisible to us at the moment. In this case, we see observation presenting
a challenge to an established scientiﬁc paradigm: Newtonian physics. Not
only was the shape and rotation of the galaxies a suggestion of dark
matter, but also, most importantly, the speed of rotation was not merely
high in the centre of the galaxies, but was also high in their outer regions.
Such speed should have made the possibility of the galaxies ﬂying apart
very high unless there was additional, unseen, gravity at play – the gravity
in the visible stars was insufﬁcient for the job. Such insight was possible
because the nature of the metaphysical had shifted. Prior to having
instruments capable of observing galaxies in sufﬁcient detail, over a
long enough period of time, the question of directly observing large
numbers of them for long enough to challenge the established
Newtonian account of the shape of galaxies was in the realm of the
metaphysical. While one could have imagined that the observation of
the movement of galaxies might reveal a fresh force that physics needs to
account for, this would have been pure speculation in the absence of the
ability to make the observation. It was the shift of the possibility of
making such observations from the metaphysical that permitted the
observations that in turn led to a need to either revise Newtonian physics
to
account
for
the
observations
(so-called
Modiﬁed
Newtonian
Dynamics, or MOND, as proposed by Milgrom 1983) or to hypothesise
the existence of a metaphysical construct, dark matter, that constitutes an
estimated 84% of the gravitational mass of the universe that accounts for
the observations and thus sustains Newtonian physics.79
79 Sanders (2010) is recommended for readers wanting deeper insight into the debates
around dark matter. While we have cited Ostriker et al. (1974) here as the start point for
the dark matter debate, earlier observations had noted the problem, though they had
not proposed the solution to the observations given in 1974. Both Oort (1932) and
Zwicky (1933) made observations using the equipment available at that time, which
suggested that there seemed to be matter in the universe that was unseen and that this
matter was important in shaping the universe. The ﬁnal paragraph of Oort is also of
interest in terms of metaphysics – his estimate of 30–40 years for a piece of follow-up
work, given the techniques available to him, neatly encapsulates how, on the cusp
between the scientiﬁc and the metaphysical, research of this sort may be set aside
until better methods, which complete the transition from the metaphysical, are avail-
able. We should also note that the metaphysical construct of dark matter may shift from
4.7 Propensity in Social Science
151
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 164 -->

We feel that at this point we should return to fallibilism. Much of what
has been written about fallibilism and the social sciences thus far may
provoke a feeling of defeatism in the reader. It is clearly a happier
proposition to imagine a quest for the truth rather than to accept the
inescapably provisional nature of knowledge. However, such despair
should be set aside for two important reasons. First, the provisional
nature of knowledge is realistic – the quest for certainty presents, at
best, the comfort of magical thinking and, more often, presents the
danger of deep disillusionment when the certain is proved to be wrong.
Second, while the focus of fallibilism may be on the negative, the
researcher should remember that, with each test passed, a theory gains
corroboration. This explains the apparent contradiction in Popper’s
(1976: 87) ﬁrst two theses regarding the logic of the social sciences:
1) we know a great deal yet 2) our ignorance is boundless. Knowledge
and ignorance are intertwined in Popper’s epistemology – one may
become the other and the classiﬁcation of any hypothesis under either
is provisional. Yet at any moment in time, we may, with some conﬁdence,
point to much that we ‘know’, with the expanding human intellectual
endeavour leading to ‘constantly increasing knowledge and our con-
stantly increasing insight that we really know nothing’ (Popper 1976:
88). In that context, knowledge brings with it a burden of ignorance:
our insights, as well as being mutable, also seek to expand our under-
standing of the problem we are exploring – for example, by discovering
forces relevant to the propensity of language in use. While it is tempting
to ﬁnish the chapter with a new principle, in reality the call of Principle 22
for an honest account of limitations, the critical realism of Principle 14
and the critical rationalism of Principle 190 have already given sufﬁcient
cause to reﬂect on what we do not know to move on at this point. It
certainly gives, for reasons outlined, substance to our view that linguistics
is a social science.
4.8 Conclusion
In this chapter, we rejected the naturalist (positivist) position and showed
how the natural and the social can be considered in their own right within
Popper’s framework. Using Popper’s threefold division of theoretical,
historical and applied aims of research, we considered the position of social
science in general and corpus linguistics in particular and developed a view
being metaphysical if new equipment designed to observe it actually succeeds in
doing so.
152
What Is Social Science and the Digital Humanities?
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 165 -->

of social science that recognises the role of the individual, institutions and
traditions. In addition, within the context of fallibilism, we have estab-
lished critical rationalism as our epistemological position and critical rea-
lism as our ontological position. This recognises the difference between the
social and physical reality and our access to it. Corpus linguistics can thus
beneﬁt from the framework that rejects subjectivism without necessarily
satisfying all requirements of a science such as physics. Finally, we have
seen that Popper’s understanding of probability as propensity aligns with
social science and corpus linguistic understanding of multiple contextual
features, which, somewhat like physical forces, act together and lead to a
particular linguistic product (sentence or utterance) or another type of
outcome of a social interaction.
4.8 Conclusion
153
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.006 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 166 -->

CHAPT ER F IVE
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
So far, we have accepted that corpus data allows us quasi-contact with the
reality of language in use. Yet exactly what is the nature of that reality?
What do we study when we study a corpus? To what extent do the writers,
speakers and hearers in our corpus make a contribution to our analysis of
language? In this chapter we will look at the practice of corpus linguistics
and the nature of the quasi-reality that we explore when we analyse a
corpus. In doing so, we will note that it is socially situated data that encode
the views of producers and consumers of language and that the data
provide us with interesting points of reference in our analyses that may
lead to falsiﬁcations arising not from theory, but from the producers of our
data. Throughout we will navigate between the normative and personalist
view of our data. We call the chapter ‘Everyday Linguistics’ because we
think that, in some ways, the professional linguist, the language learner and
the language user all approach language, at an abstract level, in the same
way. We will consider that claim as the chapter proceeds.
However, at this point we will turn from Popper to other sources to
outline our linguistic approach. While Popper offers a broad framework
encompassing both the natural and the social (see Chapter 4), when we
come to the speciﬁc question of what we believe we are doing when we
look at language data he is silent. Popper ‘had no part in linguistic philo-
sophy’ (O’Hear 1995: 10) so his guidance fails us at this point.80 While all
that we have outlined so far provides a framing for our approach to
language, the exact nature of our data still needs to be brought into focus.
In the history of science, we have identiﬁed Ockham’s dichotomy of
scientia realis and scientia rationalis (see Section 1.6), the former focusing
on empirical data as the ‘real entities’, while the latter emphasises con-
cepts, that is, the ‘rational entities’. In the history of linguistic thinking,
we can see a similar dynamic between formalism and functionalism,
hence the subtitle of this chapter: form and function. Thomas (2020),
who traces the historical sources of these two approaches, metaphorically
describes the difference between formalism and functionalism as the
80 Though see fn 66.
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 167 -->

distinction between the approach of the engineer (formalism) and the
collector (functionalism). The former creates a ‘framework of a building
. . . comprised of idealized geometrical shapes iteratively embedded
within each other’, while the latter collects specimens from their travels,
carefully juxtaposing them in a ‘cabinet of curiosity’ (Thomas 2020: 7).
Newmeyer (2010), while arguing for the essential compatibility of these
two approaches to language, distinguishes formalism and functionalism
by pointing to two principles that typically differentiate between a form-
alist and a functionalist approach: 1) autonomy of syntax (formalism) and
2) external explanation (functionalism).
Autonomy of syntax: The rules (principles, constraints, etc.) that determine
the combinatorial possibilities of the formal elements of a language make no
reference to constructs from meaning, discourse, or language use. . . .
External explanation: Grammatical structure is shaped in large part by the
functions that language serves, the most important of which is that of con-
veying meaning in the act of communication. (Newmeyer 2010: 302)
In our approach, we can clearly readily identify with the functionalist
principle of external explanation, which we reﬁne further and use to
formulate Principle 40.
Principle 40: We believe that those producing language generate output which
they believe to be appropriate and well formed from the point of view of their
speech community for the purpose of communication in the cognitive, social
and physical context in which the utterance was produced, bounded by time.
Note that this principle makes a claim about producers of a language –
our everyday linguists that corpus linguists rely on. This principle places
our approach to the study of language squarely within a frame of
contextualism – we believe that context of production and reception has
a role to play in the use and understanding of language and that as context
varies so may language and, more importantly, our interpretation of lan-
guage. Principle 40 is also clearly strongly linked to Principles 39 and 3. This
sets us apart from those whose view of knowledge is invariant – invariantists
look for knowledge that is independent of context and invariant.81 The
81 While we will not consider the invariantist position here, nor contrast it with our own,
readers interested in pursuing this debate should see Pynn (2016). We would also note that
a contextualist approach is not new – it is something that Popper clearly inclines to, as noted
by Annis (1978), and which philosophers of language such as Austin have drawn upon
(Williams 1991: 113, 168–169).
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
155
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 168 -->

Protean nature of interpretation leads to a fundamental distinction between
the natural and the social sciences. Language as socially situated action,
unlike natural events, is meaningful. Meaningfulness is the key to separating
the social from natural sciences:
It is held that the phenomena of social life, such as an event or action, are not
pieces of nature, slices through a given ﬂux of experience, but are rather
intentional objects – something, that is, created by or decided upon by human
beings. It is human beings, language-using creatures, who constitute signiﬁ-
cance out of the inﬁnity of physical phenomena. This process of attributing
signiﬁcance or meaning will differ from time to time, from culture to culture,
and even from person to person. Hence there is nothing much ‘given’ in the
social world. (Jarvie 1982: 90)82
We have to engage with a social reality, language in use, to understand it,
we would argue, by understanding the experience of language users in a
form of ‘conceptual inquiry’ (Jarvie 1982: 91). In this chapter, we ﬁrst
brieﬂy explore the philosophical sources of functionalism through the
basic tenets of the Prague School and Hallidayan functional linguistics
before looking at the connection between Popper’s framework and func-
tionalist linguistics in the form of Axiomatic functionalism (Jan W.F.
Mulder). Reﬁning our thinking further, we formulate two more princi-
ples and exemplify their applications with a number of corpus examples.
5.1 Prague Schools, Halliday and Axiomatic Functionalism
Let us focus on the theoretical underpinnings of functionalism. One of
the notable expressions of these are the Theses of the Prague Linguistic
Circle (1929). The Prague Linguistic Circle was originally a small group
of linguists and literary scholars at Charles University in Prague who
aligned with the positions of Saussure‘s linguistic structuralism and
emphasised the functional aspect of language.
Key Thinkers: Prague Linguistic Circle (1926)
The Prague Linguistic Circle started as informal meetings of three linguists: Vilém
Mathesius (1882–1945), Nikolai Trubetzkoy (1890–1938) and Roman Jakobson
(1896–1982). Other important linguists and literary scholars followed suit,
including Josef Vachek (1909–1996), who is the author of The Dictionary of
the Prague School of Linguistics (2003). Key principles of the Circle’s approach
82 After Winch (1958).
156
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 169 -->

were formulated in the Circle’s Theses (https://cercledeprague.org/pdf/theses
.pdf) in 1929, a manifesto of the Circle. The Circle still meets every other
Monday at the Faculty of Arts, Charles University in Prague.
As recorded by Vachek (2003) with reference to the Theses, the
Prague School’s position considers the functional perspective a
necessary condition for understanding language. The orientation of
language towards communication is the basis of its functionalist
epistemology:
Resulting from human activity, language partakes in its purposefulness.
Whether one analyses language as expression or as communication, it is the
intention of the speaker which can explain it in a most evident and most
natural manner. For this reason, linguistic analysis should respect the func-
tionalist standpoint. Seen from the functionalist viewpoint, language is a
system of purposeful means of expression. No fact of language can be under-
stood without regard to the system to which it pertains. (Vachek 2003: 95–96)
Note that this quote is fully in line with our Principle 40. The intention
of the speaker is not understood as a psychological state but in terms of
a purposeful communicative act. This communicative act occurs in a
particular cognitive, social and physical context that needs to be taken
into consideration when analysing language. The notion of purposeful-
ness from this quote is also the basis of the use of corpus evidence.
When we look at data in a corpus, we make the reasonable assumption
that the person producing the language believed it to be appropriate
and well formed from the point of view of their speech community for
the purpose of communication in the given context in which the utter-
ance was produced, bounded by time. In Sections 5.2–5.5, we provide
examples of how the assumption of purposeful communication operates
when analysing corpus data. Here, we will continue to further explore
linguistic functionalism and its basic tenets. It is important to realise,
however, that the Prague School does not offer a comprehensive phi-
losophical discussion of the principles behind its functionalist approach
to language – functionalism is understood as a starting point, a crucial
principle stated in the Theses of the Circle, not a position that the
School would arrive at when confronting different theoretical frame-
works within the philosophy of science. Yet we can point to numerous
applications that demonstrate the value of the principle for the analysis
of language. In this context, we will mention two current representa-
tives of the Prague School: Libuše Dušková and Eva Hajičová.
5.1 Prague Schools, Halliday and Axiomatic Functionalism
157
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 170 -->

Key Thinkers: Libuše Dušková (1930) and Eva Hajičová
(1935)
These two eminent Czech scholars represent the tradition of the Prague
Linguistic Circle, which is still active to the present day. Their work shows a
wide range of applications of the Principles of the Circle outlined in 1929. The
applications straddle the formal and the functional aspects of language,
including syntax (Dušková), computational modelling of langue (Hajičová),
the grammar–text interface (Dušková), discourse (Hajičová), the semantic
structure of sentences (Hajičová), and the information structure of sentences
– Functional Sentence Perspective (Dušková and Hajičová).
For a discussion about the role of functionalism in the context of the
scientiﬁc approach to language we need to turn to Halliday (2003).
Key Thinkers: Michael Alexander Kirkwood Halliday (1925–
2018)
Halliday was an English-born linguist who held posts at universities in the UK,
USA and Australia. In 1976, he founded the Department of Linguistics at the
University of Sydney, where he stayed until his retirement. He developed
systemic functional linguistics, a functionalist approach to linguistics inspired,
among others, by the Prague Linguistic Circle and J.R. Firth, Halliday’s profes-
sor at the University of London.
Halliday’s approach to language is captured in his systemic functional
linguistics (SFL), a complex treatment of the workings of language from
the functional perspective. Halliday (2003) recognises, as we do in
Principle 350, that phenomena in the natural world, phenomena in the
social world and the semiotic system of language differ to a degree. He
also aligns with our broadly fallibilist position (Principle 22) and recognises
the importance of falsiﬁcation (Principles 18″, 210 and 34). Halliday also
offers an important insight by looking at the process of how falsiﬁcation
operates in scientiﬁc practice, introducing the notion of prototypicality:
If anyone offers a generalization, others immediately start hunting after
counterexamples, in the belief that this is how you test a hypothesis: if you
ﬁnd one counterexample you have demolished it, and it has to be abandoned.
But this is an idealization; it is not how people actually work. What you do
with a generalization is to apply it, and when you ﬁnd it doesn’t work a
hundred percent of the time (which it never will do), you try to improve it, to
158
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 171 -->

deﬁne the limits of its applicability, and seek further generalizations to back it
up. . . . In order to escape from this trap, linguists have had to invent the
concept of the ‘prototypical’. (Halliday 2003: 2000)
The quote shows the need for understanding the speciﬁc context in which a
sentence or an utterance is produced (in line with Principle 40) because
linguistic generalisations out of context might be of a limited value especially
if we assume, as some universalist approaches do, that our linguistic general-
isations will work 100% of the time in every context. It is also important to
stress the need to understand falsiﬁcations as conditional falsiﬁcations in line
with Principle 170. Rejection of a hypothesis follows when conditional falsi-
ﬁcations accumulate. Halliday (2003) further states and discusses thirteen
principles that SFL follows. The ﬁrst two are of special importance to our
discussion of the philosophical underpinnings of functionalism. They are
concerned with reiﬁcation, and theories and descriptions:
Categories that are used in the analysis of language are general concepts
which help us to explain linguistic phenomena. They are not ‘reiﬁed‘: that is,
they are not endowed with a spurious reality of their own. . . .
The categories used in the analysis are of two kinds: theoretical [general to
all languages], and descriptive [describe particular languages]. (Halliday
2003: 200–201)
Consider reiﬁcation ﬁrst. In philosophy, reiﬁcation is the process whereby
concepts and assertions are given the ontological status of real things (res
in Latin, hence the term ‘reiﬁcation’). Halliday points out that linguistic
terms and categories that help us describe reality should not be mistaken
for reality itself. The reality is what lies behind our concepts and categories
in the real contexts of linguistic interactions. The assumption of purpose-
fulness of communication in these contexts (see Prague School earlier)
enables us to work with empirical data and base falsiﬁcations on real
contexts of communication without assuming as real the ontological status
of the categories used in linguistic descriptions. A typical example of
reiﬁcation in linguistic context is Chomsky’s notion of the ‘language
organ’, a place in the brain where language is situated. In the logic of
reiﬁcation, Chomsky ‘upgrades’ the linguistic system, which is a conceptual
abstraction, to the level of a thing associated with the biological reality of
our brain. Halliday warns precisely against these types of reiﬁcation.
Another important point raised by Halliday is the level of generality at
which linguists approach language: the level of theories and the level of
descriptions. This is a distinction that might not be immediately obvious if
using natural science as a model for the analytical pursuit of language
5.1 Prague Schools, Halliday and Axiomatic Functionalism
159
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 172 -->

because theories and descriptions, for instance, in physics (gravity, rela-
tivity theory) merge in their universal impact. Gravity is present wher-
ever there is mass or energy in the universe. In language, on the other
hand, we can use theoretical categories such as ‘system’, referring to
structuralist understanding of language in general, or language-speciﬁc
descriptions using categories such as ‘preposition’ or ‘passive’, which are
deﬁned and reﬁned for each speciﬁc language.
A similar point is maintained by Mulder (1989), who situates his
functionalist approach, axiomatic functionalism, within the context of
Popper’s framework. He also calls for a clearer distinction to be made
between theories and descriptions in linguistics, which might not be
readily available in Popper’s framework:
Unfortunately, Popper – in accord with standard philosophy of science – fails
to distinguish between theory and description, that is, between purely formal
theoretical statements and statements derived from hypotheses about phe-
nomena. (Mulder 1989: 37)
Key Thinkers: Jan Mulder (1919–2011)
Mulder was a linguist born in the Netherlands who studied in Leiden and
Oxford. He held posts at the University of Oxford and the University of St
Andrews. His doctoral work at the University of Oxford lay the groundwork for
axiomatic functionalism, which he later developed in collaboration with
Sándor Hervey. As Mulder remembers in the preface to Foundations of
Axiomatic Linguistics (1989: ix), in the early days his ‘rather weird seeming
ideas only slowly found some people, mainly students, willing to listen’.
While both the Prague School and the Hallidayan approach focus more
on the practice of linguistic analyses, the everyday workings of language
within the framework of functionalism, Mulder’s axiomatic functionalism
has an ambition to offer a theoretical (scientiﬁc) foundation of the
functionalist approach to language:
The distinctive feature of Axiomatic Functionalism, and the prime (but not
the only) reason for the axiomatic approach adopted, is that the ontogenesis
of concepts is traceable step by step. (Mulder 1989: 1)
Note that neither the Prague School nor Halliday refer speciﬁcally to
Popper’s framework. Axiomatic functionalism, on the other hand, frames
its approach explicitly with reference to Popper (2002a) as a guide towards
an approach that is, in Mulder’s (1989: 1) words ‘truly and fully scientiﬁc’.
160
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 173 -->

Axiomatic functionalism, later expanded and re-framed (e.g. Dickins 1998,
2009), positions itself against the Chomskyan universalist approach to
language, or ‘transformationalism’, as it is referred to in Mulder (1989).
In the axiomatic functionalism approach, Popper’s framework is used as a
criterion of what it means for an approach to language to be scientiﬁc as
can be seen in the following quote:
This is yet another reason why transformationalism should be rejected, as
immunity from criticism is contrary to the scientiﬁc spirit of conjecture and
potential refutation (see Popper 1965). Mulder (1989: 9)
In sum, axiomatic functionalism brings a synergy between the functionalist
tradition of language description and a Popper-inspired philosophy of lan-
guage. In general aims, our pursuit is not dissimilar from Mulder’s. The
speciﬁc realisation of this programme, the modus operandi, is however, quite
distinct. The take home message from our excursion into linguistic function-
alism is this:
Principle 41: The evidence for language is the production and reception of
language by users and learners of language. The way in which it acts as
evidence for them is a way in which it can act as evidence for linguists also.
The assumption of purposefulness of communication that Principle 41
entails is one that corpus linguistics holds and works with.
Corpora have an important role to play in understanding language in use,
though we also see, as will be discussed later, the importance of other
methods in this phenomenological approach to language. The core of the
argument is this: we are working with the observable reality of language
while being aware of the limitations of this approach. At each stage, we are
trying to maximise the potential for falsiﬁcation of hypotheses (individual
statements) that follow from our generalisations (theories). The assumption
of purposefulness of communication is an important conceptual tool in this
context as it helps us to hypothesise the imposition of meaningfulness on
linguistic interaction. Yet we must acknowledge that it is an application of a
rationality principle in itself (see Section 4.5). We therefore need to consider
purposefulness against linguistic reality by asking in turn a series of critical
questions. First, it seems that we are idealising communication and linguistic
performance. We are assuming smooth communication, that is, that our
speakers are faultless in their performance but what if they are not – how do
we handle that? Second, what of those learning a language either as their
ﬁrst or second language? Surely, we cannot always expect purposefulness of
5.1 Prague Schools, Halliday and Axiomatic Functionalism
161
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 174 -->

communication in the context of language acquisition full of trials and
errors? Third, what if a person is not producing speech or writing within
their own speech community, or is not familiar with the context within
which they are producing utterances? Let us deal with each of these ques-
tions in turn in the next three sections as a way of demonstrating how our
approach to corpus data may accommodate such apparent difﬁculties.
Throughout we will use spoken corpus material as the focus of our discus-
sion simply because it brings the questions presented into sharpest relief.
5.2 Imperfect Performance
Linguistic performance is not smooth or perfect. Spoken language, for
instance, contains false starts, repairs and abandoned utterances that are
never ﬁnished. Online communication such as text messages includes typos
and non-standard syntax. This leads the adherents to some formalist
approaches to language (e.g. Chomsky, as discussed in Section 1.1) to reject
performance data as irrelevant. Functionalism, on the other hand, relies on
performance data as the source of evidence starting with the assumption of
purposefulness of communication discussed in the previous section.
Performance is purposeful, which means appropriate in a given context
and for the given speech community. It is also in performance that we can
see Principle 41 to be broken and re-established. When considering Principle
41, let us distinguish between the principle being consciously broken – for
example, for stylistic effect (which we call the ﬂouting of the principle) – and
contrast that with when it is broken in a way that is unknowing or involuntary
(which we call a breach of the principle). Yet identifying ﬂouts or breaches
represents a danger for the analyst. The danger is that we take our own view
of what is an acceptable or unacceptable utterance, or use some other
decontextualised warrant to form that view, and begin to exclude data
from the corpus. This is too broad and dangerous a warrant to accept for
excluding data. It opens up the possibility that data that are simply incon-
venient for a theory may be excluded – this puts a very unﬂattering spotlight
on the researcher and raises the prospect of violating Principles 290 and 37.
Let us consider Jim, a speaker pseudonym in the Spoken BNC2014. If we
decide something he says should be set aside as ‘bad’ data, how do we know
that the utterance in question is not acceptable to the speech community
with which he communicates? How can we be sure that, in the context in
which the utterance was produced, it was not acceptable? If we take the
focus on the individual in our research seriously, our default position should
be to apply Principle 40 and to accept the utterance unless there is clear
contextual evidence that suggests we should not. We believe the intended
162
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 175 -->

recipients also applied a version of Principle 40 and rejected the utterances if
the claim to appropriacy (Principle 41) and well-formedness was not true.
Yet our perspective, as corpus linguists, is most likely to be that of someone
who is not in Jim’s intended audience, is not in the context where the speech
was produced and does not form part of his speech community. Hence, the
warrant we reach for to use in considering any data produced by him as
breaching Principle 41 may be a dangerously convenient one for us and our
theories. The best warrant for this conclusion is to look back in the context in
which the data were produced and to judge it there. The reactions of the
hearer such as surprise or a request for clariﬁcation may also provide clues
for evaluating the appropriacy and well-formedness of an utterance; in
spoken language most clearly, the assertion may be falsiﬁed through what
we will call ‘in situ falsiﬁcation’. Here is an example of this from the Spoken
BNC2014, ﬁle Sp2m2f115:
(1) JIM:
yeah and plays the so ﬁrst we’ll play ﬁrst of another
group I can’t remember which we played
BEATRICE:
sorry say that again
Through in situ falsiﬁcation any claim to appropriacy (Principle 41) and
well-formedness is rejected by the hearer, the person the utterance was
aimed at and for whom the principle had to be abided by.
Principle 42: In situ falsiﬁcations regarding beliefs of appropriacy and well-
formedness of a linguistic feature or structure based on Principle 40 may
occur where appropriacy and well-formedness is rejected by a person in the
intended audience of the linguistics production in question.
The hearer’s in situ falsiﬁcation of appropriacy and well-formedness is
unlikely to be biased by theoretical considerations, no more than the
decision by Jim was motivated by that in producing the utterance.
Beatrice is not inﬂuenced by some Oedipus effect to accept the utterance.
The utterance is directed at her but she rejects it, in so far as we may assume,
simply because it does not abide by Principle 41. Yet in making that
assumption we must, once again, be mindful of Principle 37: the apparent
falsiﬁcation may not, in fact, be an in situ falsiﬁcation as we have described
it. An objection to this interpretation of utterances and the reaction to them
might be to take an extreme position of saying that one does not believe that
people are committed to the well-formedness of their utterances and that
hearers are simply deliberately difﬁcult from time to time and pretend not to
believe that what is said to them is appropriate and well formed. We reject
5.2 Imperfect Performance
163
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 176 -->

the view that speakers are not committed to Principle 41. Here we follow a
well-established Gricean co-operative principle, which states the following
maxim that speakers assume in language production: ‘Make your contribu-
tion such as is required, at the stage at which it occurs, by the accepted
purpose or direction of the talk exchange in which you are engaged’ (Grice
1975: 45). Returning to our example, upon reading the utterance by Jim we
doubted the well-formedness of it. Beatrice, in situ, falsiﬁed the well-form-
edness of it through asserting its unintelligibility. The interlocutors here do
not seem to be some contrarian pair seeking to befuddle one another and
the linguists that are studying them. They are behaving, within the limits of
conversation, in exactly the way we would expect them to and where they do
not, we take the burden for that upon ourselves, as we shall show shortly.
We must modify Principle 42, however. At the moment, it accounts
only for rejection by an intended recipient. What of the producer?
Producers also sometimes explicitly falsify the beliefs of appropriacy
and well-formedness of their linguistic production in situ – repetition,
especially following a non-lexical ﬁller, is one way of achieving this:
(2) JUDITH 1:
she’s really good at dealing with the students like
she’s very erm she’s a very compassionate woman
(text Sp1m2f30, Spoken BNC2014)
or perhaps by signalling that an utterance is incomplete through an apology:
(3) ELLIOT:
you’ll get hiccups likeI do (.) sometimes I just randomly
get hiccups (.) I wonder why you get hiccups from just
TRAVIS 1:
≫this is something comp- sorry sorry sorry sorry
ELLIOT:
≫I wonder why you don’t get hiccups for breathing
too heavily cos if you hold your breath they go away
In example 3, from the Spoken BNC2014, ﬁle Sp2m2f96, Travis 1 effec-
tively aborts the utterance he has started to produce. These, inter alia, are
ways in which the speaker themselves may perform an in situ falsiﬁcation.
Accordingly, we can modify Principle 42 to:
Principle 420: In situ falsiﬁcations regarding beliefs of appropriacy and well-
formedness of a linguistic feature or structure based on Principle 40 may occur
where appropriacy and well-formedness is rejected by a person in the intended
audience of the linguistic production in question or the producer of the language.
Let us now look at possible counter arguments to Principle 420 and
consider the role of the hearer: could it not be that, from time to time, a
164
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 177 -->

capricious hearer may claim that an utterance is ill-formed when in fact
they believe it is not? Perhaps they are doing so in jest or for rhetorical
effect? Consider the following example from the Spoken BNC2014, ﬁle
Sp2m0f64:
(4) LEROY:
I don’t know I don’t we didn’t pursue that er that line
(. . .) I know what the connection is the her her her man
her partner erm the one who’s got the villa with the
swimming pool
LESTER:
≫he’s got the what?
LEROY:
he’s got a villa with a swimming pool
LESTER:
oh I thought you said Godzilla
LEROY:
well you are a little hard of hearing
LESTER:
yes
LEROY:
do you want do you want me to say that again? you are
a bit hard of hearing
LESTER:
thank you
Certainly, one reading of this interaction is that Leroy in repeating you are
a bit hard of hearing is being sarcastic, and elicits an equally sarcastic thank
you from Lester as a result. This interpretation is strengthened later in the
interaction when Leroy reveals why they think Lester is simply being
inattentive by saying you’re not paying attention are you? So, a hearer
may use a request for repetition to disguise inattentiveness. Observations
like this are helpful in reminding us that Principle 41 is grounded in the
rationality principle (see Section 4.5) for both speaker and hearer. We
assume, for the moment, that both speaker and hearer, as the agents
involved, act adequately, or appropriately. Hence, we also assume, in the
absence of an in situ falsiﬁcation, that the corpus provides a corroboration
of the appropriacy and well-formedness of linguistic structures in context.
Note also that the rejection in situ is done from the perspective of the
hearer who, as the intended audience, is crucial for contextual under-
standing of language. The speaker may have produced the utterance in a
way that is entirely felicitous with regard to Principle 41 from their
perspective. However, if their model of language varies from that of the
hearer in such a way that the utterance in question is appropriate and well
formed for the speaker but not the hearer, then the subsequent rejection
in situ is as much a comment on the hearer’s model as that of the speaker.
It is a comment on the mismatch between the two, not evidence of the
deﬁciency of either.
This example and discussion serve to make clear why Popper rejected
naturalism in the social sciences (see Section 4.2). It is possible that there
5.2 Imperfect Performance
165
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 178 -->

are falsiﬁcations to our claims made on the basis of observation of corpus
data: regarding corroborations, the speaker may not have been com-
mitted to the appropriacy and well-formedness of the utterance and the
hearer may not have thought the utterance appropriate and well formed,
but declined to say so. Similarly, the in situ falsiﬁcation may in turn be
falsiﬁed – it may be that the rejection of the appropriacy and well-
formedness of the utterance was an intentional act of deception, or
resulted in a mismatch of views of appropriacy and well-formedness
between the speaker and the hearer. In all cases we may make reasonable
assumptions of purposefulness of communication but we must be clear
that we are making assumptions and it is best that we are clear about what
they are.
By contrast, if we are studying the physical sciences, there is no ques-
tion of a hydrogen atom consciously pretending not to be a hydrogen
atom, or an oxygen atom consciously pretending not to be oxygen, simply
because they do not wish to bond to form water. If we take a substance
that we believe to be hydrogen and we introduce it to a substance we
believe to be oxygen, the result should be, in certain conditions based on
relevant and oft corroborated axioms of chemistry, water. If it is not, the
theory may be wrong or our belief in the nature of one or two of the
substances, or the conditions the experiment is being undertaken in, may
be wrong. What will not be a possible scientiﬁc explanation is a conscious
act on the behalf of the substances in question to deceive. This is in
contrast to our speaker and hearer – as we have seen in the previous
discussion, the hearer may deliberately deceive and produce a dishonest
in situ falsiﬁcation. It is this basic observation that we can use to explain
why Popper rejected naturalism and why we believe that the critical
rationalist position is the one we must adopt in the investigation of the
social sciences in general and in corpus linguistics in particular. We accept
and use Principles 40 and 420, entailing as they do Principle 41 and in situ
falsiﬁcation, but we may be wrong in our application of them – our claims
about language are taken within a frame of critical rationalism. Our
interpretation of intention in corpus data is an active, yet difﬁcult to
control, feature of our work.
Note also that exploring in situ falsiﬁcation is much easier with con-
versational spoken data – if we see in that context that the utterance has
led to a repair, then we have prima facie direct evidence that Principle 41
has been breached. On the other hand, if we see another reaction to what
appears to be an ill-formed utterance, perhaps laughter, or some form of
appreciation, then we may conclude that Principle 41 has been ﬂouted for
effect. If we see a linguist deciding, for no contextually supported reason,
166
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 179 -->

to reject an utterance as ill-formed, we should be alert to the possibility of
a violation of Principle 40. Principle 37 plays a key role here because if
linguists abide by it and state explicitly their simplifying assumptions, we
should have the grounds on which to judge their decision and possibly
challenge it. If, on the other hand, Principle 37 is not observed, linguists
risk slipping into the realm of dogmatism. In short, our main guide in
judging the appropriacy and well-formedness of linguistic features, when
examining the productions of a speaker or set of speakers, should come
from the context of production covered by Principle 40, not from any
outsider perspective.
Let us work through another example to demonstrate this. Consider the
following utterance spoken by Elliot in the Spoken BNC2014 (ﬁle Sp1m2f).
(5) ELLIOT:
mum you know those two black people we met? I can’t –
<unclear>
We might reasonably conclude looking at this utterance that both Elliot
and his intended hearer believe they have abided by Principle 41 and that
the <unclear> marker in the text is an artefact of the recording, not of the
performance of the speaker. However, the following utterance, from the
hearer (Clara) changes our view:
(6) CLARA:
say that agai- sorry I can’t hear anything you’re saying
In this case, we can reject the belief of Elliot that his utterance was well
formed because his interlocutor says that this is not the case. Note also
that Clara self-repairs at the beginning of her utterance, removing from
us the warrant to view ‘say that agai-’ as abiding by Principle 41. Only one
utterance in this sequence ‘sorry I can’t hear what your saying’ may
reasonably be considered by us as well formed.
Reasoning of this sort should not necessarily lead to us rejecting data
from our corpus as ill-formed, however. Falsiﬁcation is not rejection. We
should accept the possibility that, while falsiﬁed in situ, an utterance may
not be rejected. As shown, the falsiﬁcation may be insincere. Similarly,
the fault may lie not with the utterance, but with the hearer. If a hearer is
being inattentive and performs a repair when spoken to, what looks as
though it may be an in situ falsiﬁcation may not be anything of the sort.
Hence, we would argue that the distinction between falsiﬁcation and
rejection (Principle 170) should be ever present in our reasoning when
dealing with corpus data – our labelling of linguistic features and struc-
tures as inappropriate or ill-formed should be conservative because
falsiﬁcation may not entail rejection when more evidence emerges. So it
is more productive to think of corpus examples being partitioned into
5.2 Imperfect Performance
167
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 180 -->

those sentences or utterances that abide by Principle 40 and those which do
not. We may then consider those utterances that do not appear to abide by
Principle 40 as interesting potential falsiﬁers, which demand the search-
light be pointed towards them (Principle 33) because they may constitute
surprise (Principle 16″) that we should engage with, not discard.
So Principle 40 permits much. For example, the principle may be
ﬂouted for effect; in that case, the breach of the principle is deliberate
and has a communicative function. So, we would only permit in situ
falsiﬁcation to stand under Principle 420 where it is clearly linked to a
breach of Principle 40. Consider the following from the Spoken BNC2014
(ﬁle Sp2m0f32):
(7) FRANKLIN:
just off to work for the merchant erm navy
ANONnameM
MARCUS:
whyare youwearinga turtleneck?A blackturtleneck
FRANKLIN:
it’s just er its just the outﬁt ANONnameM
MARCUS:
what is that accent?
In this exchange, Franklin is talking to Marcus (who is also the anonymised
male indicated by ANONnameM). It becomes apparent in the transcrip-
tion that when Marcus asks about the accent, he does so because Franklin
is imitating a Liverpudlian accent – in the audio recording this is apparent
earlier. This leads to a challenge from Marcus. Yet the challenge is not to a
breach, so it does not lead to an in situ falsiﬁcation under Principle 420. It is
a challenge by the hearer to a ﬂouting of Principle 40. Franklin is speaking
in a way that would not be normal for their speech community, but this is a
deliberate act with a communicative purpose. Note also that, where they
do not lead to a direct repair, we do not consider ﬁllers and pauses to
breach Principle 41. Conversation ﬂows smoothly past ﬁllers and pauses in
utterances like ‘just off to work in the merchant erm navy’.
The safest warrant for the separation of sentences in a corpus into
those that we may assume represent the acceptable usage for the indivi-
duals represented in the data and those that appear not to is corpus
internal evidence. This is easy to operationalise in dialogic corpora. Yet
what of monologic corpora, both written and spoken? The same may be
true in these corpora also, depending upon their nature. So, for example,
in a corpus of children’s handwriting such as that used by Smith et al.
(1998), crossings out and other such repairs give similar evidence of
Principle 41 possibly being breached. However, for most written corpora
in use today, Principle 41 appears to hardly be an issue – corpora such as
the Written BNC2014 (Brezina et al. 2021) and others compiled from
professionally edited published texts comparatively rarely provide cause
168
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 181 -->

for concern with regard to well-formedness and appropriacy. Where they
do, however, the identiﬁcation of sentences that appear to breach
Principle 41 should be conservative: unless there is overwhelming evi-
dence that the intended audience rejected the appropriacy and well-
formedness of some corpus evidence, the assumption should be that the
data conforms to Principle 40.
More often when critically exploring the acceptability of data in written
corpora, but also of relevance to spoken corpora too, is what we will call a
normative conjecture – this is Principle 320 in action. If the data can be shown
to be non-normative, when compared with data to which a comparison may
reasonably be made, we retain transparent intersubjectivity in making a
decision that these data deviate from a norm. On the basis of this conjecture,
we may identify the data as non-normative, though we may not necessarily
reject it. This is distinct from where the intended recipient of a text rejects
the well-formedness of all or part of it – that gives us a warrant to conclude
that an utterance or sentence is not appropriate or well formed, and conse-
quently we can argue for the rejection of the examples in question through
in situ falsiﬁcation. The normative conjecture is based not on the views of the
audience of an utterance or sentence, it is based on an exploration under-
taken by a researcher that relies on the hypothesis that the normative is key
in the exploration of language – a frequent simplifying assumption in
linguistics. The normative conjecture gives us the warrant to take the
individual perspective and situate it relative to the group perspective. The
use of language may vary according to its propensity. If we presume the
result is a permissible range of variations conditioned by that propensity,
then, for any given utterance, we may take it and try to place it within the
bounds of variation that we can observe in our data. Various procedures
may be applied to the data – the frequency data derived from the corpus
may be used to group and cluster the data in a range of ways, allowing us
to explore the range of variation in language through different abstrac-
tions we derive from that frequency data (see Brezina 2018 for a wide
range of measures that produce such statistical abstractions for a wide
range of variations). All such procedures fall under Principle 320. For
example, we can use corpus data to investigate the conditions (propen-
sities) under which the following utterance is acceptable:
(8) CHARLOTTE 1:
. . . cos I done it for nana and grandad when
they was both alive so I am going back a while
(Spoken BNC2014, text Sp0m2f116)
These conditions may include informal speech, a particular regional
variety of English etc. Of course, our capacity to do this is limited – or
5.2 Imperfect Performance
169
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 182 -->

enabled – by the extent to which any given corpus may allow us to model
the forces composing propensity. This leads to the types of approximations
to linguistic reality, quasi-reality, as enabled by Principles 14″ and 37.
Nonetheless, the normative conjecture is particularly important as it
allows us to critically explore many written corpora in which in situ
falsiﬁcations are rare. Such falsiﬁcations may occur at the level, for
example, of personal communications, but the grounds for making the
falsiﬁcations become weaker in the case of texts with a wide audience –
the wider the audience, the greater the possibility that the text has to
accommodate linguistic practices that vary in the target audience. Hence,
proceeding through the normative conjecture in such cases is important
as any one member of the audience may represent a small part, not the
totality, of the intended audience, and any falsiﬁcation that they may
produce may be unique to them within that audience. Similarly, where
access to the intended audience is not possible or is impractical, working
with the normative conjecture may be the only way in which data may be
partitioned into what seems to gravitate towards a norm and what does
not. Consider the following two examples:
(9) By Letters from Colonel Haughton I am informed, that the
Lancashire horse are drawn near to Liverpool, and have blocked
up the town by land, as our Ships have done by Sea; and that they
have lately taken a party of horse out of the town, whence there
come divers, both horse and foot-men, to submit themselves; and it is
hoped before long, the town may be gained, and the sooner, were it
not (as is conceived) that the English and Townsmen were over-
ruled by the Irish soldiers
(10)
Example 9 is from Brereton (1644) and represents Early Modern
English. We are left with the assertion of its appropriacy and well-
formedness, but there are no remaining members of the speech commu-
nity it was intended for, removing the possibility of in situ falsiﬁcation.
We do have other texts of the period so, using the normative conjecture,
one could explore how this one example of writing from the time may be
placed relative to other contemporary documents. While the individual
linguist deeply interested in early modern English may have a sense of
what is normative based on their previous research, their knowledge is
that of the linguist, not the intended audience. Their experience of what is
normative has grown but, no matter how much they read texts from the
170
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 183 -->

period, they are never the intended audience of that text and they are
not a speaker of that variety of English. Essentially, Brereton had no
knowledge of the speech communities of present-day English. So, he
could not have made a meaningful appropriacy and well-formedness
assertion for a future speech community – the assertion was for the
speech communities he knew and intended to communicate with. As
such, our analysis should reﬂect that linguistic reality, effectively remov-
ing, in cases like this, the possibility of in situ falsiﬁcation for all time unless
we ﬁnd contemporary materials from the speech community in question
that perform that falsiﬁcation, leaving us to rely on Principle 320 and a
normative epistemology based on extant evidence of the speech
community.
The situation in example 10, taken from the EMILLE corpus (Baker
et al. 2004) is very different. It is a sentence of Assamese from the late
twentieth century. Neither of the authors of this book are speakers of
Assamese and we assume that most of the readers of it are not either.
Hence, the sentence remains an enigma to us until we call upon resources
to decode it. Irrespective of whether we decode it or not, our default
assumption is that the writer intends it to be appropriate within the
context of their speech community – we can still apply Principles 40 and
41. Yet unlike the case of Brereton, there are speakers of Assamese on
the planet who are members of the speech community that produced this
sentence. Hence, in principle, while the sentence may be a mystery to us,
there are intended recipients of the sentence who might be able to claim
an in situ falsiﬁcation and allow us to invoke Principle 420.
Alternatively, as we do not understand Assamese, we might appeal to
the normative under Principle 320 – examining the sentence in the light of
further corpus evidence, for example, to determine how typical or other-
wise of Assamese this sentence is and, thereby, to critically examine its
appropriateness. In an Assamese corpus, we can also investigate frequen-
cies of individual lexical items (see Section 5.5 for a discussion of word-
lists) that occur in the corpus, their collocations and typical contexts of
occurrence and so on. Note that in doing so we would need to make
further conjectures. For example, we would need to develop an axiom
that would deﬁne what constitutes a word in Assamese. With further
exploration and growing understanding of Assamese, we may need to
develop further axioms, for example, related to word formation in the
language. Some of this would relate, doubtlessly, to hypotheses about
how this language compares with ones we know; for example, we can see
white space between sequences of characters in the example and, as white
space in written English is an (admittedly imperfect) indicator of word
5.2 Imperfect Performance
171
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 184 -->

boundaries, we may hypothesise that this is the case also in Assamese.
Similarly, we might assume there is a rough letter/phoneme correspon-
dence between what we see on the page and its spoken realisation. Yet
other inferences would be based on the normative conjecture – by our
exploration of the range of variation that we see in the Assamese data
and how the example here relates to that variation. Rejecting the sen-
tence would be difﬁcult on such grounds – we might rightly identify it as
atypical, but atypical does not necessarily mean unacceptable. Similarly,
typical does not mean acceptable. Going back to our reasoning relating to
letter/phoneme correspondence in which the languages the authors can
write in is typical, this is not the case in Assamese, which has a writing
system that is better described as a neosyllabary (or more accurately an
abugida writing system) in which, for example, consonant/vowel
sequences are written as a single unit and the consonant may not need
a vowel to be marked on it as an inherent vowel may be assumed. What is
typical in English orthography is not typical in Assamese and vice versa.
This guidance is, at one and the same time, both radically descriptive
and aligns well with the individual focus and critical rationalist approach
of social science research as proposed by Popper. It also allows us to
reﬂect back upon older formulations within corpus linguistics that tried to
deal with this issue such as ‘total accountability’. We have a better
philosophical basis on which to understand what we are assuming when
we use corpus data. We are clear about: 1) the placement of the burden of
belief that what we are dealing with is appropriate and well formed; 2) the
grounds on which we may intervene to sort our corpus into different sets
based on corpus internal evidence; and 3) the interaction between 1 and
2. We also have a guide for those wishing to give a narrower deﬁnition of
appropriacy and well-formedness. Our formulation of it here is deliber-
ately broad and oriented towards purposeful communication. If, how-
ever, we wanted only to focus on grammar, not being interested in issues
such as accent as highlighted in example 7, then we would argue the same
overall framework for dealing with the data would still apply but be
narrower in scope.
The existence of grammaticality would require a narrower application
of the rationality principle. Following that we could proceed in a spirit of
critical rationalism on the basis that each utterance is based on an implicit
statement of belief in the grammaticality of the utterance by the speaker.
We may still ﬁnd corpus internal evidence to negate that belief and we
may still ﬁnd both conscious and unintentional grounds for that. Yet
throughout our focus would fall on the individuals in the corpus and
our acceptance would and should be that what they are doing when
172
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 185 -->

they are speaking, or writing, is committing themselves, from the point of
view of themselves and their speech community, to the well-formedness
of that utterance from a range of perspectives. Through our treatment of
the data in that way, we can then, through a normative view invoked
