through Principle 320, explore issues such as the bounds of variability
within the data and their relationship to propensity.
However, a corpus analysis that takes consideration only of the norma-
tive, and not variation from it, runs the risk of shifting analysis entirely away
from the individual and, in doing so, runs the risk of ‘missing the trees for the
forest’ (a point well made with regard to variation between texts by Egbert
and Schnur 2018: 159–160). When taking this descriptive and individual-
centred approach to our data, we move away from cataloguing utterances
towards the exploration of language through utterances that individuals, in
the communicative contexts in which language is used at a point in time,
employ language to effect in a way that we assume 1) commits them to the
appropriacy and well-formedness of their utterances (Principle 40) and
2) will lead them to mark where Principle 40 does not apply (Principle 420).
5.3 The Language Learner
Let us now move to consider the language learner, acquiring a second or
additional language (L2) – how might the assumption of purposeful
communication apply to their utterances? The brief answer is – in just
the same way as it does to those acquiring a ﬁrst language (L1), Principle
40 still applies. We presume that a person speaking in an L2 is asserting
the belief, as they produce each utterance, that it is well formed in the
same way that an L1 speaker would. That brings with it all of the beneﬁts
of the focus on the individual and a principled understanding of what our
data represents and how it might be used. We will ﬁnd repairs in the
dialogic data as surely as we will in so-called L1 speaker data, as in the
following example from the Trinity Lancaster Corpus (TLC; Gablasova
et al. 2019) in which an Italian student (learner) is in conversation with an
L1 British English-speaking examiner (ﬁle IT_2_31):
(11) L:
<laugh> but erm I don’t know maybe erm <short pause> have
you erm have you considered trying to erm to let them know
that happiness er that <laugh> happiness
E:
<laugh>
L:
I’m sorry
E:
<laugh>
L:
<laugh> I’m nervous
5.3 The Language Learner
173
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 186 -->

Here, punctuated with laughter (as marked by <laugh>) the student (L)
produces a series of false starts, apologises for them and provides an
explanation to the examiner (E) for them.
The issue arises, for both learner and non-learner corpora, of what
to do when a point of comparison is called for. Where is the norma-
tive data (in line with Principle 320) that we may use in the compar-
ison? In this case, we consider a target norm (e.g. British English) that
we assume the individual is aiming for and then assess the perfor-
mance of the individual against that. Just as we consider what people
should believe in normative epistemology (see Section 3.3), in a
normative analysis of this sort we consider what people should say.
In such a context the setting of the norm is a social, rather than a
scientiﬁc, act that brings with it many presuppositions, some clearly
warranted, others less so.
Let us consider second language learning as a case in point. When a
learner enters an exam and is being assessed, as is happening in the
preceding example, a speciﬁc normative conjecture arising from
Principle 320 seems to have a fairly strong warrant. The student is taking
an exam with a published curriculum and that curriculum, at least
notionally, is based upon a normative form of English. The exam
board makes it clear that the student will be assessed against this
normative form of English. In the preceding example, it is broadly
speaking British English. As this is a known feature of the test, we
may assume, applying a principle of rationality, that the student has
studied English using materials in line with a normative conjecture that
will orient their performance towards a normative form of British
English, which may be located within a certain range of permissibility.
However, we do not assume that they are able to speak British English
with the full range of variation that we may observe in an L1 speaker, for
example (though note that the notion of an L1 speaker itself is an
idealisation warranted by Principle 37). Nonetheless, we compare
their language with that of the speech community that we have cause
to believe they wish to join.83 Similarly, if a person in a corpus states
they are an L2 speaker of English, but they speak English, then we may,
in line with Principle 320, compare their English to a normative form of
83 Note we may assume that they have a target variety of British English, yet in reality,
unknown to us, the target community they wish to join is some other community – for
example, their peers who code switch to English in occasion. Hence, we are in the realms
of presupposition again here. For an interesting exploration of learner motivations and
the role of near-peer models, see Curry (2019).
174
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 187 -->

English from the speech community they are seeking to communicate
with.
The same reasoning applies to ﬁrst language acquisition also. The
reasoning could apply in both ﬁrst and second language acquisition
many times, in principle, as the individuals may have multiple languages
and speech communities that they are targeting and which their language
use is shaped by. Yet throughout we would be applying Principle 41, and
then applying a normative view to that data using Principle 320 as a
warrant. In doing so, we often form a judgement not of absolutes, but
of relativities: how close or far the usage is to a given norm. Thus we keep
a focus on the individual while considering some wider normative
account of language.
This leads to two views of the data, the actual and the normative. This is
clear in the following example from the TLC by a Portuguese learner of
British English (corpus ﬁle 2_7_POR_1):84
(12) L:
are you getting better? does it hurst <error target="hurt"/>
any more?
Here, we believe the speaker commits, in line with Principle 41, to the
well-formedness of both ‘are you getting better’ and ‘does it hurst any
more’. Through Principle 320, we assume that the speaker, in the second
utterance meant hurt, not hurst, as we have no evidence from British
English that hurst can be used in such a context. In the corpus, XML is
used to encode the inference arising from this normative conjecture – the
XML element <error> is used and the normative form hurt is given as an
assumed target form. The use of XML encoding clearly separates the use
of language as recorded in the corpus from the normative imposition
(a correction of the utterance). This is all achieved by what we may call a
normative conjecture. So, the division of the corpus data into different
sets is now more varied (see Figure 5.1). Through an exploration of
corpus internal evidence we have seen that we may assign the utterances
(U) to one of three sets: the set of utterances that appear to abide by
Principle 41 (we shall call this A), those that appear to breach that
principle (B) and those that appear to ﬂout it (F). By the application of
a normative conjecture, we may also form a subset of utterances (E)
within set A that represents sentences that, on the basis of corpus internal
evidence, may be assumed to be well formed but which, when Principle
320 is invoked, may have their appropriacy and well-formedness doubted.
84 Note that for purposes of clarity we have chosen an example here where the deviation
from the norm is almost certainly absolute.
5.3 The Language Learner
175
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 188 -->

The difference between A and E is crucial: A represents a set of utter-
ances that appear to be appropriate and well formed in use, while E
identiﬁes a set of those utterances that a normative view of language
would problematise.85
Note throughout this discussion, the shadow of Popper is cast. First,
many axioms necessary for the discussion are taken as given, they are
not explicit. So, for example, we have assumed an axiom of transcrip-
tion and some normative axiom must be in play as there must be
grounds on which the use of Principle 320 is warranted. Both are clearly
important – the transcription represents an interpretation in itself. The
transcriber is asserting that what is written is an accurate representa-
tion in orthography of the speech produced. Yet that is also subject to
the rationality principle: we know the transcribed form of spoken
language and speech itself are, in fact, distinct. Similarly, the error
coding must be based on some other set of observations that allow a
normative conjecture to be used to reject the evidence in favour of the
appropriacy
and
well-formedness
of
does
it
hurst
anymore.
Consequently, falsiﬁcation can apply to the observations made and
our critical realist approach allows us to proceed. Yet if the axiom of
transcription is falsiﬁed, if it is shown that the transcription is not fair
and accurate, then, depending on the extent of the falsiﬁcation of the
axiom, the ﬁndings based on the data may also fail. Similarly, we may
U (all utterance in the corpus) 
A (appropriate)
B (breaching 
principle)
F (flouting 
principle) 
E (marked as 
errors)
Figure 5.1 Utterances in learner corpus data and a normativity view
85 Note throughout that we will assume the ﬁdelity of the transcriptions in all examples
though these, of course, represent another layer of interpretation between the original
speech situation and the analyst.
176
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 189 -->

dispute the axiom underlying a normative conjecture. For example, if
we discover that the axiom supporting a normative conjecture assumes
that speakers conform to a certain prescriptivist notion of grammar
(e.g. no split inﬁnitive is allowed) and it is not permissive of individual
variation, we may want to reject that axiom and to reject normative
conjectures based upon it.
So how do we proceed with corpus collection and analysis with the
knowledge of these complexities and caveats? The response is twofold
and stems directly from Popper. First, we work on the basis of corrobora-
tion and falsiﬁcation in the creation of corpus data just as much as we do
in the formation of conjectures that we build on the basis of it, that is, our
analysis of the data. Transcription schemes gain corroborative support –
as they are used and the quality of transcription is tested, the scheme
itself gains corroboration. In brief, Principles 20 and 210, working in
concert, allow us to work with such data. We must be clear, of course,
that we are considering the empirical content and the falsiﬁcation of the
schemes when doing this.
Consider a transcription axiom that has three salient features that we
ﬁnd notable: 1) all ﬁllers (such as er, hm, ah and erm) are simply marked
by a code <ﬁller>; 2) all compounds (e.g. long-term) are transcribed with
a hyphen; and 3) transcribers are encouraged to guess what word is being
used when a section of recording is unclear. The ﬁrst feature means that,
relative to the original, the empirical content of the transcription is
reduced – information is thrown away about the phonological quality of
the ﬁller because a simple mark <ﬁller> was used. If our hypothesis relies
on that empirical content, that is, the type of ﬁller used, then it cannot be
pursued with this corpus because of simplifying assumptions made under
Principle 37. The second point is a matter of taste and consistency of
application – the transcribed forms long term and long-term are empiri-
cally equivalent. So Principles 280 and 290 cannot be used to prefer one
practice over another. Any preference would be a dogmatic rather than
scientiﬁc point. Finally, however, guessing unclear words is an issue that
impacts upon empirical content in a potentially subjective and unpredict-
able way – a vague warrant is given to transcribers to imagine what they
are hearing and provide their best guess. Given that this instruction does
not encourage transcribers even to note where they are exercising this
judgement, we would reject this element of the transcription scheme as
being indeterminate and metaphysical. A transcription scheme needs to
adhere to the theoretical requirements for accuracy, consistency and
transparency, while maintaining the practicality of the implementation
of these principles. For an example of such a transcription scheme, see
5.3 The Language Learner
177
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 190 -->

the Lancaster Spoken Language Transcription Guidelines (Gablasova
et al. 2019).
The second reaction to the question of how to proceed with corpus
collection and analysis is more pragmatic: the searchlight theory.
Sometimes the searchlight will fall on certain axioms, sometimes it will
fall on others. At any point we may, as theory demands it, switch that
searchlight. Yet at any given point we will assess parts of the system,
leaving other parts as given. This is another important dimension of
falsiﬁcation: facts, expressions of pure certainty, are unattainable in
part because our research focus is narrow in any single investigation.
That investigation rests on axioms, in line with Principles 25 and 33,
which may, when the searchlight falls on them, be corroborated, crumble
away, partly collapse or alter to varying degrees, pushing forward the
cycle of incremental reﬁnement (Principle 210).
5.4 Familiarity with Context
Knowledge of context is crucial for the interpretation of linguistic produc-
tion. So far, we have discussed the role of the speaker and the hearer as the
‘insiders’ in a given linguistic interaction within a particular speech commu-
nity. Should our view of corpus data be modiﬁed depending upon the extent
to which it is apparent that the speakers or writers in a corpus are familiar
with the context in which they are producing language? The question entails
a general exploration of some of the questions already discussed in relation
to language learning. Considering speech once again, one might reasonably
hold the provisional belief of the commitment of the speaker to Principle 41
unless there is express evidence from within the interaction to allow us to
falsify that assumption. We can ﬁnd examples in corpora where people
clearly do note overt communicative failures in unfamiliar contexts, as in
this example from ﬁle Sp1m2f84 in the Spoken BNC2014:
(13) BEVERLEY 1:
≫they just didn’t get and also some of the stuff
that I said I’ve stopped
MIKE:
yeah
BEVERLEY 1:
do- I had to stop but I would say things to
people and they would take offence
MIKE:
yeah
ETHEL 1:
like what?
BEVERLEY 1:
I can’t remember now because I stopped doing
it (.) but
MIKE:
mm
178
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 191 -->

BEVERLEY 1:
but quite erm (.) you know you’d say things to
people up north and they’d just laugh
MIKE:
that’s right
BEVERLEY 1:
say things to people down here and they just
gape at you
MIKE:
yeah that’s right
Where such overt marking is not present in the text, the normative, via
Principle 320, may be used as an analytical step to try to determine the
hypothesised location and nature of data where the commitment to
Principle 41 might be rejected.
Taken together, the purposefulness of utterances, Principle 41 and
Principle 320 form the epistemological basis of much in corpus linguistics.
All have the potential to work at different levels simultaneously. The
research question to be asked of a corpus determines the nature and number
of the levels where we assume purposeful communication. For example, if
one is interested in lexis, then one may take each word in the corpus to be
purposefully chosen and Principle 40 applies at that level. Consider an
example such as this one from ﬁle Sp0m4f9 in the Spoken BNC2014:
(14) BARBARA 2:
I know roughly dates but I can’t s- say for sure
when I came off it
We provisionally accept as true, based on the assumption of purposeful
communication, the appropriacy and well-formedness of each word in the
utterance with the exception of s-, where we have an explicit repair by the
speaker. Following from that, a normative approach might lead us to
explore whether we should reject the well-formedness of the utterance
from the grammatical point of view using Principle 320, because we believe,
on the basis of normative evidence, that dates should be preceded by a
deﬁnite article (the dates) in this context. There may be various ways, of
course, to apply such a normative intervention – in terms of the speaker’s
own usage, their speech community’s norms or some other normative
position that the analyst argues for. Note, of course, that when imposing
linguistic categories on the data, such as a deﬁnite article, we engage in
further levels of linguistic analysis where we also assume purposefulness.
All of this needs to be shown explicitly (Principle 3) so that our assump-
tions are intersubjectively testable (Principle 6″).
Importantly, we would also claim that the hearer of an utterance,
reader of a text, viewer of a sign and so on are also testing these assump-
tions and are deciding on the contextual appropriateness of the language
that they attend to. This in turn establishes a loop, with hearers, for
5.4 Familiarity with Context
179
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 192 -->

example, assessing the appropriateness of what a speaker says and then
accepting or rejecting that or, potentially, deciding to accept the appro-
priateness of a linguistic production and revising their own beliefs. This
opens the gateway for the corpus linguist to explore, in context, how
language learning, language change, the formation of representations of
groups and a whole host of other topics in applied linguistics can be
explored through corpus data. This leads to a further principle:
Principle 43: Principle 40 leads to reﬂexive behaviour on the behalf of a recipient
of a linguistic production (hearer) – they choose to accept a linguistic produc-
tion, reject it or, potentially, to modify their own view of what is appropriate
based on the communicative event in question. Accordingly, the corpus
linguist may come into quasi-contact with such reﬂexive events also.
The fact that the speaker may, or may not, be familiar with the context in
which they are producing utterances does not impact upon these basic
processes whereby the research question determines what we ask of the
data, and the data in turn is assessed according to Principles 320 and 40.
5.5 Quantifying and Generalising: The Case of Wordlists
Finally, let us shift from the individual contexts in which sentences and
utterances occur and think of generalisations in corpus linguistics.
Consider work on wordlists – frequency lists of words derived from
corpus data at the most general level. How do wordlists ﬁt into our
assumption of purposeful communication and the appropriateness of
linguistic expressions that can be falsiﬁed in situ in individual contexts?
Wordlists are interesting for us because one thing that they can do is form
a connection between L1 norms (a normative conjecture) and the lan-
guage learner as discussed in Section 5.3. The history of the production of
wordlists for language teaching is long (see Nation 2016: 10–13 for an
overview of wordlist development since the early 1900s). The production
of such wordlists based on the manual analyses of paper-based corpora
precede the construction of the ﬁrst electronic corpora. Thorndike and
Lorge (1944) based their Teacher’s Word Book on an analysis of over 4.5
million words. West (1953), with funding from sources as diverse as the
Carnegie and Rockefeller foundations, oversaw a manual analysis of an
initial 2.5 million word corpus in order to produce his General Service
List of English words, designed to help learners of English to acquire
vocabulary more efﬁciently. Such work was not limited to English –
180
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 193 -->

Buchanan (1929) produced a wordlist for Spanish based on an analysis of
1.2 million words of written materials covering a range of genres, while
Henmon (1924) produced a French wordlist, again based on a range of
written genres, using 400,000 words of corpus data.
In terms of the importance of linguistic contexts in which words
occur, by the 1920s, linguists were aware that genre and register may
impact upon frequency of word usage, hence linguists such as
Buchanan, Henmon and West collected and organised their data to
try to begin to control for this. Yet Fries (1952) analysed over 250,00
words of spoken American English in order to produce insights into the
grammar of spoken American English. In doing so he realised that the
dichotomy between speech and writing was important and he accord-
ingly concluded that ‘most frequency lists are based on counts of written
English. We cannot assume that the vocabulary of written English is the
same as the vocabulary of spoken English’ (Fries 1958: ii). Fries also
developed a sense that linguists engaged with quasi-reality and the
propensity was at play, as he noted that the way in which data was
gathered may compromise the ‘naturalness’ of that data, noting that
conversations elicited in laboratory settings ‘inevitably show many
important differences from . . . live conversations’ (Fries 1964: 245).
A further insight into the role of the context came when Fries noted
that in classroom teaching the ﬁrst language of the learner may be just as
important an object of study as the language being taught. The complex-
ities of the relation of function and form were also things that Fries
showed a keen appreciation of – he noted that the distribution of the
function of words may be very distinct from the distribution of the word
form itself and that function words are particularly rich sources of
variation (Fries and Traver 1940: 89).
So, early researchers developed wordlists and in doing so ‘showed an
increasing awareness of the effect of the nature and size of the corpus on
the words occurring in a count’ (Nation 2016: 10). By the time that Zipf’s
(1935) law was developed, linguists had already started to take genre into
account when developing wordlists. Zipf’s law is a powerful generalisa-
tion observable in wordlists: the frequency of a word and its rank are in an
inverse relationship in such a way that frequency times rank is, broadly, a
constant (Brezina 2018: 44). Yet it was Fries who later went on to show
that mode of production and ‘naturalness’ might also be issues that need
to be considered (see Brezina & Gablasova (2017) for key principles of
wordlist creation). In this brief, thumbnail sketch of the development of
wordlists, we see an initial insight – the wordlist – problematised in cycles
and the construct reﬁned. At the same time, we also have an underlying
5.5 Quantifying and Generalising: The Case of Wordlists
181
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 194 -->

observation made – Zipf’s law – that can be tested and retested as each
new insight into wordlist creation is made. The corpus has a powerfully
enabling role in this history of linguistic enquiry, a process of enquiry that
continues and in which the corpus is still playing a driving role.
In this context, how can we consider corpus evidence to be acceptable?
Corpus data are acceptable for two main reasons. First, each utterance, or
written sentence can be viewed as an act of purposeful communication, in
epistemological terms. Second, corpus data are a key source of intersub-
jective assessment of language; indeed, it is the very intersubjective experi-
ence of communication from which, we would argue, language is acquired
by children and upon which linguistic competence is judged and based.
5.6 Conclusion
In this chapter, we have discussed the functionalist foundations of corpus
linguistics and showed how the key functionalist principle of purposeful-
ness of communication informs our understanding of what is appropriate
and well formed in corpus data. We started with the claim that Popper is
silent on matters of language. Yet, Popper’s framework proves particu-
larly useful when considering language, as the connection between
Popper and axiomatic functionalism shows. Does the discussion so far
change or inform our view of what a corpus analysis achieves? Our
answer to that is ‘yes’. The attention we have paid to individual contexts,
speech situations and speech communities shows the power of in situ
falsiﬁcations; we have also considered the role of the normative in our
interpretation of corpus data. Finally, we need to be mindful of how
corpus data interacts with linguistic theory.
A cursory inspection of the range of linguistic theory appealing to corpus
data or a consideration of competing annotation schemes that seek to
analyse corpus data shows the underdetermination of corpus data very
clearly. Hence, corpora might be used to corroborate work undertaken in
construction grammar (Yoon and Gries 2016), develop lexical priming
theory (Hoey 2005) or build usage-based accounts of language acquisition
(Ellis et al. 2013). At different levels of linguistic description different
annotation schemes, which themselves represent ontological efforts, may
be applied to corpus data – annotation schemes are not ‘God’s truth’
(Leech 1997: 6) and from the range of possibilities the corpus itself is
unlikely to act as an adjudicator that determines the one and only theory
or ontological scheme to be applied precisely because of Principle 15.
Principles 3 and 20 together open up permissible choice within the onto-
logical schemes applied to corpus data: ‘for example: future in his future
182
Everyday Linguistics: Form and Function
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 195 -->

bride. Is it a noun or an adjective?’ (Leech 1997: 3). The answer can be
argued either way and the word future can accommodate either annota-
tion. While we may wish to create a clear set of rules to record our decision
on this matter, and to ensure that the rules are applied consistently, this
does not make them unchallengeable. Some analysts would argue noun,
some adjective, neither are necessarily wrong. Should we choose one it
would be on the basis of convention (Principle 20) not the God’s truth that
some linguists may seek (see Section 4.2 for a discussion). While we might
reach for some degree of consensus in the decision making that would be a
social rather than a scientiﬁc resolution of the problem, and it may prove
illusive as agreeing ‘“consensual categories” is not itself a matter on which
it is easy to gain consensus’ (Leech 1997: 7). Similarly, as noted already, we
know that fuzziness in category membership and underdetermination will
both promote this.
In our project to lay the foundations of corpus linguistics, we have
reached a stage where the pillars are in the ground and we have drawn
clear plans for our building: Principles 1–43. We have discussed these and
provided individual examples to illustrate the principles with corpus data.
These illustrations were, nonetheless, necessarily sketchy like a legend or
key in the margin of the plans for our building. What we intend to do in
the following two chapters is to bring these principles together and
demonstrate with a case study how our building can actually be built.
The case study is focused upon one the most important aspects of the
empirical analysis of data: replication. It shows, within the context of
fallibilism, the principled openness of our linguistic descriptions to falsi-
ﬁcation and the ways in which falsiﬁcation can be maximised. So, let us
embark on this last stretch of our journey together to put the foundation
of our building to a ﬁnal ‘stress test’.
5.6 Conclusion
183
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.007 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 196 -->

CHAPT ER SI X
Repetition and Replication: Laying the Groundwork
for an Empirical Study
In this chapter, we look at two cornerstones of an empirical study:
repetition and replication. In the framework that we have presented so
far, these represent the practical implications of the principles laid out in
Chapters 1–5. The logic of the thought process is as follows: in corpus
linguistics, we explore observed language in use – that represents for us a
linguistic reality on which scientia realis operates (Principle 6″).
However, we are aware that our contact with reality via our corpus is
quasi-contact and thus we approach our study in a spirit of critical realism
(Principle 14). We use this quasi-contact to investigate and explain lan-
guage usage (Principle 2) in the context in which it was produced, where
the language produced may be assumed to be appropriate and well
formed (Principle 40). In examining that contextualised usage, we are
mindful that the propensities at work, and hence the data is a necessary
amalgam of social and physical interactions (Principle 350). We also con-
sciously acknowledge that language is not necessarily homogeneous, but is,
rather, reﬂective of the institutions and traditions of language (Principle 36).
Hence, by looking at such contextual language use, we also seek to account
for, in so far as our data allow us to, the propensity of language in use in
context (Principle 39) and to undertake a study that promotes the produc-
tion of intersubjectively testable results (Principle 7).
In doing so, however, we need to be open and honest about the limita-
tions of our data and methods (Principle 22) and mount no dogmatic
defence in lieu of accepting those limitations (Principle 190). An important
limitation we acknowledge in advance of data selection is that our data,
bounded by space and time, will allow observations within that space and
time (Principle 50). A further limitation that we acknowledge is that the
problem of induction applies to the analysis we undertake (Principle 100).
Our overall approach will be quasi-inductive, hence by an appeal to con-
vention and, potentially, metaphysics, we may extend our observations
beyond the bounds imposed by Principle 50 (Principle 4).
It is important to note that our approach to our study is not credulous
and uninformed – rather than assuming that any observation is of value
(Principle 13) our study will be motivated by a searchlight approach to
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 197 -->

explore hypotheses already produced (Principle 33) that allow us to
engage with our data in a structured and controlled way (Principle 1).
This represents an important interaction between hypothesis and evi-
dence that focuses, yet also narrows, our observation of the data
(Principle 8). We accept as an important epistemological principle that
our study does not imply that other hypotheses and theories could not use
the data to support alternative perspectives (Principle 15).
The searchlight approach allows us to proceed not by freely generating
numerous novel hypotheses of our own, but by ﬁrst critically exploring
hypotheses put forward by others (Principle 9). Our approach to those
hypotheses will be guided by an attempt to falsify them (Principle 11)
either by our own large-scale analyses or by discovering data that provide
an in situ falsiﬁcation, that is, counter-examples (Principle 41).
Falsiﬁcations then provide an opportunity to form hypotheses of our
own and subject them to further falsiﬁcation attempts. We work through-
out within the conventions of corpus linguistics (Principle 20). However,
while our study will be based on previous corpus-based work, and thus
may be presumed not to engage with metaphysical hypotheses initially,
should we engage with, or produce, hypotheses rooted in the metaphysi-
cal, we should take any possible steps to ensure that the metaphysical
may become subject to principles of corpus linguistics (Principle 12). The
steps we take to achieve this may include combining methods (Principle
280). All this requires an intersubjectively observable approach (Principle
7) that rests on repetition and replication. We deal with these concepts in
detail in this and the following chapter.
6.1 Approaching a Study: Repetition
Repeatability is the ability to take the same data and the same tools and
following a method described in a previous study produce the same
results. In effect, repeatability is the conﬁrmation that all the empirical
processes in the initial study were carried out ﬂawlessly and the results
can be considered consistent and transparent. Although this might sound
like a trivial goal hardly worthy of our attention, this is clearly not the
case, especially in corpus linguistics. In corpus linguistics, repeatability
can be very difﬁcult to achieve for at least three reasons: 1) availability of
data; 2) availability of software; and 3) clarity of reporting.
Unavailability of the original data is a major problem for repeatability.
If we cannot access the original data set, and can in no other way reliably
reconstruct it, then repeatability is, in effect, impossible to achieve.
Corpus linguists were aware of this problem long before other areas
6.1 Approaching a Study: Repetition
185
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 198 -->

that are now promoting Open Science, a movement which came to the fore
in the early twenty-ﬁrst century, which aims ‘to improve transparency of
research ﬁndings by making the entire process accessible to all’ (Marsden
2019). This includes sharing notes about initial planning decisions, the
research design, methods, data and ﬁndings. From its inception, corpus
linguistics pioneered the Open Science path, with tools and data being
shared between centres with repositories such as the ICAME archive
being created in 1977, facilitating the exchange of materials (see Leech
and Johansson 2009, for details of this). A trend over time for software to
move away from bespoke, mainframe-based systems to widely shared and
reused packages also promoted this (see McEnery and Hardie 2011: 39).
However, the sharing of data and tools was not universal, in part because
of copyright or commercial issues that, on occasion, inhibited the sharing
of data. Today, many corpora developed by commercial organisations are
neither shared nor easily accessible. For example, the Oxford English
Corpus, while present on a corpus search platform, SketchEngine, is not
openly available. Researchers have to apply for permission to access the
corpus, permission that may, or may not be given.86
Repeatability may thus be limited considerably because, inter alia, a
corpus may not be available, it may not be accessible to all researchers
(e.g. because the cost to access is prohibitive or there is a restrictive applica-
tion process to access it) or it may be available in a form that does not allow
the speciﬁc repetition following the methods in the original study. To exem-
plify the ﬁnal point, it may be that the version of a corpus that is available
doesnot havethesamemetadataas thecorpus usedinastudythatwewishto
repeat. A good example of this is the BNC1994. When it was ﬁrst released in
1995, the corpus had a series of issues with its metadata, notably around the
encoding of social class in the spoken (demographic) section of the corpus.
However, using the original corpus, Leech et al. (1997) produced a series of
observations of the distribution of words across features encoded in the
metadata. Table 6.1 gives two examples of frequency differences cited in
that study, contrasting the frequencies on which the results were based with
the frequencies in the latest version of the British National Corpus 1994
(XML edition), which was searched using CQPweb (Hardie 2012).
As can be seen, a fundamental problem arises – precise repetition of the
research cannot be achieved. While, in the examples given, the claims made
stand, the results are not quite the same. Hence, one should be as concerned
with version management in corpus linguistics – storing old versions of a
86 See Nevalainen (2021: 281) for an example of how limited access to such corpora can
impact on the research process.
186
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 199 -->

corpus so that results based on it may be revisited – as researchers are about
this issue in other ﬁelds, for example, computer science.
Of course, the point stretches beyond the corpus data to the tools used to
process them. As pointed out, availability of the same software is another
requirement for repeatability. In an experiment, Brezina and Timperley
(2017) looked at the same version of the BNC1994 (XML edition) in differ-
ent software packages and observed: 1) considerable variation in the overall
token counts given by different tokenisation procedures implemented by the
tools; and 2) variation in the frequency counts of a range linguistic variables.
For example, the overall size of the BNC1994 varied from 96,263,399 in the
online implementation of the BNC from Brigham Young University (BYU-
BNC) to 112,181,015 in Sketch Engine. The frequency of the deﬁnite article
the was lowest in the BNC-BYU corpus (5,971,799), while it was highest in
the Xaira desktop tool originally developed for the BNC1994 XML edition
(6,055,159). This leads to an inevitable conclusion that without the access to
exactly the same software tool (and possibly also the same version of the
tool), repeatability is difﬁcult to achieve. It also raises a more general ques-
tion about standardisation in corpus linguistics in particular and perhaps
linguistics in general: ideally, all tools should implement the same standards
for tokenisation and searching to make results comparable and repeatable
across tools. Such standards are, however, yet to be agreedon, hence the calls

<!-- page 200 -->

of scholars such as Gries (2022), who caution that we should be mindful of,
and try to account for, variation in frequency at even this rather rudimentary
level of observation, are well founded.
Finally, we need to consider clarity of reporting of methods and results.
This caution is wise advice, as will be shown when considering two studies,
which pursue the question of word frequencies in the BNC1994. The
Leech et al. (1997) study was followed up in a fuller study by Leech et al.
(2001). Might we view (a subset of) the 2001 results as a repetition of the
1997 study? We might be tempted to conclude this, as the 1997 and 2001
studies are superﬁcially similar as they look at word frequencies in the
BNC1994 and contrasting frequencies in different groups or contexts. Yet
the results can barely be compared because, as Leech et al. (2001: 4) note:
The BNC has now been produced in two versions. The original release
(Version 1.0) was distributed in 1995. The new BNC (Version 2.0) . . . is
expected to be released in the winter of 2000/1.
This clearly makes it impossible to repeat all results from Version 1.0 on
Version 2.0 as Version 2.0:
has certain advantages over the old version, notably (a) improved accuracy of
grammatical tagging . . . and (b) correction of details of text classiﬁcation,
which has resulted in the transfer of about a million words from the ‘imagi-
native’ category of written texts to the ‘informative’ category. However, it
has also been necessary to reduce the size of the BNC . . . by 69 texts.
Based on this description, we might conclude the results of the 2001 study,
based on more accurate text classiﬁcation and grammatical tagging, effec-
tively trump those from 1997. Yet given that both corpora contain part-of-
speech errors, we may still want to verify that such a conclusion is valid by
examining the examples involved in such comparisons. If we try to use
Version 2.0 to do that, however, we encounter another problem. The 2001
study does not allow us to do this as it uses a third, bespoke, version of
the corpus in which the authors use ‘the complete BNC of Version 1.0’ yet at
the same time make ‘use of the improvements of Version 2.0’. So to
repeat the 2001 study, and to verify its results, we would need access to this
intermediateform of thecorpus–Version1.5if youwill–orhaveanaccurate
and detailed description of the procedures that led to the creation of this
version.
If we put such concerns to one side, there is still an issue with precise
reporting of the methods and results. While the 1997 study reports the
numbers of words spoken by males and females, the Leech et al. (2001: 3)
studydoesnot,preferringvaguequantiﬁcations,sayingthatthedemographic
188
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 201 -->

component ‘makes up just over 40% of the spoken corpus (over 4 million
words)’ and within that roughly 40%, 41.14% of the words are spoken by
males, 58.47% by females and 0.38% is unclassiﬁed. This does not allow us to
compute the precise ﬁgures, which we need for calculating the inferential
statistics they use. Even if the size of the corpus was given precisely, we could
not use the percentages to accurately determine how many words were
spoken by males and females as the percentages spoken by each group is
clearly subject to rounding error as the three ﬁgures given add up to 99.99%
rather than 100%. If we ignore the need to recover the precise ﬁgures and
content ourselves purely with wanting to see if the Chi-squared tests between
the two studies, as reported, were the same, we ﬁnd ourselves thwarted once
again – while the 1997 study uses Chi-squared as an inferential statistic, the
2001 study uses log-likelihood. The vague quantiﬁcations on the 2001 study
mean that we cannot use the ﬁgures from it to repeat the 1997 Chi-squared
tests.
So attempts to repeat claims from the 1997 study on the basis of the 2001
study are, at the very least, a complex undertaking requiring much more
precise data and a data set that is a hybrid of two others. The comparison of
the 1997 and 2001 study, and the ongoing nature of the development of
results from the BNC1994 are, in themselves unproblematic. As critical
realists we should expect research results and our data to be queried and
to change over time. A critical exploration of the BNC1994, when published,
led to it being revised and republished several times. Ongoing academic
debate about the adequacy, or otherwise, of the statistics that should be used
in such studies leads to changes in the measures that are deemed, by con-
vention, to be appropriate to use in such studies. Neither of these are at issue
here – in fact, they are entirely in line with the principles laid out in this book.
What is at issue is the repeatability of results. If we cannot return to a study
and verify the study, in its own terms, then we must retain some measure of
doubt about the results of such a study. This leads to Principle 44:
Principle 44: Researchers should make their best endeavours to ensure that the
research results they publish will remain repeatable. This entails archiving
versions of corpora and tools used in such studies, describing clearly the
goals and methods of the study and ensuring that analytical abstractions on
which a study is based (e.g. linguistic analyses, statistical analyses) are well
described in such a way as to promote repeatability.
So, while repetition sounds like a simple matter, currently, in practice, it
is not. It is, however, a matter that should be consciously addressed in all
6.1 Approaching a Study: Repetition
189
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 202 -->

studies in corpus linguistics, though it can be easily overlooked.
Repeatability provides an important anchor in reality and allows us to
maintain full transparency. Unless these elementary aspects of an empiri-
cal study are maintained, further progress is difﬁcult if not impossible.
While it may be argued that we would better explore the falsiﬁcation of
any work by pursuing Principles 280 and 290 – seeking different methods,
for example, to try to demonstrate falsiﬁcation of a study, if repeatability
(Principle 44) cannot be assumed, there is little to be falsiﬁed. Lack of
repeatability leads to epistemological indeterminacy. In a way similar to
that in which Schrödinger’s cat can be thought of both as dead and alive,
results of a study that cannot be repeated can be thought of as both false
and not false. If one cannot replicate ﬁndings from a study, then one has,
perhaps in a very fundamental way, achieved falsiﬁcation. Yet if one,
using different data or methods, falsiﬁes some ﬁndings, what is the value
in doing so if the ﬁndings themselves could not, in the original authors
own terms, be repeated? Has falsiﬁcation really been achieved if we are
not certain whether the ﬁndings we were trying to falsify were based on
solid empirical evidence?
While it is doubtlessly the case that corpus linguists do not undertake
repetition at times, relying on convention (Principle 20) when doing so,
the possibility of repetition should be one that is always considered and
permitted. Principle 44 is important in enabling that. In doing so it
simultaneously strengthens the epistemological basis of corpus linguistics
and ensures the probity of that work; while we are not aware of fraudu-
lent research in corpus linguistics, this is not the case in other ﬁelds, with
psychology, and in particular social psychology, being the source of some
major research malpractice in the early twenty-ﬁrst century (see Earp
and Traﬁmow 2015; Stricker and Gu¯nther 2019). Without the ability to
repeat research, the possibility of fraud is enabled, with obvious dama-
ging epistemological and other consequences.
6.1.1 Ssorg’s Report
To demonstrate how repeatability may operate, we return to Mars. We
last left Ssorg waiting for the Earth satellite to come into range again. He
has some luck – an Earth probe lands and has an uplink to a ship high
above the Martian atmosphere that establishes a data link to Earth. The
Martians are retiring souls and view the probe as an enormous inconve-
nience, forcing them to stay hidden for days at a time while the slow
moving object trundles by. However, the uplink is useful and through it
Ssorg downloads a paper, Leech (2003).
190
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 203 -->

He reads it and is delighted to discover that it appears to be based on
some familiar data – the Brown family of corpora. He knows also that, via
the uplink, he can contact the CQPweb server at Lancaster University on
which he has secretly registered as a user. He also has to hand a copy of
the principles outlined in this book, which he decides to use as an overall
framework for his study. Excitedly he plans to repeat the study in Leech
(2003), so he starts to ask himself a series of questions to test the
feasibility of doing so. He has to be very careful in his study as his work
has come to the attention of the Martian Empress, Iraxxa, and she wants
him to show, as precisely as possible, what she calls ‘Earth research logic’.
What follows is his report to Iraxxa.
Let us see if we can make sense of research logic on Earth. First, is the
study clear about the basis on which British and American written
English are compared? Yes, Leech (2003: 224–225) introduces and
clearly identiﬁes the corpus he used, and the corpora in question are
still available and are not know to have been subject to revision. Is the
study clear about the basic ontological knowledge used to classify the
data? To an extent – Leech (2003: 226) clearly deﬁnes his criteria for
allocating a word form to the category of modal verb, and provides a
suitable reference to the concept of semi-modals (Leech 2003: 229).
However, exactly how ambiguous cases were retrieved from the corpora
is not clear. While we may assume that it was by the application of the
deﬁnition of the modal verb referenced, exactly how that deﬁnition was
applied is unclear – it is very unlikely that each example of the word form
may was scrutinised to set aside proper noun uses from modal uses, for
example. At this point, Principle 20 is relevant – by convention corpus
linguists would assume that part of speech tagging, which is present in the
data described, would be used for that purpose. This is implied by Leech
(2003: 238) when he says that ‘[t]his study has been facilitated by the
word-class tagging of the LOB and F-LOB corpora’. However, this
statement is ambiguous – the paper describes one study, of modals and
semi-modals, yet the reference to ‘this study’ occurs after a discussion of a
larger project using those corpora looking at a broader range of gram-
matical categories. So, while not unambiguously referenced in the paper,
we might assume by convention that the ontological knowledge encoded
in the part-of-speech annotation was key to the retrieval of the modals
studied in the paper. The precise scheme in question is not identiﬁed, but
again, by convention we may assume it is the one encoded in the public
distributions of the LOB and FLOB corpora. However, as semi-modals
are not part of that scheme, we must assume that word sequences were
extracted and examined to retrieve the semi-modals.
6.1 Approaching a Study: Repetition
191
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 204 -->

We are also not told exactly how the data reported in the paper were
retrieved, forcing us to assume once again, by convention, that an unknown
concordance package was used for the purpose. Of course, such packages
assume some very basic axioms on which studies using them are based – for
example, the deﬁnition of what a word is. This leads to a new question: is the
study clear about these very basic axioms – for example, a deﬁnition of what
constitutes a word? Partially – the study does describe what it considers to
be boundary cases of orthographic forms that may be considered as modals,
otherwise there is a reliance on convention in the classiﬁcation of words. Yet
because the study is not clear about what tools were used to derive the
quantitative results presented, and, given that they in all likelihood dictated
what counted as a word, this is a potential drawback. However, Leech does
provide a list of all the word forms he considered to be modals and a
selection of semi-modals, so the problems that the lack of access to the
tool presents are partially addressed. If we limit ourselves to the word forms
cited in the study, we can attempt to repeat that published part of the study.
Next, is sufﬁcient quantitative data provided to allow us to repeat Leech’s
study? To some extent – Leech’s study reports raw frequencies (Leech 2003:
228) for each of the modals he discusses and raw frequencies for some of the
semi-modal he has studied (Leech 2003: 229). He also 1) uses percentages to
discuss and compare usage between varieties of English and 2) employs a
statistical test, log-likelihood, to make a claim as to whether any observed
difference, either in variety or over time, is to be viewed as statistically
signiﬁcant. To derive the percentages and the statistical tests, however, we
need more data than is present in the two tables of raw frequencies given.
We also need the precise sizes of the four corpora. Consider the following
quote, writing of ‘the modal auxiliaries, declining from 14.6 occurrences per
thousand words in LOB to 11.9 per thousand words in Frown’ (Leech 2003:
227). To reproduce these ﬁgures for ourselves, we would need to know the
size of each corpus and the total number of modal auxiliaries, within the
deﬁnition of that class of words given, in each corpus. The paper appears to
provide this information – we are told that the corpora used are ‘million-
word corpora’ and that there are 14,667 modals in LOB and 12,287 in the
Frown corpus. This would produce 14.667 per thousand in LOB and 12,287
in Frown (Leech 2003: 228). Neither ﬁgure reconciles to the ﬁgures claimed.
How may this be explained? One explanation comes from trying to repli-
cate the word count – if we explore the LOB corpus87 we discover that it is
87 We accessed the LOB and Frown corpus using CQPweb at Lancaster University.
Readers may do the same by visiting https://cqpweb.lancs.ac.uk and registering for
free access.

not a million-word corpus, and likewise neither is the Frown corpus. Using
one concordance package, CQPweb, LOB is 1,141,986 words and Frown is
1,154,283 words. If we use those corpus size ﬁgures, the LOB and Frown
modal ﬁgures in question become 12.84 and 10.64 modal auxiliaries per
thousand words, respectively.
Given the clearly divergent views on what a word is in these counts, let
us see if repetition improves when we move to the level of the individual
word form. For this purpose, Table 6.2 repeats the ﬁgures cited for ﬁve
word forms in the Leech (2003) study, for each of the four corpora
studied. Assuming that part-of-speech annotations in the corpora were
used for the study, the ﬁgures in Table 6.2 for the four corpora, as
retrieved from the corpora as held in CQPweb, are given in parentheses
after Leech’s ﬁgures. In each case, our search was for the word form
combined with the part-of-speech annotation indicating a modal verb. In
the table, those counts that are precisely repeated in our study are
emboldened.
We will not pursue this attempt at repetition further – it is clear that
we lack a piece of information that allows us to reproduce Leech’s
ﬁgures. The reasons for the mismatch may be endless – for example, it
may be that the axiom used to deﬁne a word differed between the
system that he used to explore the corpus and both CQPweb, thus giving
a different corpus size, or it may be that there is some difference
between the deﬁnition of what a modal was when the ﬁgures were
calculated relative to the reporting of raw frequencies in the paper
used here. We do not know. What we do know is that we must take on
trust the statistics reported in the paper, including the inferential sta-
tistics provided, because we cannot, at least without considerable difﬁ-
culty, repeat them. Puzzling indeed! How do they make any progress on
Earth?

<!-- page 206 -->

6.1.2 The Difﬁculty of Repetition
How typical is the experience of our slightly disappointed and bafﬂed
Martian? We suspect the answer is far from atypical. If more information
was available for the repetition, the multiple sources of mismatches
would have become apparent. For example, from Leech’s later writing
we know that different versions of the four corpora have been created. In
Leech et al. (2010) there is a lengthy description of a process whereby the
corpora were re-processed, with manual corrections being produced for
some of the part of speech tags. The consequences of this become clear if
we consider the modals previously studied and compare their frequencies
in the 2003 study with those in 2010. In Table 6.3, those counts which are
precisely repeated are emboldened.
So Ssorg, had he known this, might have realised two things. First,
there is an issue of version management with the corpora in question –
there seems to be no very clear way of discovering which version of the
corpora are being used, or what the differences between them are.
Second, as a consequence of the ﬁrst point, users are left with a question
of version identiﬁcation. Ssorg would also have had to ﬁnd out what
speciﬁc version of the corpus he had on Mars and what speciﬁc version
of the corpus he was accessing through CQPweb on Earth. Such issues
may be possible to overcome by writing to authors and doing consider-
able amounts of detective work with the corpora one has. However, such
problems at best make repetition difﬁcult and at worst may accidentally
confound it altogether.
Leech’s work on modals is far from being unique in being difﬁcult to
repeat. Doyle (2003: 272) in trying to repeat the work of Phillips (1985)
found that it was ‘more complicated than was ﬁrst envisaged’, and found
Table 6.3 The frequencies of modal verbs in four Brown family corpora

(2003) in parentheses.

Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 207 -->

that a direct repetition was not possible, a conclusion also reached also by
Brezina et al. (2015: 142–143). Many of the issues discussed here were
noted by Doyle (2003: 5), who noted the welter of practical issues that
confounded repetition as:
software becomes obsolete, algorithms are not published and data stored in
older media becomes inaccessible or physically deteriorates. Databases
recorded in old computer formats can no longer be accessed on next genera-
tion machines, while magnetic storage tapes and discs have physically
decayed.
We can certainly think of examples of work that cannot be repeated
for a host of reasons. For example, the study of closure in McEnery
and Wilson (2001) cannot be repeated easily as the data used for it
was the property of a commercial concern; it is thus not publicly
available and is no longer available to the researchers. For the same
reason, much of the work reported in Black et al. (1993) cannot be
repeated either.
Problems with repetition may occur at almost any stage in the
research lifecycle – if one is unclear in one’s methodology, unclear
about the data used and one does not make the tools and data
necessary to conduct one’s study available to others, then the
chances of repeating work will be low. Likewise, over time if version
management becomes an issue, the very least one should do is take
that issue on board and report it during future work in the area,
should one produce any. For example, McEnery et al. (2000) used
the ﬁrst version of the BNC1994’s demographic section to produce
the results on swearing reported for present-day English in that
paper. At the time, it was not known that there would be a future
version of the corpus and that the future version would differ in
many substantial ways, as discussed earlier in this chapter. So ver-
sion management and the reporting of versions at the time was an
apparent non-issue – there was only one version of the corpus and it
was not envisaged that there would be a second. However, between
the publication of that paper and the publication of McEnery (2005),
the BNC1994 was reissued with the social class markup, among
other features, being substantially changed in the spoken corpus.
So readers of the 2000 work might easily conclude that the latest
version of the BNC1994 would allow them to repeat the study – it
would not and there is no indication in the 2000 paper that this is the
case. This leads us to the next principle, which develops the require-
ments for repeatability outlined in Principle 44:
6.1 Approaching a Study: Repetition
195
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 208 -->

Principle 45: Version management for corpora and tools should be considered at
the start of a project and then maintained over time.
This, of course, has a signiﬁcant overhead of time and effort associated
with it and it remains to be seen whether the open approach to research
will prove to be practical and sustainable in the long term. However,
there is no doubt that, in principle, the overall enterprise is desirable.
Before we forget about Ssorg’s experiment, however, we may consider
one further point. Ssorg set himself a very precise goal for repetition – he
wanted to derive exactly the same numbers from the corpus used in the
2003 study. Was he right to conclude that he could go no further when he
failed to do that? Perhaps not. While he could not repeat those numbers
exactly, he did produce results that were similar enough to Leech’s that
he could conclude that Leech’s ﬁndings were not falsiﬁed. Consider
Table 6.4 in which the frequencies in Table 6.3 are replaced with ranks,
with the most frequent word in any column being ranked 1.
What we see here is a perfect correspondence between the ranks with
which modal verbs occur in the 2003 and 2010 studies. While we
obviously have cause to doubt that the versions of the corpora used
have been clearly identiﬁed, those worries do not extend to the overall
claims made by Leech about difference in modal usage between British
and American English and its change over time. So the failure to repeat
the results lead us to conclude not that the ﬁndings are necessarily invalid,
with consequences for the claims that Leech builds on these data, but that
a number of factors such as the curation of the data sets in the later study
did not promote precise repeatability.
