<!-- page 209 -->

So, it appears, we must rely, in cases such as this, which are far from
uncommon, on nothing but convention (Principle 20) by necessity rather
than choice. We are in a position where we need to decide, in the absence
of corpora and tools that would allow use precise repeatability, if we
should give a study the beneﬁt of the doubt, that is, decide whether we
believe that it was carried out and reported accurately.
Yet when we are relying on convention in a case like this, is there the
danger of crossing back into the metaphysical from the social scientiﬁc?
Obviously, the possibility exists. However, we need to make a distinction
between a metaphysical approach and empirical research that we fail to
precisely repeat. If we produce an opinion about what we believe to be
happening in British English in terms of the changing use of modals,
basing it on what we might call our intuition, and somebody tries to
repeat the exercise, but fails to come to the same conclusion, we are
obviously deep in the realms of metaphysics. Opinion versus opinion, in a
context were it is not possible to repeat the ﬁrst set of ﬁndings exactly,
places us in a position where results are not intersubjectively testable. So
even if we draw the same conclusion it is far from clear that this is a
precise repetition in the same way that Ssorg was approaching it.
Nevertheless, this is not what Leech was doing.88 While we did not
repeat Leech’s ﬁndings precisely, we can see clearly that, but for the want
of greater clarity, we could, in principle have repeated the study. It is this
beneﬁt of the doubt we are affording that study, nothing more. We are
accepting that the original study, based on the corpus, was, in principle,
intersubjectively testable and repeatable. If we knew which of a number
of versions of the corpus was used, then we could have attempted repeti-
tion, and if that test had failed, then we would have had grounds to claim
the study was not, in fact, repeatable. So convention here does not, of
necessity, imply a tacit acceptance of a claim that is metaphysical. Rather,
convention, based on the evidence we can glean about the study, suggests
that we accept the results of a potentially repeatable test.
Because the test is potentially repeatable, we may wish to go further,
however, and still subject the study to potential falsiﬁcation – we can
make attempts to replicate, rather than repeat, the study. While we must
rely on trust if using repetition, we may be able to get a better critical
88 We have no doubt that the problems discussed here arise from imprecision rather than
from some effort to conceal the evidence. This type of reporting aligns with the state-of-
the-art reporting in corpus linguistics at the time of the study and would be similar across
different studies and authors at that time.
6.1 Approaching a Study: Repetition
197
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 210 -->

insight into the ﬁndings via replication as we will see in the next section.
We will also slightly alter our perspective on the 2003 and 2010 studies.
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
Replication is a process where our current understanding of a topic based
on previous studies is confronted with new empirical evidence. This
process is in line with Principles 16″ and 290 because it allows a genuine
test of our hypotheses and offers an opportunity to increase the potential
for falsiﬁcation in different ways. The precise form of replication depends
on the research design of the original study or studies and the aspects we
intend to replicate (see Chapter 7). Nosek and Errington (2020: 1) offer
the following general deﬁnition of replication: ‘We propose that replica-
tion is a study for which any outcome would be considered diagnostic
evidence about a claim from prior research.’ In its simplest form, replica-
tion uses the same research question, same research design but a different
data set to confront previous research with new ﬁndings.
A common approach to replication is to replicate an experimental
design through time – the same experimental design, or modiﬁcations
of it, are used not simply to test once, but to repeatedly test on data
gathered over time. The same, as we will see in Section 6.2.1, may be true
of cross-sectional studies using data collected at different intervals of
time from repeated samples of the population. In that way, we pursue
falsiﬁcation not once, but many times – this is entirely in line with
Principle 290 in particular. In Leech’s (2003) study, multiple matched
samples were used, based on the same cross-section design, that per-
mitted this investigation – in it Leech tested hypotheses such as 1) modal
usage is varying in two varieties of English and 2) the rate of change in
one variety is different from the rate in the other variety. Note that in
forming these hypotheses it was assumed that variations in frequency
were a property of a variety (American vs British) and/or time. So the
crystalised probabilities at each sampling point are thought to be those
pertaining to that time rather than a matter of chance or subject to
substantial variation. Leech tested his hypotheses in the data and, on
the basis of the tests, started to hypothesise about grammatical change
operating over the cross-sections in his data. From this experiment, a
series of corroborations emerged for both hypotheses. Yet how can these
corroborations be exposed to conditional falsiﬁcation or even rejection?
We have noted already the attempt by Miller (2009) to do so – yet his
replication involved only one text type and one variety of English
(American English). So as an attempt to falsify hypotheses based on
198
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 211 -->

different varieties of English and a wide range of text types it could only
ever be partially successful at best.
However, our thinking here about replication leads us to reﬂect back on
Ssorg’s experiment (Section 6.1.1) and the nuances of the difference
between replication and repetition as well as their potential overlap. In
Leech (2003) and Leech et al. (2010) it is obvious that different versions of
the same family of corpora were used. In each there is evidence of an
increased reﬁnement in the part-of-speech tagging, and Leech et al. (2010:
33–34) discuss how they hand corrected errors in the corpora they used,
meaning that from Leech (2003) to Leech et al. (2010) the corpus has been
reﬁned (in line with Principle 23). Later Baker (2017) also used the Brown
family of corpora to explore changes in modal use, extending the period of
time into the early twenty-ﬁrst century. This, in effect, changes the assem-
blage of corpora brought together in a new overarching design – Baker
extends the study further through time using an additional pair of corpora
for American and British English in the early twenty-ﬁrst century. In doing
so, the study places a more exacting test on Leech’s ﬁndings (abiding by
Principle 290), though the assumption about crystallised probabilities
remains. Baker produced results congruent with each of the previous
studies. All three studies used slightly different versions of the same
corpora – so what we had was more akin to an initial study (Leech 2003)
followed by two replications, using similar, but not identical, data sets
(as Principle 23 permits). All three studies produce the same broad
ﬁndings, providing corroboration for the ﬁrst study, though all relied
on an assumption that in each corpus probabilities were somehow
typical of a larger body of data and that, through time, those probabil-
ities could be compared. Baker’s study also attempts falsiﬁcation of the
initial study by extending the scope of the claim through time, some-
thing that had been achieved only through Principle 50 initially. The key
is that between them there is the potential for disagreement created as
the part-of-speech tagging is not identical – the sequence of studies
abide by Principle 290.
Let us now turn to some other studies in corpus linguistics that more
self-consciously replicate earlier studies. Baker et al. (2013) drew, from
the totality of discourse about Islam and Muslims, a set of data bounded
by time (1998–2009) and genre (national as opposed to local newspapers)
and produced a corpus that allowed them to explore discourses about
Islam and Muslims in all relevant articles in the UK press.89 The work
89 This is an interesting example of where an all claim can be made – there was no more
data than that considered in the study, so the corpus, in this case, was coterminous with
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
199
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 212 -->

resulted in a number of claims based on observational data, including that
there existed a set of dominant discourses in the period (claim A) and
that these were prone to some degree of ﬂux over time (claim B).
McEnery and Baker (2019) took that corpus and introduced surprise to
it, extending the time frame to cover 2010–2014, producing a new data
set, and thus enabled studies of the ongoing effect of propensity on the
data to be observed. Their goal was expressly to see whether claims A
and B were sustained in the new data set. Both claims were corroborated.
Clarke et al. (2021) then looked at a third data set (2010–2019). As well as
introducing surprise through new data, they also used a slightly revised
methodology to attempt to falsify A and B. However, their ﬁndings
aligned with the ﬁrst replication, both A and B were corroborated.
Clarke et al. (2021) further developed the work by using a new method
to track the discourses precisely by individual newspaper over time and,
again, largely corroborated the work while also showing how propensity
caused variation in the discourse over time.
Another (different) approach to replicability in corpus studies is the
test and training corpus model. While this is not classic replicability in the
sense that the replication is done by someone other than the researchers
producing the result, it is nonetheless a way in which the generalisability
of results from a corpus may be tested. In this long-established approach
(Aarts 1991), a portion of the corpus, a so-called test corpus, is set aside
and not used for the main study, which relies on the rest of the corpus,
called the training corpus. When the study on the training corpus is
complete, the study is repeated on the test corpus, thus allowing some
insight into how generalisable the inductive reasoning arising from the
analysis of the training corpus may be.
A further approach to replicability is possible where a feature of a
corpus is not exhaustively analysed, but is, rather, analysed on the basis of
a subsample. This occurs where too many examples of a feature exist in a
corpus or where the analysis carried out on that feature is so time con-
suming that it is not possible to exhaustively analyse the feature. So, for
example, McEnery and Baker (2017) look in a corpus of over one billion
words of Early Modern English at meanings of the word WHORE. They
identiﬁed four main meanings for the word and wanted to know the
distribution of those meanings, on a per decade basis, across the
U, though this may be a slight overstatement. The articles collected relied on a query
term that had been developed by the team. If this query did not, in fact, capture all
articles about Muslims and Islam, then there would be a mismatch between the corpus
and U. However, we will assume here that there is not.
200
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 213 -->

seventeenth century. However, there are 17,911 examples of the word in
the century and they did not have the time and resources to examine each
word. Their response was to look at a random subsample of 100 examples
per decade in the data, classify those examples, and use this subsample to
show the distribution of the meanings across the century (through
Principle 4). Replicability in this case is possible – other researchers
could use the same corpus and explore how replicable these results are
by taking different subsamples. They too could look at other 100 exam-
ples per decade and see to what extent their analyses support those of
McEnery and Baker (2017). They may, if they are able, try to replicate
the result on a larger sample, perhaps the whole data set. So, where
analysis proceeds by subsampling in large corpora, replicability is possi-
ble through resampling. Resampling is a productive approach to replica-
tion that we may bring to bear on corpus data using statistical procedures
(through Principle 4).
A ﬁnal way in which replication is done is through many corpora – it
need not be based on a simple pairwise comparison. So, for example, in
a lexicographic study Brezina and Gablasova (2015) worked with a
number of corpora to establish a stable shared wordlist between them.
They were focused on the words (lemmas) in the corpora. Hence each
corpus was conceptualised as a set of lemmas and Brezina and
Gablasova (2015) studied their relative frequency and dispersion in
the union between all the corpora. As their focus was lexical, their set
deﬁnitions are lexical. Yet note that ontological knowledge is still
important – the same deﬁnition of a word had to be applied in all
corpora to achieve the result from the experimental design they pro-
duced. So we should be mindful, in establishing designs to replicate
corpus ﬁndings, of the types of replication we are subjecting our hypoth-
eses to.
6.2.1 Design of a Study
As shown previously, different types of replication are closely connected
with a study’s research design. Different types of research design will be
discussed in this section. Let us begin with a simple question. What type
of study may we conduct with a corpus? The vast majority of corpus
studies are observational. Data are gathered and organised according to
some predetermined scheme – for example, a corpus sampling scheme.
The resulting set is then observed (hence observational studies) to deter-
mine the effects of relevant factors (variables) on that set. We do not
deliberately manipulate the settings as in an experimental design to
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
201
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 214 -->

expose the members of the set to a particular factor or set of factors;
corpora thus reﬂect naturally occurring language in a variety of contexts.
Corpus observational studies are analogous to cross-sectional studies in
the social sciences – a sample is selected that becomes a proxy for a larger
population. Corpus studies, where the cross-section is repeated, are ana-
logous to trend studies ‘which focus on change over time by using
repeated cross-sectional samples’ (Elliott et al. 2008: 228). These are
distinct from cohort studies, sometimes called panel studies, ‘where the
same group of individuals are followed through time’ (Elliott et al. 2008:
228). Corpus linguistics could beneﬁt greatly from cohort studies, of
writers and speakers, as the focus in those studies are expressly on indi-
viduals and permit the type of focus required by Principle 320 – in fact,
when one looks at corpus building that has a temporal focus, Principle 320
is routinely violated as the normative trumps the individual. For example,
if we contrast the Spoken BNC1994 demographic section with the Spoken
BNC2014, we see that we cannot follow individuals through time, but we
can follow groups that individuals are assigned to. Hence any change over
time will be ascribed to groups as we cannot match individuals between
the two corpora to show how the individuals that gave rise to observations
in the ﬁrst corpus vary in the second. The same is true of the corpora we
are focusing on in the next chapter, the Brown family – we are not aware
of any writers who are present in more than one of the corpora hence with
these corpora, and the BNC corpora as discussed, we can see that the
trend study model militates in favour of a normative view of language
abstracted away from the individual, which is not ideal. However, while a
cohort approach to written material might be viable, the obstacles of
taking this approach to speech would be daunting – the gap between the
two Spoken BNC collections was twenty years, and such a gap is likely to
amplify a problem of cohort studies: attrition. Members of the sample die,
move and cannot be traced or simply refuse to take part in the study any
more. Olsen (2005) identiﬁes the National Longitudinal Study of Youth in
the United States as the outstanding example of sample retention – but in
regular contact with the cohort over twenty-three years from 1979, even
that study lost 22.25% of respondents. Attrition was the main reason that
the Spoken BNC2014 corpus was not part of a cohort study – when
explored, this approach was thought to promise a 100% attrition rate
largely because the group building the corpus had no access to the contact
details of the original members of the data collection from which the 1994
corpus was built.
So, corpus studies exploring change over time tend to be trend studies,
based on cross-sectional samples focused on group categories rather than
202
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 215 -->

individuals. In written corpus construction, the data collection is typically
retrospective (published works are gathered) and in spoken corpus
building the collection is prospective (speakers are recruited and then
data are gathered from them). The resulting study of the cross-section
allows us to assesses the impact of factors on the cross-section and, based
on that, we infer the impact of the factor on the larger population from
which the cross-section was composed.
There are, however, corpora that are slightly different and that have a
purpose much closer to that of an experimental study. In a corpus such as
the Trinity Lancaster Corpus (TLC; Gablasova et al. 2019), one factor
controls the composition of the corpus overall: language proﬁciency.
While that may sound like the beginning of an observational study, the
corpus is composed of elicited responses to pre-set tasks, with those tasks
being thought important for the purpose of assessing language proﬁ-
ciency. So the corpus is much closer to an experimental study than an
observational one precisely because the focus of the corpus, speakers,
experience an intervention – their output is expressly elicited according
to pre-set tasks that are varied according to the level of proﬁciency to be
tested. This is more clearly understood if we consider the two stages of
experimental studies: treatment design and experimental design. The
treatment design of the corpus is determined by how the subjects are
treated differently – in the TLC the test administered to the speakers
varies by level of presumed language ability. Some tasks are only given at
higher levels of language attainment. This treatment design then links to
an experimental design in which factors such as the volume and type of
data necessary to be gathered are considered. At this point such corpora
depart from classic experimental studies in that the corpus sampling
structure is, in essence, the basis of a treatment design that later con-
strains the experimental designs that may be based on it. The decisions
made in building the corpus on the basis of this treatment design then
determines issues such as how much data are gathered and how overall
balance against categories in the metadata is achieved. So a corpus such
as the TLC, while it looks like an experimental study, need not be built
with a speciﬁc experimental design in mind – rather it has a general,
abstract, experimental design that permits a number of future experi-
mental designs to be taken from it. Yet throughout, one may only vary
the experimental design in ways that the treatment design that collected
the data permits. So, with the TLC we might, for example, look at a host
of research questions that the basic treatment of the data matches. Yet
when building the corpus, the corpus builders worked to an overall
experimental design and it may be that, in doing so, they collected
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
203
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 216 -->

insufﬁcient data to permit the precise experimental design a user may
wish to pursue with the data.
In both cases, there is an application of linguistic knowledge when the
cross-section is designed (on the observational study route) or the treat-
ment and broad experimental design is considered (on the experimental
study route). Decisions at that stage, made in line with Principles 1 and 3,
are vitally important as they permit, or deny, work with speciﬁc goals
later on. So, we have factors we wish to explore and need to ﬁnd data that
allows us to explore them. If we are constructing our own corpus, then we
decide all aspects of either the treatment or cross-section design, with our
speciﬁc research goals foremost in our minds. If, as is more common, we
use existing corpora, that relationship changes slightly – we need to ﬁnd a
corpus, which comes with a predetermined design, that permits our
hypotheses to be tested. We then work to observe the effect of factors
on that data as allowed by that design in a way that tests the hypothesis or
hypotheses we have.
So Leech (2003), for example, in expanding the original Brown corpus
by building LOB and then working to help to build the other corpora
within the Brown family, expanded the number of observations that
might be made using those corpora, permitting the effect of genre, time
and variety on English to be observed, for example. He did that with the
goal of exploring features of grammatical change by variety and through
time. Hence based on those corpora, which together provided the possi-
bility of a trend analysis, Leech could test his hypotheses, for example,
related to changes in modal usage.
Yet as noted already, the initial decision – those made in corpus
construction – limits the hypotheses that may be tested on the corpus
once constructed and thus the range of observations or experimental
designs that may be based upon them. The mismatch between the possible
uses and any given use may be realised by degrees or may be absolute. For
example, if we want to explore the language of Twitter using nothing but the
Brown family, then the attempt is doomed to fail – the design of the Brown
family does not include Tweets, hence any hypothesis focused on Tweets
would, by design, ﬁnd no data in the Brown family to work on. No linguis-
tically meaningful inference could be drawn from that absence. However,
mismatches may still allow well-founded tests to be conducted – imagine
that a researcher claims that there is a distinctive linguistic feature, x, in
Twitter (T is the set of linguistic features in Twitter) as distinct from general
written English (W is the set of linguistic features in general written English)
that is unique to communication via Twitter. We can still use the Brown
family of corpora to test the claim to uniqueness as, logically, within the set
204
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 217 -->

W we are dealing with an inverse existential claim, namely ¬∃(x). Should we
ﬁnd x ∈W, then we have not merely falsiﬁed the claim about Twitter, we
may reject it (in line with Principle 24). Should we ﬁnd x ∉W, we may send
the author of the Twitter paper a note to say that we have corroborated their
claim, though we would, at the same time, be interested in exploring the
basis on which they made the claim x ∈T in their Twitter data, of course. So
mismatch is important and may sit at the root of our research design.
Thinking about the relationship of the corpus design to the concept of
testing and making claims is clearly an important step in any study in
which existing corpora are reused. Of equal importance, however, and
of central importance to Principle 50, is the ability of our experiments to
allow us to corroborate the attempts we make to break the bounds of
our limited observations through Principle 50 by using Principle 4. To
move from making observations linked only to the data at hand
(Principle 50), we use processes of inference to project beyond that
context to a less constrained one (Principle 4) – so, for example, using
the Brown family we escape the bounds of commenting only on the
words in our corpora to making an observation of the totality of what
the research design seeks to represent, in the corpora used by Leech
(2003), that is, British and American English in the latter half of the
twentieth century. Leech could do so because cross-sectional studies, as
discussed, may be repeated to become trend studies that provide a view
through time. So, with the Brown family, working from an initial
American cross-section, a matching cross-section was gathered to
expand the scope of possible observations across what we might con-
ceptualise of as space (from American to British English) and both
cross-sections have been repeated through time to permit observations
to span time. At the time of writing, the repeated cross-section collec-
tion has been repeated for both varieties of English four times (1931,
1961, 1991, 2006), with a ﬁfth collection (focused on 2021) just begin-
ning. The advantage of doing this is apparent if we look again at the
Spoken BNC1994. By extending the initial collection of spoken data
from 1994 to another point in time, 2014, Love et al. (2017) enable
researchers to make observations and discriminate between age grading
and cohort effects in the data. On the basis of the 1994 data alone, it was
impossible to say whether a variable sensitive to age was sensitive to
something that actually changed as an individual grew older (age grad-
ing) as opposed to indicating that there was a difference between gen-
erations in the data (a cohort effect). The addition of a second point
allowed hypotheses based on the ﬁrst cohort (e.g. by McEnery 2005) to
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
205
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 218 -->

be tested on both cohorts (e.g. by Love 2021) to try to determine what
type of effect was being observed.
6.2.2 Formalising Replication
One might conceive of replication as being very precise – one must
always try to match the design of replications as precisely as possible.
As already discussed, this is not true, though it is one obvious approach to
replication. In corpus linguistics, it is only one of a number of approaches
taken to date (see Section 6.2). While precision in replication may clearly
desirable, the type of replication we conceived Leech et al. (2010) and
Baker (2017) to be, however, is arguably more useful. That undertaken
by Clarke et al. (2021), in varying methods, is useful also (in line with
Principle 280). Yet to explain how varying from the original study can be a
good idea, we need to ﬁrst understand clearly how the design of our
corpus inﬂuences a study and any replication of it.
Our ﬁrst instinct in using a different corpus, such as the BNC1994, to
replicate another study, for example, produced using BE06, may be
caution. To explore whether this is warranted, let us return to our ﬁrst
example of replication, in which we replicated not with a corpus of the
same size as BE06 (Baker 2009), but with a much larger corpus, the
BNC1994.
Researchers in replicating ﬁndings may want to eliminate corpus size
as a variable in explaining the results. For example, it may be that in the
1,000,000-word BE06 some things simply do not appear because the
sample size is not large enough to support the observation of this feature.
Let us consider this, shifting to using the FLOB corpus (a million words
of British English, collected using the same sampling structure as the
BE06) to control time as a variable in making comparisons to the
BNC1994 in the research design. We are interested in one word only in
this treatment: the word peregrinating. Our hypotheses, are that 1) per-
egrinating is a word in English and 2) that it is a rare word with a
correspondingly low frequency. FLOB has no evidence to offer us, the
word does not occur in the corpus. May we consider that we have proved
¬∃(peregrinating), hence conditionally falsifying 1) as we believe that this
word does not exist in English? While it may be tempting to consider 1)
conditionally falsiﬁed, we should be mindful of Principle 24 – corpora are
only really of use in corroborating existential statements, not inverse
existential statements. The disparity between the universal set of words
in English and those in our corpus is so substantial that rejection is not
warranted. However, in the approximately 90 million words of the
206
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 219 -->

Written BNC1994, the word occurs once – so we may conclude that 1) is
corroborated as the existential statement ∃(peregrinating) has been satis-
ﬁed. While we may wonder how infrequent something has to be to count as
rare, one example of peregrinating in approximately 90 million words of
data does seem to suggest rarity, so let us assume that that words occurring
once per 10 million words count as rare, so 2) is corroborated also.
Have we not proven 2? No, we have evidence that in the data observed
propensity works in such a way that the frequency of peregrinating is low
but, as propensity varies across language use, we may, with a different
corpus, ﬁnd a different solution – hence our falsiﬁcations are conditional
because we are conscious of the possibility of propensity varying across
A, B, C, D and U in Figure 6.1 below. While we may try to control
propensity by generating sampling frames that we believe limit and
contain propensity meaningfully, the possibility exists that we may ﬁnd
variation caused by propensity within those frames (as Principle 39 tacitly
acknowledges). For example, consider that, in another version of the
FLOB corpus, it just so happens that the corpus builders have included
the International Journal of Peregrinating Studies.90 This new corpus,
because it samples, quite by chance, from a context in which propensity
militates in favour of peregrinating occurring frequently, produces ﬁfty
examples of the word, thus asserting both ∃(peregrinating) and that the
word is not rare as deﬁned by a minimum agreed baseline for frequency,
which, of course, is an analytical construct. Such concerns have been
considered by corpus linguists and the possibility of such a ﬁnding is far
from an abstract possibility; as Egbert and Biber (2019) show single texts
can indeed skew word frequency to the extent that we need to consider
the possibility in our analyses. Through this one example we can see why
single observations may be misleading, and why multiple replications
may be needed, especially in the context of trying to establish a normative
epistemology (Principle 320) in which both the singular and the normative
may be acknowledged.
We will now use FLOB as a corpus on which to base a test, considering
the hypothesis that the following words are frequent (hypothesis 1) and
that they are listed here in descending order of frequency (hypothesis 2):
should, could and must. These words occur 6,527, 10,170 and 5,461 times,
respectively, in FLOB, so 1) is corroborated and 2) is conditionally
falsiﬁed. So we revise our hypothesis to say that the order is as in
FLOB, could>should>must (hypothesis 20). To attempt to falsify this
90 Of course, no such journal exists, but for the sake of both argument and levity let us
assume it does.
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
207
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 220 -->

we use the Written BNC1994. In it we ﬁnd these three words with a
frequency of 139,674, 96,889 and 63,890, respectively. Both hypothesis
1) and 20) are corroborated. More than that, we have also shown corro-
boration between two distinct samples, with slightly different cross-sec-
tion designs, adding plausibility to our use of Principle 4.

However, when using different corpora like this one would need to be
cautious. Any differences that are found may be the result of differences
in the sampling structures of the corpora used. Figure 6.1 illustrates how
A
C
D
B
U
Figure 6.1 The population of sentences (U) in British English in the early 1990s, sampling
frames/structures (A and B) and samples (two corpora, C and D)
208
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 221 -->

problems may arise. The population that is being studied in Figure 6.1 is
the written sentences of British English produced in 1990–1992. This is
the universal set that we conceive of for this study. Yet we do not have
all of those sentences, and we need to work with a subset of them, on the
basis of which we may make claims about U – hence Principle 4 is active
again. We conceive of a structured subset of U, A, based on a sampling
structure – this deﬁnes the parameters for the sentences we wish to
include in our study – in this case let us consider this to be sentences
written between 1 January 1990 and 31 December 1991. Within that
notional set, we then select a subset of sentences for our corpus, C, with
100 sentences taken, at random, from each month in the sampling struc-
ture. We wish to replicate the study, so we also produce sampling structure
B. This notionally covers sentences produced between 1 January 1991 and
31 December 1992. Within that sampling structure, we then select a subset
of sentences for our corpus, D, with 100 sentences, at random, from each
month in the sampling structure.
It is the interaction between those four sets of utterances, A, B, C
and D, coupled with the operation of propensity across them, that
may both guide and confound replicability. As can be seen in Figure
6.1, there are a number of relations between the sets. We will invoke
Principle 37 at this point and apply a simplifying assumption that
within the sets A and B, propensity is controlled, though we will
return to this issue in Chapter 7.
First, both A and B are proper subsets of U (A⊂U, B⊂U). There is also
a non-empty intersection between A and B (A⋂B ≠∅). Yet while C is a
proper subset of A, it only has a non-empty intersection with B (C⊂A,
C⋂B ≠∅). Similarly, while D is a proper subset of B, it only has a non-
empty intersection with A (D⊂B, D⋂A ≠∅). While the union of A and B
might be considered to be equal to U (A ⋃B = U), these are notional
collections, not the corpora themselves. Our corpora, C or D do not cover
the totality of sentences in the language in the period – there are many
more sentences written in each month than 100. Principle 4 will thus
guide our reasoning about the language that falls within the sample and
what we may infer about language beyond it – for example, we should at
the very least be cautious about making claims about speech as spoken
utterances are not within the universal set being considered. Second,
while there is a similarity between the sampling structures (represented
by A⋂B), the sets are not identical. Hence the capacity to directly
replicate results from A within B is problematic. If we change our con-
ceptualisation of the sets from being about overlapping periods of time,
to overlapping linguistic categories, a problem arises. The data are
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
209
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 222 -->

categorised on the basis of sentences. However, when we examine C and
D, we discover that the intersection is in fact empty; C⋂D= Ø occurs as
the chance of a sentence repeating itself is low (see McEnery and Wilson
2001: 176–183). So if we view sentences in C as the basis of ontological
knowledge, a catalogue of sentence types where we examine sentences
simply as strings of words, and then use Principle 4 to make inferences
about U based on C, those inferences are not likely to be falsiﬁed using D,
as the rules based on C have low empirical content – we never ﬁnd
sentences repeating, hence all D will allow us to do is to progressively
enumerate, sentence by sentence, our catalogue of sentence types. While
we may be able to say that Principle 40 applies to each sentence, the
overall approach has empirical content approaching zero as we do not
seem to be able to falsify anything. We see a sentence, we have no cause
to believe it does not abide by Principle 40, but we cannot corroborate
that view as we never see the sentence again. Seen from this perspective,
the chances for replication seem low. However, this is taking a sentence-
based view of language – which is precisely what corpus linguists do
not typically do. In corpus linguistics, sampling structures are not
looking at an inventory of sentence types, as structuralist linguists
may have done. Rather sampling structures in corpus linguistics are
seeking to divide language into chunks within which it may be expected
that language varies, that is, the sampling structure is an abstraction of
contexts within which propensity varies, typically genres, registers or
modes of communication.91 Principle 46 sums up the approach of corpus
linguists.
Principle 46: Sampling structures in corpus linguistics are seeking to divide lan-
guage into chunks within which it may be expected that language varies, that
is, the sampling structure is composed of nominal forms within which language
variation occurs, typically genres, registers or modes of communication. They
embody ontological knowledge.
Principle 46 entails, of course, a form of ontological knowledge. This
changes our view of the FLOB and BNC1994 sampling structures – they
are expressions of ontological knowledge, they represent a conscious
design and they are key to understanding how the sets in Figure 6.1
91 This is itself, of course, an a priori assumption of corpus linguistics included within
Principle 1, but it is an hypothesis that had been widely corroborated, especially in the
context of register studies – see Biber (1988, 1995).
210
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 223 -->

interact. While if we take a sentence inventory approach to our sampling
structure, the sets C and D do not (or are highly unlikely to) have an
intersection, when our corpus is composed of types of text, then the
intersection is formed at the ontological level, with similarity in design
across corpora being possible and replication is enabled.
For example, let’s shift the population being studied to all texts and
spoken interactions in British English produced in 1990–1992, shown in
Figure 6.2. Let us assume set A is the set of texts that fall within the FLOB
sampling structure and set B is the set of texts (both written and repre-
senting orthographically transcribed speech) that fall within the
BNC1994 sampling structure. These sampling structures represent a
structured view of language (in line with Principle 1), based on the a
priori assumption that meaningful linguistic variation occurs, to lesser or
greater degrees, across the elements that make up the sampling structure
as propensity varies. C and D are the FLOB and the BNC1994 corpora,
respectively. Both corpora include types of text – for example, academic
writing, newspaper material – though D also includes orthographically
transcribed spoken interactions. While none of the types of spoken
interaction are shared between the two, some of the text types are
shared– for example, newspapers. Hence, unlike the example discussed
in relation to Figure 6.1, there is a non-empty intersection (C⋂D ≠∅) at
A
C
D
B
U
E
F
Figure 6.2 The population of texts and spoken interactions (U) in British English in the early
1990s, sampling frames/structures (A and B) and two corpora, FLOB and the BNC1994 (C and D)
6.2 Approaching a Study: Replication
211
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

<!-- page 224 -->

this ontological level, but not equivalence. For example, FLOB contains
belle lettres, a category not present in the BNC1994 sampling structure.
Similarly D\C because, for example, the BNC1994 contains spoken
language, FLOB does not. Logically, E=Ø and F=Ø as the sampling
structure is either 1) shared between A and B or 2) it is not shared by
A and B. Hence, we cannot, for example, have something which is neither
in C⋂D nor C\D.
The existence of non-empty C⋂D ≠∅means that, as long as results
are replicated with reference to this intersection, then some degree of
exact replication is possible if, and only if, this intersection can be asserted
between the two sampling structures. Yet, as noted previously, C\D and
D\C are important too and help us to ensure that a replication based on
these two corpora allow us to begin to test hypotheses that may extend
beyond the corpus itself – if, for example, we ﬁnd corroboration for a
claim based on C in C⋂D, but a conditional falsiﬁer for that claim in D\C,
then we know that extending our claim through Principle 4 is problematic
– what we are saying is true of what is observed (Principle 50), yet we
have cause to doubt that it goes beyond that (using Principle 4) because of
the ﬁnding in D\C. For example, we make a claim based on the writing
in C that is corroborated by the writing in D, but we ﬁnd a conditional
falsiﬁer for the claim in the spoken material in D. This is the important
way in which differences in replication may lead to insights that repeti-
tion based on exact similarity cannot. We gain a control on the infer-
ences we may make beyond what is directly observed in the data used
to develop a hypothesis. While through Principles 4 and 50 we may
make observations in a set within U, by inference we make assump-
tions about the totality of U. Importantly, the approach increases the
chance of falsiﬁcation. If we make an inference based on C, then,
during replication, in D\C we ﬁnd a conditional falsiﬁer for that infer-
ence, replication has been helpful and it has represented a more
rigorous test of our hypothesis, fully in line with Principle 290, and
will contribute to the cyclical reﬁnement of the hypothesis as Principle
210 allows.
6.3 Final Points
So what have we learnt from this discussion of replication? The suitability
of corpora for carrying out replication studies is, crucially, determined by
the research design of the original study, the sampling structure of the
corpus and the conception that the researcher has for what determines
the set of objects that need to be studied to permit replication. The view of
212
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 225 -->

a corpus as a set of sentence types is likely to confound replication. This is
largely because the deﬁnition of sentence type within the structuralist tradi-
tion, for example, produced ontological knowledge with low generalisability.
As shown in McEnery and Wilson (2001), if we look at sentence types in a
number of ways, sentences appear unique, that is, each is one element of any
such ontology, and the chance of classifying another sentence in this category
is vanishingly low beyond trivial examples such as boilerplate statements at
the start of books. Yet the ontological knowledge corpus linguists rely upon
to construct corpora does not make the assumption that sentence type is the
root of the ontological knowledge that we might express – this was an
advance in corpus linguistics as it permitted a social scientiﬁc approach to
observational study that encourages replicability. So, for example, by con-
ceiving of a range of more generalisable ontological categories (e.g. genres/
registers) and using them to drive the process of corpus construction and
comparison, an intersection of corpora is much more likely to be observed.
This leads to a further principle:
Principle 47: The process of investigating corpus data has helped linguists show,
by repeated investigation, how elements of ontological knowledge can, by
convention, be assumed to be meaningful when used to structure our obser-
vation of language through sampling. This, in turn, controls our view of the
process of replication enshrined in Principle 48.
Note that, for simplicity, we have considered, with one exception
(Brezina and Gablasova 2015), the comparison of two corpora in this
chapter. Other test designs may require different conﬁgurations of data.
For example, assuming that we are building corpora of our own, then:
• In contrasting varieties of English using the Brown family model, we
might alter U to cover all varieties of English and apply the same
sampling structure to that set to build corpora by selecting a speciﬁc
variety of English, for example, we build three corpora representing,
American, British and Canadian English. The corpora can be con-
ceived of as being identical at the level of corpus design but, when
exploring the corpora, we would be looking for the actions of propen-
sity on the corpora and may hypothesise that the cause of any such
difference relates to a variety of English.
• In contrasting languages we would set U to cover all human languages
and then proceed as we would in the preceding study of American and
6.3 Final Points
213
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 226 -->

British English, with the exception that ﬁndings may be the result of
both propensity and features of the language in question.
We conclude this chapter by returning to its start point – Leech (2003).
Leech initially had a research design in mind related to modal and semi-
modal verbs and their variation in time and across varieties. His study
was observational, drawn from U (the universal set of texts in English in
the late twentieth century). Within U, he gathered new cross-sections of
written language (which we will call corpora B and D) based on the same
genre-based sampling structure used to produce the ﬁrst members of the
Brown family (LOB and Brown, which we will call corpora A and C). That
sampling structure rested on an a priori assumption that this frame repre-
sented a linguistically meaningful ontology. The four corpora represent
distinct samples produced by slightly varying the parameter of time and
variety when applying the sampling structure to take a cross-section – these
variations generated proper subsets of the texts that fell within the sam-
pling structure. Two sets (A and B) selected only British English texts
while two (C and D) selected only American English texts. A and C were
drawn from texts written in 1961 while B and D were drawn from texts
written in 1991. Conceptually, overlap between the texts in terms of the
texts included should not happen – the three parameters used to create the
corpora (sampling structure, variety of English and time) should ensure
that the four sets generated are discrete from one another in terms of
speciﬁc texts that are members of A, B, C and D. However, all four are
equivalent in terms of the ontological scheme behind the sampling struc-
ture and the sampling structure itself. Based on his observations of these
corpora, Leech began a trend analysis by testing hypotheses including 1)
there is a variation in modal use between varieties (A and B compared to C
and D) and 2) modal usage is changing over time (A compared to B and C
compared to D). His tests indicated that both hypotheses were corrobo-
rated, and that they could be reﬁned to claims such as 1) American English
uses fewer modals than British English; 2) modal usage is declining over
time and 3) the decline in American English is more pronounced than in
British English. Leech et al. (2010) represents a replication of this work,
providing a genuine test of the 2003 work as surprise (Principle 16″) is
provided by a reﬁnement of the four corpora to exclude the possibility
that errors in part-of-speech tagging may have produced erroneous
results. The study then proceeds to corroborate, rather than condition-
ally falsify, the 2003 ﬁndings. Based on these studies, hypotheses are
formed to seek to explain the results and, by induction based on these
ﬁndings, future trends are anticipated. This is then tested in Baker (2017),
214
Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 227 -->

who adds two further corpora into the study (E, British English and F,
American English) drawn from around 2006, as will be discussed in more
detail in the next chapter. That study replicates the previous study and
corroborates it, while the surprise provided by the extension of the period
of time observed also allows the hypothesis of continued decline over time to
be corroborated.
This summary shows how the studies looked at in the earlier part of the
chapter can be viewed, with some beneﬁt of the doubt given, within the
framework of replication introduced. Of course, the replication is not
expressed formally in any of the works cited – also, the precise goal of the
replication is not formally expressed either. Nonetheless, these studies
are replications.
However, given the trouble we had in conceiving them as such, and the
trouble we may have in deﬁning precisely the surprise produced in each
case (e.g. we lack a summary of changes made to the data from the 2003
study in the 2010 study), corpus linguists should abide by Principle 48:
Principle 48: In undertaking repetition and replication, corpus linguists should be
clear about what they are doing, and their goals in doing so. Treatment design
and experimental design should be clearly identiﬁed and, in the case of
replication, a clear explanation given of what surprise has been introduced,
what hypotheses are potentially impacted by this, what potential outcomes
were anticipated and what results were achieved.
Principle 48 is important as replications, providing as they do corro-
borations and falsiﬁcations as well as a way of testing inferences made
on the basis of previous observations, are an essential feature of corpus
linguistics, not an afterthought. To be truly effective, however, the
purpose and goals of replication and repetitions must be explicit as
‘replications are organized in some speciﬁc scheme’ with replications
being ’complete, including all treatments; or incomplete, including only
some’ (Gauch 2012: 183). With such a conscious design and purpose in
mind for replication, it then becomes easier to invoke Principle 4 and
select, for example, suitable statistical tests to apply to the results of the
experimental design – such tests are particularly important in working
on the incomplete replications, ‘reducing estimated errors to increase
statistical signiﬁcance and adjusting treatment estimates closer to their
true values’ (Gauch 2012: 183). This in turn clearly links to propensity –
6.3 Final Points
215
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 228 -->

while we cannot derive true probabilities, through working with our
corpus data and replication, we may be able to better, though not
perfectly, model propensity. As critical realists this is a credible goal
for us to aim for.
In the following chapter, we undertake an explicit replication of the
Leech modals study, focusing on modals rather than semi-modals. This
replication returns to two concepts, propensity and sampling structure,
and considers a rarely performed type of replication in corpus
linguistics.

Repetition and Replication
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.008 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 229 -->

CHAPTER SEVEN
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
In this chapter, we demonstrate how replication operates in practice in
corpus linguistics. In Chapter 6, we deﬁned replication broadly as a
process in which our understanding based on prior research is confronted
with some new empirical evidence consonant with a previous study.
Replication thus introduces an element of surprise. We also discussed
the topic of repetition, a conﬁrmatory process that attempts to achieve
the same results as previous research using the same data, tools and
methods. It is important to stress, at this point, that there exists a con-
tinuum between repetition and replication (and beyond). This conti-
nuum is a continuum between simple repetition and new original
research, which empirically tests hypotheses for the ﬁrst time.
Generally, we recognise ﬁve major types of studies (approaches) on
the continuum between repetition and an original study. These are listed
below.
1. Direct repetition: The use of the same data, tools and methods for the
purposes of checking the accuracy and transparency of the original
study.
2. Repetition with variation: Repetition with one or more of the three
components – data, tools and methods – slightly modiﬁed, for exam-
ple, a corpus with more accurate annotation.
3. Direct replication: The use of new data and the same methodology to
answer a research question from previous research.
4. Broad replication: Confrontation of any aspect of prior research with
new evidence.
5. Original study: A study that tests a hypothesis for the ﬁrst time.
Of these, 2–4 are part of the broad deﬁnition of replication; however,
even an original study (5) requires openness to falsiﬁcation according to
Principle 16″ and thus becomes inherently a part of the replication cycle.
Replication is thus not a Cinderella of (social) science, ‘a vanishing art’ as
Fresse and Peterson (2017) refer to it in the context of social science, but
an essential part of (social) scientiﬁc epistemology. This has been recog-
nised in the joint statement by editors of thirty major biomedical journals,
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 230 -->

which points out that ‘[r]eproducibility, rigour, transparency and inde-
pendent veriﬁcation are cornerstones of the scientiﬁc method’ (Nature
2014: 7).
To return to our metaphor of foundations introduced in Chapter 1, the
replication cycle is the process of building scientiﬁc knowledge, an ediﬁce
that gradually emerges from the mist of the metaphysical and that rests
on the pillars – principles – outlined throughout this book. In line with
critical rationalism (Principle 190), we need to admit that this building will
never be fully completed, new replication cycles will bring new elements
of surprise that will require us to go back to the drawing board and
redesign the structure, in whole or part. Nonetheless, openness to falsi-
ﬁcation as a prerequisite (Principles 16″, 170, 18″, 210, 24 and 34) and
replication as a direct consequence (Principle 48) prevent our building
from becoming an ivory tower of the metaphysical, an unassailable castle
in the air that rests on our preconceptions and wishes rather than on
evidence drawn from our contact with quasi-reality.
In this chapter we explore replication with a practical example. We
return to modal verbs as discussed in Chapter 6 with reference to Leech
(2003) and Leech et al. (2010). We also include evidence about modals
from Baker (2017: 163ff.), which extends the discussion in Leech et al.
(2010). Yet rather than seeking to explore these through repetition alone,
we engage with replication. Table 7.1 presents an overview of the three
studies and includes our replication study, which is discussed in this
chapter. Note that the studies include elements of the ﬁve major types
of studies discussed previously with Leech (2003) representing an origi-
nal study, Leech et al. (2010) repetition with variation, Baker (2017)
broad replication and direct replication of the previously listed study
(Chapter 6).
The focus in all four studies is on the investigation of the use of modal
verbs in British and American English. Modal verbs are an example of a
grammatical category with a well-established pattern of variation
between British and American English, that is, an overall decline in
both varieties, as indicated in the original study (Leech 2003). Our
replication study will cast a searchlight on speciﬁc claims made in
Leech et al. (2010) in particular to see whether these can be falsiﬁed or
corroborated, speciﬁcally with regard to claims made about differences
between British and American English. Likewise our results have the
potential to corroborate or falsify Baker’s (2017) corroboration of Leech
et al. (2010).
To proceed, we will need suitable data. In our case, we replicate BE06
and AmE06, two corpora from the Brown family of corpora, which were

<!-- page 233 -->

used in Baker (2017). This allows us to carry out direct replication, a
process that is very rare in corpus linguistic largely for practical reasons,
that is, the time and resources required for compiling corpora.
Baker (2017) introduced surprise to his broad replication of Leech et
al. (2010) by extending the trend analysis, for some of the modals studied,
through time. In our replication, we are going to 1) increase the empirical
content of the Leech et al. (2010) and Baker (2017) studies diachronically
and synchronically, respectively; 2) use the same sampling structure as
Leech et al. (2010) and Baker (2017); 3) introduce surprise to the replica-
tion; 4) increase the potential falsiﬁability of the ﬁndings; and 5) con-
tribute to the shift from the metaphysical to the social scientiﬁc.
In the Brown family, as we have seen, we may assemble corpora to look
at synchronic variation, in this case between two varieties of English, while
also considering diachrony by providing further matched corpora through
time. However, at any point in time, we have a potential problem – we have
no guarantee that our set of documents is typical. While we may try to
replicate our ﬁndings by subdividing a corpus we have – as happens in a
basic way with the test and training corpus model (see Section 6.2) – if we
have a generally atypical sample, it will not reveal itself to be atypical by
this method. That would cast a potential metaphysical veil over our
replication.
To address this issue, we need multiple matched corpora for each
variety of English at each time point so that we could see whether, in
fact, there is a strong equivalence to the corpora that supports inferencing
using Principle 4. This is where we encounter the metaphysical realm. To
test whether something like the BE06 corpus may be assumed to be
typical of the set of documents it is drawn from, we would, at the very
least, need two other BE06 corpora, what we may call BE060 and BE06″,
on which we could try to replicate the ﬁndings from BE06. With BE060
alone we generate the possibility of disagreement. With BE06″ as well we
have the minimum number of corpora we would need to see where what
we might call a majority view on any inference might be formed. Yet
BE060 and BE06″ do not exist – they are initially metaphysical constructs.
Our interest here is to explore the idea of replication in practice and to
highlight issues that attempting a replication of a corpus analysis raises.
Some of these relate to the way in which replication is carried out while
others lead us to consider what we may expect of replication in the social
sciences and also to a consideration of what statistical measures may – or
may not – help us to achieve when seeking to undertake both replication
and falsiﬁcation. Throughout, our focus will be upon the process of
replication. While we do replicate the modal verbs study, we do not
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
221
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 234 -->

pursue that goal as fully as we would if we were solely interested in the
outcome of the replication. Our study is guided more by the desire to
explore and critique the process of replication itself.
7.1 Method: Genuine Test and Surprise
In our study, in line with Principle 12, we have replicated two corpora
twice: the BE06 (Baker 2009) and the AmE06 (Potts and Baker 2012),
producing BE060, BE06″, AmE060 and AmE06″.92 Using these corpora,
we may start testing existing hypotheses about modal verbs as well as the
typicality of the original BE06 and AmE06 corpora. This is part of the
direct replication of Baker (2017), which itself is a broad replication of
Leech et al. (2010). We therefore both confront hypotheses from Leech
et al. (2010) with new empirical evidence and extend the empirical range
of the study by providing a greater chance of falsiﬁcation. In doing so, we
add surprise to the study.
Table 7.2 lists basic information about the original BE06 and AmE06
corpora used in Baker (2017). These corpora have a similar structure
across ﬁfteen major genre categories, which are displayed in Table 7.3.
Overall, each of the corpora consists of 500 texts with each text contain-
ing approximately 2,000 words.
The replication corpora AmE060, AmE06″, Be060 and BE06″ follow
exactly the same sampling structure (shown in Table 7.3) as the original
corpora used in Baker (2017). A summary of the makeup of the replica-
tion study corpora is shown in Table 7.4.
Thus we have a trio of corpora available for each variety of English
that we will refer to as two sets: AM = {AmE06, AmE060, AmE06″} and
BR = {BE06, BE060, BE06″}.

corpora.

<!-- page 236 -->

RQ2: In either set AM or BR, will results from one corpus be replicated
approximately in another?
#LancsBox v. 5 (Brezina et al. 2015, 2021) was employed to annotate
and search the corpora. The annotation (pos-tagging and lemmatisation)
is provided by TreeTagger (Schmid 1994), integrated in #LancsBox. The
following search terms were used in combination with the MD pos-tag
from the PENN-tagset:93 can, could, may, might, must, shall, should, will,
would.
A few further points about the design of the replication study need to
be made clear. BE06 and AmE06 were both generated using the same
sampling structure, O, from U, all texts that were in English current
around 2006 (T1). Within U, we considered two notional sets, based on
an axiom by convention: B, the set of all British English texts, bounded
by time, and A, the set of all American English texts, bounded by time.
Both A and B are bound by T1. By sampling twice within U, ﬁrst using O
and B, and then using O and A, we produce BE060, BE06″, AmE060 and
AmE06″. Within each set, B and A, an axiom by convention has
assigned texts from B and A, in accordance with the Brown sampling
structure (O), such that there are no texts shared in common between
the corpora. So, for example, for AM, texts are selected from A such
that the intersection (∩) between the corpora is an empty set (Ø). This
can be formally written as: AmE06∩AmE060=Ø, AmE060∩AmE06″=Ø
and AmE06∩AmE06″=Ø. The same applies for BR: BE06∩BE060=Ø,
BE060∩BE06″=Ø and BE06∩BE06″=Ø.

<!-- page 237 -->

So far we have drawn on three axioms by convention. AC1 con-
trols membership of the set of British or American English texts.
The second and third axioms derive from O – AC2 determines the
proportion of texts to occur in each corpus while AC3 controls the
selection of speciﬁc texts or text samples for inclusion in a corpus.
AC1 is operationalised here as the axiom used by Baker (2009) in the
construction of the BE06 and AmE06, discussed here with reference to
the BE06:
At the start of the corpus building project it was hypothesised that academic
journals containing the word British would be a good potential source of
data, although this did not prove to be the case as many contributors to such
journals were American or Australian. Deﬁnitions based on the author
were considered instead, although here a distinction could be made
between an author who was born in the UK, an author who currently
lived in the UK, and an author who (had) mainly lived in the UK. It was
decided that the latter case would be the most fruitful in yielding British
texts. (Baker 2009: 318–319)
In addition, the two sets B and A are bounded by time, which also
forms part of AC1. However, there is a slight mismatch in the period
of time represented in both corpora – BE06 sampled from 2005 to
2007 (Baker 2009: 316). AmE06 sampled from 2004 to 2008 (Potts
and Baker 2012: 302). We retained these time spans for the replica-
tion, hence this element of AC1 varies by variety. We note, however,
that the operation of propensity across slightly different timeframes
weakens the approximation between the two varieties represented in
the corpora.
The axiom itself is acknowledged not to be ideal (Baker 2009: 319) but,
as in trend studies, for the beneﬁt of ensuring that the study undertaken
was as close to the original study as possible, we retained this axiom.
However, the variable application of it by the analysts who constructed
AM and BR is, we note, one possible explanation for any difference
observed.
The development of AC2 is described by Baker (2009: 313) who
explains how the Brown corpus sampling structure was created:
The Corpus, which contained 500 texts, each approximately 2,000 words
in length, consisted of four main genres of writing: Press (about 18%),
Fiction (25%), Academic Writing (16%) and General Prose (41%),
which were further divided into ﬁfteen sub-genres. These genres and
their subdivisions had been decided by the participants at a conference
held at Brown University in 1963 (Francis and Kučera 1979), and the
7.1 Method: Genuine Test and Surprise
225
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 238 -->

proportions were based on the amount of actual publications during
1961.
In using AC2, we are consciously adopting the framework from 1961,
which, of course, entails issues such as those discussed with regard to
trend studies in Section 6.2.1. For historical reasons, and reﬂecting genre
differences between British and American English (Johansson et al.
1986), there is a slight variation in the number of texts in categories E,
F and G. All other categories are represented by exactly the same
number of texts.
AC3 is largely a function of the Brown sampling structure – that
determines the number and type of each text as well as the length of
each text sample. We also matched Baker (2009: 319) in sampling 1)
proportions of beginnings, middles and ends in texts and 2) a sample of
whole texts.
In applying AC3 to build the BE06, Baker (2009: 317–318) dis-
cusses an attempt to produce matched samples, that is, building
something closer to a cohort study by taking samples from the
same source as had provided the texts in the immediately preceding
corpus in the Brown family. However, as he concludes that it was
done ‘where possible’ but that typically ‘collection had to be more
opportunistic’ (Baker 2009: 318), we set aside this attempt at produ-
cing a matched sample for the purpose of this study as it would have
been, at best, partially and erratically possible, in our opinion, for
example, as some texts, or parts of texts (beginnings, middles and
ends) are so short that a further pair of samples could not be taken
from them.
We will investigate all corpora in AM and BR using #LancsBox v 5. This
software encodes both the axiom by convention that deﬁnes what a word is
in our study (AC4) and the statistical procedures that form the axioms by
convention that we explore our hypotheses with (these will be named and
numbered when used). We will take as an a priori assumption that there
exists a set of core modals, M, in English.94 Membership of M is determined
by an axiom by convention (AC5) taken from Leech et al. (2010: 72) which
states that:
core modals . . . can be said to express central modal meanings of possi-
bility, necessity, permission, obligation, prediction and volition, as well as
94 We exclude ought (to) and need (to) as they have low frequencies in the original study
and their frequencies are low in the new corpora also.
226
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 239 -->

to share the syntactic properties associated with the class of auxiliaries in
English – including the so-called NICE properties (negation, inversion,
code, emphasis).95
The result is M={can, could, may, might, must, shall, should, will,
would}.96 For the purpose of the hypotheses outlined in the following
text, we will conceive also of two versions of this set, AM-M and BR-M,
which covers the modals in American and British English, respectively.
While the membership of those sets is identical, if viewed as a set ordered
by frequency of occurrence, the ordering of the modals between AM-M
and BR-M may vary.
Based on an exploration of the corpora in AM and BR, we will attempt
to falsify the following hypotheses in Leech et al. (2010) that have been
partially explored in Baker (2017). In what follows, we will cite Leech et
al. (2010) and formulate hypotheses based on that quotation. The
hypotheses use some basic mathematical notation for precision. This
notation is explained in the following box.
Mathematical Notation: Explanation

point Tn.
Function (argument1, argument2 . . . argumentn) . . . this format is used for
noting functions and their arguments, such as the frequency function F of a
set of modals M at a particular point in time T1 written as F(M, T1).

ple, M.
∅indicates an empty set, which is a set with no elements.

The ‘overall picture of the modal auxiliaries in gradual decline is
unquestionable’ (Leech et al. 2010: 71).
Hypothesis 1: Between a point in time T1 and a following point of
time T2, the frequency F of members of modal set M at each
points, F(M, Tn), will be such that F(M, T1)>F(M, T2).97
Hypothesis 2: Hypothesis 1 is true for both British and American English.
Leech et al. (2010: 73) claim that in the decline of modals there is a
‘bottom-weighting’ of the frequency loss . . . less common modals have
declined more than frequent ones . . . . The four most common modals,
would, will, can and could account to 67.5% of all modal usage in 1961,
and in 1991/2 this ﬁgure has risen to 71.9%.
Hypothesis 3: The following is true about two subsets of modals
within M, the high-frequency modals H={would, will, can, could}
and
the
low-frequency
modals
L={may,
might,
must,
shall,
should}: between a point in time T1 and a following point in
time T2, 1) the ratio F(H, T1)/F(M, T1) < F(H, T2)/F(M, T2)
and 2) the ratio F(L, T1)/F(M, T1) > F(L, T2)/F(M, T2).
Hypothesis 4: The subsets H and L have the same members over time
and across British and American English.
[T]here is constancy in the pattern of change: the order of frequency of the
modals is the same in both 1961 and 1991/2, and what is particularly striking
(except in the case of can) is the consistency of the overall decline. (Leech et al.
2010: 73).
Hypothesis 5: The sets AM-M and BR-M are ordered sets, with the order
being determined by frequency of occurrence in Brown F(Brown) and
LOB F(LOB) respectively.
Based on the ﬁndings provided in Leech et al. (2010: 73–74) we can also
hypothesise:
97 The interval between T1 and T2 is not really discussed in any great depth other than to
say that the thirty-year gap between, e.g. LOB and FLOB, represents generational
change (Leech et al. 2010: 27). Accordingly, this hypothesis should be assumed to
have a space of one or more decades between T1 and T2 in all hypotheses in which
these points of time are discussed from this point.
228
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

<!-- page 241 -->

Hypothesis 6: Between a point in time T1 and a following point of time T2,
the ordering of the set AM-M, based on frequency of occurrence, will be
the same.
Hypothesis 7: Between a point in time T1 and a following point of time
T2, the ordering of the set BR-M, based on frequency of occurrence,
will be mostly the same.
Hypothesis 8: Irrespective of the variety of English, the frequency of any
member of sets AM-M and BR-M declines over time, with the excep-
tion of can and could in set BR-M.
Note the interdependence between the hypotheses. For instance, if
Hypothesis 1 is not true, Hypothesis 2 cannot be true. Note also that
the hypotheses rely on a number of assumptions such as the a priori
deﬁnition of the set of modals provided by AC5. Before any empiri-
cal study, it is, therefore, important to clearly state the assumptions
we make (e.g. axioms by convention) as well as the hypotheses that
we are testing. The mathematical notation used helps us with the
precision and conciseness of the formulations.

7.2 Results: Hypothesis 1 and Hypothesis 2
First, let us look at the results comparing frequency and rank order of
FLOB against BR and Frown against AM in Tables 7.5 and 7.6,
respectively.
Let us now consider Hypothesis 1 and Hypothesis 2. Both Tables 7.5
and 7.6 show clearly that we fail to falsify these hypotheses. No matter
which member of AM we use, or which member of BR we use, both
hypotheses are corroborated. For both British and American English
(Hypothesis 2), the frequencies of modals in FLOB and Frown, respec-
tively, are considerably higher than frequencies of modals in any of BR
and AM sets of corpora. Here, F(M, T1)>F(M, T2), as Hypothesis 1
states, thus holds true.
These two ﬁndings then lead us to reﬂect back on our overarching
research questions. From the perspective of the replications of
Hypothesis 1 and Hypothesis 2, the answer to both RQ1 and RQ2 is
‘yes’: two of Leech et al.’s (2010) hypotheses were tested on BR and AM
and there was no variation in the outcome in the terms established by the
hypotheses in data gathered to test the hypotheses in new, yet similar,
data sets. This might suggest that, for Hypothesis 1 and Hypothesis 2,
propensity is modelled well enough in AM and BR that it has no effect on
our tests of it. However, we should not jump to conclusions yet. As can be

<!-- page 243 -->

noted from the last column in Tables 7.5 and 7.6, the results for BR and
AM are in some ways not exactly the same: the max-min distance shows a
wide range of variation in the observed frequencies of the core modals in
AM and BR – as high as 129%. We never see exact replication of the
frequencies (a max-min of 0%) across the three corpora. So some degree
of variation is clearly at play, yet it is not enough to falsify Hypothesis 1
and Hypothesis 2. In this case we may speculate that the effect identiﬁed
by Leech et al. (2010) – the decline of core modals in English over time –
is so pronounced that it sets, within the sampling structure used, the
variations caused by propensity in the shade. So might it be, as we
proceed to more precise claims, that we will see the operation of propen-
sity falsify some of the hypotheses? Hypothesis 3 and Hypothesis 4 allow
us to start that process.
7.3 Results: Hypothesis 3 and Hypothesis 4
Here we are reducing the range of our observation, in line with Principle
210 and testing hypotheses about two smaller sets. Based on Leech et al.
(2010: 73) we can specify the sets we need to test these hypotheses, which
have the same members in both American and British English:
H = {would, will, can, could}
L = {may, might, must, shall, should}
A simple initial test of Hypothesis 3 can be performed by looking at Tables
7.5 and 7.6. If the membership of H is stable, then in all corpora of AM and
BR the members of H should occupy ranks 1–4 in terms of frequency. This is
corroborated for both AM and BR, lending credence to the membership of
both sets. Yet there is a claim relating to frequency linked to H and L that
this does not test. The H modals could retain their rank but lose frequency as
fast as L. Indeed, they may even lose frequency faster than L to an extent and
still retain their ranks. What we need to see, between the data from 1991/2
and that in the corpora collected around 2006, is a shifting of proportions of
H and L in BR and AM: F(H, T1)/F(M, T1) < F(H, T2)/F(M, T2) and
F(L, T1)/F(M, T1) > F(L, T2)/F(M, T2) – if the loss is ‘bottom-weighted’ as
Leech et al. (2010: 73) claim, then the proportion of modals in any given
corpus of AM or BR should have shifted in favour of H. Table 7.7 shows
what has happened.
Hypothesis 3 is not falsiﬁed in BR, but it is falsiﬁed, once, in the
pairing of Frown at T1 and the original AmE06 at T2 because, accord-
ing to that data, 1) F(H, T1)/F(M, T1) ≮F(H, T2)/F(M, T2) and 2)
F(L, T1)/F(M, T1) ≯F(L, T2)/F(M, T2). Looking at speciﬁc numbers, 1)
7.3 Results: Hypothesis 3 and Hypothesis 4
231
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 244 -->

74.47% ≮73.54% and 2) 25.53% ≯26.46%. Hypothesis 3 is thus falsi-
ﬁed. Interestingly, in pairings of Frown with the replication corpora
AmE060 and AmE06″, Hypothesis 3 is corroborated.
Overall, while we do not claim that the hypothesis is rejected on the
basis of this single result, we may see propensity step from the shadows
at this point: there is variation between the matched corpora that can
lead to falsiﬁcation. Of course, we may also wish to turn our searchlight
in the direction of this ﬁnding and explore it in more detail. For the
moment, we will pass on to consider Hypothesis 4. It is difﬁcult to assess
Hypothesis 4 because we have not really provided the axiom by con-
vention that deﬁnes the sets H and L. That in large part is because it is
not very clearly deﬁned by Leech et al. (2010). There is simply an
assertion of which set each modal belongs to. The intention, however,
seems to be to prise apart those modals that are declining rapidly from
those that have a shallower decline. So, to judge whether we can see the
sets being stable on those grounds, we should focus on this behaviour.
That is shown in Table 7.8.
It is hard to make a coherent axiom that supports the current
membership for H and L. If we look at American English (Brown
to Frown in Table 7.8), we might say there is an arbitrary cut-off at
−11.1% – losses bigger than that is part of deﬁning L. To factor in
frequency we will, once again arbitrarily, say that L members must
also have an occurrence less than 1,000 in the corpus. That leaves H
composed of the frequent and more slowly declining verbs. Unfortunately,

decline in a way that at least repeats Leech et al.’s (2010) categorisations.

H and L being homogeneous through time. Table 7.9 shows the results of

multiple falsiﬁcations to the extent that it looks like the rejection of the
hypotheses as they stand seems warranted. While no modals transition
from one set to the other, there is no corpus in AM or BR that does not, to
