some degree, falsify the two hypotheses. Of the two elements of AC6, only
one, the frequency criterion, seems to still separate the two groups – in all
cases the top four modals have frequencies in excess of 1,000 and the
remainder are below that threshold. While we might say that, as a group,
they take a greater proportion of any corpus through time, the idea that the
frequent are each becoming more frequent and the infrequent are becom-
ing increasingly less frequent does not stand – some of the frequent are
showing rapid decline (notably will in both BR and AM) while some of the
infrequent are static or barely declining (e.g. shall in AmE06 and might
BE060).
Propensity is clearly at work both through time and across the sets AM
and BR. There is not even one member of H selected by AC6 consistently
for both varieties and there is only one member of L that is consistently
selected by AC6 in AM and BR: must. So at the very least we must reject

<!-- page 247 -->

Hypothesis 4, as H and L clearly do not have the same members over
time, nor between varieties. As for Hypothesis 3, while we can ﬁnd
corroboration for the hypothesis by aggregating the top four frequencies
and setting those against the rest combined, we would have to question
the rationale for doing so. First, the behaviour of the members of each set
is not homogeneous – could is clearly acting very differently from the
other members of H and might seems to be far less subject to the
pressures associated with the set L, showing, in both varieties of
English, a rate of loss in both AM and BR that is lower than for all
other members of L and is consistently lower than for one member of H:
will. Second, AC6 is relatively arbitrary and breaks Principle 190 – the
setting of the parameters for AC6 was almost dogmatic, an axiom devel-
oped purely to align with a ‘truth’ that we were presented with – the four/
ﬁve split of modal verbs into H and L. This arbitrariness masks a wealth
of choice. For example, if we look back at Tables 7.5 and 7.6 and apply
our initial check more freely, we see that we could say that the top 6, top 8
or even all the modals represented a stable set – for example, in the top 6
for each variety in each corpus, the same modals occur. What if we had
placed our arbitrary cut-off there? We could alter our frequency thresh-
old for H to 600 and the two groups would separate out quite nicely and
Hypothesis 3 would still be satisﬁed.99 In reality, the behaviour of can and
could are likely drivers for the behaviour of the set H. Overall, while it
might be possible to revise Hypothesis 3 to match the observations made,
it does not seem to provide as much power and insight as we may have
thought – rather than characterising two homogeneous sets of modals
that are varyingly consistently, it presents us with a view in which modals
with different patterns of behaviour are aggregated in a way that masks
this variation, thus violating Principle 320. Crucially, it also masks the very
different behaviours of two modals: could, which appears to be increasing
in usage in American English, and might, which seems to be stemming its
decline. While we may split the modals so that we can generate an
observation in line with Hypothesis 3, in doing so we would suggest we
are running foul of Principle 13 – the observed in this case does not seem
to be of importance or utility. Accordingly, we will consider Hypothesis 3
and Hypothesis 4 as being rejected.

BE060 (88.43/11.57), BE06″ (87.64/12.36).

7.4 Results: Hypothesis 5, Hypothesis 6 and Hypothesis 7
Based on the analyses so far we can note that Hypothesis 5 is falsiﬁed
already. Table 7.10 shows the ranking data previously shown in Tables 7.5
and 7.6, supplemented with rank data for LOB and Brown.
Hypothesis 5 is corroborated by LOB and falsiﬁed once by Frown, yet
each corpus in AM and BR falsiﬁes Hypothesis 5 so we can assume it is
rejected – the frequency-based ordering of the modals in English over
time is not static. We might at this point want to assert that the order is
approximately the same over time. To do this, we would need an axiom
by convention that allows us to give some meaningful measure of the
similarity of ranks and then determine a threshold for where we believe

Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study

---

<!-- page 249 -->

things are similar enough to be called approximately the same. We will
not do that here as such an axiom will be called for shortly anyway (see
AC7 later in text). Yet before leaving the discussion of Hypothesis 5, we
note that its rejection points, once again, to the fact that propensity is at
work within the conﬁnes of our sampling structure. It stretches across
time and across varieties and while our sampling structure may limit it, it
does not, and we believe cannot, eliminate it.
Hypothesis 6 and Hypothesis 7 may be viewed as two hypotheses
that effectively replace Hypothesis 5. The effect of propensity on
variety is accommodated by providing separate hypotheses for each
variety. Hypothesis 6 retains a view of the ordering of modals that
excludes propensity operating upon them to effect change, while
Hypothesis 7 avoids the certainty of Hypothesis 6, but in doing so
begs a new axiom, as noted earlier, which gives a principled and
intersubjectively testable answer to the question of what does
‘mostly’ mean? Ideally, the answer to that question should have
some explanatory force – for example, we might posit that a rate
of change, over time, will be relatively constant and, in turn,
develop hypotheses about the rate and the cause of language
change more generally. Also, while we have worked, in the same
way as Leech et al. (2010), on the basis of simple statistics such as
ranks and proportions so far, we also could, in line with Principle 4,
use some suitable mathematical apparatus to support more rigorous
reasoning. Mathematics provides us with a wealth of choices when it
comes to implementing axioms by convention. Yet because they are
axioms by convention, the precise choice of which statistic to use
may be guided as much by personal preference as objective decision
making. In this case, a rather obvious choice for us to make would
be to use a rank correlation statistic as our new axiom: AC7. In our
case, we will use Spearman’s rank correlation (rs) (Brezina 2018:
142). We use this to divide results into positive, negative and no
correlation. We are interested in testing whether two points in time
are potentially correlated, so will focus upon positive correlations.
We also use as part of AC7 the work of Cohen (1988: 79–80) that
allows us to claim a small effect (SE) if 0.1< rs ≤0.3, a medium effect
(ME) if 0.3< rs ≤0.49 and a large effect (LE) if 0.49 < rs. It is
important to note that this convention is based on Cohen’s analysis
of correlations in behavioural sciences and that the decision about
the interpretation of a particular effect size such as rs is also disci-
pline speciﬁc (Plonsky and Oswald 2014).
7.4 Results: Hypothesis 5, Hypothesis 6 and Hypothesis 7
237
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 250 -->

In our replication study, we experimented by computing Spearman’s
correlations for pairs of corpora for both varieties of English. First we
looked at corpora through time (e.g. LOB to FLOB, FLOB to BE06,
FLOB to BE060) and then at the corpora in AM and BR in pairs. This
way we present the hypothesis with surprise once more – as well as seeing
whether it holds in the same variety of English between different points
in time, we also test how consistently it may be achieved in the same
variety of English at the same point in time. Table 7.11 shows the results
of these tests.
The results of the comparison are quite clear – they show not only that
the results are strongly correlated, but also show that the pattern of
variation between Brown, Frown and AM, on the one hand, and LOB,
FLOB and BR, on the other, are almost identical, as evidenced by the
scores given to the pairs of comparison in Table 7.11. So, AC7 gives us a
way of capturing numerically and consistently a set of relations that we
may have captured, as Leech et al. (2010) did, less formally. On the basis
of this, we reject Hypothesis 5 and revise Hypothesis 6 and Hypothesis 7
thus:

Hypothesis 60: Between a point in time T1 and a following point of time, T2,
the rank ordering (R), based on frequency, of the set AM-M will be such
that R(AM-M,T1)≈R(AM-M, T2), where approximation is satisﬁed if the
Spearman’s score (rs) of the comparison produces a large effect.
Hypothesis 70: Between a point in time T1 and a following point of time,
T2, the rank ordering (R), based on frequency, of the set BR-M will be
such that R(BR-M,T1)≈R(BR-M, T2), where approximation is satisﬁed
if the Spearman’s score (rs) of the comparison produces a large effect.
The replication thus brought new evidence that led us to answer ‘no’ to
both research questions in relation to Hypotheses 5–7 and produce a
restatement of Hypotheses 6 and 7 that is consistent with the new empiri-
cal evidence.
7.5 Results: Hypothesis 8
Table 7.12 sets out, in clear, binary terms (yes/no), whether a given word,
in a speciﬁc corpus, has declined in frequency relative to 1991/2. The data
used to make the decision are drawn from Tables 7.5 and 7.6. The axiom
applied here, AC8, is basic: if a word’s frequency has declined, the word/
corpus combination is rated as ‘yes’ (Y); if it is the same or has increased,
it is rated ‘no’ (N).
Hypothesis 8 is corroborated for may, must, shall, should, will and
would. For might we have a falsiﬁcation arising in British English from
BE060, that is, it differs from the result provided by BE06.

Hypothesis 8 also states that there is an exception to the decline for can
and could in BR-M. However, this exception is falsiﬁed for can in all BR
and for could in BE060 and BE06″; it is corroborated for could in BE06.
Interestingly, in falsiﬁcation of Hypothesis 8, which offers no exception
for can and could in AM-M, could is reported as not to have declined (N)
in AmE06 and AmE06″.
7.6 Discussion
In line with our principles, we have exposed the hypotheses we started
with to ever more exacting tests and have reduced the range of our
hypotheses to make increasingly precise predictions, both by performing
more exacting tests and by increasing the empirical content of our study.
We need to consider these ﬁndings further because in the replication we
have not applied any tests from the inferential statistical toolbox. This is
because although Leech et al. (2010) note statistical signiﬁcance in their
study of modals, they do not rely on signiﬁcance tests to justify claims
they make; the same is true for Baker (2017). The reasons for this are not
stated expressly, but may be seen in part in Leech (2003: 228), which
explains that ‘too much should not be made of signiﬁcance tests in
comparative corpus studies’.
So what is our position on using tests of statistical signiﬁcance in corpus
linguistics? A cursory examination of the corpus linguistics literature will
swiftly show that, by convention, corpus linguists often appeal to statis-
tical signiﬁcance in a way not dissimilar to other disciplines. This raises
two questions: 1) should they and 2) what is the epistemological value of
such tests?
First, should we be swayed by signiﬁcance tests? Such tests have a long
history and play an important role in one approach to understanding prob-
ability in a complex world; they link a sample (corpus) to a population – all
texts and spoken data, if relevant, that the sample has been taken from. This
link is made through the null hypothesis and the p-value, ‘the probability
that the data would be as extreme as that observed if the null hypothesis
were true’ (Brezina 2018: 13). We covered our approach to probability, in a
rudimentary fashion, in Chapter 4. One of the key arguments in favour of
using probability in this way is that if we make observations, then, in spite of
the apparent disorder that may initially confront us, we can soon be sur-
prised by the ‘tendency of statistical averages to remain stable if the condi-
tions remain stable’, which is ‘one of the most remarkable characteristics of
our universe’ (Popper 1990: 12). This surprise and regularity may be seen in
our replication study – it is remarkable, in many ways, that some of the
240
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 253 -->

hypotheses in Leech et al. (2010) survived our attempts at falsiﬁcation and,
where they did not, survived sometimes in a modiﬁed form. It is particularly
remarkable because, unlike the ‘remarkable characteristics’ Popper was
commenting on, which were in the physical world, our surprise comes
from a construct in the social world, language, where we know that we are
not modelling all the potential relevant factors that we know of, even before
we consider the role that propensity may play in bringing unknown and
unseen factors to bear on the probabilities we see. Yet the very patterns that
we see are a further conﬁrmation of the play of propensity – consider the
following quote from Popper (1990: 18f.) who sees a world of propensities as
an unfolding process of realizing possibilities and of unfolding new possibi-
lities . . . This is clear in the physical world where new elements, new atomic
nuclei, are produced under extreme physical conditions of temperature and
pressure: elements survive only if they are not too unstable. And with the
new nuclei, with the new elements, new possibilities are created, possibilities
that previously simply did not exist. In the end, we ourselves become
possible.
It is very easy to see how the preceding quote could be repurposed to
encompass language change of just the sort we have seen operating on
core modals here. The play of probabilities in language use in context
brings about the possibility for enduring change where that change is
adopted within the speech community in which it is spawned. Such
change can indeed create new possibilities (e.g. meaning change, gram-
matical change). Yet the issue with the probabilities is that they are
unknown and this creates the ﬁrst problem for signiﬁcance statistics – in
the propensity view of probability ‘the theoretical concept of probability
is not deﬁned in terms of observables, but is introduced as an undeﬁned
term which is connected indirectly with observation’ (Gillies 1995: 109).
So while the frequencies we observe may give rise to reasonably stable
patterns, they are at best estimates as our contact with the reality of
probability is very similar to our contact with reality in general – in our
terms we are engaging with quasi-reality. This makes us reﬂect on the
epistemological status of any falsiﬁcation asserted on the basis of statis-
tical testing. Its status is such that we should, in part for reasons touched
upon in the preceding discussion, set falsiﬁcations based upon it into a
special class of falsiﬁcations, so-called methodological falsiﬁcations;
these are falsiﬁcations based on probability that, because of the operation
of propensity ‘are not strictly speaking falsiﬁable’ though ‘they should be
used in practice as falsiﬁable statements’ (Gillies 1995: 111). Consider the
following. The axiom AC8, which we worked with in Section 7.5, did
7.6 Discussion
241
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 254 -->

nothing more than compare two quantities and, on the basis of a compar-
ison rooted within simple mathematics, provided a yes/no outcome that,
in terms of mathematics itself, was secure. Yet if we are to apply statistical
testing, we are asking the question of whether the difference observed is
merely due to chance (sampling error) or is likely to be a real difference
in the population. This brings a number of additional assumptions con-
nected with the application of a given statistical test. So, while the
mathematical model behind a statistical test operates internally with an
appropriate rigour – we do not wish to question the fundamental princi-
ples of mathematics – the application of the test to linguistic reality
should not be taken for granted. Ultimately, the question of which
model ﬁts best a particular type of data is a matter of informed
judgement.
Hence we should be cautious when working with a methodological
falsiﬁcation – it is a falsiﬁcation that, in time, may be falsiﬁed itself as the
convention that accepts it as evidence changes. Those conventions do
change – over time earlier statistical tests such as the Chi-squared test
have been argued to be deﬁcient and replaced with other measures such as
the Log-likelihood test (Dunning 1993), also known as Likelihood Ratio
test, precisely because the probability distributions underlying them have
been assumed to be ‘invalid in most cases of statistical text analysis unless
either enormous corpora are used, or the analysis is restricted to only the
very most common words’ (Dunning 1993: 71). Later, the general applica-
tion of the Log-likelihood test and the Chi-squared test in corpus linguis-
tics has been criticised (e.g. Brezina and Meyerhoff 2014; Bestgen 2014;
Lijfﬁjt et al. 2016) for the violation of one of the basic assumptions of these
tests: the assumption of independence of observations, leading to largely
false positive results being reported in published literature. This shows that
when methodological proposals such as those of Dunning – or of others
who criticised the application of the test Dunning proposed in corpus
linguistics – are accepted, this should lead to turning the spotlight of
research onto methodological falsiﬁcations produced using the statistical
procedures now argued to be inappropriate. The possibility exists that the
new approach (such as Log-likelihood in the 1990s or Mann–Whitney U
test in 2010s) may also lead to methodological falsiﬁcations of previous
falsiﬁcations. Other methods have been argued for since then, including
proposals to abandon entirely the so-called null hypothesis model of sig-
niﬁcance testing (Kilgarriff 2005; Koplenig 2019; Vasishth and Galeman,
2021).
Such views are not limited to linguistics (cf. Woolston 2015) – in the
study of statistics itself there is a lively debate regarding the validity or
242
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 255 -->

otherwise of the type of approach that lies behind signiﬁcance tests, the
so-called frequentist approach. Some reject it and argue for a ‘new
statistics’ (Cumming 2012), central to which is a focus on effect sizes
rather than signiﬁcance tests, a focus we may contrast as ‘how big a
difference’ versus ‘how much evidence’ for a difference.100 Measures of
effect size are, once again, based in convention. In the replication study
(Section 7.4), we used Spearman’s correlation (rs) as a standard effect
size measure, while other frequently used effect size measures such as
Cohen’s d can also be usefully applied in corpus linguistics (Brezina 2018:
275ff.). The question readers are doubtless asking themselves is how to
reliably evaluate sizes of the effect, to put it prosaically ‘how big is a big
effect?’ This is where convention applies, which needs to be clearly
recognised (Plonsky and Oswald 2014). The new statistics also employs
conﬁdence intervals, estimations of ‘the true value of a statistical measure
(such as the mean) or a difference between two statistical measures (such
as the difference between two means) in the population’ (Brezina 2018:
13–14). Yet those conﬁdence intervals, just as discussed in Chapter 4 and
in this section, rely upon estimations based on distributions – and as such
are as vulnerable as null hypothesis testing to the type of attack launched
by Kilgarriff (2005). They are certainly no more immune to the type of
critique we have developed based on propensity – their power comes
from quasi-contact with what we may term real probabilities, not the real
probabilities themselves.
Even if we settle on an approach based on ‘new statistics’, choice still
lies before us. For example, the range of effect sizes we may use are as
many as the range of inferential statistics we may use and our guide in
using them is quite similar – we need to understand what the effect size
statistic is doing and the assumptions it encodes, as these constitute an
axiom by convention that, if we do not understand its operation, may
mean that we fundamentally misunderstand what the statistic is showing
us. This point is made clearly by Gablasova et al. (2017b), who describe
the assumptions made by a range of effect size measures used as axioms
by convention to determine collocation strength, showing that while we
may think of a set of measures as ‘effect size statistics’, they are not
homogeneous with regards to the assumptions they make.
So, while we may be satisﬁed that we were applying ever more rigorous
efforts to falsify the hypotheses of Leech et al. (2010) in our replication,
100 There are other features of the new statistics of course – including the pre-registration
of studies, the use of conﬁdence intervals and replication. However, for brevity we will
focus on effect size here because of its central place in the enterprise.
7.6 Discussion
243
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 256 -->

as we moved to methodological falsiﬁcations it may be the case, ulti-
mately, that we were relying more heavily on convention in asserting our
falsiﬁcations than we may have thought. This may make us reﬂect upon
what replication means in the study of language.
7.7 Afterglow
We have now tried to replicate the ﬁndings of Leech et al. (2010). In doing
so, we were interested in pursuing replication both to check some of the
ﬁndings and to reﬂect on the nature of replication in the study of language.
Our ‘searchlight’ moved from one hypothesis to the next, seeking falsiﬁca-
tions and, where necessary, amending hypotheses. We did not introduce
surprise in as many ways as we could – we could also have extended this
study further, using the Written BNC2014 corpus (Brezina et al. 2021) to
explore, at least in written British English, the hypotheses we examined
further through time.101 We could also have looked at Leech et al.’s (2010)
claims about modals in speech using the Spoken BNC2014, as Love and
Curry (2021) have in their study of modals in spoken British English
between the early 1990s and the early 2010s. This replication also achieved
mixed results while showing that some of Leech et al.’s (2010) claims about
core modals withstood falsiﬁcation.
In our study, we exposed Leech et al.’s (2010) ﬁnding to enough
surprise to see how, by introducing surprise, replication can make us
think about both claims made on the basis of corpus data and on the
nature of replication itself. Yet now the probing with our searchlight is
over, what light remains? What can we say about replication in corpus
linguistics?
The ﬁrst point we would make is that, given the demands made of the
Leech et al. (2010) study by surprise in our replication, the results stood
up remarkably well. While our hypotheses were less likely to survive the
more speciﬁc they were, in general, there is very little doubt that the
replication gave support to the view that core modals are generally in
101 For readers who wish to do this for themselves, they should revisit the exploration of the
hypotheses adding the following information. In the 90-million-word Written BNC2014,
the modals explored occur with the following frequency: will (223,406), can (202,711),
would (185,044), could (145,572), may (78,898), should (73,879), must (38,494), might
(54,415) and shall (6,433). Comparing these ﬁgures with the other corpora in this chapter
should be done with due caution, of course, as the sampling structure of the BNC2014
Written is not a perfect match for the Brown sampling structure. The size of the Written
BNC2014, as reported by #LancsBox X, is 89,689,740 words (separated by white space)
and 91,379,407 words (identiﬁed by morphological analysis).
244
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 257 -->

decline in both American and British English. Similarly, the decline is
more pronounced in American English. There is a corollary to these
ﬁndings – Leech et al.’s (2010) theorising about what this means in
terms of a theory of language change are broadly sustained by the
replication. So, while the challenges presented to Leech et al. (2010) by
falsiﬁcation did cause sufﬁcient falsiﬁcation to merit rejection in some
cases, it also greatly reinforced some of the main ﬁndings of their study.
We say that the results survived ‘remarkably well’. Why remarkably?
Much of the reasoning in Chapter 4 of this book explains our view. It is far
from clear that replication, of the sort that can be applied to the natural
sciences, should be expected in the social sciences. To set such an expec-
tation is to engage in another form of replacement naturalism (a view that
ﬁnds strong echoes in Grieve, 2021). As we reject replacement natural-
ism, we should also reject replication as practised in the natural sciences
and the strict demand for it in a whole range of areas, including in the new
statistics. That is why, early in this chapter, we introduced a lower barrier
for replication, approximation, that we view as being a more appropriate
measure by which to judge such attempts at reproducing ﬁndings in which
there is an element of surprise, that is, not a simple direct corrobora-
tion.102 At the same time we note that in this chapter although we have
engaged in falsiﬁcation when checking hypotheses, we have generally not
engaged in rejection. We are permissive of other studies, on other data
sets or using different methods, possibly corroborating what we here
conditionally falsify, precisely because we reject replacement naturalism.
What is not corroborated on our data sets using our methods may yet
prove to be corroborated on other data sets or through other methods.
While we believe this perhaps to be unlikely and proceed ourselves as
though it were not ‘true’, we permit the possibility that we may be wrong.
Yet beyond approximation and proceeding cautiously through falsiﬁca-
tion rather than rejection, more seems to be needed – and in our linking
of linguistics to the social sciences some possible ways forward are
revealed.
Bythinkingofcorpuslinguisticsasanalogoustoquantitativesocialscience,
we are able to draw on a long tradition of thinking about how to reconcile the
dynamism of the social with the focus on the quantitative. For example, the
results presented in our replication were normative – in spite of our appeal in
Principle 320 for the role of the individual not to be obscured in corpus
linguistics, the study we replicated did just that and, in turn, our replication
did the same. Yet quantitative social science has also warned against an
102 Note also the link to uncertain and ampliative reasoning as discussed in Section 3.3.
7.7 Afterglow
245
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 258 -->

‘unhealthy preoccupation with relationships between variables that fre-
quently obscure relations between people’ (Elliot 2005: 99). Language as
exploredinChapter5 has thegoalofenablingandmediatingcommunication
between people, not between variables. The call in this book for a pragmatic
approach to the use of corpus data, rooted in indeterminism and fallibilism, is
also found in the quantitative social sciences where a ‘pragmatic approach’
(Elliot 2005: 100) is called for in trying to balance the many pressures that are
brought to bear in trying to study the social. The result in quantitative social
science has often been to be realistic about what a quantitative approach to
the social can achieve, eschewing more complex models in favour of
principles that guide analyses, such as those of Lazarsfeld,103 who devel-
oped simple principles to guide researchers interpreting quantitative data in
a search for causation between two variables such as:
1. Cause must precede effect.
2. The two variables examined must be correlated or associated so that
variation in one variable predicts variation in the other.
3. The variation cannot be explained by a third variable that inﬂuences
both of the variables of interest.
Such rules certainly ﬁnd echoes in current practice in corpus linguistics
such as Gries’ call for a consideration of mixed effects models (Gries
2015) and the work of Brezina et al. (2015) looking at the effects shared
between pairs of collocations. They also connect clearly with the argu-
ment linking repeated sampling using a corpus sampling structure to
survey research – these rules, arising from survey research, clearly and
neatly encapsulate rules of guidance to be followed in diachronic corpus
analysis using such data.
Links discovered through the application of such procedures may not
all be of the same type, of course. What is, for example, the link between
may in American English and may in British English that, over time,
means that a shift in the frequency of may in American English may
foreshadow a similar shift in British English at a later point in time? The
link is almost certainly not proximal – that is, there is no immediate
connection between may declining in American English and may declin-
ing in British English. There is not some invisible bond between the two
that forces one to move as the other moves. That is why Leech et al.,
without labelling them as such, look at what quantitative social scientists
call distal factors – in this case social processes that generate the link, for
example, the prestige of one variety (American English) causing
103 The following summary of Paul Lazarsfeld’s thinking is after Elliot (2005: 99).
246
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 259 -->

variation in another variety (British English). The explanation is not only
social, but also clearly has the capacity to vary across a population – just
because enough speakers in one variety perceive another to be a prestige
variety to create a visible effect when language use is observed at the level
of the group, it is by no means certain that this attitude and behaviour will
manifest itself equally, or at all, in all individuals in that group. So an
accommodation of one variety to another in a case like this may vary
across a population, or more than one process may be involved, that is,
because enough speakers have shifted to accommodate American
English, over time those that are not inﬂuenced by American English
shift because they are inﬂuenced by their fellow British English speakers,
many of whom have accommodated to the American norm. While we will
not explore these possibilities here, we draw them out simply to show
how Elliot’s warning may apply – we should not let a focus on the links
between variables (the varying frequencies of modal verbs) obscure what
we are really looking at: links between people and, moreover, the varying
nature of those distal (governed by intervening variables) or proximal
(directly related) factors. These factors also apply in complex patterns to
primary (e.g. A causes B) and secondary (e.g. A causes B, but to an extent
is inﬂuenced by C) effects.
Hanging over all of this is the shadow of propensity – we can account
for and model for a subset, not all, of the relevant factors inﬂuencing
individuals and groups. If propensity leads to problems for modelling
natural processes, with no volition, its role is, as argued, much greater for
social actions, such as language, controlled and used by agents with
volition. Remember the caution against not trying to model anything as
complex as a horse race (Kő rner 1957: 79) – replicating an experiment
with a natural process is difﬁcult enough, and propensity may yet con-
found or make approximate such a replication. What chance do we have
of producing a successful replication of a horse race or any other complex
act involving social interaction and socially situated practices? If we
succeed in doing so, the challenge arising from such a replication is the
explanation of the ﬁndings arising from the application of Principle 4
where we may use statistics to guide our hypothesis formation, not
necessarily the work using Principle 4 itself, though that admittedly
creates the need for explanation, and sets the parameters within which
such explanation may be achieved.
If we craft a good explanation, and it rests on a sound statistical model
of some pattern of interaction of variables, should we then be satisﬁed?
Should we point to the mountain of data that the hypothesising conforms
with and the complex statistics we employ through Principle 4 and,
7.7 Afterglow
247
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 260 -->

contented, present our perfectly corroborated theory? Of course,
given the arguments in this book, we would say ‘no’. Yet again it
could be that corpus linguistics is following trends apparent in social
science where
The increased availability of longitudinal data . . . coupled with the develop-
ment of high-powered computers and new statistical techniques for dealing
with temporal data has led to optimism, among some authors, about the
advances in the ability of social sciences to make strong claims about caus-
ality. (Elliot 2005: 109)
Yet in corpus linguistics, as much as in social science in general, there is ‘a
growing body of evidence that stresses that social scientists should be
cautious in assuming that constructing a good model is equivalent to
establishing causality’ (Elliot 2005: 110–111). We would argue that such
caution should not simply be expected as a consequence of propensity,
but also be our epistemological position. As fallibilists, the task facing the
producers of models that comply with large data collections is to deﬁne
new tests, perhaps based on new data collections or transformations (e.g.
linguistic annotations), in a rigorous attempt to falsify the claims they are
making. In turn, by doing that we may seek to falsify, or amend, the
explanations built upon those models. At the same time, applying
Principle 15, we may turn to the explanation itself and seek to develop
alternative explanations that can explain the same patterns revealed in
our analysis of the data. This in turn can help to control the movement of
our spotlight through the social world as we seek to falsify claims made
arising from observations in that world and seek new observations that
may help us to adjudicate between competing explanations based upon
our observations.
This balance between explanation and observation is an important
one and one that Leech et al. (2010) pursued. It is also the relationship
in which the mathematical, if mathematics was the basis of inferencing
used in a study, is placed in context. What we are looking for in our
accounts of language based upon observation are what we might call
causal laws – this goes beyond the set of inputs that we may correlate
with an output as causal laws should ‘describe the manner and mechan-
ism of the generation of the effect by the cause’ Harré (1970: 104).104
Statistical models focus on effect and, while it may be that the
104 Though Harré was more inclined to talk of ‘efﬁcacious bringing about of consequences’
(Harré 1997: 208) rather than simply causes. We use cause here as a shorthand for these
processes.
248
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 261 -->

assumptions that they make may set parameters on the mechanism that
creates the effect – for example, in terms of the distributions presumed
to be associated with it – they do not specify it. That is an interpretative
step for the human analyst and it is an area where contestation is clearly
possible – to go back to our horse race, we may all agree on the winner,
but the mechanism by which the winner was positioned to win the race
may be the source of dispute. Likewise, we may be able to agree on the
decline of core modals in two varieties of English yet the mechanism by
which we account for it may be in dispute. The search for the mechanism
is essential as ‘Merely knowing that C has generally been followed by E
is not enough: we want to understand the continuous process by which C
produced E, if it did’ (Sayer 1992: 107). Likewise:
The search for mechanisms means that we are not satisﬁed with merely
establishing systematic covariation between variables or events; a satisfac-
tory explanation requires that we are also able to specify special social ‘cogs
and wheels’ that have brought the relationship into existence. (Hedström and
Swedberg 1998: 7)
As part of seeking the mechanism, it is almost certainly the case that, as in
the social sciences more generally, corpus linguists should invoke
Principle 280 – they should draw on other methods, including qualitative
modes of investigation in developing hypotheses about mechanisms,
because ‘background knowledge and theory about the substantive
focus of the research is essential in order to form hypotheses about the
generative process that might be thought to constitute the causal relation-
ships observed’ (Elliot 2005: 113).
One area where mechanism, propensity and effect powerfully interact
is in the sampling structure used in our investigations. Seen from one
angle, the sampling structure is an important device that imposes a form
of principled order on chaos – we know that the different registers of a
language are closely linked to the form of language used within them, so
the application of a sampling structure such as the Brown sampling
structure is warranted. The repeated application of it through time also
allows longitudinal observations to be made that can be argued to be
comparable. Finally, because of an assertion of the frame linking to
written language in general, we can make a claim to generalise from the
data collected to the language our corpus aims to represent. It is probably
for these reasons combined that the observations made by Leech et al.
(2010) fared remarkably well in replication. However, from another
perspective, especially one wed more closely to replacement naturalism,
our sampling structures are hollow promises. It is a work of moments to
7.7 Afterglow
249
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 262 -->

point out ﬂaws in them, those ﬂaws become ampliﬁed through time and
we do not know the population of language well enough to be able to
create a sampling structure in which balance and representativeness are
dealt with sufﬁciently well to permit inferences beyond the corpus to
language in general. In such a view of the world, as characterised by
Stefanowitsch (2020: 38), we should abandon such efforts and focus
instead on promoting linguistic diversity in our corpora by a simple
appeal to scale. A notable argument against that view is found in the
work of Egbert, Biber and Gray (2022). An engagement with scale,
where that scale runs beyond what is necessary for a research question,
may be termed oversampling. Oversampling is inefﬁcient as more data
that necessary may be processed (Egbert et al. 2022: 128) leading to
resources being ‘unnecessarily squandered on a sample too large for
the purpose’ (Stuart 1984: 32). Yet another issue is proposed by Egbert
et al. that harks back to issues with statistical testing discussed in Chapter
4 – large corpora may lead to too much statistical power as the corpora
may be so large that ‘nearly any quantitative pattern in the sample results
in a statistically signiﬁcant effect’ (Egbert et al. 2022: 129). This, in
essence, raises the spectre of corroborations arising from such vast data
sets simply because, as Principle 13 warns, the sheer volume of data may
lead ‘to statistical signiﬁcance based on quantitative patterns that have no
importance’ (Egbert et al. 2022: 129).
We would add a further, linked, reason to the arguments against a
mere reliance on scale and diversity. We would not abandon corpus
sampling structures as they are a perfect illustration of how we believe
a social scientiﬁc approach to corpus analysis should proceed. We use our
background linguistic and social knowledge in order to build a good
approximation of the sampling structure that would be needed to make
general claims about language. Such an application of knowledge is, in
fact, essential if we are ever to reasonably estimate how large a sample
needs to be if we are to determine the scale of data we need for our
investigation, even if it is ‘intelligent guesswork’ (Stuart 1984: 33). We use
that knowledge both to explore those features represented in the corpus
and to project inferences from the corpus to the general population it
represents. In our encounter with that general population, while we are
searching for mechanisms for the effects we observe, we may also ﬁnd
examples that sit uneasily with the approximation that our corpus repre-
sents. That can then provide feedback to future research, via Principle
210, to inform the next generation of corpus construction – for example,
for it is in use that we see that our ignorance, which we are aware of
(Biber 1993; Phillips and Egbert 2017), is great also. If we never
250
Replication: Carrying out an Empirical Study
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 263 -->

operationalise the knowledge we have, it remains a moot point whether
we will be able to problematise that knowledge and improve upon it. In
the absence of the perfect, we proceed with the pragmatic – and through
that we move closer to, but as fallibilists accept we will never reach, a
perfect model of language, for even ‘if the absolute goal of representa-
tiveness is not attainable in practical circumstances, we can take steps to
approach closer to this goal on a scale of representativity’ (Leech 2007:
140, note 5) because:
It is best to recognize that these goals are not all-or-nothing: there is a scale of
representativity, of balancedness, of comparability. We should seek to deﬁne
realistically attainable positions on these scales, rather than to abandon them
altogether. (Leech 2007: 144)105
As critical realists we accept that our models of language and the reality
of language are quite distinct. Our goal, guided by principles such as
those outlined in this book, is to close that gap, though in the full knowl-
edge that we can never, ever, be certain it is closed. So our models remain
the best we can manage at any moment in time, our results the best we
have yet produced and the mechanisms we posit on the basis of those
results may remain contested. Yet this is normal in science and is to be
expected in social science. Whatever other principle guides us, one which
Popper offers us should be ever in our minds ‘I may be wrong and you
may be right, and by an effort we may get nearer to the truth’ (Popper
1945b: 224).
105 See Mukherjee (2004: 114) for a further clear articulation of how the work of Popper
can help in the cyclical reﬁnement of corpus design.

251
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.009 Published online by Cambridge University Press

CHAPT ER EI GHT
Conclusion
We have now come to the end of our journey in which we searched for
theoretical foundations of corpus linguistics as an empirical discipline. In
Chapters 1–6 we outlined forty-eight principles (also available in
Appendix 1), which deﬁne our ontological position of the critical realist
approach to (social)science in general and to corpus linguistics in parti-
cular; Chapter 7 then offered a practical example of an application of
these principles to replication, one of the cornerstones of the empirical
approach. On this journey, we were guided by Popper in particular and
also other thinkers in philosophy of science; where a genuinely external
perspective was needed, we reached out for the assistance of our friendly
Martians Ssorg and Iraxxa when carrying out thought experiments.
In our metaphor of the foundations of a building, the forty-eight
principles form the load-bearing pillars of our effort to systematically
explore the areas of science and social science. We started with the
metaphor of the ﬂexible pillars (see Figure 1.1), which can be further
reﬁned to absorb the scrutiny and critique in further thinking about this
subject. We have presented the method of provisional statement and
further reﬁnement throughout this book. This method points to our
core theoretical starting point of fallibilism, which accepts that any
human effort and understanding is limited and provisional. The episte-
mological position of critical rationalism is thus an answer to what we can
do within the fallibilist framework. Instead of giving up on the quest for
knowledge and truth as sceptics would do, we need to realise both the
limitations of human knowledge, on the one hand, and the power of
empirical evidence and a systematic rational approach, on the other
hand. We also need to recognise the difference between the natural and
the social, realising that in social science we need to keep in mind the
complexity of social traditions and institutions.
So, what would be the message we would like to leave our readers
with? While as shown in this book, the arguments can be fairly complex,
the message itself is very simple: be open to falsiﬁcation and maximise the
potential for falsiﬁcation of corpus linguistic statements. In this book, we
have provided the justiﬁcation of this approach, contextualising it and
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.010 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 265 -->

explicitly stating the principles upon which it rests. One thing, which we,
however, have left out of the equation is the person who is carrying out
the research, their preferences and sensitivities: there is a clear tension
between the requirement for (social) science to actively seek and max-
imise the potential for falsiﬁcation and the scientist or the theoretician,
who might be reluctant to be proven wrong. The best way to illustrate this
tension is with a famous story from Popper’s life, which created much
controversy in philosophical circles. This a story is situated at Cambridge
University shortly after World War II. Popper was invited as a guest
speaker to give a paper at the Cambridge Moral Science Club. After
almost thirty years after the event, he recalls the story in his
autobiography:
I began my paper (read on October 26, 1946, in R. B. Braithwaite’s room in
King’s College) by expressing my surprise at being invited by the Secretary to
read a paper ‘stating some philosophical puzzle’; . . . I then mentioned moral
problems and the problem of the validity of moral rules. At that point
Wittgenstein, who was sitting near the ﬁre and had been nervously playing
with the poker, which he sometimes used like a conductor’s baton to empha-
size his assertions, challenged me: ‘Give me an example of a moral rule!’ I
replied: ‘Not to threaten visiting lecturers with pokers.’ Whereupon
Wittgenstein, in a rage, threw the poker down and stormed out of the
room, banging the door behind him. (Popper 2002d: 140–141)
This account has been widely disputed. Wittgenstein is another important
philosopher, who was largely in disagreement with Popper.
Key Thinkers: Ludwig Wittgenstein (1889–1951)
Although the poker story portrays Wittgenstein, an Austrian-British philoso-
pher of language, as Popper’s nemesis in the questions of epistemology, the
range of Wittgenstein’s philosophy is much wider. The main epistemological
disagreement between the two philosophers is about whether philosophy can
deal with real problems (Popper) or whether, as Wittgenstein came to believe
in his later work, purely solves linguistic puzzles, which philosophers created in
the ﬁrst place. Wittgenstein’s early work (Tractatus Logico Philosophicus)
deals with the problems of logic and its relationship with reality. It is fairly
technical in nature. His later work (Philosophical Investigations), which is less
technical and which has a character of philosophical notes, represents a
critique of his own early work as well as much of the existing philosophical
tradition – hence the inevitable conﬂict with Popper.
Conclusion
253
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.010 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 266 -->

In Popper’s version of the story, Wittgenstein is portrayed as a short-
tempered person, who left the room after being stung by a witty repost
from Popper. Interestingly, we can look at Popper’s account of the story
as a series of statements – hypotheses, which can be either falsiﬁed or
corroborated by further evidence.
1. Popper’s lecture took place on 26 October 1946.
2. Wittgenstein was playing with a poker during the lecture.
3. Wittgenstein left after Popper’s witty remark.
That evening, there were about thirty witnesses to this event, many of
whom were reached by Edmonds and Eidinow (2001), who tried to
painstakingly reconstruct the events after an even greater gap of almost
sixty years. So what is the conclusion? Strictly speaking, the ﬁrst hypoth-
esis is easily falsiﬁed by external evidence. As the records show, the
meeting took place on Friday 25 October, rather than on Saturday 26
October. However, there is no dispute about the fact that the lecture
actually took place. Modiﬁed hypothesis 10, which states that Popper’s
lecture took place on 25 October 1946, is therefore corroborated.
The other two hypotheses are more difﬁcult to falsify due to the
nature of the evidence – eyewitness accounts after an extended period
of time and personal and intellectual investment of the eyewitnesses in
the Wittgenstein vs Popper dispute. Edmonds and Eidinow (2001)
conclude that 2 is probably corroborated and 3 can probably be refuted,
because the remark of this sort was uttered after Wittgenstein had left
the room. Popper was, however, reportedly convinced about the accu-
racy of his account until his death in 1994. This story shows the complex
relationship between the statements about reality, with which we have a
quasi-contact, and the sensibilities of a scientist or a theoretician –
something even Popper, one of the greatest minds of the twentieth
century, was not impervious to. Yet despite this complexity, the princi-
ples and the scientiﬁc method are still applicable, regardless whether we
like or dislike the conclusions – falsiﬁcations and corroborations – as the
example of Popper’s story clearly demonstrates.
We will ﬁnish on a note about us as authors and you as a reader. As
authors, we have proposed a version of the foundations of corpus linguis-
tics, which we believe is internally consistent. We have also spelt out, we
hope clearly, the grounds on which we believe we should use corpus data.
The work, however, does not stop here. As should be apparent from this
book, rigorous epistemology is based on constant revision. As we move
forward and encounter new problems and potential solutions, principles
will need to be revised, reformulated and perhaps new principles will
254
Conclusion
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.010 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 267 -->

need to be added. You the reader may wish to participate in this venture.
This may involve an elaboration of the position or a critique of our
principles and their application. We truly welcome both and believe
that the method of epistemological exploration we pursued in this book
should afford you clear grounds on which you may express potential
disagreement. In this book, we have laid out as clearly as possible what
we think we are doing when we study language using corpora – our
epistemological position. This book thus serves as an invitation for you
to lay out your version of what it means to study language, with or
without a corpus – your epistemological position – when contrasting
your position with ours.
Conclusion
255
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.010 Published online by Cambridge University Press

AP PENDIX 1
Fundamental Principles of Corpus Linguistics
Principle 1: Either when building a corpus or when using one, corpus
linguists should use research questions in order to engage with their
data in a structured and controlled way.
Principle 2: The study of observed language is a means by which language
can be investigated and explained.
Principle 3: Corpus linguistics, especially in the form of corpus annota-
tion, is an area where the ontological presuppositions of linguistics
become clear.
Principle 4: Because we have a presupposition of reality, through which
we can measure hypotheses against observable, relatively objective
data, we are able to use the apparatus that has been developed over
centuries to support inductive reasoning, including through statistical
analysis.
Principle 40: Because we have a presupposition of reality and through that
we can measure hypotheses against observable, relatively objective
data, we are able to use the apparatus that has been developed over
centuries to support inductive and quasi-inductive reasoning, for
example, through statistical analysis.
Principle 5: By studying corpora, that is, ﬁnite samples of language, we
can make general claims about language itself; these claims are prob-
abilistic in nature.
Principle 50: By studying corpora, that is, ﬁnite samples of language
bound by space and time, we can make claims claim about language
itself, with those claims bound by space and time in the same way as our
data.
Principle 6: Corpus linguistics inclines to scientia realis – it is the study of
observable language.
Principle 60: Corpus linguistics inclines to scientia realis – it is the study of
observable language on which experience may be tested in accordance
with Principles 7 and 11.
Principle 6″: Corpus linguistics, drawing on scientia realis, works, as
a social science, in a way which is informed by concepts from science –
Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 269 -->

it is the study of observable language on which experience may be
tested in accordance with Principles 7 and 11.
Principle 7: Corpus linguistics promotes and is based upon an intersub-
jectively observable approach to language in which results are repea-
table and replicable.
Principle 8: In any given corpus investigation, data and theory are criti-
cally considered.
Principle 9: Corpus analysis does not pave a pathway to certainty – it is
a problem-solving exercise and requires a constant engagement with
new problems and the revisiting of old problems.
Principle 10: Corpus linguistics, being closely linked to processes of
induction (see Principle 4), also entails the problems of induction
which in turn points towards uncertainty (Principle 9).
Principle 100: Corpus linguistics, being closely linked to induction and
quasi-induction (see Principe 4), also experiences the problem of
induction which in turn points towards uncertainty (Principle 9).
Principle 11: A corpus is of use for testing the falsiﬁability of a hypothesis
about language.
Principle 12: In developing new tools and data sets, corpus linguists are
mindful of the need, where possible, to transition hypotheses from the
realm of metaphysics to one where important principles of corpus
linguistics may be brought to bear, especially Principles 4, 5, 6 and 11.
Principle 13: The observable is not always of importance or utility.
Principle 14: Corpus linguists are critical realists who use corpus data as
a way to come into quasi-contact with language in use.
Principle 15: Corpora are theoretically underdetermined and hence may
support a range of theoretical perspectives.
Principle 16: When building and using corpora, our goal is to facilitate
(corpus building) or perform a genuine test (corpus use) and a genuine
test must permit the possibility of falsiﬁcation.
Principle 160: When building and using corpora, our goal is to facilitate
(corpus building) and/or perform a genuine test (corpus use) and
a genuine test must permit the possibility of falsiﬁcation while also
achieving surprise.
Principle 16″: When building and using corpora, our goal is to facilitate
(corpus building) and/or perform a genuine test (corpus use) and
a genuine test must permit the possibility of conditional falsiﬁcation
while also achieving surprise.
Appendix 1
257
Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 270 -->

Principle 17: When conducting a test with corpus data, one must consider
the overall impact of any falsiﬁcation produced by that test.
Principle 170: When conducting a test with corpus data, one must consider
the overall impact of any conditional falsiﬁcation produced by that test.
As conditional falsiﬁcations accumulate, rejection may be warranted.
Principle 18: Reporting falsiﬁcations is arguably more important than
reporting corroboration. At the very least, both should be reported.
Principle 180: Reporting falsiﬁcations is arguably more important than
reporting corroboration. At the very least, both should be reported
unless the report is on a previously falsiﬁed hypothesis; then neither
corroboration or falsiﬁcation is of value.
Principle 18″: Reporting conditional falsiﬁcations is arguably more
important than reporting corroboration. At the very least, both should
be reported unless the report is on a previously rejected hypothesis –
then a falsiﬁcation of a falsiﬁcation that led to rejection is of particular
interest.
Principle 19: In research practice and debate, corpus linguists should not
produce arguments proceeding from dogmatic positions. They are the
negation of everything we wish to achieve through an appeal to evi-
dence and objectivity.
Principle 190: In research practice and debate, corpus linguists, as critical
rationalists, should not produce arguments proceeding from dogmatic
positions. They are the negation of everything we wish to achieve
through an appeal to evidence and objectivity.
Principle 20: Corpus linguistics proceeds by convention to use methods,
data sets, ontologies etc. accepted by a community of scholars as ﬁt for
the investigation of language.
Principle 21: Corpus linguistics proceeds incrementally as testing by
falsiﬁcation proceeds and the cycle of hypothesis formation is driven
onwards.
Principle 210: Corpus linguistics proceeds incrementally as testing by
falsiﬁcation proceeds and the cycle of hypothesis formation is driven
onwards with the goal of reducing the range of those hypotheses.
Principle 22: Corpus linguists should be open and honest about the
limitations of their data and methods.
Principle 23: Corpus data are as provisional as hypotheses and in princi-
ple may pass through a similar life cycle.
Principle 24: Corpora can provide corroborations and falsiﬁcations of
inverse existential statements. However, they only provide corrobora-
tion of existential statements.
258
Appendix 1
Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 271 -->

Principle 25: Corpus linguists should seek to explore and develop axioms,
which are integrated, non-redundant, parsimonious and necessary.
Principle 250: Corpus linguists should seek to explore and develop axioms
which are integrated, non-redundant, parsimonious, necessary and as
precise as possible.
Principle 26: When using corpora to explore theory, the critical lens
provided by Principle 25 should be applied to that theory.
Principle 27: Principles 25 and 26 apply equally to axioms and axioms by
convention.
Principle 28: Methods should be combined so as to maximise the poten-
tial falsiﬁcation of any hypothesis explored.
Principle 280: Methods should be combined so as to 1) maximise potential
falsiﬁability; 2) maximise experimental falsiﬁers; 3) maximise the
empirical content of the system under examination and 4) minimise
range. All of this is done in the pursuit of simplicity.
Principle 29: Corpus linguists, when selecting data and tools to examine
a hypothesis, should select those which maximise the potential falsiﬁa-
bility of that hypothesis while also abiding by other principles, notably
Principle 7.
Principle 290: Corpus linguists, when selecting data and tools to examine
a hypothesis, should select those which 1) maximise potential falsiﬁa-
bility; 2) maximise experimental falsiﬁers; 3) maximise the empirical
content of the system under examination and 4) minimise range, while
also abiding by other principles, notably Principle 7. All of this is done
in the pursuit of simplicity.
Principle 30: Consistency is a necessary goal of corpus analysis, annota-
tion and construction.
Principle 31: Intuition is a useful guide to hypothesis formation and
revision in corpus linguistics.
Principle 32: Corpus linguistics can be used as the basis for a normative
epistemology of language.
Principle 320: Corpus linguistics can be used as the basis for a normative
epistemology of language. However, this abstraction away from the
individual should not exclude or obscure the individual and variation
from the normative.
Principle 33: Corpus linguistics typically works within a ‘searchlight’
paradigm of research. The needs of theory and axiom construction
guide us to speciﬁc areas of enquiry.
Appendix 1
259
Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 272 -->

Principle 34: The weight of any given corroboration that we derive from
a study, and thus the conﬁdence we may express in convention, is
proportionate to the severity of the test to which we subject our
hypotheses.
Principle 35: A corpus represents an amalgam of social and physical
interactions.
Principle 350: A corpus, representing socially situated data, is an amalgam
of social and physical interactions. Our approach to analysing it should
take this into account, recognising the value of the theoretical, the
historical and the applied.
Principle 36: Corpora facilitate the study of the institutions and traditions
of language.
Principle 37: By applying simplifying assumptions that ﬁlter and select
corpus data, corpus builders and users may weaken the nature of the
quasi-contact with reality established in Principle 14. When doing so,
the rationale for the simpliﬁcation should be presented and its impact
on theory and the validity of any tests undertaken considered.
Principle 38: Our approach to probability in corpus linguistics relates to
propensity – we believe that there are a large number of forces acting
on linguistic propensity, which we need to actively model to the best of
our ability, in order to use statistical inferencing meaningfully. The
forces form an important context within which language is produced,
interpreted and transmitted between generations.
Principle 39:
Where:
U = the set of all forces relevant to the propensity of language
A = the set of forces that the corpus selected allows, on publication,
the corpus user to explore
B = the set of forces which, post-publication of the corpus, a corpus
user may infer
C = the set of forces available on publication that the corpus user may
also seek to derive after publication of the corpus
Wherever possible, 1) corpus builders should maximise the size of
A\B; 2) corpus users should increase the size of B\A; 3) both corpus
builders and users should maximise the size of A⋃B as a subset of U.
Principle 40: We believe that those producing language generate output
which they believe to be appropriate and well formed from the point of
view of their speech community for the purpose of communication in
the cognitive, social and physical context in which the utterance was
produced, bounded by time.
260
Appendix 1
Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 273 -->

Principle 41: The evidence for language is the production and reception
of language by users and learners of language. The way in which it acts
as evidence for them is a way in which it can act as evidence for linguists
also.
Principle 42: In situ falsiﬁcations regarding beliefs of appropriacy and
well-formedness of a linguistic feature or structure based on Principle
40 may occur where appropriacy and well-formedness is rejected by
a person in the intended audience of the linguistics production in
question.
Principle 420: In situ falsiﬁcations regarding beliefs of appropriacy and
well-formedness of a linguistic feature or structure based on Principle
40 may occur where appropriacy and well-formedness is rejected by
a person in the intended audience of the linguistic production in ques-
tion or the producer of the language.
Principle 43: Principle 40 leads to reﬂexive behaviour on the behalf of
a recipient of a linguistic production (hearer) – they choose to accept
a linguistic production, reject it or, potentially, to modify their own
view of what is appropriate based on the communicative event in
question. Accordingly, the corpus linguist may come into quasi-contact
with such reﬂexive events also.
Principle 44: Researchers should make their best endeavours to ensure
that the research results they publish will remain repeatable. This
entails archiving versions of corpora and tools used in such studies,
describing clearly the goals and methods of the study and ensuring that
analytical abstractions on which a study is based (e.g. linguistic ana-
lyses, statistical analyses) are well described in such a way as to pro-
mote repeatability.
Principle 45: Version management for corpora and tools should be con-
sidered at the start of a project and then maintained over time.
Principle 46: Sampling structures in corpus linguistics are seeking to
divide language into chunks within which it may be expected that
language varies, that is, the sampling structure is composed of nominal
forms within which language variation occurs, typically genres, regis-
ters
or
modes
of
communication.
They
embody
ontological
knowledge.
Principle 47: The process of investigating corpus data has helped linguists
show, by repeated investigation, how elements of ontological knowl-
edge can, by convention, be assumed to be meaningful when used to
structure our observation of language through sampling. This, in turn,
controls our view of the process of replication enshrined in Principe 48.
Principle 48: In undertaking repetition and replication, corpus linguists
should be clear about what they are doing, and their goals in doing so.
Appendix 1
261
Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 274 -->

Treatment design and experimental design should be clearly identiﬁed
and, in the case of replication, a clear explanation given of what
surprise has been introduced, what hypotheses are potentially
impacted by this, what potential outcomes were anticipated and what
results were achieved.

epistemology of language.
Principle 320: Corpus linguistics can be used as the basis for a normative
epistemology of language. However, this abstraction away from the
individual should not exclude or obscure the individual and variation
from the normative.
Principle 33: Corpus linguistics typically works within a ‘searchlight’
paradigm of research. The needs of theory and axiom construction
guide us to speciﬁc areas of enquiry.
Appendix 1
259
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.011 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 279 -->

Principle 34: The weight of any given corroboration that we derive from
a study, and thus the conﬁdence we may express in convention, is
proportionate to the severity of the test to which we subject our
hypotheses.
Principle 35: A corpus represents an amalgam of social and physical
interactions.
Principle 350: A corpus, representing socially situated data, is an amalgam
of social and physical interactions. Our approach to analysing it should
take this into account, recognising the value of the theoretical, the
historical and the applied.
Principle 36: Corpora facilitate the study of the institutions and traditions
of language.
Principle 37: By applying simplifying assumptions that ﬁlter and select
corpus data, corpus builders and users may weaken the nature of the
quasi-contact with reality established in Principle 14. When doing so,
the rationale for the simpliﬁcation should be presented and its impact
on theory and the validity of any tests undertaken considered.
Principle 38: Our approach to probability in corpus linguistics relates to
propensity – we believe that there are a large number of forces acting
on linguistic propensity, which we need to actively model to the best of
our ability, in order to use statistical inferencing meaningfully. The
forces form an important context within which language is produced,
interpreted and transmitted between generations.
Principle 39:
Where:
U = the set of all forces relevant to the propensity of language
A = the set of forces that the corpus selected allows, on publication,
the corpus user to explore
B = the set of forces which, post-publication of the corpus, a corpus
user may infer
C = the set of forces available on publication that the corpus user may
also seek to derive after publication of the corpus
Wherever possible, 1) corpus builders should maximise the size of
A\B; 2) corpus users should increase the size of B\A; 3) both corpus
builders and users should maximise the size of A⋃B as a subset of U.
Principle 40: We believe that those producing language generate output
which they believe to be appropriate and well formed from the point of
view of their speech community for the purpose of communication in
the cognitive, social and physical context in which the utterance was
produced, bounded by time.
260
Appendix 1
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.011 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 280 -->

Principle 41: The evidence for language is the production and reception
of language by users and learners of language. The way in which it acts
as evidence for them is a way in which it can act as evidence for linguists
also.
Principle 42: In situ falsiﬁcations regarding beliefs of appropriacy and
well-formedness of a linguistic feature or structure based on Principle
40 may occur where appropriacy and well-formedness is rejected by
a person in the intended audience of the linguistics production in
question.
Principle 420: In situ falsiﬁcations regarding beliefs of appropriacy and
well-formedness of a linguistic feature or structure based on Principle
40 may occur where appropriacy and well-formedness is rejected by
a person in the intended audience of the linguistic production in ques-
tion or the producer of the language.
Principle 43: Principle 40 leads to reﬂexive behaviour on the behalf of
a recipient of a linguistic production (hearer) – they choose to accept
a linguistic production, reject it or, potentially, to modify their own
view of what is appropriate based on the communicative event in
question. Accordingly, the corpus linguist may come into quasi-contact
with such reﬂexive events also.
Principle 44: Researchers should make their best endeavours to ensure
that the research results they publish will remain repeatable. This
entails archiving versions of corpora and tools used in such studies,
describing clearly the goals and methods of the study and ensuring that
analytical abstractions on which a study is based (e.g. linguistic ana-
lyses, statistical analyses) are well described in such a way as to pro-
mote repeatability.
Principle 45: Version management for corpora and tools should be con-
sidered at the start of a project and then maintained over time.
Principle 46: Sampling structures in corpus linguistics are seeking to
divide language into chunks within which it may be expected that
language varies, that is, the sampling structure is composed of nominal
forms within which language variation occurs, typically genres, regis-
ters
or
modes
of
communication.
They
embody
ontological
knowledge.
Principle 47: The process of investigating corpus data has helped linguists
show, by repeated investigation, how elements of ontological knowl-
edge can, by convention, be assumed to be meaningful when used to
structure our observation of language through sampling. This, in turn,
controls our view of the process of replication enshrined in Principe 48.
Principle 48: In undertaking repetition and replication, corpus linguists
should be clear about what they are doing, and their goals in doing so.
Appendix 1
261
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.011 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 281 -->

Treatment design and experimental design should be clearly identiﬁed
and, in the case of replication, a clear explanation given of what
surprise has been introduced, what hypotheses are potentially
impacted by this, what potential outcomes were anticipated and what
results were achieved.
