APPENDIX 2
Glossary
a priori assumption
A generally accepted statement that is assumed to be
true; it therefore needs no further (empirical) proof to
support it. Such assumptions are also linked to the
problem of induction and the position known as
a priorism.
acceptability
A judgement made about a linguistic feature, utterance and
so on that it can successfully operate within a particular
context. For example, an utterance can be deemed
acceptable within a speech community when speakers use
it for successful communication in the speech community.
Note that this may be distinct from other judgements such
as grammaticality.
algorithm
A series of logically ordered steps through which one must
pass in order to achieve an outcome.
ampliative reasoning
A process whereby logical inferencing relies on degrees of
belief rather than Boolean truth values (true vs false).
anti-naturalism
A position that argues that the methods of the natural
sciences may not be used as an appropriate framework for
the exploration of the social world. A term coined by Karl
Popper that is, for our purposes, the equivalent of
conventionalism.
annotation
The process whereby ontological knowledge is made
explicit – for example, a part-of-speech scheme is applied
to a text with each word being clearly associated with
a part of speech.
antecedently
That happening before.
anticipation
A hypothesis for which there is, as yet, no ﬁrm
corroboration. It is a new hypothesis, a bold conjecture.
Note that we follow Popper in classifying beliefs about the
innateness of language as an anticipation.
a priorism
The doctrine of assuming something to be true a priori to
avoid, for example, the problem of induction.
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 283 -->

Aristotelian
Following the procedures for investigation as set out by
Aristotle (384–322 BC).
attempt at falsification
Whenever an attempt is made to present evidence against
a hypothesis, this may be called an attempt at falsiﬁcation.
axiom
For our purposes, a fundamental assumption on which
a theory is built. The axioms work in such a way that,
through a clear logical inference from the axioms, the
actions of a phenomenon or system modelled by the theory
can be explained.
axiom by convention
A term used in this book to highlight axioms that rely on
convention to operate rather than appealing to extra-
logical constants.
axiomatic functionalism
An approach to the study of language developed by
J. Mulder (1919–2011) that attempts to offer a theoretical
(scientiﬁc) foundation for the functionalist approach to
language.
balance
The aspiration, in corpus linguistics, to achieve a wide,
though not comprehensive, and proportionate range of
different types of language that vary from each other on
some principled grounds.
basic statements
We might call basic statements the fundamental assumptions
on which a theory is built or, more precisely for Popper, the set
of logical observations on which a theory is built.
Bayesianism
An epistemological position in which belief is expressly
ampliative; we update the degree of belief in a hypothesis
as new evidence emerges.
behaviourist
An approach to the study of the human mind linked to the
work of B.F. Skinner (1904–1990). The classical
behaviourist approach is based solely on observable
phenomena. The human mind and mind internal processes
are then understood exclusively in terms of the stimuli
received and how the mind and its processes becomes
conditioned to respond to respond to them through
interaction in a process that constitutes learning.
bounded by time
For example, an observation of language based on a corpus
covering language in a particular year is bound in time to
the year in question.
breach
The process whereby one of the principles in this book is
placed under such strain that it is violated to the extent that
it does not apply in any meaningful sense.
264
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 284 -->

broad replication
Confrontation of any aspect of prior research with new
evidence.
Brown family of
corpora
Started by the Brown corpus of American English (1967),
this family of corpora have been providing snapshots of
language, using the same sampling structure, at roughly
thirty-year intervals for two varieties of English: British and
American.
Cartesian
Something following the approach of René Descartes
(1596–1650), who saw the mind and body as being entirely
separable. In this dualism, the mind was to be the guide of
enquiry because processes in the mind gave access to
certainty and truth.
causation
The process whereby something is brought about by
something else. So, if a piece of wood catches ﬁre and is
reduced to ashes, we may claim that the process of
transformation from wood to ash is caused by the ﬁre. Of
course even a simple example like this shows that
causation is complex – there are intervening processes
between the moment ﬁre starts and ﬁre stops, all of which
lead through to the production of ash. Likewise, we might
look for the causes of the ﬁre that reduced the wood to ash.
Causation is typically a hugely complex question.
change over time
The process whereby we may, through the use of
appropriately structured data, discern change in an object
of study across time, though our observation is necessarily
bounded by the time that our observation covers.
cohort studies
Studies through time in which the same individuals or
objects are returned to again and again to develop, for
example, a trend analysis.
common sense
A good down-to-earth (pre-theoretical) judgement immune to
scepticism. An appeal is made in this book to common sense,
what we might reasonably expect from our interaction with
the physical and social world around us, as a curb on what we
view as excessive positions sometimes adopted in philosophy.
conditional falsification
All falsiﬁcations are conditional. This acknowledges that,
for example, falsiﬁcations may in turn be falsiﬁed and that,
like corroboration, single falsiﬁcations are typically not
decisive and that falsiﬁcations have to accumulate before
we may feel, by convention, that we have sufﬁcient
conditional falsiﬁcations to merit rejection
Appendix 2
265
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 285 -->

conjecture
A hypothesis that we make that, while it may initially be
unsupported, we then seek to corroborate through
subsequent investigation.
consistency
The process of ensuring that, in the application of
ontological knowledge to a corpus, for example, we ensure
that, given the same circumstances, we assign a linguistic
example from the corpus to the same ontological category.
This is of clear importance when dealing with potential
ambiguity in the application of a scheme. While forcing
consistency does not negate the ambiguity, it does ensure
that one position is systematically followed.
construction grammar
Theories of language based on the conjecture that the
pairing of structures with associated meaning forms the
basis of human language learning and use. These pairings
are acquired in linguistic interaction.
convention
Convention is the way in which hypotheses and theories are
deemed to be true when, in fact, they cannot strictly be
viewed as such. Convention should be a function of
corroboration within scientiﬁc investigation, though
conviction, at least, may also play a role.
conventionalism
The position that the social cannot be (fully) explained via
naturalism.
convictions
Strong beliefs in something – these belong in the
metaphysical realm.
corpus based
A study which uses corpora to explore prior ontological
knowledge and/or conjectures arising from previous works.
corpus driven
A study which explores a corpus using no a priori
ontological schemes or conjectures. It is difﬁcult to
conceive of a purely corpus-driven study as even a well-
balanced corpus – for example, represents an encoding of
prior knowledge such as genre categories, text boundaries.
corpus internal
evidence
Evidence that is strictly drawn from within the corpus itself
rather than by appeals beyond the corpus.
correspondence to
reality
The degree to which we believe a hypothesis, for example,
is in agreement with reality insofar as we can gauge that
through quasi-contact with reality.
corroboration
When a hypothesis withstands a genuine attempt at
falsification, it gains corroboration.
critical rationalism
An epistemological position and an approach to the
exploration of hypotheses and theories that emphasises
266
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 286 -->

argument and experience in an effort to approach the
unattainable, the truth. A key feature of critical
rationalism is that those working in its framework
expressly accept that they may be wrong and others may
be right and expect others to respond to them in the
same way.
critical realism
An ontological position, which accepts that our contact
with reality is not direct; we can talk about quasi-reality. In
a context where one is in contact with reality, one should
be permissive of alternative perspectives and ﬁndings while
pursuing one’s own perspectives and ﬁndings in the
understanding that they may, or may not, prove in the long
run to be sustainable.
cross-sectional studies
Studies that, typically in a narrowly deﬁned period
bounded by time, look across a structure sample of
a population. For example, the Brown corpus can be viewed
as a cross-sectional study of American English in the early
1960s.
crystalised
Probabilities become crystalised at the point at which an
event we have modelled in terms of probabilities occurs. At
that point, for the speciﬁc event in question, it has
a probability of 1.
culturomics
The attempt to understand human society and behaviour
through the computational analysis of large collections or
texts – these may, or may not, match our deﬁnition of
a corpus. In practice, they are typically not well-ordered
samples of language seeking to be representative of
language in general.
dark matter
An as yet to be directly observed substance that appears to
constitute the bulk of matter in the universe. Its posited
existence is based on the interplay of Newtonian physics
and observational science.
deduction
In deductive logic, the premises of a well-formed (valid)
argument entail the conclusion; if these are correct, the
conclusion must be correct.
deem
The process whereby, given sufﬁcient corroboration, we may
choosetodealwithahypothesisastrue,eventhoughweknow
that it is not strictly possible to attain truth. The idea links to
ampliative reasoning, axioms of convention and convention.
Appendix 2
267
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 287 -->

degree of
corroboration
The extent to which we believe am hypothesis to have
achieved corroboration by withstanding falsification. This is
a function of the severity of the attempt at falsification
more than the number of corroborations achieved.
degree of falsifiability
The extent to which any system of belief being tested may
be exposed to attempts at falsification.
degrees of freedom
In Popper’s philosophy, this is an abstract measure of the
extent to which a hypothesis or system of belief
corresponds to reality.
deterministic
Those that believe that an event is deterministic are
subscribing to the position that events, such as human
actions, are determined by forces other than volition and
can be modelled as such.
direct repetition
The use of the same data, tools and methods for the purposes
of checking the accuracy and transparency of the original
study.
direct replication
The use of new data and the same methodology to answer
a research question from previous research.
doctrine of revisability
Proposed by W.V.O. Quine (1908–2000), this doctrine
states that all beliefs are rationally revisable.
dogmatic
When a belief is held that denies the outcome of
falsification and tries instead to argue the falsiﬁcation
away, the defence is becoming dogmatic, based on the
belief that something must be true rather than something
has been shown to be corroborated and is thus assumed to
be true by convention. Dogmatic beliefs may be close,
conceptually, to those held by conviction.
effect size
Statistical measures that focus on effect size try to show how
big, or otherwise, an observed effect is. This is in contrast to
what happens in signiﬁcance testing that focuses upon
establishing the amount of evidence against the null
hypothesis, rather than determining the size of what we
observe.
eliminative inference
By eliminative inferencing, we try to test hypotheses to
eliminate them – falsification is a form of eliminative
inferencing as it proceeds to sift through hypotheses by
attempting to eliminate them. What is not eliminated is
corroborated.
empirical content
Thelargerthesetofpotentialfalsifiers,thehighertheempirical
content of the system that may be tested by that set. Systems
268
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 288 -->

with high empirical content are preferable to those with lower
empirical content, all other considerations being equal.
empirical evidence
For our purposes, observable evidence available to us via
our senses or through experimentation. This is the evidence
on which we may base rational beliefs.
empirical range
The extent to which our empirical evidence spans contexts
where it may vary – the individual, registers, space and
time, for example.
empiricism
Modern empiricism essentially stems from the scientia
realis approach, with its focus on exploring observable
evidence and, through that, trying to make hypotheses of
theorise about reality.
epistemology
A theory or framework by which we claim to establish
knowledge – the system we use in order to establish
knowledge.
essentialism
The Aristotelian approach to the analysis of objects of
study by reducing them to their ‘essences’ – their basic
building blocks.
existential statement
A simple statement asserting the existence of something.
experimental design
For the purposes of this book, we consider experimental
design to be the overall design of an experiment – the data
and methods used, their conﬁguration and the hypotheses
to be examined with this experiment.
experimental falsifiers
For our purposes, those sentences or utterances, within
a corpus, that may, in principle, act as falsifiers for
a hypothesis or theory under examination. See potential
falsifiers for further detail.
explanatory power
The greater the correspondence to reality (or quasi-reality)
that a model has, the greater its explanatory power is, that
is, it is more successful in explaining the world as it is.
external explanation
An important principle of the functionalist approach to
language. It is an assertion that grammar is not
autonomous, but is, instead, largely shaped by the
functions that language is called upon to fulﬁl, paramount
of which is meaningful communication.
extra-logical constants
Axioms typically rely on extra-logical constants, statements
about empirical objects, in order to operate. To give an
example used by Popper (2002a: 53), a line in geometry
may be viewed as such a constant as it can be mapped
Appendix 2
269
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 289 -->

systematically and reliably to something in reality – for
example, light rays in homogenous media.
extra-scientific
Extra-scientiﬁc research results impact beyond the system
of science itself – in this book we have broadly labelled it as
applied research.
fallibilism
Fallibilism is a position that recognises that our knowledge is
not deﬁnitive because human beings are potentially fallible
(hence the name) and our knowledge needs to be constantly
revised.
falsifiable
A belief is falsiﬁable if it is possible to conceive of at least
one plausible test of sufﬁcient severity which may, in
principle, show it to be false.
falsification
The process whereby a suitably test of sufﬁcient severity is
carried out on a belief where that test leads to an outcome
that we may argue shows the belief to be untrue.
in situ falsification
The process of falsification applied by speakers and hearers
in a speciﬁc context.
falsifier
The test that leads to a claim of falsification.
flout
Where a violation of a principle outlined in this book occurs
but where that violation is being used for effect.
focus on the individual
The injunction to ensure that our epistemology accounts for
individual variation and choice as well as normative positions.
formalism
Often held as being in opposition to functionalism, in
linguistics formalism argues for the autonomy of syntax
(form of language). Language, while it may be used in
context, is not shaped or acquired in interaction in
a meaningful way.
frequentist
Closely allied to signiﬁcance testing and in opposition to
Bayesianism, frequentists use frequency information as
a key piece of evidence in deciding on whether any
contrasting observations are signiﬁcant or not.
functionalism
Often held as being in opposition to formalism, functionalism
argues against the autonomy of syntax (form of language),
focusing instead upon the claim that language is largely
shaped by the functions it serves – and that is primarily
conveying meaning in context. In this view of language,
language arises largely from interaction, interaction is not
simply the arena in which language may be observed.
fuzziness
Something displaying a degree of acceptable
approximation or inherent uncertainty.
270
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 290 -->

genuine test
A genuine test is one that leads to the possibility of
falsification – where a test cannot possibly lead to
falsiﬁcation, it is not a genuine test.
God’s truth linguistics
An approach to linguistics in which it is believed that our
understanding of the system of language can take on
a character similar to those of the natural sciences, with
broad, experimentally based evidence of the reality of our
ontological knowledge and hypothesising.
Great Interlocutor
A purely metaphysical construct introduced in this book to
demonstrate a hypothesis about language that cannot lead
to a genuine test.
historicism
The doctrine that it is possible, based on past trends of
human society, to predict what will happen in the future.
hocus-pocus linguistics
An approach to linguistics in which the difﬁculty of
producing an approach to the subject within the structures
of natural sciences leads to an extreme counter position
emerging in which there is little, if any, consistency in the
ontological knowledge or theorising of linguistics. It is an
extreme form of relativism at best and an overly permissive
free-for-all at worst.
hypothesis formation
The process whereby we form the hypotheses that we wish
to base our theorising upon. These will be the focus of
falsification as they are formed and thereafter.
idealism
The philosophical position that claims that only the mind
exists and objects and actions are, effectively, imagined by
the mind.
incremental refinement
The process whereby a hypothesis or theory develops
slowly, with testing and retesting slowly pushing the
hypothesis or theory towards a closer correspondence with
reality.
incrementalism
The process whereby we accept that progress in theorising,
in the sciences and social sciences, is formed by a series of
reﬁnements and improvements, often in response to
falsification undertaken in the searchlight paradigm of
research, we argue.
indeterminism
A philosophical doctrine, in opposition to determinism (see
deterministic), that claims that not all events are entirely
determined by something that went before.
individual names
We treat this term, used by Karl Popper, as a synonym of
singular objects.
Appendix 2
271
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 291 -->

induction
In inductive logic the premises of an argument, which are
individual statements, provide some degree of support to
a general conclusion.
inference
Drawing upon evidence and reasoning on the basis of it, we
may draw a conclusion – and that conclusion is an
inference. The inference may be challenged in part based
upon an exploration of the data and reasoning that
produced it.
infinite regress
The process whereby a chain of reasoning is set up that, by
recursion, leads to an inﬁnite loop (a vicious circle). The
problem of induction is one such example of a problem
producing an inﬁnite regress.
innate predispositions
Such predispositions have provided an escape from the
problem of induction, for example, for linguists for
some time – a genetic predisposition to acquire
language may be assumed as a prior to accounting for
language acquisition. However, in the absence of
evidence for such genetic predispositions, we align
ourselves with Popper’s view that such things remain
anticipations, though as critical rationalists we accept
that seeking evidence to substantiate such anticipations
remains worthwhile
institution of language
This phrase characterises language as an institution –
institutions are systems that occur spontaneously, with no
or minimal planning, in human society.
instrumentation
Those tools that we use in the pursuit of gathering
empirical evidence and testing hypotheses and theories.
intended audience
The audience a speaker intended to communicate with
when they produced an utterance.
inter subjectively
testable
Something that may be tested by multiple testers using the
same criteria for their test.
interdependence
Interdependence can be set up in systems of inference such
as theories – where interdependence exists, the outcome of
reasoning with one statement may determine the outcome
of another.
invariantist
Invariantists look for knowledge that is independent of
context and thus invariant with regard to it.
inverse existential
statement
A simple statement asserting the non-existence of
something.
272
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 292 -->

irrationalism
In distinction to the rational approach to belief, the
irrationalist believes that there are no rational grounds on
which choices of belief may be made.
justificationism
A position that continually asks for an irrefutable proof of
a belief being true or false, which is impossible. This is
a position common among dogmatic sceptics.
likelihood
The probability that we ascribe to something. Note that the
basis on which probability is ascribed may vary.
linguistic reality
The system of language itself – this linguistic reality is
something that, in common with other realities, we only
have quasi-contact with.
metaphysics
A belief system that cannot be subject to falsification
according to the scientiﬁc approach outlined in this book.
metatheoretical
Theorising about theory.
methodological
falsification
A special class of falsification – where falsiﬁcation relies on
method, and that method rests on convention, then any
resulting falsiﬁcation is a methodological falsiﬁcation and, as
the convention supporting that method changes, the outcome
of the test of falsiﬁcation may, in principle, change also.
monism
Monism is a reductionist position, where everything is
sought to be explained by a single principle such as either
the natural or social when interpreting language.
monologic corpora
Corpora constructed of data in which there is only one
apparent speaker or writer evident.
mysticism
An approach to knowledge where claims proceed from
a non-rational belief or authority.
naïve falsificationism
The approach to science in which a single falsification is
seen as enough to lead to the rejection of a theory or
hypothesis can be viewed as naïve falsiﬁcationism as it
does not accept that the falsiﬁcation may be falsiﬁed nor
does it seek to accumulate stronger evidence for rejection
through multiple falsiﬁcations.
naïve realism
The view that, through our senses, we both perceive and
are able to interpret the world accurately ‘as it is’.
natural sciences
Those sciences trying to derive insights into natural forces
around us. Those sciences, such as chemistry and physics
are those that most clearly operate within the framework
proposed by Popper. See also observational sciences.
naturalism
This is a broad philosophical position that states that the
only reality is nature, the physical reality around us.
Appendix 2
273
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 293 -->

new statistics
An approach to statistics in which concepts such as
signiﬁcance testing are set aside in favour of other
approaches to assessing the importance of observed
phenomena – for example, effect sizes.
Newtonian physics
The model of physics as developed by Isaac Newton (1642–
1727) and elaborated upon by others.
no prior selection of
data
One of the early principles of corpus linguistics, calling on
those using corpora not to select their data, for example, to
ﬁt it to their hypothesis. The call for no prior selection is, in
a rudimentary form, an early version of a number of
principles outlined in this book, mainly 1600, 280 and 290.
nominalism
The doctrine of nominalism, as outlined by Karl Popper,
would have us rely on intuition only as a heuristic to guide
our investigation – we may use that to produce shorthand
labels for a phenomenon and then seek to understand what
the label relates to through scientia realis.
normative
epistemology
An epistemological in which the normative, what we might
conceive of as the ‘usual’, is the dominant goal of inquiry.
Such an approach suppresses the importance of individual
variation, sometimes viewing it as mere ‘noise’ that
deﬂects from the normative view sought.
numerical universality
To attain this, we need to be able to observe all relevant
examples throughout space/time. On the basis of those
observations, we may be able to construct a statement or
statements based on that complete knowledge
objective indeterminism
In this approach, for example, to language, we accept that
language exists in a form that is observable, independent of
the mind that produced it, but the perception and
subsequent analysis of it is potentially unpredictable, hence
giving rise to indeterminism in our ﬁndings.
objectivist
The view that reality exists independent of the mind and
that we can attain contact with that reality through our
senses. Note that in this book we accept this to an extent,
but follow Popper’s view that the result is indeterministic as
a result of our senses actually connecting with quasi-reality.
observability
The question of whether or not a researcher is able to make
the observations possible, for example, to attempt
falsification. If we have a theory of how human language
looked in 10,000 BC, for example, we may identify a set of
potential falsifiers, the utterances of humans alive then,
274
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 294 -->

but, as their observability is non-existent, observability
alone renders the attempt at falsification null and void.
observational sciences
Those sciences relying on observation of events as the basis
of their hypothesis formation, testing and theorising.
Astronomy, geology and meteorology are all good
examples of observational sciences. Of course, the
boundary between the natural and observational sciences
is a matter of degree rather than absolutes.
Ockham’s razor
A conceptual tool developed by William Ockham (c. 1287–
1347) – in a situation where two theories or hypotheses
appear to be supported and capable of accounting for
something we are studying, preference should be given to
the simpler of the two.
Oedipus effect
Named after a member of the deeply unfortunate Theban
royal family that was the subject of a cycle of Greek myths,
this effect is claimed to be a possible problem in social
sciences. It states that because we hypothesise that
something will happen, the process of hypothesis itself will
increase the likelihood of it happening.
ontogenesis
The origin and development of something from its
inception to maturity.
ontological knowledge
Our understanding of the basic categorisations related to
observed reality. In linguistics, for example, if we explore
morphosyntax and generate a part-of-speech tagset that
we intend to use to categorise the examples in our data, the
tagset represents a statement of ontological knowledge.
ontological
presuppositions
Given that the generation of an ontology is not a process of
deriving ‘truths’ about the categories in the exploration we
are conducting, our eventual ontological system is based on
ontological presuppositions, that is, we assume that the
system we are using is a given prior to our use of it to test
and reﬁne hypotheses. These presuppositions, however,
are rooted in convention and may be subjected to
falsification and corroboration.
open to falsification
If something is, in principle, falsifiable, then it is open to
falsiﬁcation.
opportunistic
In corpus building, opportunistic corpus building is
sometimes necessary as a response to issues in data
collection – for example, copyright restrictions, limited
availability of certain types of data or the difﬁculty of
Appendix 2
275
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 295 -->

acquiring certain types of data. Opportunistic corpus
building usually proceeds on a ‘best effort’ basis to work
towards a sampling frame but should be explicit about the
distance between the planned and achieved in doing so.
original study
A study that tests a hypothesis for the ﬁrst time.
ostensive definition
An approach to deﬁnition in which we show what we mean –
for example, if we point at a drinking vessel and label it ‘cup’
that would clearly be an attempt at ostensive deﬁnition.
paradigm
A paradigm in science represents a framework of concepts,
methods and approaches within which scientiﬁc work
proceeds; at the sociological level, researchers working
within a paradigm work and interact with each other to
further knowledge.
parsimony
Closely linked to ideas like Ockham’s razor, the call for
parsimony is one that calls upon the researcher to favour
systems that rest on the fewest assumptions and
operations without compromising the power of the system,
that is, as with Ockham’s razor, if we have two systems that
seem to account for our data equally well, our preference
should be for parsimony, we choose the simpler of the two
systems.
particularist
A premise on which an inference is based, for example, is
particularist if it takes different forms in different domains
of enquiry, or within different traditions within the same
domain of enquiry.
permitted statement
A key requirement for any statement/hypothesis that we
explore with a corpus is that it is in principle falsifiable – if it
is, then, the statement/hypothesis is permitted.
persistence
The injunction to push past dogmatic and rhetorical
defences – where a system escapes falsification by such
means, sets that apparent escape aside and persists in
promoting the falsiﬁcation.
personalist
A personalist approach is rooted in individuals and stands
in contrast to the normative view that aggregates
individuals and looks to what is typical or the norm for
a group, for example.
perspectivism
The doctrine that epistemology is linked to our perspective
on, and knowledge of, the object of inquiry. This leads to
the interpretative perspective varying across observers, for
example.
276
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 296 -->

phenomenological
approach
An approach based on the lived experience of, for example,
an object of study. The experience of that object at the
point it occurs is the cornerstone of this approach rather
than any post-hoc interpretation of it.
philosophy of science
This area of philosophy is concerned with accounting for
how science is constituted in terms, for example, of
methods and theorising, and how those relate to the goals
of science. In turn it also considers implications arising for
science from its goals, methods and theorising.
physical reality
The reality formed by the physical universe in which we live.
pluralism
Stands in contrast to monism – there is no one consistent
principle or approach we may take to explain reality, rather
we need different approaches that vary, for example,
according to context or the object of study.
positivism
An extreme form of naturalism. An approach that permits
only what is perceived as strictly scientiﬁc; all claims must
be based on statements that are capable of logical or
mathematical proof. Positivism claims that social reality is
subject to the same general laws as physical reality and
should be studied in the same way.
potential falsifiers
For example, if we have a set of observations that a hypothesis
may be in conﬂict with then we have identiﬁed a set of
potential falsiﬁers. So, if we claim that inﬁnitives are never
split in present-day English, then a notional set of potential
falsiﬁers is produced: any utterances in present-day English in
which the split inﬁnitive occurs. If we have a corpus in which
these are apparent, then we have a set of experimental
falsifiers. An inspection of these will reveal whether our claim
about split inﬁnitives is well founded (these utterances should
be excluded on some rational ground) or it is falsiﬁed (there
are no grounds for excluding these utterances).
potential for
falsification
The notional capacity of falsification that we may say
exists – if we explore the languages of 10,000 BC then, in
terms of corpus-based analysis, the potential for
falsiﬁcation is nil, while the potential for falsiﬁcation when
exploring languages in the present using this method is
clearly greater because of observability.
poverty of stimulus
The dogma that child language acquisition is not
explicable – or even possible – based on the input that the
child receives from speakers they encounter.
Appendix 2
277
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 297 -->

Prague Linguistic Circle
Prague Linguistic Circle started as informal meetings of
three linguists: Vilém Mathesius (1882–1945), Nikolai
Trubetzkoy (1890–1938) and Roman Jakobson (1896–
1982). The Circle still meets every other Monday at the
Faculty of Arts, Charles University in Prague.
precision
Precision is desirable as precise, as opposed to vague,
systems are more amenable to corroboration. A precise
statement will reliably divide apart and account for the set
of potential falsifiers, for example. A less precise system
may not characterise that set, nor account for it, as well.
premise
Premises are individual statements that may be used as the
basis for an inference.
prescriptivism
The practice of asserting, on non-rational grounds, the rules
of language that may divide, for example, usage into
grammatical and non-grammatical usage.
presupposition
A priori assumptions that are explicitly, or tacitly,
understood as a starting point for a process of inferencing.
priming
The assumption that an initial exposure to a stimulus
(prime) in turn inﬂuences the response to a similar stimulus
in future – by such a process one might begin, for example,
to account for language acquisition in whole or part.
privileged cynicism
A fallacy based upon the belief that sceptical views held by
perceived elites must be true.
problem of induction
The problem of induction is that it is generally impossible
to exhaustively analyse something and arrive at a general
conclusion (e.g. scientiﬁc law) based on individual
observations.
propensity
In a particular context, propensity is a tendency or ‘force’ to
produce a speciﬁc outcome. Popper interprets probabilities
as propensities.
protocol
In Rudolf Carnap’s (1891–1970) approach to knowledge
and belief, one would protocol the basis on which one
believed something in the form of protocol sentences –
sentences that formally stated the grounds for a belief in
order to explicate theory.
prototypicality
A form of normative reasoning in which a central example –
a prototype – is considered key; for instance, this can be the
central tendency in the deﬁnition of a word’s meaning. It
provides a fuzzy, potentially ampliative, approach to
understanding linguistics.
278
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 298 -->

provisional truth
Well-corroborated hypotheses or theories that we deem
have been corroborated well enough for us to be able to
treat them as provisionally true. We tacitly accept,
however, that falsification may yet prove them not to be.
proximal causation
In exploring causation in human social behaviours,
proximal links are those that immediate responsible for
causing some event. Nonetheless, those proximal causes
may have less directly related, or so-called, distal causes.
psychological reality
If something reﬂects psychological reality, it is a reﬂection
of the processes of the mind, that is, a claim can be made
that the model of the process is some reﬂection of an actual
mental process. Note that contact with this form of reality is
no easier than other forms of reality.
psychologism
A view that logical or linguistic processes directly reﬂect
human thought processes.
purposeful
communication
An important feature of the Prague School’s approach to
language – the intention of the speaker is paramount and
the speaker’s intention is to engage in purposeful
communication. This functionalist perspective sees the
system of language as one of purposeful communication.
To view it as rooted in psychological states instead is
psychologism.
quasi contact with
reality
In a context where full contact with reality is not possible,
we are forced to rely in quasi, that is, indirect or mediated,
contact with reality.
quasi-induction
The process of quasi-induction is pursued by Karl Popper.
While seeming to eschew induction, Popper’s quasi-
inductive model of reasoning seems to blend deduction
and induction, though the preference is loaded in favour of
deduction. Nonetheless, quasi-induction is a blend of both
processes.
quasi reality
The experience of reality that we have and that we may
study – it may approximate to, but never be, reality itself.
radical realism
Radical realism argues that there can be no understanding
of reality because of the varying perceptions of it from one
person to another. It follows, they would argue, that
intersubjective knowledge of reality is impossible.
radical scepticism
Radical scepticism may question the very nature of reality
itself, claiming that we can know nothing as nothing may
exist, for example.
Appendix 2
279
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 299 -->

range
How close, or otherwise, the degree of correspondence
with reality attained by our system is.
rationalism
A form of scientia rationalis – it sees reason as the main
way in which we can derive and test knowledge. Note that
it is distinct from critical rationalism.
rationality principle
A simplifying assumption that humans behave rationally or
language use is rationally based, for example.
realism
As used in Chapter 1 of this book, this is a simple
commitment to the proposition that human thought and
individual physical actions and objects exist.
recursion
Using the same logic to analyse something repeatedly into
ever more reﬁned parts until the process concludes. The
main problem with recursion is its potential for infinite
regress – it may not conclude, leading to a vicious circle.
reductionist
One who is committed to an often simplistic view that one
area of study can be subsumed under another area without
any loss of explanatory power.
redundancy
Redundancy is to be avoided in hypothesising and
theorising – if we see redundancy in a system we see, for
example, statements that are in effect identical. The drive for
simplicity and parsimony means that redundancy should be
avoided.
refractory evidence
Refractory evidence is well suited to producing a test of
sufﬁcient severity – it is evidence that, prima facie, is
difﬁcult for a system or hypothesis to account for.
refutation
We will interpret Karl Popper’s use of refutation as
a synonym of rejection rather than falsification.
reification
The process whereby concepts and assertions are given the
ontological status of real things.
rejection
Once we deem, by convention, that sufﬁcient conditional
falsifications of a theory or hypothesis have been made, we
may deem it to be rejected, that is, we treat it as though it is
false.
relativism
The belief that truth is for the individual only hence no
shared and objective truth is possible.
repetition with
variation
Repetition with one or more of the three components –
data, tools and methods – slightly modiﬁed, for example,
a corpus with more accurate annotation.
replacement naturalism
A position strongly associated with W.V.O. Quine (1908–
2000), which seeks, in the study of human behaviour and
280
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 300 -->

society, to replace the methods of inquiry with those of the
natural sciences.
replication
Generally, a form of surprise whereby the test of
a hypothesis or theory is repeated but a variation is
introduced into that process. The resulting, motivated,
mismatch between the original test and the new one
represents a form of surprise into the process. See
different forms of replication: direct and broad
replication.
representativeness
An ideal reached for in the production of corpora, based on
structured samples, which seek to represent language or
some of its domains. Representativeness seeks to ensure
that the resultant sample-based model reﬂects relevant
characteristics of the population – language being
modelled, allowing for the extension of observations from
the model to the population.
resampling
Given a corpus based on a sampling of a given
population of language, the samples within that
corpus may be resampled. For example, the ordering
of the samples may be varied, new subcorpora built
from a subset of the existing samples or the samples
further split and recomposed into new corpora, inter
alia.
research design
As with experimental design, though without a commitment
that formal experimental methods form part of the planned
work. Hence, research design is, for the purposes of this
book, the overall design of a piece of research – the data and
methods used, their conﬁguration and the hypotheses to be
examined as a result of this research.
research questions
The research question or questions that we want a research
design, or perhaps an experimental design, to address.
sample
A collection of material taken from a larger population,
typically according to a sampling structure.
sampling structure
The framework within which a sample of a population is
gathered with the goal of representing, in a structured way,
potentially meaningful variation within that population.
Corpus sampling structures (e.g. Brown family sampling
structure) are one such example.
sampling frame
A list of all items in a population. When creating corpora,
a full sampling frame is not always available.
Appendix 2
281
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 301 -->

scientia rationalis
A precursor of rationalism. Scientia rationalis foregrounds
reason, logic and rational constructs as sources of
knowledge.
scientia realis
A precursor of empiricism. Scientia realis relies on sense
data and experience as the primary source of knowledge.
scientific method
A principle focus of the philosophy of science, the term,
while apparently simple, is complex in terms of both
deﬁnition and operation. This book relies heavily on one
account of the scientiﬁc method, that of Karl Popper, but
that is one of many accounts of what the scientiﬁc method
may be. Those accounts vary to lesser or greater degrees. In
brief, for us a scientiﬁc method is one that gives rise to
rational beliefs based on empirical evidence that is
reﬂective of quasi-reality.
scientism
A form of reductionism (see reductionist) in which
everything is claimed to be subsumed under the scientiﬁc
method. Sometimes this implies outward appearance of
science, which is, on closer inspection, not particularly
scientiﬁc.
searchlight paradigm of
research
This is the basis of much research in science and is the
cornerstone of the corpus-based paradigm of research. Our
work as researchers is directed by investigations of systems
of belief about language – as a theory, for example, makes
a claim about language, we turn our metaphorical spotlight
on the data that may falsify that claim in order to expose
the system of belief to an attempt at falsification of
sufﬁcient severity. Claims in existing research, claims
arising from it, the incremental refinement of the system
based on previous falsiﬁcations, the availability of data and
the role of a hypothesis in supporting a series of linked
hypotheses, inter alia, may be factors that determine where
the searchlight falls.
set of permissible
statements
In contrast to the set of potential falsifiers, the set of
permissible statements is deﬁned by statements that our
hypothesis or theory permits.
severity
The extent to which an attempt at falsification is seen as
a strong, or challenging, test for a system. For example, if
we claim that the split inﬁnitive is not in use in British
English in the twenty-ﬁrst century and we test that by
imagining ﬁve sentences in which it does not occur, that is
282
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 302 -->

not as severe a test as looking at ﬁve thousand examples of
the use of the inﬁnitive derived from a corpus.
significance tests
Statistical tests in inferential statistics undertaken to assess
the amount of evidence against the null hypothesis.
simplicity
Closely linked to ideas such as Ockham’s razor and
parsimony, the call for simplicity is one that invites
researchers to favour systems that rest on the fewest
assumptions and operations without compromising the
power of the system. As Einstein is apocryphally believed to
have said ‘Everything should be made as simple as possible,
but not simpler’.
simplifying
assumptions
A set of assumptions adopted, preferably explicitly, that
simplify a complex problem to the extent that it becomes
amenable to modelling. Note that simplifying assumptions
have an important role to play in further distancing the
researcher from reality in quasi-reality.
singular object
A basic object of our studies – so, for example, if we are
lexicographers, then we may view different word forms as
singular objects.
singular statement
A statement composed of a constant about which
something is predicted – and is thus generally limited in
scope. ‘Tony is a man’ may be viewed as a singular
statement where it refers to one of the authors of this book.
By contrast, the all statement ‘All people called Tony are
men’ is not a singular statement.
social context
Speech and writing is produced in social context – it is
produced with social purpose and consumed with social
purpose. Those purposes are, in turn, linked to broader
social processes operating in human society. All this forms
the social context relevant to the understanding and
production of human language.
social science
The study of social phenomena undertaken in a framework
akin to those of the sciences. The approach to the social
sciences in this book is rooted in the belief that the inherent
instability in elements of the social means that a purely
scientiﬁc, replacement naturalism, approach to the social
sciences is not possible. The social sciences are subject to
volition, the natural sciences are not.
solipsism
The belief that the only thing that one knows to actually
exist is oneself.
Appendix 2
283
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 303 -->

speech community
A group of speakers who are relatively homogeneous in
their socially situated linguistic behaviour, for example, the
norms and expectations of usage.
statement
In logic, a statement is a formulation that gives rise to
a truth value of TRUE or FALSE – for example, ‘The Earth is
an oblate spheroid’. Statements may combine to form
logical systems whereby extensive truth conditional
testing may be undertaken, for example, a grammar can
be viewed as a series of statements. Note that, in non-
truth conditional logic, for example, ampliative
reasoning, the nature of the statement may change
fundamentally.
statement equation
A statement in which variables occur that, when
instantiated, provide a function. For example, collocation
may be expressed as a statement equation within which
there are variables that may be instantiated, for example,
span.
statistical significance
An assessment of how likely it to observe an effect of the
given size or even more extreme by chance alone. The
lower that likelihood, the higher the statistical signiﬁcance
we ascribe to the observation.
structuralism
A view of features of social life, including communication,
that believes that these features can only be understood in
terms of their interrelations. These interrelations form
a complex structure (system) that provides the basis for
understanding of these features.
subjectivism
Standing in contrast to objectivism, subjectivism believes
that one’s own mental activity is the only fact of our
existence. Hence personal experience is afforded primacy in
terms of the exploration and generation of knowledge. The
position is strongly associated with scientia rationalis and
a Cartesian worldview.
superset
A set that contains one or more sets.
surprise
The introduction of something new to the process of testing
a theory or hypothesis. The surprise may be unseen data,
such as a test corpus, for example.
system of statements
A collection of statements organised in pursuit of the
exploration of some aspect of reality. These statements
may interact in the attempt to model that feature. Systems
of statements may, in this way, be constitutive of theory.
284
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 304 -->

test corpus
Such corpora arise from the practice of splitting apart
corpora into training corpora, on which hypotheses are
trialled and reﬁned and test corpora, which are then used to
attempt the falsification of hypotheses based upon the
training corpus.
tetradic problem-
solving model
A four-stage problem-solving model developed by Karl
Popper. In Popper’s system problem solving proceeds by
the 1) identiﬁcation of a problem, 2) the development of
a tentative theory to address it, 3) the attempt to eliminate
errors from the theory and 4) the identiﬁcation of
subsequent problems arising from the ﬁrst three steps.
the assumption of
purposefulness
A key assumption of work in the tradition of the Prague
School is purposeful communication.
topic modelling
An approach to the grouping of language data based on
ideas arising originally from information retrieval research.
The approach typically seeks to cluster data and then to
label, where possible, the resulting clusters in some
meaningful way.
total accountability
One of the early guiding principles of corpus linguistics. It is
an injunction not to discard any data when analysing a data
set, for example, when testing a hypothesis on a corpus one
must use all data from the corpus, not select data from it.
Total accountability is clearly linked to Principles 1600, 190
and 290 in this book.
tractable
If a problem is tractable in formal systems, notably
algorithmic systems, it may be efﬁciently dealt with in real
time. Note that inverse, non-tractable problems, are
considered not easily dealt with in real time and may require
computationally expensive, brute force, approaches if they
are to be addressed, and may not be addressable at all.
traditions
Traditions are social norms and practices developed over
time that are transmitted across generations. Unlike
institutions traditions are generally explicitly planned.
training corpus
Such corpora arise from the practice of splitting apart corpora
into training corpora, on which hypotheses are trialled and
reﬁned and test corpora, which are then used to attempt the
falsification of hypotheses based upon the training corpus.
transcription scheme
The process followed to render non-textual language data
in textual form. For example, an orthographic transcription
scheme takes speech and renders the speech as a transcript
Appendix 2
285
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 305 -->

using the standard rules of orthography, usually, though
not necessarily, for the writing system linked to the
language being spoken.
transformationalism
The Chomskyan universalist approach to language.
trend analysis
For the purpose of this book, this refers to the process of
looking at a change across time and trying to discern
a direction in that change. This process links strongly to time-
bound observations – trends may be bounded by time but the
injunction to make time-bound observations may be flouted
by those wishing to use trend analyses to make predictions
about the future based on time-bound observations. While we
make no attempts to do that in this book, it is a use of trend
analyses that readers should be aware of.
triangulation
The process of exploring the same object from a range of
perspectives. Those perspectives may vary by data, method
or theory, for example. Such variation may be undertaken
as part of a test of falsification of sufﬁcient severity.
typicality
Typicality refers to some judgement of the degree to which
something may be generalised and called ‘typical’. Note that
this links to ampliative reasoning and normative
epistemology.
underdetermination
In the context of this book, underdetermination is linked to
the theoretical underdetermination of corpora. Theoretical
underdetermination is extended in this book from the
original, natural science, base of Duhem to cover the social
world in line with Quine’s extension of Duhem’s thinking.
The theoretical underdetermination of corpus data means
that a corpus is not of use in selecting the one and only
theory that may be based upon it – corpus data may, in
principle, support a range of theoretical positions.
universal concepts
A special case of the distinguishing apart of the individual
(e.g. Earth) from a broad class to which it belongs (planet),
in this case with concepts.
universal statement
A hypothesis claimed to be generally true.
universalist
The idea that a concept has universal applicability.
version management
The process of maintaining a record of the differences
between different editions, or versions, of something.
Corpora should ideally be version managed and the version
of a corpus being used should be clearly identiﬁed when
286
Appendix 2
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---

<!-- page 306 -->

reports based upon it are produced. If this is not done, the
process of repetition in particular can be very difﬁcult.
volition
The exercise of will – for example, a speaker may, by
default, seek to produce well-formed utterances but they
may, by the exercise of will, decide to produce ill-formed
utterances instead.
well corroborated
A hypothesis or theory that has withstood multiple sever
(see severity) attempts at falsification may be referred to as
well corroborated.
well formed
Well-formedness usually refers to a judgement of whether
or not the form of an utterance is felicitous with regard to
some system of rules, typically the rules of grammar.
Appendix 2
287
https://doi.org/10.1017/9781107110625.012 Published online by Cambridge University Press

---
